Book I

My mind moves me to tell of forms changed into new bodies. Gods — for you are the ones who changed them — breathe your favor on what I begin, and from the world's first origin draw down one unbroken song, all the way to my own days.

Before there was any sea, or land, or sky that arches over everything, the whole face of nature wore one and the same look across the entire world. Men have named it Chaos: a raw and unsorted mass, nothing but a dead weight, a heap into which the warring seeds of things, badly matched, had all been crammed together. No Sun yet gave the world its light. No Moon renewed her horns by slow growing. No Earth hung balanced in the surrounding air, poised on its own weight, and no Amphitrite had reached out her arms along the far margins of the coasts. Land was there, and sea, and air — but the land could not be stood on, the water could not be swum, the air had no light of its own. Nothing kept its own shape. Everything got in the way of everything else, because within that single body cold fought hot, wet fought dry, soft fought hard, and things with weight fought things with none.

This quarrel a god, and a kinder nature, settled. He cut the earth away from the sky, and the waters away from the earth, and separated the clear heavens from the thick lower air. And once he had untangled these elements and freed them from that blind heap, he bound them, each in its own place, in a harmony that held peace between them. The fiery, weightless force of the domed sky leapt upward and made a place for itself at the very summit. Nearest to it, in lightness and in position, came the air. Denser than these, the earth drew down the heavier elements and was pressed together by its own gravity. The water, flowing around it all, took the lowest place and hemmed in the solid globe.

When the god — whichever of them it was — had thus divided the mass and, so divided, marshaled it into its parts, he first rolled the earth into the shape of a great ball, so that it should not be uneven on any side. Then he ordered the seas to spread out, to swell under the driving winds, and to wrap themselves around the shores of the encircled land. He added springs, and vast ponds, and lakes, and hemmed in the downhill rivers with sloping banks. These, different in different places, are partly swallowed by the earth itself; partly they reach the sea and, taken up into that wider field of freer water, beat against coastlines instead of banks. He commanded the plains to stretch wide, the valleys to sink low, the woods to clothe themselves in leaves, the stony mountains to rise. And just as the heavens are cut by two zones on the right and as many on the left, with a fifth between them hotter than the rest, so the god's care marked out the mass enclosed below with the same number, and the same number of climate-belts are pressed upon the earth. Of these, the middle one is not fit to live in, for its heat; deep snow buries two of them; and between each pair he set two more and gave them a mild climate, blending warmth with cold.

Over these hangs the air, which is heavier than fire by exactly as much as the weight of water is lighter than the weight of earth. There he ordered the mists to settle, and the clouds, and the thunder that would shake the minds of men, and, together with the lightnings, the winds that make the flashes. But even to these the world's maker did not grant the sky to hold at random. Even now, though each of them governs his own gusts in his own quarter, they can barely be kept from tearing the world apart — so fierce is the discord of these brothers. Eurus, the East Wind, drew off toward the dawn and the kingdoms of Nabath and Persia and the mountain ridges laid bare to the morning rays. The West — the evening lands, the shores that grow warm as the sun goes down — these border on Zephyr. Into Scythia and the far North burst the shuddering Boreas; the opposite quarter of the earth grows sodden with endless cloud and the rainy South. Above all these the god set the clear upper sky, the pure aether, weightless and holding not one trace of earthly dregs.

Scarcely had he fenced off everything within its own fixed bounds when the stars, long buried and hidden beneath that blind mass, began to blaze out across the whole of heaven. And so that no region should be left without its own living creatures, the stars and the shapes of the gods took the floor of heaven; the waters fell to the gleaming fish to live in; earth received the wild beasts, and the restless air the birds.

Still missing was a creature holier than these, able to hold a higher mind, one that could rule over the rest. And so Man was born. Either the maker of all things, the seedbed of a better world, fashioned him from divine stuff — or else the young earth, so lately parted from the high aether, still held the seeds of its kindred sky, and the son of Iapetus, Prometheus, mixed that earth with rainwater and molded it into the image of the gods who govern all things. And where the other animals bend their gaze down toward the ground, to Man he gave a face lifted high; he told him to look at the sky and to raise his eyes upright toward the stars. So the earth, which just now had been raw and shapeless, was changed and put on the never-before-seen forms of human beings.

The Golden Age was the first sown. With no one to enforce it, of its own free will, without laws, it kept faith and did right. Punishment and the fear of it were nowhere; no threatening words were read on tablets of fixed bronze, no crowd of the accused trembled before the face of its judge. All were safe with no one to guard them. No pine had yet been felled from its native mountains and gone down into the flowing waves to visit a foreign world, and mortals knew no shores but their own. No sheer moats yet ringed the towns; there was no straight war-trumpet, no coiled horn of bronze, no helmet and no sword. Without any use for soldiers, the untroubled peoples passed their days in soft ease. The earth herself, too, unforced, untouched by the hoe and wounded by no plowshare, gave everything of her own accord; and men, content with food that grew under no compulsion, gathered the fruit of the arbutus and mountain strawberries, cornel-cherries and blackberries clinging to the tough brambles, and the acorns that had fallen from Jupiter's spreading tree. It was an everlasting spring, and the mild west winds caressed with warm breath the flowers that had sprung up from no seed. Before long the unplowed earth was bearing grain as well, and fields that had never lain fallow whitened with heavy ears of wheat. Now rivers of milk, now rivers of nectar ran, and golden honey dripped from the green oak.

Afterward, once Saturn had been sent down into shadowy Tartarus and the world lay under the rule of Jupiter, the Silver Age came on — lower than gold, but more precious than tawny bronze. Jupiter shortened the span of that old-time spring and split the year into four seasons — winters, summers, unsteady autumns, and a brief spring. Then, for the first time, the air burned white with dry heat, and ice hung frozen hard in the winds. Then, for the first time, men went indoors; their houses were caves, dense thickets, and branches lashed together with bark. Then, for the first time, the seeds of Ceres, the grain, were buried in long furrows, and the young oxen groaned under the yoke.

After that age, third in order, followed the Bronze, fiercer in temper and quicker to reach for cruel weapons — yet not wicked outright. The last age was of hard iron. At once every kind of evil broke loose into that age of baser vein: modesty fled, and truth, and good faith, and in their place came fraud and trickery and treachery, and violence, and the criminal hunger to have more. The sailor now spread his sails to winds he still barely knew, and hulls of timber that had long stood high on the mountains now leapt over unknown waters. The land, once shared like sunlight and open air, the wary surveyor now marked off with a long boundary line. Nor was the rich soil asked only for its harvests and the food it owed; men went down into the very guts of the earth, and the wealth she had hidden away and moved close to the ghosts of the underworld was dug up — kindling for evil. And now hurtful iron had come forth, and gold more hurtful than iron; and War came forth, who fights with both, and rattles clashing weapons in his bloody hand. Men lived by plunder. Guest was not safe from host, nor father-in-law from son-in-law; even between brothers goodwill was rare. The husband schemed his wife's death, and she her husband's; ghastly stepmothers brewed pale poisons; and the son inquired into his father's years before their time. Duty and love lay conquered, and the maiden Astraea — Justice herself — last of the immortals, left the earth now soaked in slaughter.

And so that the high aether should be no safer than the earth, the Giants — so the story goes — made a grab for the kingdom of heaven and built the piled-up mountains high, toward the stars. Then the almighty Father hurled his lightning, split Olympus, and blasted Pelion off Ossa beneath it. When the monstrous bodies lay crushed under the ruin they themselves had raised, they say the Earth was drenched, soaked with the streaming blood of her sons; that she gave life to the warm gore; and, so that no trace of her offspring should remain, she turned it into the shape of men. But that breed, too, was a scorner of the gods above, greedy for savage killing, and full of violence: you could tell they were born of blood.

When the Father, Saturn's son, saw all this from his highest citadel, he groaned; and, calling to mind the foul banquet on Lycaon's table — an outrage still fresh and not yet widely known — he conceived a vast anger in his heart, an anger worthy of Jupiter, and summoned a council. No delay held back those he called.

There is a road on high, easy to see when the sky is clear; the Milky Way it is called, marked out by its very whiteness. Along this the gods go up to the halls of the great Thunderer, to his royal house. To the right and the left stand the open-doored courts of the noble gods, thronged with visitors. The lesser gods live scattered in their own quarters; here, along the front, the powerful and famous immortals have set their household shrines. This is the place — if boldness may be given to my words — that I would not be afraid to call the Palatine hill of high heaven. So when the gods had taken their seats in the marble hall, Jupiter himself, raised above them on his throne, leaning on his ivory scepter, shook the awful hair of his head three times and four; and with it he set the earth, the sea, and the stars trembling. Then, in words like these, he opened his indignant lips:

"I was no more anxious for the rule of the world even in that hour when each of the snake-footed monsters was reaching a hundred arms to seize the captured sky. For though that enemy was savage, still the whole war hung from one stock and sprang from a single source. Now, wherever Nereus roars around the whole globe, the race of mortals must be destroyed by me. I swear it by the rivers of the underworld that glide beneath the earth through the Stygian grove: everything else has already been tried, but a wound past healing must be cut away with the blade, so the sound part is not dragged down with it. I have demigods to think of; I have the country spirits, the nymphs, the fauns and satyrs and the woodland gods of the hills. Since we have not yet thought them worthy of the honor of heaven, we should at least let them live safely on the earth we have given them. But can you truly believe, gods, that they will be safe, when Lycaon — a man known for his savagery — has laid a plot against me, against the very one who holds and wields the thunderbolt and rules over you?"

Every god roared, and with burning zeal they demanded punishment for the man who had dared such a thing. It was just so when an unholy gang raged to blot out the Roman name in Caesar's blood: the human race stood stunned with sudden terror at so great a ruin, and the whole world shuddered. And the loyal love of your people, Augustus, was no less dear to you than that of the gods was to Jupiter. When he had checked their outcry with voice and hand, all of them fell silent. Once the clamor had died, held down by the weight of their ruler's authority, Jupiter broke the stillness again with these words:

"He has paid his penalty already — set that worry aside. But what he did, and what the punishment was, I will tell you. Word of the age's infamy had reached my ears. Hoping to find it false, I came down from the peak of Olympus and, a god in human shape, walked the earth. It would take too long to count up how much guilt was found everywhere: the rumor itself fell short of the truth.

"I had crossed Maenalus, dreaded for its dens of beasts, and Cyllene, and the pine woods of cold Lycaeus. From there, as the late dusk was drawing on the night, I entered the realm and the unwelcoming house of the Arcadian tyrant. I gave a sign that a god had come, and the common people began to pray. Lycaon first mocked their pious prayers; then he said, 'I'll find out by a clear test whether this fellow is a god or a mortal — and the truth will not be in doubt.' He made ready to kill me by surprise in the night, sunk in sleep: that was his idea of testing the truth. Not content with that, he took a hostage sent to him from the Molossian people, cut his throat with a blade, and softened part of the still-twitching limbs in boiling water while he roasted the rest over a fire. The moment he set this on the table, I, with avenging flame, brought his house down on its household gods — gods that deserved such a master. Terrified, he himself fled. He reached the silence of the fields and howled, and tried in vain to speak. His mouth drew rabid fury from himself, and, in his old lust for slaughter, he turned it against the flocks and even now delights in blood. His clothes became shaggy hair; his arms became legs. He is a wolf, and he keeps the traces of his former shape: the same gray, the same violence in his face, the same eyes glinting, the same look of savagery. One house has fallen — but one house alone did not deserve to die. As far as the earth reaches, the savage Fury reigns. You would think men had sworn an oath to do evil! Let all of them, and quickly, suffer the punishment they have earned — for that is my judgment."

Some of the gods approved Jupiter's words aloud and spurred him on in his anger; others played their part with silent assent. Still, the destruction of the whole human race grieved every one of them, and they asked what the earth would look like once emptied of mortals — who would lay incense on the altars? Did he mean to hand the world over to the wild beasts to ravage? To their questions the king of the gods answered that they should not be alarmed — the rest would be his care — and he promised to raise, from a marvelous source, a people unlike the one before.

And now he was about to scatter his lightning bolts over all the lands; but he feared that the sacred aether might catch fire from so many flames and the whole long vault of the sky burst into blaze. He remembered, too, that it stood in the decrees of Fate that a time would come when sea and land and the seized palace of heaven would all burn, and the hard-built mass of the world would strain in its agony. So the weapons forged by the Cyclopes' hands were laid aside. A different punishment pleased him: to destroy the mortal race beneath the waves and to loose the rains from every quarter of the sky.

At once he shut the North Wind up in the caves of Aeolus, along with every gust that drives the gathered clouds away, and sent out the South Wind. Out flew the South Wind on soaking wings, his terrible face wrapped in pitch-black gloom. His beard was heavy with storm; water streamed from his gray hair; mists sat on his brow; his wings and the folds of his robe dripped. And as he squeezed the hanging clouds in his broad hand, there came a crash, and dense rains poured down from the sky. Iris, the messenger of Juno, robed in her many colors, drew up water and carried it back as food for the clouds. The standing crops were beaten flat; the farmer's prayers lay ruined and mourned; and the work of a long year was lost, gone for nothing.

Jupiter's anger was not content with his own sky; his blue-gray brother Neptune helped him with reinforcing waves. He called the rivers together, and once they had come into their king's house he said, "There's no time now for a long speech. Pour out all your strength — the crisis demands it. Open your homes, and with every barrier thrown aside, give your streams full rein." So he ordered; and they went back, opened wide the mouths of their springs, and rolled toward the sea in an unbridled race. Neptune himself struck the earth with his trident, and it shuddered, and with the shock laid open the channels of the waters. The rivers burst their bounds and rushed across the open fields, sweeping away orchards along with the crops, flocks and men, houses, and temples too, with their sacred things. If any house stood firm and, unthrown, could resist so great a ruin, still the water rose higher than its roof and covered it, and the towers lay hidden, drowned beneath the flood. And now sea and land had no boundary between them: everything was ocean, and even the ocean had no shores.

One man seized a hilltop; another sat in his curved boat and pulled the oars where he had just been plowing; another sailed over his grainfields or the roof of his drowned farmhouse; another caught a fish in the top of an elm. An anchor, if chance so dropped it, bit into a green meadow, or curved keels grazed the vineyards below them. Where slender goats had lately cropped the grass, ungainly seals now laid down their bodies. The sea-nymphs, the Nereids, marveled at woods and cities and houses under the water; dolphins slipped into the forests and rammed the high branches and struck the shaken oaks. The wolf swam among the sheep; the wave carried tawny lions along, the wave carried tigers along. The lightning-force of his tusks did the boar no good now, nor his swift legs the stag, both swept away. And the wandering bird, long looking for land to light on, at last fell into the sea on failing wings. The boundless riot of the ocean had buried the hills, and unfamiliar waves beat against the mountaintops. The greatest part were carried off by the water; and those the water spared, long fasting broke, worn down by the want of food.

Phocis separates the fields of Aonia from those of Oeta — a fertile land, while it was land, but in that hour a part of the sea and a broad plain of waters that had risen all at once. There a steep mountain reaches toward the stars with two peaks, Parnassus by name, and its summit rises above the clouds. When Deucalion, in a small boat with the partner of his bed, ran aground here — for the sea had covered everywhere else — the two of them prayed to the Corycian nymphs and the gods of the mountain and to prophetic Themis, who at that time kept the oracle. No man was ever better than Deucalion, none more devoted to what was right, and no woman more reverent of the gods than Pyrrha.

When Jupiter saw the world one standing marsh of water, and saw that out of so many thousands only one man remained, out of so many thousands only one woman — both harmless, both worshippers of the gods — he scattered the clouds, and, the storms swept off by the North Wind, he showed the earth to the sky again and the sky to the earth. The sea's anger did not last either. Laying aside his three-pronged spear, the ruler of the deep calmed the waters and called on sea-green Triton, who stood up out of the deep with his shoulders crusted in native shellfish. He told him to blow his echoing horn and, at that signal, to call the waves and the rivers back. Triton took up his hollow, spiraling trumpet, the one that widens in coils from its narrow base — the trumpet that, once it has drawn in the sea's own air, fills the shores lying under either sunrise and sunset with its voice. Now, too, as soon as it touched the god's lips, wet with his dripping beard, and, filled with breath, sounded the ordered retreat, it was heard by all the waters of land and sea, and every water that heard it, it held in check. Now the sea had a shore again; the channels held their brimming rivers; the streams sank; the hills seemed to rise into view. The ground came up, the dry places spread as the waters shrank, and after a long day the woods showed their bare tops, holding the mud still left on their branches.

The world was restored. When Deucalion saw it emptied, and saw the desolate lands keeping a deep silence, tears welled up and he spoke to Pyrrha: "O sister, O wife, O only woman left alive, whom a shared descent and a common ancestor and then the marriage bed joined to me, and whom now our very dangers join — of all the lands the East and the West look upon, we two are the whole population. The sea has taken all the rest. And even now our hold on life is not sure enough; the clouds still frighten my mind. What would your heart be now, poor woman, if you had been snatched from destruction without me? How could you bear this terror alone? Who would comfort you in your grief? For I — believe me — if the sea had taken you too, I would have followed you, wife, and the sea would have taken me as well. If only I could remake the peoples by my father's arts and breathe souls into the molded earth! As it is, the mortal race survives in us two alone. So it has seemed good to the gods, and we remain as the last examples of mankind."

He finished, and they wept. They resolved to pray to the powers of heaven and to seek help through the sacred oracle. Without delay they went together to the waters of the Cephisus, not yet clear but already cutting their familiar channel. When they had sprinkled the drawn water on their clothes and their heads, they turned their steps to the temple of the holy goddess, whose gables were pale with ugly moss and whose altars stood without fire. As they reached the temple steps, each fell face-down on the ground and, trembling, kissed the cold stone. And they said: "If the gods can be softened, won over by just prayers — if the anger of heaven can be bent — tell us, Themis, by what art the ruin of our race can be repaired, and bring help, most gentle one, to a drowned world."

The goddess was moved, and gave this answer: "Leave the temple, veil your heads, loosen your girded robes, and throw the bones of your great mother behind your backs." For a long time they stood stunned. Pyrrha was the first to break the silence, refusing to obey the goddess's command; with trembling lips she begged pardon, afraid to wrong her mother's ghost by casting her bones. Meanwhile they turned over and over between them the dark, riddling words of the oracle they had been given. At last the son of Prometheus soothed the daughter of Epimetheus with gentle words: "Either my wit is deceiving me, or — since oracles are holy and never urge a crime — our great mother is the earth. The stones in the earth's body, I think, are what she calls her bones; those are what we are told to throw behind our backs." The daughter of Titan was moved by her husband's reading, yet her hope hung in doubt — so little did they both trust the warnings of heaven. But what harm in trying?

They went down; they veiled their heads, ungirded their tunics, and threw the stones, as ordered, behind their steps. And the stones — who would believe it, if the old tradition were not a witness? — began to lay aside their hardness and their stiffness, and little by little to soften, and, once softened, to take on a shape. Soon, when they had grown larger and a gentler nature had come over them, a kind of human form could be seen in them — not clear, but like a figure begun in marble, not yet finished off, very like rough-cut statues. The part of them that was damp with any moisture, and earthy, was turned into the stuff of a body; what was solid and could not bend changed into bone; what had just been a vein kept the same name still. And in a short while, by the will of the gods above, the stones thrown by the man's hands took on the look of men, and from the woman's throwing woman was made anew. That is why we are a hard race, well used to toil, and give proof of the source we sprang from.

The rest of the animals, in their different forms, the earth brought forth of her own accord, once the old moisture had been warmed through by the fire of the sun, and the mud and the wet marshes swelled with the heat, and the fertile seeds of things, nursed in the life-giving soil as in a mother's womb, grew and, given time, took on some shape. So it is when the seven-mouthed Nile draws back from the sodden fields and returns its waters to their old bed, and the fresh silt burns under the heavenly star: the farmers, turning the clods, come upon countless living things — some just begun, at the very moment of birth, some still unfinished and short of their limbs; and often, in the same body, one part is alive while the other is raw earth. For where moisture and heat have been mixed in just measure, they conceive, and from these two all things are born. And though fire fights with water, a moist warmth creates all things, and that discordant harmony is right for bringing life. So when the earth, still muddy from the recent flood, glowed hot again beneath the heavenly sun and a deep, soaking warmth, she gave birth to countless kinds — partly bringing back the old shapes, partly creating new monsters.

Against her will she bore you, too, then, enormous Python: a serpent never seen before, a terror to the newborn peoples, so much of the mountainside you covered. The bow-bearing god, Apollo, who had never before used such weapons on anything but fleeing deer and does, all but emptied his quiver upon him, laying him low with a thousand arrows as the venom oozed out through the black wounds. And so that the passing of years should not blot out the fame of the deed, he founded sacred games with famous contests, the Pythian games, named for the serpent he had conquered. There, whoever of the young men won with hand or foot or chariot-wheel took as his prize a wreath of oak leaves. There was no laurel yet, and Phoebus would bind his temples, lovely under their long hair, with garlands from any tree at all.

Daphne, the daughter of the river-god Peneus, was Apollo's first love — and it was no blind accident that gave her to him, but the savage anger of Cupid. Apollo, still flushed with pride from killing the serpent, had lately seen the boy bending his bow, drawing the string taut, and had jeered at him. "What are you doing with a grown man's weapons, you spoiled child?" he said. "Gear like that suits my shoulders — mine, who can deal a wild beast an unerring wound, deal a wound to an enemy, who just now laid low the bloated Python with a thousand arrows, that plague sprawled over so many acres with its poisonous belly. Be content to stir up who-knows-what little love affairs with your torch, and don't go claiming glory that belongs to me."

The son of Venus answered him: "Your bow may hit everything else, Apollo, but mine will hit you. And as much as every animal is beneath a god, that much less is your glory than mine." So he spoke, and beating the air with his wings he lit, quick and eager, on the shaded height of Parnassus, and drew two arrows out of his quiver — two arrows of opposite work. One kindles love; the other drives it off. The one that kindles it is gold, and its point glints sharp; the one that drives it off is blunt, with lead set under the reed. This second arrow the god fixed in the nymph, the daughter of Peneus; with the first he wounded Apollo, driving it clean through his bones into the marrow. At once the one is in love; the other flees the very word "lover," happy in the deep hiding-places of the woods and in the pelts of the beasts she has caught, a rival of the virgin Diana. A single band held back her hair, tied up without any care for arrangement. Many men courted her; she turned every suitor away, and, wanting no part of a man, unacquainted with men altogether, she roamed the trackless forests and cared nothing for what marriage, or love, or a wedding might be. Time and again her father said to her, "Daughter, you owe me a son-in-law." Time and again her father said, "Daughter, you owe me grandchildren." But she, hating the wedding torch as though it were something shameful, would flush that lovely face with a modest blush and, hanging on her father's neck with coaxing arms, would say, "Dearest father, let me enjoy my virginity forever. Diana's father granted her exactly this." He gave in, of course. But that very beauty forbids you to be what you wish, Daphne, and your loveliness fights against your prayer.

Apollo is in love. He has seen Daphne, and he wants to marry her, and what he wants, he expects to have — his own oracles fail him. And as light stubble burns once the ears of grain are stripped away, as a hedge catches fire from a torch that some traveler has held too close, or left behind at daybreak, so the god went up in flames — so his whole heart caught fire, and he fed a hopeless love on hope. He looks at her hair falling unadorned along her neck and thinks, "And what if it were done up?" He sees her eyes, glittering like stars with their own fire. He sees her lips, and it is not enough merely to see them. He praises her fingers, her hands, her wrists, her arms bare well past the elbow — and whatever is hidden he imagines to be lovelier still. But she flees, quicker than the light wind, and does not stop when he calls after her:

"Nymph, daughter of Peneus, wait — please! I am no enemy chasing you down. Nymph, wait! This is how the lamb runs from the wolf, the deer from the lion, the dove on trembling wing from the eagle — each one fleeing its enemy. But love is why I follow you. I am wretched! Don't fall on your face, don't let the brambles scratch those legs that never deserved a hurt — don't let me be the cause of your pain. The ground you race across is rough. Slow down, I beg you, hold back your flight, and I will follow more slowly too. Only ask yourself who it is you please. I am no mountain rough, no herdsman; I am not some shaggy creature guarding cattle and flocks out here. You don't know, rash girl — you don't know who it is you're running from, and that is the only reason you run. The land of Delphi serves me, and Claros, and Tenedos, and the royal house of Patara. Jupiter is my father. Through me is revealed whatever will be, whatever has been, whatever is; through me song comes into tune with the strings. My arrow flies true — yet there is one arrow truer than mine, the one that has struck this wound in my untouched heart. Medicine is my invention; all over the world I am called the bringer of help, and the power of every healing herb is mine to command. But look — there is no herb that can cure love, and the arts that heal everyone else are no use to their own master."

He would have said more, but the daughter of Peneus fled in fear and left him there, his words unfinished, and left the god behind along with them. Even then she looked lovely. The wind bared her body; the gusts came against her and set her clothes rippling, and a light breeze blew her hair out behind her — her beauty was only heightened by the flight. But the young god will not waste his sweet talk any longer. As love itself urged him on, he ran after her at full stride. Like a Gallic hound that has spotted a hare in an open field — the one straining after its prey, the other after its life; the dog, seeming just about to close, hopes at every stride to have her now, this instant, and grazes her heels with his outstretched muzzle, while the hare, unsure whether she is already caught, is torn from the very bite and slips clear of the mouth just touching her — so ran the god and the girl, he swift with hope, she with fear. Yet the pursuer, borne up on the wings of love, is faster; he gives her no rest, looms right over her fleeing back, and breathes on the hair scattered across her neck. Her strength was spent; she went pale, and, worn out by the effort of that headlong run, she looked toward the waters of Peneus and cried, "Help me, father! If you rivers hold any divine power, destroy this shape by which I have pleased too well — change it, take it away!"

The prayer was barely finished when a heavy numbness seizes her limbs. A thin bark closes over her soft breast; her hair lengthens into leaves, her arms into branches; her feet, so swift a moment ago, stick fast in sluggish roots; a treetop takes the place of her face. Only her shining beauty is left. Even so Apollo loves her. He lays his hand against the trunk and feels her heart still fluttering under the new bark, and folding the branches in his arms as if they were limbs, he kisses the wood — and even the wood shrinks back from his kisses. "Since you cannot be my wife," the god said to her, "you will at least be my tree. My hair will always wear you, laurel, and my lyre, and my quiver. You will stand with the Roman generals when the glad voice sings out the triumph and the long processions climb to the Capitol. You will guard the gateposts of Augustus, standing most faithful before his doors, keeping watch over the oak wreath set between them. And just as my head is young, my hair uncut, so you too shall wear the everlasting honor of your leaves." Apollo had finished. The laurel nodded with its new-made branches and seemed to shake its crown just like a head.

There is a grove in Thessaly, closed off on every side by a forest set on steep rock. They call it Tempe. Through it the river Peneus, pouring down from the foot of Mount Pindus, rolls along in foaming waters, and in its heavy fall it gathers clouds that scatter a fine mist like smoke, sprinkling the tops of the woods with spray and wearying places far off with its roar. This is the home, this the seat, these the innermost chambers of the great river. Here, in a cave hewn from the cliffs, he sat and gave law to the waters and to the nymphs who dwell in them. Here first the local rivers gather, not knowing whether to congratulate the father or to console him: the poplar-lined Spercheus, the restless Enipeus, old Apidanus, gentle Amphrysus, and Aeas — and soon after, the other streams that, wherever their current has taken them, lead their wave-weary waters down to the sea. Only Inachus is absent. Hidden deep in his cavern, he swells his own waters with weeping and mourns his daughter Io as lost — most wretched of all. He does not know whether she is still alive or already among the shades; but since he cannot find her anywhere, he believes she is nowhere, and in his heart he dreads worse.

Jupiter had seen her as she came back from her father's stream, and had said, "You girl, worthy of Jove, who will make some lucky man happy in your bed — come to the shade of these deep woods" (and he pointed out the shade of the woods) "while the day is hot and the sun stands at the very peak of its course. And if you are afraid to go alone into the haunts of wild beasts, you will enter the secret places of the grove under the protection of a god — and no common god, but the one who holds heaven's great scepter in his hand, who hurls the wandering lightnings. Don't run from me!" For she was already running. She had left behind the pastures of Lerna and the tree-planted fields of Lyrcea when the god cloaked the wide land in a rolling darkness, halted her flight, and took her modesty by force.

Meanwhile Juno looked down into the middle of Argos, and, puzzled that fast-moving clouds had made the look of night in the bright of day, she saw that these were not river-mists and were not rising from any wet ground; and she looked around for her husband, knowing all too well by now the schemes of a mate she had caught so often. When she did not find him in heaven, she said, "Either I am mistaken, or I am being wronged," and gliding down from the top of the sky she came to rest on the earth and ordered the clouds to draw back. He had foreseen his wife's arrival and had already changed the daughter of Inachus into a sleek heifer. Even as a cow she is beautiful. Juno, daughter of Saturn, admires the look of the animal, however grudgingly, and asks — as if she did not know the truth — whose it is, and where it came from, and what herd it belongs to. Jupiter lies, says it was born from the earth, so she will stop asking after its owner. Then Juno asks for the cow as a gift. What can he do? To hand over his own beloved is cruel; not to give her up looks suspicious. Shame urges him one way, love argues him the other. Shame would have lost to love — but if so trifling a gift as a cow were refused to his own sister and wife, it might well seem to be no cow at all.

Even with her rival handed over, the goddess did not put aside all her fear at once. She was still wary of Jupiter, still afraid of a theft, until she gave Io into the keeping of Argus, the son of Aristor. Argus had a head ringed with a hundred eyes. Two of them took their rest in turn while the rest kept watch and stayed on duty. However he stood, he was looking toward Io; even with his back turned he had Io before his eyes. By day he lets her graze; when the sun has sunk below the deep earth, he shuts her in and ties a rope around her undeserving neck. She feeds on the leaves of trees and on bitter grass, and for a bed the poor thing lies on ground that is not always even grassy, and drinks from muddy streams. When she wanted to stretch her arms out to Argus in appeal, she had no arms to stretch; and when she tried to voice her grief, only a lowing came out of her mouth — and she was terrified at the sound, frightened by her own voice. She came, too, to the banks where she used to play, the banks of her father Inachus, and when she saw her new horns in the water she recoiled from herself in terror and ran. The Naiads do not know her; Inachus himself does not know who she is. But she follows her father, and follows her sisters, and lets herself be touched, and offers herself to their admiring hands. Old Inachus plucked some grass and held it out; she licks his hand, kisses her father's palms, and cannot hold back her tears — and if only words would come, she would beg for help, would tell her name and her disaster. Instead of words, letters that her hoof drew in the dust told the sad story of her changed body. "Oh, wretched me!" cries her father Inachus, and clinging to the horns and the snow-white neck of the moaning heifer, he says it again: "Wretched me! Are you my daughter, that I searched for across every land? Undiscovered, you were a lighter grief to me than now, found. You say nothing; you give no words back to mine; you only heave sighs from deep in your chest, and — the only thing you can do — you answer my words with a low. And I, knowing nothing, was preparing a bridal chamber and wedding torches for you. My first hope was a son-in-law, my second grandchildren. Now you must take a mate from the herd, now your children come from the herd. And I cannot even end so great a grief by dying — it is a curse to be a god, for the gate of death is shut against me, and my mourning stretches on into ages without end." As he grieved, the star-eyed Argus dragged her away, tore the daughter from her father and led her off to distant pastures. He himself took up a high mountain peak far off and sat there, keeping watch on every side.

The ruler of the gods can bear the granddaughter of Phoroneus's suffering no longer. He calls his son Mercury, whom the shining Pleiad Maia bore, and orders him to put Argus to death. There is little delay in strapping the wings to his feet, taking the sleep-bringing wand in his strong hand, and setting the cap on his hair. Once these were in order, the son of Jupiter leapt down from his father's high citadel to the earth. There he took off the cap and laid aside the wings, keeping only the wand. With this, dressed like a shepherd, he drives a flock of she-goats he has rounded up along the byways through the wild country, and plays as he goes on a set of reed pipes. The keeper Juno had set on guard was charmed by this strange new sound. "Whoever you are," Argus said, "you're welcome to sit here with me on this rock. Nowhere is the grass richer for your flock, and you can see how good the shade is for a shepherd." So the grandson of Atlas, Mercury, sat down, and passed the daylight in a long, easy stream of talk, and by playing on his joined reeds tried to overcome the watching eyes. But Argus fought to master soft sleep, and though slumber had taken part of his eyes, with the rest he still kept watch. He asked, too — since the pipe had only lately been invented — how it had come to be discovered.

Then the god said: "In the cold mountains of Arcadia, among the wood-nymphs of Nonacris, there was one water-nymph famous above the rest. The nymphs called her Syrinx. More than once she had slipped away from the satyrs who chased her, and from every god the shadowy forest and the fertile fields hold. In her devotions and in her virginity she followed the goddess of Ortygia — Diana; dressed after Diana's fashion, she could fool anyone and be taken for Latona's daughter, if only her bow were not of horn and Diana's of gold. Even so she deceived them. As she came back from the hill of Lycaeus, Pan caught sight of her, his head wreathed in sharp pine, and he spoke these words to her —" It remained to tell the words, and how the nymph, scorning his pleas, fled through pathless country until she came to the calm stream of sandy Ladon; how there, the waters blocking her run, she begged her watery sisters to change her; and how Pan, when he thought he had caught Syrinx at last, found himself holding not the nymph's body but marsh reeds; and how, while he sighed in disappointment, the breeze stirring in the reeds made a thin sound, like someone in mourning; and how the god, captivated by this new art and the sweetness of the sound, said, "This is a way I can keep talking with you, at least" — and so, with reeds of different lengths joined by wax, he kept the girl's name. But as the Cyllenian god was about to tell all this, he saw that every one of Argus's eyes had given way, his sight buried in sleep. At once he stops his voice and deepens the slumber, stroking those drowsy eyes with his enchanted wand. Without a pause he strikes the nodding head where it joins the neck with his hooked sword, and hurls the bloodied thing down from the rock, staining the sheer cliff with gore. Argus, you lie low; the light you carried in so many eyes is put out, and one night has taken your whole hundred at once.

Juno takes them up and sets them on the feathers of her own bird, filling its tail with star-bright jewels. At once she blazed with rage, and did not put off the hour of her fury. She loosed a horrifying Fury before the eyes and mind of her Argive rival, drove invisible goads deep into her breast, and hounded her, a fugitive, across the whole world. You, O Nile, were the last thing left at the end of her endless labor. The moment she reached you, she dropped to her knees at the water's edge, threw back her neck, and — lifting to the stars the only thing she could still lift, her face — with her groans and her tears and her grieving lows she seemed to plead with Jupiter and to beg an end to her miseries. He threw his arms around his wife's neck and asked her, at last, to end the punishment. "Set aside your fears for the days to come," he said. "She will never again be a cause of grief to you." And he called the Stygian marshes to hear the oath.

Once the goddess was soothed, Io takes back her earlier form and becomes what she was before. The bristles fall away from her body; her horns shrink; the round of her eye grows smaller; her gaping jaw draws in; her shoulders and her hands come back; the hoof, dissolving, is lost and becomes five nails again. Nothing of the cow is left in her but the whiteness of her skin. Content now with the use of two feet, the nymph stands upright — yet she is afraid to speak, in case she should low like a heifer, and shyly tries out the words she has gone so long without. Now, as a goddess, she is worshipped by the linen-robed crowds of Egypt.

To her at last, it is believed, Epaphus was born from the seed of great Jupiter, and throughout the cities he holds temples set beside his mother's. He had a friend, Phaethon, child of the Sun, his equal in spirit and in years. Once, when Phaethon was boasting grandly and would not yield an inch, all puffed up about his father Apollo, the grandson of Inachus could not stand it. "You believe your mother about everything, you fool," he said, "and you're swollen with pride over a father who isn't real." Phaethon blushed and swallowed his anger in shame, and carried Epaphus's taunts to his mother Clymene. "To hurt you all the more, mother," he said, "I — the bold, the free-spoken one — held my tongue. I'm ashamed that such an insult could be flung at us and that we couldn't throw it back. But you — if I really am born of heavenly stock — give me some proof of that high descent, and claim me for the sky." So he spoke, and threw his arms around his mother's neck, and begged her by his own life, and by the life of Merops her husband, and by the wedding torches of his sisters, to give him some sign of his true father. Clymene — moved, perhaps, by Phaethon's pleading, or perhaps more by anger at the slander against her — stretched both arms toward the sky and, gazing up at the light of the sun, said, "By that radiance bright with darting rays, my son, which both hears and sees us, I swear to you: you were fathered by this Sun that you are looking at, this Sun that governs the world. If I am lying, let him refuse ever to be seen by me again, and let this daylight be the last my eyes ever know. And it is no long journey for you to find your father's home. The house where he rises borders on our own land. If you have the heart for it, go, and ask him yourself." At once Phaethon springs up, overjoyed at his mother's words, and heaven is already his in his mind. He passes by his own Ethiopians and the Indians who live beneath the sun's fires, and eagerly makes his way toward his father's rising.

Book II

The palace of the Sun rose high on soaring columns, ablaze with glittering gold and with bronze-red pyrope that mimics living flame. Polished ivory sheathed its topmost gables, and its double doors threw back the light from panels of silver. But the craftsmanship outshone even the costly stuff it was made of. For there Vulcan, the fire-god and smith of the sky, had chased into the metal the seas that ring the enclosing lands, and the round world, and the heavens that arch over the world. The waves hold their sea-blue gods: Triton blowing his echoing shell; shifting Proteus, who is never twice the same; Aegaeon, his arms flung across the vast backs of whales; and Doris and her daughters, the sea-nymphs — some of them shown swimming, some sitting on a rock to dry their green hair, some riding on the backs of fish. Their faces are not all one face, and yet not wholly unlike — just as sisters' faces should be. The land bears men and cities and forests, wild beasts and rivers, nymphs and all the other spirits of the countryside. And over them all is set the image of the shining sky, with six signs of the zodiac on the right-hand door and six on the left.

By a steep, climbing path the son of Clymene came at last to that place and stepped inside the house of the father he had only been promised. At once he turned his steps toward his father's face — and stopped a long way off, for he could not bear that light any nearer. Wrapped in a purple robe, Phoebus the Sun sat on a throne bright with clear-cut emeralds. To his right and his left stood the Day, the Month, the Year, the Ages, and the Hours, ranged at even spaces; young Spring stood there, wreathed in a crown of flowers; Summer stood naked, wearing garlands of grain; Autumn stood stained with the trodden juice of grapes; and icy Winter, his white hair stiff and shaggy.

From his place at the center, the Sun — with those eyes that see all things — saw the young man flinching at the strangeness of it all, and said, "What has brought you all this way? What do you come seeking in this high house, Phaethon — a son no father would deny?"

He answered, "Light shared by the whole measureless world, Phoebus, my father — if you let me use that name, if Clymene is not hiding some fault behind a lie — give me a token, father, some proof that lets the world believe I am truly your child; take this doubt out of my heart." So he spoke; and his father laid aside the blaze of rays that ringed his head and told the boy to come closer. He folded him in his arms and said, "You do not deserve to be denied as mine, and Clymene has told you your true birth. And so that you may doubt no longer — ask any gift you like, and you shall have it from my hand. Let the marsh the gods swear by be witness to my promise, the pool my own eyes have never seen."

Scarcely had he finished when the boy asked for his father's chariot, and for the right to drive the wing-footed horses for a single day.

His father repented the oath. Three times, four times he shook his shining head, and said, "Your words have made mine reckless. If only I were free not to grant what I promised! I confess it, son — this one thing, and only this, I would refuse you. But I am still allowed to argue you out of it. What you want is not safe. You ask for something too great, Phaethon, a gift that suits neither your strength nor your boyish years. Your lot is a mortal's; what you long for is not for mortals. In your ignorance you reach even beyond what the gods above are allowed. Let each of them please himself as he likes — still, not one of them but me can stand on the fire-bearing axle. Even the ruler of vast Olympus, who hurls the savage thunderbolts with his terrible right hand, could not drive this chariot; and what have we greater than Jupiter?

"The first stretch of road is steep, so steep that the horses, fresh in the morning, can barely struggle up it. At the very top of the sky it runs highest of all, and even I am often afraid to look down from there on the sea and the land, and my heart pounds with trembling dread. The last stretch drops sheer, and needs a sure hand on the reins; then even Tethys, the sea that takes me in below, is afraid I will come plunging headlong down. And the whole sky is forever wheeling round, dragging the high stars with it and spinning them in a rushing whirl. I drive against it — that force which overpowers all else does not overpower me — and I ride the opposite way to the racing world. Suppose the chariot were yours: what would you do? Could you go against the spinning poles and keep the swift axle from sweeping you away? Perhaps you picture groves up there, and cities of the gods, and temples rich with offerings? The way runs through ambushes, and the shapes of savage beasts! Even if you hold your course and are led astray by no wandering, still you must pass through the horns of the Bull that fronts you, past the Thessalian archer's bow and the jaws of the raging Lion, past the Scorpion curving his cruel arms in one long sweep and the Crab that curves his arms the other way. And the horses, hot with the fire they carry in their chests and breathe out from mouth and nostrils — those you will not easily rule. Even I can barely hold them when their fierce spirit heats up and their necks fight the reins.

"But you, my son — take care I am not the giver of a deadly gift; while there is still time, mend what you are asking for. You want a sure proof that you are sprung from my blood? I give you a sure proof by my fear for you: by a father's dread I am shown to be your father. Look — look at my face! I wish you could sink your eyes into my breast and catch the father's cares inside it. And, last of all — look round you at everything the rich world holds, and out of all the blessings of heaven and earth and sea, so many and so great, ask for anything; you shall meet no refusal. This one thing only I beg to be let off, which by its true name is a punishment, not an honor. You are asking, Phaethon, for a punishment dressed up as a gift. Why do you throw your coaxing arms around my neck, you foolish boy? Have no doubt — whatever you choose will be given; I have sworn it by the waters of the Styx. But choose more wisely!"

He had finished his warnings; but the boy fought them off all the same, held to his purpose, and burned with longing for the chariot. So the father, having stalled as long as he could, led the young man out to the tall chariot, Vulcan's handiwork. The axle was gold, the pole was gold, gold ran round the rims of the wheels, and the spokes were ranged in silver. Along the yoke, chrysolites and gems set in rows threw the bright light back to Phoebus above. And while the high-hearted Phaethon marveled at the work and studied it, look — wakeful Dawn threw open her purple doors in the reddening east and her halls full of roses. The stars fled; the Morning Star herded the last of their ranks and was himself the last to quit his post in the sky.

When the Sun saw him make for the earth, and the world turning red, and the horns of the waning Moon seem to melt away, he ordered the swift Hours to yoke the horses. The goddesses did his bidding quickly, leading the beasts from their lofty stalls — snorting flame, gorged on the juice of ambrosia — and buckling on the ringing bits. Then the father touched his son's face with a holy salve and made it able to bear the scorching flame; he set the rays on the boy's hair, and, drawing sigh after sigh from a heart that already foreknew grief, he said:

"If you can obey at least this much of your father's advice — go easy on the whip, boy, and pull hard on the reins. They race of their own accord; the work is to hold them back when they want to run. And do not let the road tempt you straight across the five circles of the sky. There is a track cut slantwise in a broad curve, kept within the bounds of three zones, that shuns the far southern pole and the Bear that keeps to the north wind — go that way; you will see the clear ruts of my wheels. And so that heaven and earth may share the heat alike, do not press too low, and do not force the chariot up through the highest air. Go too high, and you will scorch the roofs of heaven; too low, and you will burn the earth. The middle way is safest. Do not let the wheel veer right toward the coiled Serpent, or the left carry you down to the low Altar. Hold between the two. The rest I leave to Fortune, and I pray she helps you and looks after you better than you look after yourself. While I have been speaking, damp Night has touched her turning-post on the western shore. We are not free to wait; I am called for. Dawn is blazing out, the darkness put to flight. Take the reins in your hand — or, if your heart can still be changed, use my advice and not my chariot, while you still can, while you still stand on solid ground, before you have climbed, in your ignorance, into the axle you so wrongly wanted. Let me give the light to the earth — a light you can watch from a safe place!"

But the boy had already sprung into the light chariot with his young body, and stood tall, and rejoiced to take the light reins in his hands, and thanked his unwilling father for them.

Meanwhile the Sun's winged horses — Pyroeis and Eous and Aethon, and Phlegon the fourth — filled the air with fiery neighing and beat their hooves against the barriers. When Tethys, knowing nothing of her grandson's fate, pushed the barriers back and gave them the whole open sky, they leapt to the road, and, working their feet through the air, tore through the clouds that blocked them, and, lifted on their wings, outran the east winds that had risen from the same quarter. But the load was light — too light for the Sun's horses to feel — and the yoke lacked its usual weight. As curved ships without their proper ballast reel and are carried unsteady over the sea for their very lightness, so the chariot, empty of its accustomed burden, bounded up into the air, tossed high and pitching like a thing with nothing in it.

The moment the team felt it, they bolted, left the beaten track, and no longer ran in their old order. The boy himself was in a panic. He did not know which way to turn the reins put into his hands, did not know where the road lay, and, if he had known, could not have made them obey. Then, for the first time, the cold Wain-stars — the northern Bears — grew warm under the sunbeams, and tried in vain to dip themselves in a sea forbidden to them. The Serpent that lies coiled nearest the frozen pole, sluggish before with cold and a threat to no one, grew hot and took a new fury from the heat. And you too, Boötes, they say, fled in confusion, slow as you were and held back by your wagon.

But when the luckless Phaethon looked down from the roof of the sky at the lands lying far, so far below, he went white, and his knees shook with sudden fear, and in all that light darkness swam across his eyes. Now he wished he had never touched his father's horses; now he was sorry he had learned his birth and had won his own request; now, longing to be called the son of Merops, he was swept along like a ship driven by a screaming north wind, whose pilot has thrown down the helm he can no longer master and left her to the gods and his prayers. What could he do? Much of the sky lay behind him now, but more of it stretched ahead. He measured both in his mind. Now he looked forward to the west, which fate would never let him reach; now he looked back to the east. Numb, not knowing what to do, he neither let the reins go nor had the strength to hold them, and he did not even know the horses' names. And scattered here and there across the strange sky he saw, shaking with terror, monstrous shapes of enormous beasts. There is a place where the Scorpion bends his arms into two great bows, and, with his tail and his claws crooked on either side, stretches his limbs across the space of two signs. When the boy saw him, wet with the sweat of black poison and threatening a wound with the curved barb of his sting, his wits left him and, in a chill of horror, he let the reins fall.

When the reins dropped and settled on the horses' backs, the team broke loose. With no one to hold them they went careening through the air of an unknown country, and wherever their frenzy drove them, there they rushed, lawless: they crashed against the stars fixed high in the heavens and dragged the chariot through trackless wastes. Now they made for the very top of the sky, now they plunged down headlong ways to a strip of earth closer below. The Moon watched in amazement as her brother's horses ran lower than her own, and the singed clouds smoked.

The earth caught fire, each place as it stood highest first; it split and cracked open in gaping chasms and, its sap drawn off, went dry. The pastures whitened; the trees blazed with their leaves; and the ripe standing grain gave fuel to its own ruin. But these are small griefs to mourn. Great cities perished with their walls, and the flames turned whole nations, peoples and all, to ash; woods burned together with the mountains. Athos burned, and Cilician Taurus, and Tmolus, and Oeta, and Ida — dry now, once thick with springs — and Helicon, home of the virgin Muses, and Haemus, not yet called Oeagrian. Etna burned past all measure with doubled fire, and two-peaked Parnassus, and Eryx, and Cynthus, and Othrys, and Rhodope — this time to lose its snows at last — and Mimas, and Dindyma, and Mycale, and Cithaeron, born for holy rites. Its own cold could not save Scythia: Caucasus burned, and Ossa with Pindus, and Olympus taller than both, and the airy Alps, and the cloud-carrying Apennines.

Then indeed Phaethon saw the whole world ablaze on every side, and could not endure such heat; he breathed in scalding air as if from the bottom of a furnace, and felt his own chariot going white-hot beneath him. He could not bear the ashes and flung-up cinders any longer; on every side he was wrapped in scorching smoke, and, buried in a pitch-black murk, he no longer knew where he was going or where he was, and was carried off at the whim of the winged horses.

It was then, men believe, that the peoples of Ethiopia took on their dark color, as the blood was drawn to the surface of their bodies. It was then that Libya was made a desert, its moisture seared away; then that the nymphs, hair loose, wept for their springs and lakes. Boeotia looked for Dirce, Argos for Amymone, Ephyre for the waters of Pirene. Not even rivers with wide-set banks stayed safe: Tanais steamed in the middle of its waters, and old Peneus, and Teuthrantian Caicus, and quick Ismenus, and Phocian Erymanthus, and Xanthus — doomed to burn a second time — and yellow Lycormas, and Maeander playing in its winding streams, and Mygdonian Melas, and Taenarian Eurotas. Babylonian Euphrates burned; Orontes burned, and swift Thermodon, and Ganges, and Phasis, and Ister. Alpheus boiled; the banks of Spercheus blazed; the gold that Tagus carries in its current ran molten in the flames. And the river birds, the swans that had made the Maeonian banks famous with their song, scorched in the middle of the Cayster. The Nile fled in terror to the ends of the earth and hid its head, which is hidden still; its seven mouths lay empty and dusty, seven valleys with no river in them. The same fate dried the Ismarian streams, Hebrus with Strymon, and the western rivers — the Rhine, the Rhone, the Po, and the Tiber, to whom the mastery of the world was promised.

All the ground burst apart; light drove down through the cracks into Tartarus and terrified the king of the underworld and his queen. The sea shrank in, and where lately there had been open water was now a plain of dry sand; mountains the deep had covered rose up and swelled the number of the scattered Cyclades. The fish sank to the bottom; the arched dolphins no longer dared to lift themselves into the air above the water as they used to; the bodies of seals floated lifeless on their backs on the surface of the deep. The story goes that even Nereus himself, and Doris and their daughters, hid in their warm caves. Three times Neptune, with a grim face, dared to thrust his arms up out of the water; three times he could not endure the fiery air.

Yet kindly Earth, the mother, ringed round as she was by sea, among the waters of the deep and the shrunken springs that had buried themselves in her dark body — Earth, parched, lifted her smothered face as far as her neck, put a hand to her brow, and, shaking everything with a great tremor, sank a little lower than she is wont to be, and spoke in a broken voice:

"If this is your will and I have earned it, why do your lightnings hold back, highest of the gods? If I must perish by the power of fire, let me perish by your fire, and lighten my ruin by knowing whose hand it was. I can barely open my throat for these very words" — the heat had choked her mouth — "Look at my scorched hair! Look, so many cinders over my eyes, so many over my face! Is this the return you give me, this the reward of my fruitfulness and my service — that I bear the wounds of the curved plough and the harrows and am worked hard all the year long; that I furnish leaves for the cattle, and gentle food, the grain, for the human race, and incense for you gods? But even suppose I have earned destruction — what have the waters done, what has your brother done? Why do the seas handed to him by lot shrink away and pull back farther from the sky? And if regard for your brother and for me does not move you, then take pity at least on your own heavens! Look around: both poles are smoking. If the fire ruins them, your own halls will come crashing down. See — Atlas himself is struggling, and can barely hold the white-hot sky on his shoulders! If the seas, if the lands, if the palace of heaven are lost, we are hurled back into the chaos of old. Snatch from the flames whatever is still left, and take thought for the sum of things!"

So spoke Earth; for she could bear the vapor no longer, and could say no more, and drew her face back into herself, into the caverns nearest the ghosts of the dead.

But the almighty father, having called the gods above to witness — and the one, too, who had given the chariot — that unless he acted all things would be lost to a grim fate, climbed to the topmost height from which he is used to spreading clouds across the broad lands, from which he rouses his thunder and hurls his brandished bolts. But this time he had no clouds to draw over the earth, no rain to send down from the sky. He thundered, and, poising a bolt level with his right ear, flung it at the charioteer and struck him out of his life and his seat at once, and with his own ruthless fire he curbed the fire below. The horses panicked, leapt apart, wrenched their necks from the yoke, and left the broken harness behind. Here lie the reins; there the axle, torn from the pole; there the spokes of the shattered wheels; and the wreck of the smashed chariot was scattered far and wide.

But Phaethon, the flames devouring his red-gold hair, was flung headlong and fell in a long trail through the air, as sometimes a star from a clear sky seems to fall, though it has not really fallen. Far from his homeland, in another quarter of the world, the great river Eridanus, the Po, took him in and washed his smoking face.

The nymphs of the west laid his body, still smoking from the three-forked flame, in a tomb, and carved these lines on the stone:

Here lies Phaethon, who drove his father's chariot. If he could not hold it, still he fell in a great daring.

His wretched father, sick with bitter grief, had covered his face and hidden it; and — if only we can believe it — they say one whole day went by without a sun. The burning world gave light; so even that disaster was good for something.

But Clymene, when she had said whatever there was to say in so great a sorrow, wild and out of her mind and tearing at her breast, went over the whole earth, hunting first for his lifeless limbs, and then for his bones. She found the bones at last, buried on a foreign bank. She threw herself down on the spot, bathed with her tears the name she read on the marble, and warmed it against her open breast.

The daughters of the Sun mourned no less, and gave their tears, a gift useless to the dead, and beat their breasts with their hands, and called on Phaethon night and day — Phaethon, who would never hear their sad laments — and lay stretched about the tomb. Four times the Moon had filled her disk, joining her horns; and the sisters, in their custom (for grief had made a custom of it), kept up their wailing. Then Phaethusa, the eldest of them, when she tried to throw herself on the ground, cried out that her feet had gone stiff. Fair Lampetie, trying to come to her, was held fast by a root that sprang up out of nowhere. A third, when she went to tear her hair with her hands, tore off leaves instead. One wailed that her legs were held in a trunk of wood; another that her arms had become long branches. And while they stared at this, bark closed over their loins, and by degrees wrapped their bellies, their breasts, their shoulders, their hands, until only their mouths were left, crying out for their mother. What could their mother do but run this way and that, wherever her frenzy took her, and press her lips to theirs while she still could? It was not enough. She tried to pull their bodies out of the trunks and to snap the tender branches off with her hands — and out of them dripped blood as from a wound. "Spare me, mother, I beg you," each one cried as she was hurt, "spare me! It is my own body being torn in the tree. And now — goodbye." The bark came over their last words. From the branches the tears still flow, and the amber that drips from the new-formed boughs hardens in the sun; the clear river catches it and sends it on to be worn by the brides of Latium.

Cycnus, the son of Sthenelus, was there for this strange event — Cycnus, who, though he was kin to you, Phaethon, on his mother's side, was closer still to you in his heart. He had left his kingdom (for he had ruled the peoples and the great cities of Liguria) and was filling the green banks and the river Eridanus, and the wood the sisters had thickened, with his laments, when the man's voice thinned to a shrill note, gray feathers hid his hair, a long neck reached out from his breast, a web bound his reddening toes, plumage clothed his sides, and a blunt bill closed over his mouth. Cycnus became a new bird, the swan; but he would not trust himself to the sky or to Jupiter, remembering the fire hurled so unjustly from up there. He kept to the ponds and the wide lakes, and, hating fire, chose the streams, the very opposite of flame.

Meanwhile Phaethon's father, unkempt and stripped of his own splendor — just as he is when he goes into eclipse — hated the light, hated himself and the day, gave his mind over to grief, and to his grief added anger, and refused his service to the world. "From the very beginning of time," he said, "my lot has been restless enough, and I am sick of labors without end and without honor. Let anyone else drive the chariot that carries the light! And if there is no one, and all the gods admit they cannot do it, let Jupiter himself drive it — so that, while he is trying my reins, he may at least, for a while, lay down the bolts that leave fathers childless. Then, when he has felt for himself the strength of the fire-footed horses, he will know that the one who could not steer them well did not deserve to die."

As the Sun said such things, all the gods stood around him and begged him with pleading voices not to plunge the world into darkness. Jupiter, for his part, made excuses for hurling his lightning, and, like a king, added threats to his pleas. So Phoebus gathered his horses, still crazed and trembling with terror, and in his pain lashed out at them with goad and whip — lashed at them savagely, for savage he was — and threw his son in their faces and blamed his death on them.

But the almighty father went the round of the great walls of heaven, checking that no part, weakened by the violence of the fire, was about to fall. When he saw them firm and sound in their own strength, he looked over the earth and the works of men. Yet his own Arcadia was the deeper care to him. He restored its springs and the rivers that had not yet dared to run again; he gave grass back to the ground and leaves to the trees, and bade the wounded forests grow green once more. And as he went to and fro, again and again, he stopped short at a girl of Nonacris in Arcadia, and the fire caught and burned in his bones.

She was no girl for softening wool by teasing it out, or for varying the style of her hair. A clasp held her dress, a plain white band held her carelessly falling hair; and now she carried a light javelin in her hand, now a bow. She was one of Diana's soldiers, and no nymph who ranged Maenalus was dearer to the goddess than she. But no favor lasts long.

The high sun had passed the middle of its course when she came into a grove that no age had ever cut. Here she slipped the quiver from her shoulder, unstrung her supple bow, and lay down on the ground the grass had covered, her painted quiver a pillow under her neck. When Jupiter saw her there, tired and unguarded, he said, "Here at least is one stolen love my wife will know nothing of — or if she does find out, oh, her scolding is well worth it!" At once he put on the face and dress of Diana, and said, "O girl, one of my own company — on what ridges have you been hunting?" The girl raised herself from the turf and said, "Hail, goddess — greater in my eyes than Jupiter himself, though he hear me say it." He smiled to hear it, glad to be set above himself, and kissed her — kisses too long, and not such as a virgin goddess would give. As she began to tell him in what wood she had been hunting, he cut her off with an embrace, and gave himself away by the outrage he did her. She fought back, as hard as a woman could — if only you had seen it, Juno, you would have been kinder to her — she fought, but what girl could ever have overcome him, and who could overcome Jupiter? He won, and made for the upper sky; and now the grove and the wood that had witnessed it were hateful to her. Turning back from that place, she almost forgot to take up her quiver and its arrows and the bow she had hung on a branch.

Look now — Diana the huntress, coming along the heights of Maenalus with her band around her, proud of the beasts she had killed, caught sight of the girl and called to her. At the call she shrank back, afraid at first that this too might be Jupiter in disguise; but when she saw the nymphs walking with the goddess, she knew there was no trick, and joined their number. Alas, how hard it is not to betray a fault in the face! She could barely lift her eyes from the ground; she no longer walked, as she used to, at the goddess's side or at the head of the whole company, but was silent, and by her blushes gave the sign of her wounded honor. And Diana — but that she is a virgin, and could not guess it — might have read the girl's fault by a thousand signs. The nymphs, they say, did read it.

The horns of the Moon were rising for the ninth time when the goddess, faint from her brother the Sun's flames, came upon a cool grove out of which ran a stream, murmuring as it went and turning the fine-worn sand. She praised the place, and touched the surface of the water with her foot; and, praising the water too, she said, "No one is watching, far or near — let us bathe our bodies with the stream poured over them." The Parrhasian girl blushed. They all took off their clothes; she alone looked for reasons to delay. As she hesitated, her dress was taken from her, and with it off, her fault stood plain along with her naked body. She stood stunned, and tried to hide her belly with her hands. "Away from here, far away," said Diana, "and do not defile these sacred springs" — and the goddess ordered her out of her company.

The wife of the great Thunderer had long since noticed all this, and had put off her heavy punishment to a fitting time. Now there was no reason to wait: the boy Arcas — the very thing that galled Juno — had already been born to her rival. The moment she turned her cruel thoughts and her cruel eyes that way, she said, "So this was all that was still left, you adulteress — that you should be fruitful, and your childbearing make the wrong known, and stand as proof of my Jupiter's disgrace! You will not go unpunished. I will strip you of that shape you please yourself with, you troublemaker — the shape that pleases my husband." She spoke, and, seizing the girl by the hair over her forehead, threw her face-down on the ground. Callisto stretched out her arms to beg — and the arms began to bristle with coarse black hair, and her hands to curve and grow into hooked claws and serve her for feet, and the face that Jupiter had once praised was ruined into a wide, gaping jaw. And so that no prayers, no pleading words might sway anyone, the power of speech was torn away from her: only an angry, threatening growl, thick with terror, came from her hoarse throat. Yet her old mind stayed with her, even made a bear as she was, and with unbroken groaning she bore witness to her grief, and lifted such hands as she had to the sky and the stars, and, though she could not say it, felt that Jupiter was cruel. Ah, how many times, not daring to sleep alone in the wild wood,

How often she wandered before her own old house, through the fields that had once been hers! How often the baying of hounds drove her over the crags, and she — a huntress in her day — fled in terror from the hunters! Often she cowered at the sight of wild beasts, forgetting what she now was, and, a bear herself, shuddered at the bears she saw on the mountains, and went in dread of the wolves, though her own father ran among them as one of them.

Then look — Arcas comes, the child of Lycaon's daughter, knowing nothing of the mother who bore him. Some fifteen birthdays had passed over him. He was hunting the wild game, picking out the likeliest glades, ringing the Erymanthian woods with his woven nets, when he stumbled on his mother. She stopped dead at the sight of Arcas, and looked for all the world like something that knew him. He drew back, unnerved, not understanding why this bear kept her eyes fixed on him and would not look away; and when she pressed closer, eager to come to him, he was on the point of driving his hunting-spear straight through her breast. But the Almighty stayed his hand. In one stroke he swept away both of them and the horror about to be done, carried them off together through the empty air on a rushing wind, and set them in the sky, and made them neighboring constellations — the Great Bear and the Little.

Juno swelled with fury when her rival blazed among the stars. Down she went to the sea, to gray old Tethys and ancient Ocean, whom even the gods so often hold in awe; and when they asked the reason for her coming, she began: "You want to know why I, the queen of the gods, have left my home in heaven and come down to you? Because another woman holds the sky in my place! Call me a liar if, when night has darkened the world, you do not see stars newly honored in the very height of heaven — my open wound — set there where the last and smallest circle wheels around the topmost point of the axis. So now there is every reason to think twice before wronging Juno, every reason to tremble at offending me — me, whose only way of helping is to harm! Oh, what a great thing I have done! How vast my power is! I forbade her to be human — and she has been made a goddess! This is how I punish the guilty; this is my mighty authority! Let him give her back her old face and strip off this beast-shape, the way he did before for that Argive girl, Io. Why not divorce Juno outright, marry the creature, move her into my bed, and take Lycaon for a father-in-law? But you — if the insult done to your foster-child touches you at all — bar the seven stars of the Wain from your blue depths, drive off from the sky those constellations taken up as the wages of adultery, and let no mistress bathe in your clean waters!"

The gods of the sea gave their consent. Juno stepped up into her nimble chariot through the clear air, drawn by her painted peacocks — peacocks so lately given their colors, when Argus was killed, just as lately, chattering raven, as you were suddenly turned to black-winged, you who had been white before. For this bird was once silver, with snowy feathers, the equal of any spotless dove; he yielded nothing to the geese whose watchful cries would one day save the Capitol, nor to the swan that haunts the rivers. His tongue was his ruin: because of that gossiping tongue, the color that was white is now the very opposite of white.
