There was no one lovelier in all of Thessaly than Coronis of Larissa. She certainly pleased you, god of Delphi — Apollo — as long as she stayed faithful, or went unwatched. But Apollo's bird, the raven, caught her out in her betrayal, and the pitiless informer set his course straight for his master, to lay her hidden guilt bare. After him, flapping her wings, came the chattering crow, wanting to hear the whole story; and when she learned the reason for his errand, she said, "You're flying an unlucky road. Don't scorn what my voice foretells. Think what I once was and what I am now, and ask what I did to deserve it — you'll find that loyalty was my downfall.

"There was a time when Pallas Minerva had shut up the infant Erichthonius, a child born of no mother, in a basket woven of Athenian osier, and handed it to the three daughters of two-formed Cecrops to guard, with strict orders: they were not to pry into her secret. Hidden in the light leaves of a thick elm, I watched to see what they would do. Two of them — Pandrosos and Herse — kept the trust without treachery. Only Aglauros called her sisters cowards, and undid the knots with her own hand; and inside they saw a baby, and a snake stretched out beside it. I carried the news to the goddess — and this is the thanks I get for it: I am cast out from Minerva's protection, and ranked below the bird of night, the owl! Let my punishment be a warning to birds not to go courting danger with their tongues.

"And mind — it wasn't as if she came looking for me, when I never asked for such a favor. Ask Pallas herself, if you doubt it; angry as she is, not even her anger will deny me this. I am the daughter of Coroneus, a man famous in the land of Phocis — this is common knowledge — and I was a princess, a virgin of royal blood, courted by wealthy suitors; so don't look down on me. My beauty was my misfortune. For as I was pacing the shore one day with my slow, usual step, along the top of the sand, the god of the sea, Neptune, saw me and caught fire. When he had wasted his time in vain, coaxing me with soft words, he turned to force and gave chase. I ran, I left the packed shore behind and wore myself out for nothing in the soft sand. Then I called on gods and men — but no mortal ear caught my voice. A virgin goddess was moved for a virgin, and came to my aid. I was stretching my arms toward the sky when those arms began to darken with soft feathers. I fought to throw the clothing off my shoulders — but it was already plumage, its roots sunk deep into my skin. I tried to beat my bare breast with my palms, but I no longer had either palms or bare breast. I ran, and the sand no longer dragged at my feet as before; I was lifted clear of the ground, and soon I was carried high through the air and given to Minerva as her blameless companion.

"And what good has it done me? — when Nyctimene, made a bird for a monstrous crime, has taken my place of honor. Or haven't you heard the thing they all know throughout Lesbos, how Nyctimene defiled her own father's bed? She is a bird, true enough — but knowing her guilt, she flees the sight of men and the daylight, and hides her shame in the dark, and every bird in the whole sky drives her out."

To all this the raven only said, "Choke on your warnings, I pray — I've no use for an empty omen." And he did not give up the road he had begun, but told his master he had seen Coronis lying with a young Thessalian man. At the word of his beloved's guilt, the laurel slipped from Apollo's brow; in the same instant the god's face changed, his color drained, the plectrum dropped from his hand. And as his heart boiled up with swelling rage, he snatched his familiar weapons, bent the bow from horn to horn, and drove an arrow no one could dodge through that breast — the very breast so often pressed against his own. She cried out at the blow, and drawing the steel from the wound she drenched her white limbs with crimson blood, and said, "I could have paid you what I owed, Phoebus — but I should have given birth first. Now two of us die in one." That was all; her life poured out with her blood, and the deadly chill of death crept over the body that no longer held a soul.

Too late — too late! — the lover repents of his cruel revenge, and hates himself for listening, for flaring up so fast. He hates the bird that forced him to learn the crime and the cause of his grief; he hates the bowstring, the bow, his own hand, and, with the hand, those reckless weapons, the arrows. He gathers her fallen body up and tries, too late, to beat back fate with his skill, and works his healing arts on her for nothing. And when he saw that all his efforts failed, that the pyre was being built and her limbs were about to burn in the last flames, then he groaned from the very bottom of his heart — for a god's face may not be wet with tears — groaning like a heifer who stands watching while the hammer, swung from beside the butcher's right ear, crashes down and shatters the hollow temples of her suckling calf. Yet after he had poured perfumes she could not feel over her breast, held her in a last embrace, and paid the funeral rites she should never have needed so soon, Apollo would not let his own seed sink into those same ashes. He tore the child from the flames and from his mother's womb and carried him to the cave of two-formed Chiron. And the raven, who had counted on a reward for telling no lie — him he forbade forever to take his place among the white birds.

Meanwhile Chiron, the half-beast, was delighted with a pupil of divine blood, glad of the honor that came with the charge. And look — the Centaur's daughter arrives, her shoulders wrapped in her red-gold hair. The nymph Chariclo had once borne her on the banks of a rushing stream and named her Ocyrhoe. This girl was not content to master her father's arts; she sang the secrets of the Fates. So when she had caught the prophetic frenzy in her mind and glowed with the god shut up inside her breast, she looked at the infant and said: "Grow, child — you who will bring the whole world healing! Again and again mortal bodies will owe their lives to you. You will be allowed to call back the dead — and once, having dared that against the will of the gods, you will be stopped from doing it again by your grandfather's fire. From a god you will become a bloodless corpse, and from that corpse a god once more, and twice you will make your own destiny new.

"And you too, dear father — immortal now, born under the law of living forever — you will long to be able to die, that day when you writhe in agony, the blood of the deadly serpent seeping through your torn limbs. The gods will make you mortal, you who cannot die, and the three goddesses of fate will loose your thread at last." Something more remained to be said. She sighed from the depths of her breast, tears welled and slid down her cheeks, and she said: "The Fates cut me short; I am forbidden to say more, and the use of my voice is being taken from me. My arts were not worth the price — not worth drawing a god's anger down on me. I wish I had never known the future! Already the human shape seems to be draining out of me; already grass is a delight to eat; already I long to gallop over the wide plains. I am turning into a mare, into a body like my father's. And yet — why wholly a mare? My father, after all, is of two shapes."

As she spoke, the last part of her lament grew hard to follow; her words ran together. Soon they were not words at all, nor quite the sound of a mare, but of someone imitating one, and in a little while she was neighing outright and had lowered her arms into the grass. Then her fingers drew together, and a single smooth hoof of unbroken horn bound her five nails into one; her face and her neck stretched out long; the greater part of her sweeping robe became a tail, and the loose hair that streamed down her neck fell over to the right and became a mane. Voice and shape were made new together — and the marvel gave her a new name as well.

Chiron, son of Philyra, wept, and begged your help, god of Delphi, but in vain. For you could not undo great Jupiter's decrees, and even if you could have, you were not there to do it — you were away in Elis and the fields of Messenia. This was the season when a shepherd's hide clothed you, when a rough cudgel filled your left hand and, in the other, a pipe of seven unequal reeds. And while love was your whole concern, while your own pipe was soothing you, some cattle — so they say — wandered untended into the plains of Pylos. The son of Maia, the daughter of Atlas — Mercury — spotted them and, with his usual cunning, hid the stolen beasts away in the woods. No one had noticed the theft except one old man, well known in that country; the whole neighborhood called him Battus. He served as watchman over the glades and grassy pastures of rich Neleus and his herds of fine mares.

Mercury was wary of him, and drew him gently aside by the hand. "Whoever you are, stranger," he said, "if anyone comes asking after these cattle, say you never saw them — and so you don't go unrewarded for it, take this fine cow." And he gave him one. The old man took it and answered, "Go on, you're safe with me. That stone there will tell of your theft before I ever do" — and he pointed to a stone. The son of Jupiter pretended to leave. But soon he came back, changing his shape and his voice, and said, "Countryman, if you've seen any cattle come along this path, help me out and don't keep the theft a secret — you'll have a cow and her bull together as your reward." And when the price was thus doubled, the old man said, "They'll be under those hills over there" — and under those hills they were. Mercury laughed and said, "You'd betray me to my own face, would you, you cheat? Betray me to myself?" And he turned that perjured heart into hard flint, the stone that even now is called the Informer — an old disgrace clinging to a rock that never earned it.

From there the god of the winged sandals lifted himself on level wings and, as he flew, looked down on the fields of Munychia, on the land dear to Minerva, and on the tended groves of the Lyceum. It happened that on that day the chaste young women were carrying, in their purity, the sacred offerings on their heads in flower-crowned baskets, up to Pallas Minerva's festal citadel. The winged god watched them coming back down, and did not hold his course straight on but wheeled around in a circle — the way the kite, quickest of birds, when it spots the entrails of a sacrifice but is nervous, with the priests crowded thick around it, banks in tight rings and dares not stray far off, and hovers, greedy, on beating wings above the thing it wants. Just so the swift Cyllenian god leaned his flight over the towers of Athens and circled the same stretch of air. As much as the Morning Star outshines the other stars, and as much as the golden Moon outshines the Morning Star, so much did Herse outshine every other girl as she walked — the crown and glory of the whole procession and of her companions. The son of Jupiter was struck dumb by her beauty; hanging there in the air, he caught fire — the way a leaden pellet slung from a Balearic sling flies and grows red-hot as it goes, finding under the clouds a fire it never had before.

He turned aside, left heaven, and took a new road, and made no attempt to hide who he was — such was his confidence in his own good looks. And though that confidence was justified, still he took pains to help it along: he smoothed his hair, arranged his cloak to hang just so, letting the border and all its gold show, made sure the polished wand — the one that summons and banishes sleep — was in his right hand, and that his winged sandals gleamed on his spotless feet.

Deep in the house there were three bedchambers finished in ivory and tortoiseshell. Yours, Pandrosos, was on the right, Aglauros had the left, and Herse the one between. Aglauros, in the left-hand room, was the first to notice Mercury coming, and dared to ask the god his name and the reason for his visit. And the grandson of Atlas and Pleione answered her: "I am the one who carries my father's commands through the air — and my father is Jupiter himself. I won't make up excuses. Just be loyal to your sister, be willing to be called aunt to my child. Herse is the reason I've come; help a lover, I beg you." Aglauros eyed him with the very same look she had turned on the hidden secrets of golden-haired Minerva, and demanded, as the price of her help, a great weight of gold — and meanwhile made him wait outside.

The warrior goddess turned the orbs of her stern eyes upon her and heaved a sigh so deep it shook her breast and the aegis that lay against her mighty chest. It came back to her: that this was the hand that had laid bare her secret, when Aglauros had looked, against Minerva's own command, on the motherless child of Vulcan, the god of Lemnos — and that now she would be dear both to the god and to her sister, and rich besides on the gold her greed had demanded. At once Minerva went to the house of Envy, filthy with black corruption. Her home lies hidden in the depths of a valley, starved of sun, closed to every wind, dismal, brimming with a numbing cold, forever without a fire and forever choked with fog.

When the goddess who is a terror in war reached the place, she stopped before the door — for she thought it wrong to go under that roof — and struck the doorposts with the tip of her spear. The doors shuddered open. Inside she saw Envy eating the flesh of vipers, the food that feeds her own vices, and at the sight she turned her eyes away. But Envy dragged herself up sluggishly from the ground, left the half-gnawed bodies of the snakes, and shuffled forward with a listless step. And when she saw the goddess in her beauty and shining armor, she groaned and drew a long sigh at the very sight. Pallor sits on her face, and all her body is wasted; she never looks at you straight; her teeth are rotted black; her breast is green with gall, her tongue drips venom. She never smiles, unless the sight of another's suffering moves her to it; she gets no sleep, kept awake by gnawing cares, but watches men's successes with misery and pines away at the watching. She tears at others and is torn apart in the tearing, her own perpetual torment. Still, hateful to her as Envy was, Minerva spoke to her briefly: "Infect one of the daughters of Cecrops with your poison. It has to be done. Aglauros is the one." Saying no more, she pushed off from the ground with her planted spear and was gone.

Envy, watching the goddess flee with a sidelong glare, muttered under her breath, grudging Minerva her success, and took up her staff, wreathed all over with brambles. Wrapped in black clouds, wherever she goes she tramples the flowering fields, scorches the grass, and crops the tops off the blossoms; with her breath she fouls whole peoples, cities, and homes. At last she caught sight of the citadel of Athens, thriving in arts and wealth and glad peace — and she could barely hold back her tears, because she saw nothing there to weep at. But once she had entered the bedroom of Cecrops's daughter, she carried out her orders. She laid her hand, stained with rust, on the girl's breast, packed her heart with barbed thorns, breathed a poisonous venom into her, and spread it black as pitch through her bones and deep into the middle of her lungs. And so that the seeds of the sickness would not stray too widely, she set the girl's sister before her eyes — the fortunate marriage, the god in all his beauty — and made every detail of it enormous.

Goaded by all this, Cecrops's daughter is gnawed by a hidden grief; she groans, tormented by night and tormented by day, and wastes away, wretched, in a slow decline, like ice worn down by a fitful sun. She smolders at happy Herse's good fortune — smolders the way a fire smothered under thorny brushwood sends up no flame but burns with a low, creeping heat. Again and again she wanted to die, so as not to see such a thing; again and again to tell it all, as if it were a crime, to her stern father. In the end she sat herself down across the threshold to shut the god out when he came. And when he tried his coaxing, his pleas, his gentlest words, she said, "Stop this. I'm not moving from here until you're driven off." "Done — let's hold to that bargain," said the swift Cyllenian god, and touched the carved door open with his wand. But when she tried to rise, the parts we bend to sit down would not move for their own dead weight. She fought to lift herself straight up, but the joints of her knees were rigid; a chill slid down through her nails, and her veins went pale as the blood drained away. And as an incurable cancer spreads in every direction and adds the sound flesh to the ruined, so, little by little, the deadly cold crept into her breast and closed off the passages of life and breath. She made no attempt to speak — and if she had, there was no road left for her voice. Stone already held her neck; her face had hardened, and she sat there, a bloodless statue. And the stone was not white: her own mind had stained it dark.

When the grandson of Atlas had punished her words and her unholy heart this way, he left the land that bears Pallas's name and rose into the sky on beating wings. His father called him aside, and, not admitting the real cause — his own new love — said, "My son, faithful carrier of my orders, shake off delay, drop down swift as ever on your usual course, and make for the country that looks up on the left at the constellation your mother is part of — the natives call it Sidon. There's a royal herd you'll see grazing far off on the mountain grass; drive it down to the shore." He spoke, and already the young bulls, driven off the mountain, were making for the shore he had named, where the great king's daughter — Europa — used to play, attended by her Tyrian companions.

Majesty and love do not sit well together, nor keep house in the same place. So the father and ruler of the gods, whose right hand is armed with the three-forked lightning, who shakes the world with a nod, laid aside the weight of his rule and put on the shape of a bull. Mixing in with the cattle, he lowed and strolled, beautiful, over the tender grass. His color was the white of snow that no hard hoof has trodden and no rain-bringing south wind has melted. The muscles bunched on his neck, and a dewlap hung down from his shoulders; his horns were small, true — but you'd have sworn a craftsman made them by hand, clearer and more shining than any polished gem. There was no menace in his forehead, nothing to fear in his eye; his whole face wore peace.

Agenor's daughter marveled that he was so beautiful, and that he threatened no attack; but gentle as he was, at first she was afraid to touch him. Little by little she came closer and held out flowers to his white mouth. The lover rejoiced, and, while the pleasure he hoped for held off, kissed her hands — barely, oh barely, could he put off the rest. Now he frolicked and skipped on the green grass; now he laid his snowy flank down on the yellow sand. And as her fear ebbed away, bit by bit, he offered his chest now for her girl's hand to pat, now his horns to be tangled with fresh garlands. At last the princess, not knowing whose back she pressed, even dared to climb up and sit on the bull — and the god, easing gradually off the land and away from the dry shore, set the false hoofs of his feet first in the shallows at the water's edge. Then he went further out, and carried his prize away over the open middle of the sea. She is terrified, and, borne off, looks back at the shore she is leaving; her right hand grips a horn, the other rests on his back, and her fluttering robes billow in the wind.

Book III

And now the god had set aside the shape of the lying bull, confessed himself for who he was, and reached the fields of Crete. But her father knew nothing of any of this. He ordered Cadmus to go and search for his stolen sister, and — loving and cruel in the very same command — added exile as the penalty if he failed to find her. So Cadmus wandered the whole world over. For who could ever track down the thefts of Jupiter? A fugitive from his own country and his father's anger, the son of Agenor went as a suppliant to the oracle of Apollo and asked what land he was meant to live in.

"A heifer will meet you," Apollo said, "out in the lonely fields — a cow that has never worn a yoke, never known the curved plow. Follow where she leads. Where she lies down in the grass, there build your city walls, and call that country Boeotia."

Cadmus had barely come down from the Castalian cave at Delphi when he saw a heifer, unguarded, moving slowly along with no mark of servitude on her neck. He fell in behind her and tracked her footsteps at a measured pace, silently thanking Apollo for showing him the road. He had already crossed the fords of the Cephisus and the fields of Panope when the cow stopped, lifted toward the sky a forehead crowned with tall handsome horns, and set the air ringing with her lowing. Then she looked back at his men following behind, sank down, and laid her flank in the soft grass. Cadmus gave thanks. He pressed his lips to the foreign ground and greeted the unfamiliar mountains and fields as his own.

He meant to make a sacrifice to Jupiter. He sent his servants to go and fetch running spring water for the offering. An ancient forest stood nearby, never once violated by an axe. In the middle of it was a cave thick with brush and twisted branches, its low arch built up out of joined stones, rich with a plentiful spring. Hidden away in that cave lay a serpent sacred to Mars, splendid with a golden crest. Its eyes flashed with fire; its whole body was swollen with venom; three tongues flickered in its mouth, and its teeth stood in a triple row.

When the men who had set out from the Tyrian people reached that grove with their ill-fated steps, and the urn plunged into the water gave its splash, the sea-blue serpent lifted its head from deep in the cave and hissed a horrifying hiss. The urns dropped from their hands. The blood drained out of their bodies, and a sudden shudder seized their stunned limbs. The serpent twisted its scaly coils into rolling spirals, sprang, and bent itself into enormous arcs. Rearing up more than half its length into the light air, it looked down on the whole forest — a thing as huge, if you could see it all at once, as the Dragon in the sky that separates the two Bears. There was no delay. Whether the Phoenicians were reaching for their weapons or for flight, or whether sheer terror froze them from doing either, it was on them: some it killed with its bite, some crushed in its long coils, some it poisoned with the deadly breath of its venom.

The sun stood at its peak now and shrank the shadows to almost nothing. The son of Agenor began to wonder what was keeping his men, and went to look for them. His covering was a lion's hide stripped from the beast; his weapon a lance of shining steel, and a javelin — and a courage greater than any weapon. When he entered the grove and saw the lifeless bodies, and above them the victorious enemy, immense, licking the horrible wounds with its bloody tongue, he cried, "Either I will avenge your deaths, my faithful friends, or I will share them." With that he lifted a boulder in his right hand and hurled the vast weight with a tremendous heave. Walls and high towers would have shaken at that blow, but the serpent took it without a wound. Its scales shielded it like a coat of mail, and the toughness of its black hide turned the mighty stroke aside. But that same toughness did not beat the javelin. It stuck fast in the middle of the pliant spine and drove its whole iron point down into the guts. Wild with pain, the serpent wrenched its head back over its own body, looked at its wound, bit at the shaft lodged there, and worked it loose on every side with all its strength — barely tearing it free at last, though the iron stayed buried in the bone.

Then, when this fresh cause was added to its usual rage, the throat swelled with gorged veins, white foam frothed around its pestilent jaws, the ground scraped by its scales rasped and hissed, and the black breath pouring from its Stygian mouth fouled the tainted air. Now it wound itself into vast circling coils; now it stood up straighter than a long beam; now, driven forward with enormous force like a river swollen with rain, it swept along and battered down the trees in its path with its chest. The son of Agenor gave a little ground, taking the charge on his lion's hide, holding off the lunging mouth with his spear-point thrust out before him. The serpent raged and drove its teeth uselessly against the hard steel, biting at the point. Now blood began to run from its venomous palate and stained the green grass with its spray — but the wound was slight, because the serpent kept pulling back from the blow, drawing its hurt neck away, and by retreating kept the stroke from sinking and biting deep. At last the son of Agenor drove the iron into its throat and kept coming, following it, pressing after it, until an oak stood in the serpent's way as it backed off and its neck was pinned through, nailed to the wood. The tree bent under the serpent's weight and groaned as the tip of that lashing tail whipped against its trunk.

While the victor stood measuring the size of his beaten enemy, a voice was suddenly heard. Where it came from was impossible to say — but heard it was. "Why, son of Agenor, do you stand staring at the serpent you have killed? You too will be gazed upon as a serpent." For a long time he stood there terrified, his color and his wits drained away together, his hair stiff with a cold horror. Then, look — Pallas, the hero's champion, came gliding down through the upper air and stood beside him. She told him to plow the ground and sow the serpent's teeth in it, the seeds of a people yet to come. He obeyed. He opened a furrow with the pressing plow and scattered the teeth as ordered across the earth — mortal seed. Then, past all belief, the clods began to stir. First the point of a spear rose out of the furrows; next helmets, their painted crests nodding; then shoulders, chests, arms already loaded with weapons came up; a whole crop of shielded men sprang into being. It was like a theater on a festival day, when the curtain is drawn up and the painted figures rise into view, showing their faces first, then the rest little by little, drawn up in one smooth motion until they stand there entire, feet planted on the lower edge of the stage.

Frightened by these new enemies, Cadmus reached for his weapons. "Don't!" shouted one of the men the earth had made. "Keep out of our civil war!" And with that he struck one of his earth-born brothers with a stroke of his sword, hand to hand — then fell himself to a javelin thrown from a distance. The one who had killed him lived no longer than his victim, and breathed out the very air he had so lately breathed in. The whole crowd went mad in the same way, and these brothers, sprung up together only moments before, fell in battle against each other, wound trading for wound. So the young men, whose share of life had been so brief, beat their bloodied mother earth with dying chests — until only five were left. One of them was Echion. At the warning of Minerva he threw his weapons down and both offered and received a pledge of brotherly peace. These were the companions the wanderer from Sidon had when he built the city Apollo's oracle had commanded.

And now Thebes stood. Now, Cadmus, you might have seemed happy even in your exile. Mars and Venus themselves were your father-in-law and mother-in-law; add to that children by so glorious a wife, so many sons and daughters, and grandchildren too, those dear pledges of love, already grown to young men and women. But of course — a man's last day must always be waited for, and no one should be called happy before his death and his final funeral rites.

Among so much good fortune, Cadmus, it was your grandson who first became your cause of grief — the stranger's horns set on his forehead, and you, his hounds, glutted on your own master's blood. And yet, if you look at it honestly, you will find in him the fault of bad luck, not of crime. For what crime is there in an honest mistake?

There was a mountain stained with the killing of every kind of wild beast. The day had shortened the shadows to their noon length, and the sun stood equally far from both ends of its course, when the young Boeotian called out in a gentle voice to the companions who shared his work as they roamed the pathless wilds: "Our nets are wet, my friends, and our spears too, with the blood of the beasts; the day has given us sport enough. When Dawn returns tomorrow, riding her saffron chariot to bring back the light, we'll take up our task again. Right now the sun stands halfway between east and west and splits the fields open with its heat. Break off the work you're at, and gather up the knotted nets." The men did as he asked and set their labor aside.

There was a valley dense with pine and sharp-pointed cypress, called Gargaphie, sacred to Diana, who tucks up her robe to hunt. Deep in its farthest recess was a woodland grotto shaped by no art at all — nature, with her own genius, had counterfeited art. She had drawn a natural arch out of the living pumice and the light tufa stone. A spring ran murmuring on the right, clear and shallow, its wide pool fringed with a border of grass. Here the goddess of the woods, when tired with the hunt, used to bathe her virgin limbs in the clear water.

She came to it now. She handed her javelin, her quiver, and her unstrung bow to one of the nymphs, her armor-bearer; another slipped her arms under the robe as it came off; two more unlaced the sandals from her feet. Crocale, daughter of Ismenus, more skilled than the rest, gathered up the goddess's hair, scattered loose over her neck, and bound it into a knot — though her own hair hung free. Nephele, Hyale, Rhanis, Psecas, and Phyale scooped up water and poured it from their broad urns. And while Diana was there, washing in the water where she always washed, look — the grandson of Cadmus, his day's hunting cut short, came wandering with uncertain steps through unfamiliar woods and into that grove. So his fate led him.

The moment he entered the cave dripping with spring water, the nymphs, naked, seeing a man, beat their breasts and filled the whole forest with sudden shrieks, and crowded around Diana to shield her with their own bodies. But the goddess stood taller than they were, head and shoulders above them all. The color that fills the clouds when the sun strikes them from across the sky, or the color of rosy Dawn, rose in the face of Diana caught without her robes. Hemmed in as she was by the packed ring of her attendants, she still turned herself sideways and twisted her face back — and how she wished she had her arrows within reach! What she did have within reach was the water, and she scooped it up and flung it into the man's face. Drenching his hair with that avenging stream, she added these words, the forewarning of the doom to come: "Now you may tell how you saw me undressed — if you can tell it at all!" No further threat. She set on his dripping head the antlers of a full-grown stag, drew his neck out long, tapered the tips of his ears to points, changed his hands into feet and his arms into long forelegs, and wrapped his body in a dappled hide. And she added fear. The son of Autonoe fled — and marveled, even as he ran, at how fast he was. But when he saw his face and the antlers in the water, he tried to say, "Oh, this is misery" — and no voice came. He groaned; the groan was all his voice now, and tears ran down a face that was no longer his. Only his old mind stayed behind.

What was he to do? Go home, back to the royal halls? Or hide in the woods? Shame blocked the one, terror the other. While he wavered, the hounds caught sight of him. Melampus and the keen-nosed Ichnobates were the first to bay the signal — Ichnobates of Knossos, Melampus of Spartan stock. Then the rest came rushing, faster than the racing wind: Pamphagus, Dorceus, and Oribasus, all bred in Arcadia; powerful Nebrophonus, and savage Theron running with Laelaps; Pterelas, best of them for speed, and Agre, best of them for scent; fierce Hylaeus, gored by a wild boar not long ago; Nape, sired by a wolf; Poemenis, who used to herd the flocks; Harpyia with her two pups at her side; Ladon of Sicyon, lean in the flank; Dromas and Canache and Sticte and Tigris and Alce; Leucon with his snow-white coat and Asbolus with his black one; brawny Lacon and Aello, strong at the run; Thous, and swift Lycisca beside her Cyprian brother; Harpalus, his black face marked with a white blaze down the middle; Melaneus, and Lachne with her shaggy body; Labros and Agriodus, sons of a Cretan father and a Spartan mother; and shrill-voiced Hylactor — and more it would take too long to name. The whole pack, wild for the kill, poured after him over rocks and cliffs and crags no path could reach, wherever the way was hard and wherever there was no way at all.

He ran along the very trails he had so often hunted down — running, God help him, from his own servants. He longed to cry out, "I am Actaeon! Recognize your own master!" but the words would not come. The air rang with barking. Melanchaetes was the first to tear a wound in his back, Blackfoot the next; Oresitrophus sank into his shoulder. They had set out late, but a shortcut over the hill had brought them up in time. While those three held their master down, the rest of the pack closed in and buried their teeth in his body. Soon there was no room left for more wounds. He groaned and made a sound that was not a man's, though no stag could make it either, and filled the ridges he knew so well with his anguished cries. Down on his knees, like a suppliant begging for mercy, he turned his silent face this way and that, as if it were his pleading arms. His companions, knowing nothing, egged the ravening pack on with their usual shouts, hunting the grove with their eyes for Actaeon and calling out "Actaeon!" over and over, as though he were somewhere else — he lifted his head at the name — complaining that he wasn't there, that the lazy man was missing the fine spectacle their prey was giving them. He wished with all his heart he were somewhere else, but he was here. He wished he could watch, and not feel, the savage work of his own dogs. They stood around him on every side, sank their muzzles into his flesh, and tore their master to pieces under the false shape of a stag. Not until his life had run out through wound after wound, they say, was the rage of the quiver-bearing goddess satisfied.

People took it different ways. To some, the goddess seemed harsher than she had any right to be; others praised her and called the punishment worthy of her strict virginity. Both sides found their reasons. Only Jupiter's wife did not so much say whether she condemned it or approved as take open joy in the ruin of the house sprung from Agenor — carrying over onto the whole line the hatred she had first conceived for her Tyrian rival, Europa. And now, look, a fresh grievance was heaped on the old one: she was galled that Semele was pregnant with the seed of great Jupiter. She loosed her tongue to rage — then checked herself. "And what," she said, "have I ever gained by all this railing? It's the woman herself I have to go after. Her I will destroy — if I am rightly called almighty Juno, if it becomes me to hold the jeweled scepter in my hand, if I am the queen, and the sister and wife of Jupiter — well, the sister, at least, beyond dispute. But I suppose she's content with a stolen tumble, and the insult to my bed is a small thing. She's pregnant — that was all that was missing! — carrying the plain proof of his guilt in her swollen belly, wanting to be made a mother by Jupiter, which is more than I ever managed for myself. That's how sure she is of her beauty. I'll see to it her beauty fails her. Let me be no daughter of Saturn if I don't send her down to the waters of the Styx, sunk there by her own dear Jupiter."

At that she rose from her throne and, wrapped in a tawny cloud, went to Semele's door. She did not shed the cloud until she had disguised herself as an old woman — set gray hair at her temples, plowed her skin into wrinkles, hunched her body and moved it with a trembling, palsied step. She made her voice an old woman's voice too. She became Beroe herself, Semele's nurse from Epidaurus. So, drawing the girl into conversation and talking on and on, they came around to the name of Jupiter, and the old woman sighed and said, "I do hope it's Jupiter — but I'm afraid of everything. Plenty of men have talked their way into a decent girl's bed under a god's name. And it's not enough that he's Jupiter. If he really is the true one, let him give you some proof of his love: ask him to hold you exactly as great and glorious as he is when he's welcomed by lofty Juno, and to put on all his royal splendor first." That was how Juno primed the unsuspecting daughter of Cadmus. And Semele asked Jupiter for a gift without naming it. "Choose," the god said, "you'll be refused nothing. And so you'll believe me all the more, let the divine power of the Stygian torrent be my witness too — the terror and the god even of the gods."

Delighted with what would destroy her, too successful, and about to die of her lover's compliance, Semele said, "Show yourself to me just as the daughter of Saturn holds you when the two of you seal the bond of love — give yourself to me like that." The god wanted to clap a hand over her mouth as she spoke, but the words had already rushed out into the air. He groaned. She could not now unwish it, and he could not now unswear it. So in the deepest grief he climbed the high sky and drew the trailing clouds along after him with a nod, and added rainstorms and lightning mixed with wind, and thunder, and the bolt there is no escaping. Yet as much as he could, he tried to strip himself of his own power. He did not arm himself with the fire that had brought down hundred-handed Typhoeus — there was too much savagery in that one. There is a lighter bolt, into which the hands of the Cyclopes had forged less cruelty, less flame, less rage; the gods call it the second-rank weapon. That one he took up, and entered the house of Agenor's line. Her mortal body could not bear the storm of heaven, and she burned to ash in the fire of her wedding gift.

The infant, still unformed, was pulled from his mother's womb and — if this is worthy of belief — sewn, tender as he was, into his father's thigh, to finish out the months that belonged to the mother. His aunt Ino secretly nursed him in his first cradle; then the nymphs of Nysa took him, hid him in their caves, and fed him milk.

And while all this was going on down on earth by the law of fate, and the cradle of twice-born Bacchus lay safe, they say that Jupiter, well soaked in nectar and off his guard, set his heavy cares aside and fell to trading idle jokes with Juno in a slack hour. "There's no question," he said, "the pleasure you women get out of love is greater than what falls to us men." She said no. They agreed to ask the learned Tiresias for a ruling, since he had known love as both. For once, in a green wood, he had struck with his staff two great serpents coupling, and — the marvel of it — had been changed from a man into a woman and lived seven autumns so. In the eighth he came upon the same pair again and said, "If there's such power in a blow struck against you that it turns the striker into his opposite, I'll strike you now once more." He struck those same snakes, and his earlier form came back, the shape he was born with. So he was chosen as judge in this playful quarrel and backed Jupiter's word. Saturn's daughter, they say, took it harder than the matter warranted, out of all proportion, and condemned the eyes of her judge to everlasting night. But the almighty father — since no god is permitted to undo what another god has done — gave him, in return for his lost sight, the knowledge of things to come, and eased the punishment with that honor.

Famous now throughout the cities of Boeotia, Tiresias gave flawless answers to all who came to consult him. The first to test his voice and prove it true was sea-blue Liriope, a water nymph, whom the river Cephisus once wound in his curving current and, trapping her inside his waters, forced. In the fullness of time the loveliest of nymphs bore a child, one who even then could stir love, and she named him Narcissus. Asked about the boy — whether he would live to see the long years of a ripe old age — the prophet who spoke destiny said, "If he never comes to know himself." For a long time the seer's answer seemed empty. Then the outcome proved it — the way it all turned out, the manner of his death, the strange new kind of madness.

For the son of Cephisus had added one year to three times five, and could pass for either a boy or a young man. Many a youth desired him, many a girl — but there was so hard a pride in that tender beauty that no youth, no girl could touch him. Once, as he was driving frightened deer into his nets, a nymph with a voice caught sight of him — resounding Echo, who had never learned to keep quiet when another spoke, nor to speak first herself. Echo still had a body then, she was not just a voice; and yet, chatterer that she was, she could do no more with her mouth than she can now — give back, out of many words, only the last. Juno had done this to her. Time and again, when the goddess might have caught the nymphs lying with her Jupiter in the mountains, Echo would cunningly hold Juno in long conversation while the nymphs slipped away. When the daughter of Saturn saw through it, she said, "That tongue of yours, that made a fool of me — I'll give you only the smallest power over it, the briefest use of your voice." And she made good on the threat. So now Echo only doubles back the end of what is said and returns the words she hears.

So when she saw Narcissus wandering through the trackless country and caught fire, she followed his tracks in secret, and the more she followed, the closer the flame, the hotter she burned — the way quick sulphur smeared around the top of a torch snatches up a flame brought near it. Oh, how often she longed to come up to him with coaxing words and soft pleas! Her nature fought her and would not let her begin. But what her nature did allow, she was ready for — to wait for his sounds and send her own words back to them. By chance the boy, having lost the trusty band of his companions, called out, "Is anyone here?" and "Here," Echo answered. He stood amazed, and casting his eyes in every direction, shouted, "Come!" — and she called back the one who called. He looked around, and when no one came, said, "Why do you run from me?" — and got back exactly the words he had spoken. He stood his ground, fooled by the answering voice that seemed another's, and said, "Here, let us meet" — and Echo, who would never answer any sound more gladly, called back, "Let us meet," and made good on her own words, and came out of the woods and started toward him, to throw her arms around the neck she had longed for. He ran. And as he ran he cried, "Keep your hands off me! I'll die before I'll give myself to you!" She answered nothing but "I'll give myself to you." Spurned, she hid in the woods, covering her shamed face with leaves, and from that day lived alone in caves. But her love clung on and grew with the pain of being refused. Sleepless heartache wasted her wretched body away; her skin shriveled with a wasting thinness, and all the sap of her body drained off into the air. Only her voice and her bones were left. Then only her voice — her bones, they say, took on the shape of stone. Ever since, she hides in the woods and is seen on no mountain, but is heard by all: it is sound, and sound alone, that lives on in her.

So he had toyed with her, and with other nymphs born of the water or the mountains, and before them with whole crowds of admiring young men. At last someone he had scorned lifted his hands to the sky and prayed: "Then let him love the same way — and never have the thing he loves!" It was a fair prayer, and Nemesis, the goddess who punishes such pride from her shrine at Rhamnus, granted it.

There was a spring, clear and unclouded, its waters bright as silver, that no shepherd had ever muddied, no she-goat grazing down from the hills, no cattle of any kind. No bird had troubled it, no wild beast, no branch fallen from a tree. Grass grew all around it, fed by the nearby water, and a ring of trees that let no sun in to warm the place. Here the boy came, worn out by the heat and the work of hunting, drawn by the beauty of the spot and its cool spring. He lay down, and while he bent to quench his thirst, another thirst began to grow in him. As he drank, he was captivated by the reflection of the face he saw in the water, and fell in love with a hope that had no body — mistaking for a real thing what was only a shadow.

He stared at himself, stunned, and hung there motionless with the same fixed expression, like a statue carved from Parian marble. Stretched on the ground, he gazed at the twin stars that were his own eyes, at hair worthy of Bacchus, worthy of Apollo, at his smooth boyish cheeks, his ivory neck, the loveliness of his mouth, the rosy flush blended into snow-white skin. Everything he admired was exactly what made him admirable. Without understanding, he wanted himself. The one who praised was the thing being praised; as he chased, he was chased; he lit the fire and he burned in it. How many times did he lean down to give useless kisses to that lying spring! How many times did he plunge his arms into the middle of the water to catch the neck he saw, and never catch himself! He did not know what he was looking at, but what he looked at set him alight; the same illusion that fooled his eyes drove them wild.

Poor gullible boy, why do you keep grabbing at a fleeting image? What you want is nowhere. What you love — only turn away, and you will lose it. The thing you see is the shadow of a reflection; it has nothing of its own. It comes with you and stays with you; it will leave with you — if only you could leave.

No thought of food could pull him away, no thought of rest. Sprawled in the shaded grass, he stared with eyes that never had enough at the lie of that beautiful shape, and through his own eyes he was destroyed. Raising himself a little, he stretched his arms toward the surrounding woods and cried, "Oh, you woods — has anyone ever loved more cruelly than this? You would know; you have been a good hiding place for so many lovers. In all the long ages you have lived, do you remember anyone who wasted away like this? He delights me, and I see him — but the thing I see and love, I still cannot reach. That's how completely a lover can be deceived. And this makes my grief worse: no vast sea keeps us apart, no long road, no mountains, no city walls with the gates shut. Only a little water stands between us. He wants to be held himself — for every time I stretch down my lips to the clear pool, every time he strains up toward me, face lifted. You'd think I could touch him. It is the smallest thing that keeps lovers apart.

"Whoever you are, come out to me! Why do you fool me, one boy in all the world? Where do you go when I reach for you? It can't be my looks or my age that drive you off — nymphs have loved me too. That friendly face of yours promises me some kind of hope. When I reach my arms out to you, you reach yours back. When I smile, you smile. And often I have watched your tears while I was weeping. You answer my nods with nods, and — as far as I can guess from the movement of your lovely mouth — you send back words that never reach my ears. That boy is me! I understand it now; my own reflection does not fool me anymore. I am on fire with love for myself. I light the flames and I suffer them. What do I do? Ask, or be asked? And then, ask for what? What I want is already mine. My riches have made me a beggar. Oh, if only I could step out of my own body! It's a strange wish for a lover, but I want the thing I love to be gone from me. And now grief is stealing my strength; not much of my life is left. I am dying young. But death is no hardship — it will lay my sorrows down. I only wish the one I love could live longer. As it is, the two of us, one soul, will die together in a single life."

He spoke, and, out of his mind, went back to the same face, and blurred the water with his tears until the shape rippled and dimmed. Seeing it start to vanish, he cried out, "Where are you going? Stay! Don't abandon me, cruel one — don't leave the one who loves you! Let me at least look at what I'm not allowed to touch, and feed my wretched madness that way." Grieving, he tore his tunic down from the top and beat his bare chest with hands white as marble. The struck chest took on a soft red glow — the way apples do, pale on one side, red on the other, or the way grapes in their dappled clusters, still unripe, begin to blush with purple. As soon as he saw this in the water, now grown clear again, he could bear no more. As yellow wax melts before a small flame, as morning frost melts under a warming sun, so he wasted away, thinned by love, slowly consumed by the fire hidden inside him. The white blended with red was gone; gone was the strength, the vigor, all the things that had been so lovely to see a moment ago. Nothing was left of the body that Echo had once loved.

And when she saw it — still angry, still remembering how he had used her — she grieved anyway. Every time the pitiful boy said "Alas," she gave back "Alas" in her echoing voice; and when he struck his arms with his hands, she gave back the same sound of the blow. His last words, as he looked into the familiar pool, were these: "Oh boy I loved in vain!" — and the place gave back just as many words. When he said "Farewell," Echo said "Farewell" too. He let his weary head sink into the green grass, and death closed the eyes that still marveled at their master's beauty. Even then, taken down into the world below, he kept gazing at himself in the waters of the Styx. His sisters the Naiads wept for him and cut off their hair to lay upon their brother; the Dryads wept too, and Echo echoed their grieving back. They were already making ready the pyre, the shaken torches, the bier — but the body was nowhere to be found. In its place they found a flower, saffron at its heart, with white petals ringed around it.

When the story got out, it brought the prophet the fame he deserved through the cities of Greece, and the name of Tiresias the seer stood high. Yet one man alone despised him — Pentheus, son of Echion, a scorner of the gods. He laughed at the old man's warnings and threw his blindness in his face, the ruin of his lost sight. Tiresias only shook his temples, white with age, and said: "How lucky you would be if you too were robbed of this light, and could not watch the rites of Bacchus. For the day is coming — and I foretell it is not far off — when a new god will arrive here, Liber, the son of Semele. Unless you honor him with a temple, you will be torn to a thousand pieces and scattered, and with your blood you will defile the woods, your own mother, and your mother's sisters. It will happen. You will refuse the god his honor, and then you'll be sorry that in this darkness I saw all too clearly." As he said it, Echion's son shoved him away — but truth came behind the words, and the seer's prophecy was carried out.

Liber arrived, and the fields roared with festive shrieking. The people rushed out — mothers, brides, and men all mixed together, common folk and nobles alike, swept along to rites they had never known. "What madness," Pentheus shouted, "has stunned your minds, you sons of the Dragon, you children of Mars? Can bronze clashed against bronze do so much? A pipe with its curved horn, and cheap magic tricks? Are you men whom no war-sword could frighten, no trumpet, no ranks with drawn weapons — and now women's voices, and drunken raving, and packs of degenerates, and empty tambourines defeat you? Should I be amazed at you old men, who sailed across the wide seas and set up a new Tyre here, your household gods carried into exile — and now let them be taken without a fight? Or at you, the younger and stronger, closer to my own age — you should be gripping weapons, not fennel-wands, your heads covered by helmets, not leaves! Remember, I beg you, the stock you sprang from, and take on the spirit of that serpent who, one against many, destroyed so many. He died for his spring and his pool — but you, conquer for your own good name! He put brave men to death; you can drive off these soft ones and hold on to the honor of your fathers. If fate forbids Thebes to stand for long, I'd rather it were war engines and soldiers tearing down her walls, iron and fire roaring. Then we'd be wretched without disgrace; our fate would be worth grieving, but nothing to hide, and our tears would carry no shame. But now Thebes will fall to an unarmed boy, one who cares nothing for battles or weapons or horses, but only for hair soaked in myrrh, soft garlands, purple robes, and gold woven into embroidered cloth. I'll force him soon enough — just stand aside — to confess his father is a lie and his rites a fraud. Acrisius had spirit enough to scorn this hollow god and shut the gates of Argos in his face; and shall this foreigner terrify Pentheus and all of Thebes? Go quickly" — this to his servants — "go, and drag their leader here in chains. No sluggish delay in carrying out my orders!"

His grandfather Cadmus, and Athamas, and the rest of his household rebuked him and tried in vain to hold him back. Their warnings only made him fiercer. Curbed, his fury was goaded and grew, and the very restraint did harm. I have watched a torrent do the same — where nothing blocked its path, it ran gently, with a modest murmur; but wherever fallen logs and rocks stood in its way, it went foaming and boiling, wilder for the barrier.

Then — look — the servants came back covered in blood, and when their master demanded where Bacchus was, they said they had not seen him. "But we captured this one," they said, "his companion, a servant of the rites" — and they handed over a man, his hands bound behind his back, someone of Etruscan stock who had followed the god's worship. Pentheus fixed him with eyes that anger had made terrible, and though he could barely put off the moment of punishment, he said: "You there — soon to die, and by your death to be a lesson to the rest — tell me your name, your parents' names, your country, and why you take part in these newfangled rites." Without fear, the man answered: "My name is Acoetes; my homeland is Maeonia, my parents were common folk. My father left me no fields for hardy oxen to plow, no woolly flocks, no herds. He himself was poor, and made his living with line and hooks and rod, tricking the leaping fish and reeling them in. His skill was his whole fortune. When he handed the trade down to me, he said, 'Take what I have, heir and successor to my craft,' and dying, he left me nothing but the waters — the one thing I can call my inheritance from him. Soon, so I wouldn't be stuck forever among the same rocks, I learned to steer a ship, my right hand guiding the keel. I marked with my eyes the rainy star of the Olenian she-goat, and Taygete, and the Hyades, and the Bear, and the homes of the winds, and the harbors fit for ships.

"Once, making for Delos, I happened to touch the coast of the island of Chios. I brought us to shore with a pull of the right-hand oars, sprang lightly out, and landed on the wet sand. We spent the night there. As soon as dawn began to redden, I got up and told my men to bring fresh water, pointing out the path that led to a stream. Then I climbed a high hill myself to see what the breeze promised, called my crew, and headed back to the ship. 'Here we are!' said Opheltes, first of my mates, and — thinking he'd found a prize in that lonely field — he led along the shore a boy with the beauty of a girl. The boy seemed heavy with wine and sleep, staggering, barely able to keep up. I looked at his clothes, his face, his walk — I could see nothing about him that seemed mortal. I sensed it, and told my crew: 'What god is in that body I can't say — but there is a god in that body! Whoever you are, be kind to us, help our labors, and forgive these men.' 'Stop praying for us!' said Dictys — no one quicker at scrambling up to the top yardarms or sliding back down a rope. Libys agreed, and blond Melanthus, watchman of the prow, and Alcimedon, and Epopeus, who called out the rhythm to the oars and cheered their spirits — all of them agreed. So blind is the greed for plunder.

"'I will not let this ship be defiled by carrying off something sacred,' I said. 'Here I have the greatest authority.' And I blocked the gangway. Lycabas, the boldest of the whole crew, went into a rage. He had been driven out of an Etruscan town, paying for a brutal murder with exile. As I held my ground, he punched me in the throat with his young fist and would have thrown me overboard into the sea, if I hadn't caught a rope and clung to it, dazed as I was. The godless crew cheered him on. And then Bacchus — for it was Bacchus — as if the shouting had shaken off his sleep and the wine were draining from his mind and his wits returning, said, 'What are you doing? What's this shouting? Tell me, sailors, how did I get here? Where do you mean to take me?' 'Have no fear,' said Proreus. 'Just name the harbor you want to reach. We'll set you down on whatever land you ask.' 'Steer your course for Naxos,' said Liber. 'That is my home; it will be a friendly shore to you.'

"By the sea and by all the gods those liars swore it would be so, and told me to give sail to the painted ship. Naxos lay to our right. As I set the canvas for the right, each man in his own way hissed at me: 'What are you doing, you fool? What madness has hold of you, Acoetes? Steer left!' Most signaled their meaning with a nod; some whispered it in my ear. I was stunned. 'Let someone else take the helm,' I said, and pulled myself out of their crime and my own craft. The whole crew reviled me, muttering. One of them, Aethalion, said, 'As if all our safety depended on you alone!' — and he took over my job himself, and, leaving Naxos behind, steered the other way. Then the god, toying with them, as though only now he'd caught on to the trick, gazed out at the sea from the curved stern and, as if weeping, said: 'These are not the shores you promised me, sailors. This is not the land I asked for. What have I done to deserve this? What glory is it for you — grown men — to cheat a boy? For so many to deceive one?' I had been weeping already. The impious gang laughed at my tears and drove the sea faster with hurrying oars.

"Now I swear to you by the god himself — for no god is nearer than he is — that what I'm telling you is as true as it is beyond belief. The ship stood still on the water, as if it were held fast in a dry dock. Bewildered, the men kept working the oars, unfurled the sails, and tried to run with both together. Ivy tangled the oars, twisting up them in curling coils and spreading over the sails in heavy clusters of berries. And the god himself, his brow wreathed in bunches of grapes, brandished a lance draped in vine-leaves. Around him lay tigers, and the phantom shapes of lynxes, and the fierce spotted bodies of panthers.

"The men leaped overboard — whether from madness or from terror — and first Medon began to blacken all over, his body flattening, his spine bending into a curve. 'What monstrous thing are you turning into?' Lycabas said to him — and as he spoke, his own mouth gaped wide, his nose hooked, and his hardening skin drew scales across it. Libys, trying to pull back the resisting oars, saw his hands shrink into a small space — no longer hands, more like fins now. Another, reaching his arms toward the twisted ropes, found he had no arms, and, arching his limbless body, plunged into the waves; the tip of his tail was hooked, curved like the horns of a half moon. They flung themselves everywhere, spattering the ship with spray, surfacing and diving back under, playing like a dancing troupe, tossing their frolicking bodies and blowing the seawater out through their wide nostrils. Of the twenty who had been aboard just before — for the ship carried that many — I was the only one left. As I shook, frozen with fear, barely master of myself, the god steadied me. 'Cast the fear from your heart,' he said, 'and steer for Dia.' Landing there, I joined his worship, and I keep the rites of Bacchus to this day."

"We've lent our ears to a long rambling story," said Pentheus, "so that time might wear down our anger. Servants — drag this man off headlong, rack his body with hideous tortures, and send it down to the night of the Styx!" At once the Etruscan Acoetes was hauled away and locked in a solid cell. But while the cruel instruments of the death he'd ordered were being prepared — the iron, the fire — the story goes that the doors swung open by themselves and the chains slipped from his arms by themselves, with no one loosening them.

Echion's son held firm. Now he did not send others; he went himself, to where Mount Cithaeron, chosen for the sacred rites, rang with singing and the shrill cries of the Bacchantes. As a spirited warhorse whinnies when the trumpeter signals battle on the ringing bronze and it seizes on the lust for the fight, so the air, struck by those long wailing cries, stirred Pentheus, and hearing the clamor his rage flared white-hot again. About halfway up the mountain, ringed at its edges by woods, there was a clearing, free of trees, open to view on every side. Here, as he watched the sacred rites with unhallowed eyes, the first to see him was his own mother; the first to charge at him in a frenzy; the first to wound her son Pentheus, hurling her wand. "Come, my two sisters!" she cried. "That boar, the huge one roaming our fields — that boar is mine to strike!" The whole raving crowd rushed at him alone; they closed in together and chased him, trembling now, now speaking with less fury, now blaming himself, now confessing his sin.

Wounded, he still managed to say, "Help me, my aunt Autonoe! Let the ghost of Actaeon move your heart!" But she did not know who Actaeon was; she tore off his right hand as he pleaded, and his other arm was ripped away by Ino. The wretched man had no arms left to stretch toward his mother; showing her the raw wounds where his limbs had been torn away, he said, "Look at this, Mother!" At the sight, Agave howled, threw back her neck, tossed her hair through the air, and, gripping his severed head in her bloody fingers, cried out, "Look, my companions — this victory is our doing!" No faster does the wind strip from a tall tree the leaves touched by autumn's cold, barely still clinging, than the man's limbs were torn apart by those unspeakable hands. Warned by such an example, the women of Thebes flocked to the new rites, burned their incense, and worshiped at the holy altars.

Book IV

But Alcithoë, daughter of Minyas, will have none of it. She refuses to accept the god's wild rites, and in her recklessness she still denies that Bacchus is any son of Jupiter — and she has her sisters for partners in that impiety.

The priest had commanded the whole city to keep the festival: mistresses and serving-women alike were to lay their work aside, to cover their breasts with skins, to loosen the ribbons that bound their hair and wind garlands through it, and to take up the leaf-wreathed thyrsus in their hands; and he had prophesied that the anger of the slighted god would be savage if he were offended. The matrons obeyed, and the young brides too. They put away their weaving and their work-baskets and their half-finished wool, and they offered incense and called on the god by all his names — Bacchus, and Bromius, and Lyaeus; the Fire-born, the Twice-born, the only god ever given two mothers; and they added Nyseus, and unshorn Thyoneus, and Lenaeus lord of the winepress who first planted the joy-giving vine, and Nyctelius of the night-rites, and father Eleleus, and Iacchus, and Euhan — and all the other names besides that you bear, Liber, up and down the peoples of Greece.

For your youth is never used up. You are the boy who never ages; you are seen, most beautiful of all, high in the sky; and when you stand without your horns you have the face of a girl. The East was conquered by you, all the way to where dark-skinned India is washed by the far Ganges. It was you, god worthy of our worship, who struck down Pentheus and axe-bearing Lycurgus for their sacrilege, you who flung the Etruscan sailors into the sea, you who press the necks of the yoked lynxes under their painted reins. Bacchant women and satyrs come streaming after you — and that drunken old man, Silenus, propping his tottering limbs on a staff and clinging none too steadily to the swayback of his donkey. Wherever you go, the shouting of young men and the cries of women rise together, and hand-beaten drums, and the hollow clash of bronze, and the boxwood pipe with its long throat all sing out. "Be with us," the women of Thebes pray, "calm and kind" — and they keep the sacred rites as they were told.

Only the daughters of Minyas stay indoors, spoiling the festival with work no one should be doing on such a day. They card their wool, or twist the thread between their fingers, or bend to the loom, and keep their maids hard at it. At last one of them, drawing out her thread with a light thumb, said: "While the others are idling, crowding to these made-up rites, let us, whom Minerva keeps at her better craft, lighten our useful work with a bit of talk. Let's take turns telling a story, each to the others' unoccupied ears, so the long hours won't drag." The sisters liked the idea, and told her to be first.

She turned over in her mind which of the many tales she knew to tell — for she knew a great many — and could not decide. Should it be your story, Dercetis of Babylon, whom the Palestinians believe took on a new shape, scales sheathing your limbs, and stirred their pools? Or how your daughter grew wings and lived out her last years in whitewashed towers? Or how a water-nymph, with her spells and her too-powerful herbs, turned the bodies of young men into silent fish, until at last the same fate came for her? Or how the tree that once bore white fruit now bears it dark, stained by the touch of blood? That last one pleased her — it was no everyday tale — and so, while the wool ran out along her thread, she began.

"Pyramus and Thisbe — he the handsomest of young men, she the loveliest girl the East could show — lived in houses that shared a wall, in the city where Semiramis is said to have raised her high walls of baked brick. Being neighbors brought them together; it was where they first met, and where love first took its first steps. In time it grew. They would have joined hands in marriage, too, but their fathers forbade it. One thing, though, no father could forbid: they burned, both of them, their captured hearts on equal fire. There was no one they could confide in; they spoke by nods and signs. And the more the fire was smothered, the hotter it burned under the smothering.

"In the wall the two houses shared there was a thin split, a crack it had taken on long ago, when it was built. No one in all those years had ever noticed the flaw — but what does love not notice? You lovers were the first to find it, and you made it the road for your voices. Through it, safely, in the softest whisper, your endearments used to cross. Often, when they had taken their places — Thisbe here, Pyramus there — and each had caught the other's breath as it came, they would say, 'Jealous wall, why do you stand between two lovers? How hard would it be to let us join, our whole bodies together? Or if that's too much, at least open enough for a kiss. And don't think us ungrateful — we know we owe it to you that our words are given this crossing to loving ears.' So they would talk, apart and hopeless on their two sides, until, as night came on, they said 'Goodbye,' and each pressed kisses to their own face of the wall that could never reach across.

"The next morning had swept away the fires of the stars, and the sun had dried the frost from the grass, when they met again at the usual spot. First, in low murmurs, they poured out their long complaints; then they made a plan. That night, in the silence, they would try to slip past their guards and out their doors; and once out of the house, they would leave the city behind as well. But so as not to lose each other, wandering across the open country, they would meet at the tomb of Ninus and hide in the shadow of a tree — a tall mulberry, thick with snow-white fruit, standing close beside a cold spring. The plan pleased them, and the daylight, which seemed so slow to leave, at last sank into the sea, and out of that same sea the night came up.

"In the dark, clever Thisbe worked the door on its hinge and slipped out, unseen by her household. With her face veiled she reached the tomb and sat down under the appointed tree; love made her brave. And now — look — a lioness came, her foaming jaws smeared with the fresh kill of cattle, to quench her thirst at the neighboring spring. By the light of the moon Thisbe of Babylon saw her, still far off, and on trembling feet fled into a dark cave — and as she fled, the veil slipped from her shoulders and she left it lying behind. When the fierce lioness had drunk her fill of water and was turning back into the woods, she happened on the thin veil, without the girl in it, and shredded it in her bloodied mouth.

"Pyramus, who came out later, saw the unmistakable tracks of a wild beast in the deep dust, and the color drained from his whole face. But when he found the veil as well, soaked in blood, he said, 'One night will destroy two lovers. She was the one who deserved a long life; mine is the guilty soul. I killed you, my poor love — I, who told you to come by night to a place full of terror, and did not come here first myself. Tear my body apart, rip out my guilty guts with your savage jaws, all you lions who lurk beneath this rock! — But wishing for death is the coward's part.' He gathered up Thisbe's veil and carried it with him into the shade of the tree they had chosen, and when he had wept over the well-known cloth and covered it with kisses, he said, 'Now take a drink of my blood too.' And he drove the sword he wore into his side, and at once, dying, drew it back out of the hot wound. He fell on his back to the ground, and the blood leapt up high — the way water bursts from a lead pipe when the metal splits, hissing out through the narrow crack in a long jet, cutting the air. The fruit of the tree, spattered by that spray of death, turned dark, and the roots, drenched with his blood, dyed the hanging mulberries a deep purple.

"And now — look — with her fear not yet put aside, but afraid she might fail her lover, Thisbe came back, searching for the young man with her eyes and with all her heart, longing to tell him what great dangers she had escaped. She knew the place, she knew the shape of the tree — but the color of the fruit made her doubt: was this really it? While she wavered, she saw limbs on the bloody ground, quivering, twitching. She drew her foot back. Her face went paler than boxwood, and she shuddered like the surface of the sea when a light breeze skims across it and it trembles. But when she paused, and recognized her own love, she beat her innocent arms with loud blows of grief, and tore her hair, and threw her arms around the body she loved, filling his wounds with her tears, mixing her weeping into his blood; and pressing kisses on his cold face she cried out, 'Pyramus! What accident has torn you from me? Pyramus — answer me! It's your own Thisbe, your dearest, calling your name. Hear me — lift your fallen face!'

"At the name of Thisbe, Pyramus raised his eyes, already heavy with death, and once he had seen her, closed them again. When she saw her own veil, and saw the ivory sheath empty of its sword, she said, 'It was your own hand, and your love, that destroyed you, poor one. I too have a hand brave enough for this one thing, and I too have love — it will give me the strength to strike. I will follow you into death, and they will call me the most wretched cause and companion of your fate. You who could be torn from me by death alone — not even death will tear you from me now. And this one thing we ask of you both, with the words of us two: O our most wretched parents, mine and his, do not begrudge us this — that those whom a sure love and a last hour have joined be laid together in one tomb. And you, tree, whose branches now shade one poor body, and soon will shade two: keep the marks of this death. Bear fruit forever dark and fit for mourning, in memory of two lovers' blood.' She spoke, and set the point beneath her breast, and leaned down onto the blade still warm with his blood.

"Her prayers reached the gods; they reached the parents. For the color of the ripened fruit is black, and what the fire left of the two of them rests in a single urn."

She finished, and after a short pause Leuconoë began to speak, and her sisters fell silent.

"Even the Sun — who governs all things with his heavenly light — even he was caught by love. Let me tell you the loves of the Sun. This god is thought to have been the first to see the affair between Venus and Mars; this god sees everything first. Grieved at what he saw, he went to Venus's husband — Vulcan, the son of Juno — and showed him the wrong done to his marriage bed, and the place where it was done. Vulcan's mind reeled, and the work he was holding in his craftsman's hand dropped from it. At once he began to file out fine chains of bronze, and nets, and snares too delicate for the eye to catch — thread finer than the finest weaving, finer than the cobweb that hangs from the top of a rafter. He made them to yield to the lightest touch, the smallest movement, and arranged them cunningly around the bed. When his wife and her lover came together into that one bed, both were caught by the husband's craft, snared in the fetters he had rigged by this new trick, and pinned fast in the very middle of their embrace. The Lemnian god threw open the ivory doors and let the other gods in. There the two lay, bound in their shame — and more than one of the gods, and not the solemn ones, wished he could be shamed just so. The gods of heaven laughed, and for a long time this was the best-known story in all the sky.

"But the goddess of Cythera — Venus — did not forget the one who had exposed her. She took her revenge, and made the god who had wounded her secret love feel a wound of the same kind. What good now, son of Hyperion, is your beauty, your heat, your radiant light? You who scorch every land with your fire are burning with a strange new fire yourself. You who ought to be watching everything are watching Leucothoë, and on one girl you fix the eyes you owe to the whole world. Sometimes you rise too early in the eastern sky; sometimes you sink too late into the waves, and by lingering to look at her you stretch out the short hours of midwinter. Sometimes you fail altogether, and the trouble of your mind passes into your light, so that, darkened, you frighten the hearts of mortals. And it is not the moon, standing closer to earth, that blocks your face and makes you pale: it is this love that gives you that color. You love her alone. Clymene cannot hold you now, nor Rhodos, nor Perse, the lovely mother of Circe of Aeaea, nor Clytie, who longed for your embrace though you scorned her, and who at that very time was nursing a deep wound of love. Leucothoë made you forget them all — Leucothoë, whom Eurynome, loveliest woman of the perfume-bearing lands, brought into the world; but once the daughter grew up, she outshone her mother as far as her mother had outshone all others. Her father, Orchamus, ruled the Persian cities, seventh in line from old Belus at the root of the family.

"Under the western sky lie the pastures of the Sun's horses. Instead of grass they feed on ambrosia; it restores their limbs, worn out by the day's labor, and refits them for their work. And while the horses cropped their heavenly fodder there, and night was taking her turn at the sky, the god slipped into the chamber he longed for, changed into the shape of Eurynome, the girl's mother. There, among twice six handmaids by the lamplight, he saw Leucothoë drawing out smooth threads on her turning spindle. And so, like a mother kissing her dear daughter, he kissed her, and said, 'There is a private matter — maids, leave us; don't rob a mother of the right to speak with her own daughter alone.' They obeyed, and with no witness left in the room the god said, 'I am the one who marks out the long year, who sees everything, through whom the earth sees everything — the eye of the world. Believe me: you please me.' She was terrified; in her fear the distaff and spindle fell from her loosened fingers. Even her fear became her. He waited no longer, but returned to his true form and his familiar brightness. And the girl, though shaken by the sudden sight, was overcome by the god's radiance, and, all her protests set aside, she let his violence be done to her.

"Clytie was jealous — for her own love of the Sun had been anything but moderate — and, stung by rage at a rival, she spread the affair abroad and, once it was talked of, carried it to Leucothoë's father. He was fierce and unforgiving. As his daughter reached out her hands toward the Sun's light and pleaded, crying, 'It was he who forced me against my will,' he buried her alive, deep in the ground, and heaped a mound of heavy sand on top of her. The son of Hyperion scattered it with his rays and opened a passage for her to lift her buried face through — but by now, nymph, you could not raise your head, crushed under the weight of earth, and you lay there, a bloodless body. The driver of the winged horses is said to have seen nothing more heartbreaking since the fires that killed Phaethon. He tried, if he could, to call the cold limbs back to living warmth with the force of his rays; but since fate stood in the way of so great an effort, he sprinkled her body and the place with fragrant nectar, and, after a long lament, said, 'Still, you will reach the sky.' At once the body, steeped in that heavenly nectar, dissolved and soaked the earth with its perfume, and a shoot of frankincense, sending its roots down slowly through the soil, rose up and split the mound with its tip.

"But to Clytie the giver of light came no more — though her love might have excused her grief, and her grief her betrayal — and he ended everything between them. From that time she wasted away, having used her passion so madly. She could not bear the company of the other nymphs, and day and night, under the open sky, she sat on the bare ground, her hair loose and uncared for. For nine days she went without water, without food, feeding her fast on nothing but dew and her own tears, and never stirred from the ground.
