She only gazed at the face of the god as he moved across the sky, and turned her own face to follow him. They say her limbs took root in the soil; a sickly paleness turned part of her color into that of a bloodless plant, but part of her is red, and a flower much like a violet hides her face. And though a root holds her fast, she turns toward the Sun, and, changed as she is, she keeps her love."

She had finished, and the wondrous tale had caught their ears. Some of the sisters said it could never have happened; others insisted that true gods can do anything — but Bacchus is not among them. When they had fallen silent, Alcithoë was called on next. Running her shuttle through the threads of the loom strung before her, she said, "I'll pass over the well-known love of Daphnis, the shepherd of Ida, whom a nymph's jealous rage — she was his lover, and he wronged her — turned into a stone: such is the pain that burns those in love. Nor will I tell how Sithon, when nature's law was overturned, was of both sexes, now a man, now a woman. You too I skip over, Celmis — now hard adamant, but once the most faithful playmate of the infant Jupiter — and the Curetes, born from a heavy shower of rain, and Crocus, changed together with Smilax into little flowers. Instead I'll hold your minds with a sweet novelty.

"Learn how the spring of Salmacis got its evil name, and why it saps a man's strength and softens the limbs of whoever bathes in it. The reason is hidden, but the power of the fountain is famous. To Mercury the goddess of Cythera — Venus — bore a boy, and the water-nymphs raised him in the caves of Mount Ida. In his face you could make out both his mother and his father, and from them he took his name too. As soon as he had turned fifteen, he left his native mountains, left Ida where he had been reared, and delighted in wandering strange places and seeing unknown rivers, his eagerness making the hardship light. He came at last to the cities of Lycia, and to the Carians who border on them. There he found a pool of water, clear all the way down to the bottom — no marsh-reeds around it, no barren sedge, no sharp-tipped rushes; the water was pure and see-through, and the edges of the pool were ringed with fresh turf and grass forever green.

"A nymph lived there — but one no good at hunting, who never learned to bend a bow or race for speed; the only water-nymph swift Diana never knew. Her sisters, it's said, often told her, 'Salmacis, take up a javelin, or a painted quiver, and mix some hard hunting into your idleness.' But she took up no javelin and no painted quiver, and mixed no hunting into her idleness. Instead she would bathe her lovely limbs in her own spring, or comb her hair smooth with a boxwood comb from Mount Cytorus, and, looking into the water, ask its reflection what suited her best. Or, wrapping her body in a sheer robe, she would lie on the soft leaves or the soft grass, and often she gathered flowers. And she happened to be gathering them just then, when she saw the boy, and, seeing him, longed to have him.

"Still, though she was eager to go to him, she did not go before she had set herself in order, arranged her robe, composed her face, and made sure she looked beautiful. Then she began: 'You boy, worthy to be taken for a god — if you are a god, you could be Cupid; but if you are mortal, then blessed are the parents who made you, lucky your brother, and fortunate indeed any sister you have, and the nurse who gave you her breast. But far, far happier than all of them is she — if you have any bride, if you would ever grant a girl the honor of your marriage bed. If there is such a girl, let my pleasure at least be stolen; and if there is none, then let it be me — let us go into one bed together.' The nymph said no more. A blush colored the boy's face — he did not know what love was — but even the blush became him: it was the color of apples hanging on a sunlit tree, or of stained ivory, or of the moon reddening under her own brightness when the bronze cymbals ring out to rescue her, in vain. When the nymph would not stop begging for kisses — at least a sister's kisses — and was already laying her hands on his ivory-white neck, he said, 'Will you stop, or must I run for it and leave this place, and you with it?'

"Salmacis took fright. 'I give the place up to you freely, stranger,' she said, and pretended to turn and go. But she kept looking back, and hid herself in a thicket of shrubs, sinking down on one bent knee. The boy, thinking himself alone and unwatched on the empty grass, wandered here and there, and dipped the tips of his feet, then his feet up to the ankles, in the playful water. And with no delay — charmed by the gentle warmth of it — he slipped the soft clothes from his tender body. Then indeed Salmacis was lost, on fire with desire for his naked beauty. Her eyes blazed, no differently than when the sun, at its brightest, is caught and flung back from the face of a mirror. She could hardly bear to wait, hardly hold off her joy now, longing to hold him, out of her mind and barely in command of herself. He slapped his body with his cupped palms and dove quickly into the pool, and, throwing one arm after the other, shone through the clear water like an ivory statue, or white lilies, set behind clear glass.

"'I've won — he's mine!' the nymph cried, and, flinging every stitch of clothing far off, she plunged into the middle of the water. She seized him as he fought her, tore reluctant kisses from him, forced her hands beneath him, touched his unwilling body, and wound herself around the boy now one way, now another. At last, as he strained against her and tried to slip free, she twisted around him like a serpent that the eagle, the king's own bird, snatches up and carries aloft — the snake, hanging, binds its head and feet and wraps its coiling tail through the spread wings. Or as ivy weaves itself along tall tree-trunks; or as the octopus holds an enemy it has caught underwater, letting down its suckers from every side — so the boy held out and refused the nymph the joy she hoped for. She pressed harder, and clinging to him with her whole body, gripping fast, she said, 'Fight all you like, cruel one — you still won't escape. So ordain it, you gods: let no day part him from me, or me from him.' The gods heard her prayer and granted it. The two bodies, mingled, were joined together, and a single face was drawn over them both. Just as, when someone grafts branches under a single bark, he sees them join as they grow and mature together, so, when their limbs met in that stubborn embrace, they were no longer two, and their form was double: it could be called neither woman nor boy — it seemed neither, and both.

"So when Hermaphroditus saw that the clear water he had gone down into as a man had made him only half a man, and that his limbs had gone soft in it, he lifted his hands — but no longer with a man's voice — and said, 'Grant your son this gift, father and mother, since I bear the name of you both: whoever comes into these waters a man, let him go out half a man, and let him weaken the instant he touches the pool.' Both his parents were moved, and made the words of their two-shaped son come true, and tainted the fountain with a strange, unnatural drug."

That was the end of the stories — and still the daughters of Minyas pressed on with their work, still scorned the god and profaned his festival, when all at once drums no one could see boomed out with their harsh, jarring beat, and the pipe with its curved horn and the ringing bronze sounded, and the air went sweet with myrrh and saffron. And then, past all belief, the weaving began to grow green: the cloth hanging in the loom put out leaves like ivy; part of it turned into grapevine; what had been threads a moment before became tendrils; vine-shoots sprang from the warp; and the purple dye lent its glow to the painted grapes.

And now the day was spent, and there came that hour you could call neither dark nor light, but the very border of doubtful night mixed with the last of day — when suddenly the house seemed to shake, and torches thick with oil seemed to blaze, and the whole place to glow with red fires, and phantom shapes of savage beasts to howl through it. The sisters hid, scattering through the smoke-filled house, ducking into different corners to escape the fires and the light. And while they groped for the dark, a thin membrane stretched over their shrinking limbs and sheathed their arms in delicate wings. The darkness would not let them know how they had lost their old shape. No feathers lifted them, yet they held themselves up on see-through wings, and when they tried to speak they gave off a voice as tiny as their bodies, and wrung out their little complaints in a faint, thin squeak. They keep to houses, not woods; they hate the light, and fly by night — creatures that take their name from the fallen dusk of evening: bats.

But now the power of Bacchus was the great story in every corner of Thebes, and his aunt Ino went about telling everyone of the mighty new god's deeds — she alone, of so many sisters, free of grief, except for the grief her sisters had brought on her. Juno watched her: watched her carry her head high because of her children, her marriage to Athamas, and above all the god she had reared. She could not stomach it. "The son of my rival," she said to herself, "was able to change the sailors of Lydia into fish and drown them, was able to hand a son over to his own mother to be torn to pieces, was able to fold three daughters of Minyas into new-grown wings — and Juno can do nothing but weep over wrongs no one has answered for? And that is to be enough for me? That is the whole of my power? He himself shows me what to do. There's no shame in learning even from an enemy — and what madness can do, he's proved past all doubt in the butchering of Pentheus. So why shouldn't Ino be goaded by madness too, and driven down the same road her sisters took?"

There is a downward path, shaded with dismal yew, that leads through the depths of silence to the halls of the dead. Here the sluggish Styx breathes up its fogs, and this is the way the newly made ghosts come down, and the shades of those who have had their burial rites. A deathly pallor and an unending winter hold those bleak wastes far and wide, and the newcomer dead do not know the road that leads to the Stygian city, or where the grim palace of black Pluto stands. The vast city has a thousand entrances and gates thrown open on every side; and as the sea takes in the rivers of the whole earth, so that place takes in every soul, and is never too small for any tide of people, and never feels the crowd grow. The shades wander bloodless, without body or bones; some throng the place of judgment, some the hall of the king below; some ply the trades they knew, in imitation of the life they had before, while their own punishment holds the rest fast.

Juno, daughter of Saturn, so far gave in to her hatred and her rage that she left her home in heaven and went down there. The moment she stepped in, and the threshold groaned under the weight of her sacred body, Cerberus lifted his three heads and let out three barks at once. She called on the Sisters born of Night, terrible and unappeasable goddesses. They sat before the prison door, shut close with adamant, combing black vipers out of their hair. As soon as they knew her, there among the shadows of the dark, the goddesses rose. This is the place they call Accursed. Here Tityos gave up his innards to be mangled, stretched across nine acres of ground. You, Tantalus, could reach no water, and the tree that leaned over you kept snatching itself away. Sisyphus, you were forever chasing or shoving that stone that must roll back again. Ixion spun on his wheel, fleeing himself and following himself at once. And the granddaughters of Belus, who had dared to plot the murder of their cousins, kept fetching over and over the water they kept losing.

When Juno had looked over all of them with a hard eye — Ixion first of all, and then, turning from him, Sisyphus — she said, "Why should this one, alone of all the brothers, suffer punishment without end, while a rich palace keeps proud Athamas, who along with his wife has always held me in contempt?" And she laid out the reason for her hatred and her coming, and what she wanted. What she wanted was this: that the house of Cadmus should not stand, and that the Furies should drag Athamas into some horror. Command, promise, and plea she rolled into one, and pressed the goddesses hard. When Juno had finished, Tisiphone shook her tangled white hair as it was, tossed the snakes back from across her face, and said: "No need for a long speech. Whatever you order, count it done. Leave this loveless kingdom and take yourself back to the better air of heaven." Juno went back overjoyed; and as she made ready to enter the sky, Iris, the rainbow's goddess, purified her with a sprinkle of dewy water.

There was no delay. Grim Tisiphone caught up a torch soaked in gore, pulled on a cloak red with dripping blood, wound a writhing snake about her waist for a belt, and went out from her house. Mourning went with her as she walked, and Fright, and Terror, and Madness with its trembling face. She stopped on the threshold. The doorposts of Athamas, the son of Aeolus, are said to have shuddered; the maple doors went pale, and the sun fled the spot. His wife was terrified by these signs; Athamas was terrified too, and the two of them were getting ready to leave the house — but the deadly Fury planted herself in the doorway and blocked the way out. She spread wide her arms knotted with vipers and shook her hair. The snakes she shook cried out; some lay along her shoulders, some slid around her breast, hissing and spewing corruption and flickering their tongues. Then she wrenched two snakes from the middle of her hair and threw them, in her plague-bringing hand, at the pair of them; and the snakes went crawling over the breast of Ino and the breast of Athamas, breathing their foul breath into them. They left no wound on the body — it was the mind that felt the dreadful blows.

She had brought with her, too, a monstrous brew of liquid poison: foam from the mouths of Cerberus, and the venom of Echidna, and blind wanderings, and the forgetfulness of a darkened mind, and crime, and tears, and rabid fury, and the love of killing — all of it ground together, mixed with fresh blood, and boiled in a hollow bronze pot, stirred with a stalk of green hemlock. And while the two of them stood shaking, she poured the maddening poison over both their hearts and stirred it down into their inmost being. Then, whirling her torch round and round the same circle, she chased fire with fire until the flames blurred into one ring. So, triumphant, her orders carried out, she went back to the empty kingdom of great Pluto and unwound the snake she had worn as a belt.

At once the son of Aeolus, out of his mind, cried out in the middle of his hall: "Ho, men! Spread your nets here in these woods! I just saw a lioness with a pair of cubs!" And, insane, he chased the tracks of his wife as if she were a wild beast, and snatched Learchus — the boy smiling and stretching out his little arms from his mother's breast — and swung him two or three times through the air like a stone in a sling, and, savage, smashed the baby's small face against the hard rock. Then at last the mother, roused — whether by grief, or by the poison spread through her — screamed aloud and ran, out of her senses, with her hair streaming, carrying you, little Melicerta, in her bare arms, and shouting "Euhoe, Bacchus!" At the name of Bacchus, Juno laughed. "May your foster-child do you many such favors," she said.

A cliff hangs out over the sea. Its base is hollowed by the waves and shelters the water beneath it from the rain; its top is jagged and stretches its brow out over the open water. Ino climbed it — madness had given her the strength — and, held back by no fear, threw herself and her burden into the deep; the water, struck by her fall, turned white with foam. But Venus, pitying the undeserved sufferings of her granddaughter, coaxed her uncle with soft words: "God of the waters, Neptune, to whom fell a power second only to heaven's own — I ask a great thing, but take pity on my own blood, whom you see tossed on the boundless Ionian sea, and add them to your gods. I have some claim on the sea myself, if it's true that once, in the middle of the deep, I formed out of the foam — and from that foam my Greek name still comes." Neptune granted her prayer. He took from the two of them all that was mortal, laid on them a majesty to be revered, and made both their names and their shapes new: he called the son Palaemon, and his mother Leucothoë.

Her Sidonian companions had followed the prints of Ino's feet as far as they could, and saw the last of her at the edge of the rock. Sure that she was dead, they beat their breasts and tore their hair and their clothes for the house of Cadmus, and heaped the blame on the goddess as unjust and far too cruel to her rival. Juno would not bear their reproaches. "I'll make you the greatest monuments of my cruelty," she said — and the deed came hard on the word. The one who had loved Ino most cried, "I'll follow my queen into the sea!" and was about to leap, but could not stir a muscle: she stuck fast, fixed to the rock. Another, as she tried to strike her breast with the old blow, felt her arms stiffen mid-motion. One, who had chanced to stretch her hands out over the waves of the sea, turned to stone with her hands still reaching over those same waves. Another, tearing at the hair she had caught at the crown of her head, you would have seen — her fingers suddenly hardening in the hair. Whatever posture each was caught in, she froze in it. And some turned into birds — the Ismenides, who to this day skim the surface of that same water with the tips of their wings.

The son of Agenor does not know that his daughter and his little grandson are now gods of the sea. Broken by grief, by one calamity heaped on the next, and by all the omens he had seen, Cadmus the founder fled his own city, as though the bad luck of the place, and not his own, were bearing down on him. Driven by long wandering, he came at last, an exile with his exiled wife, to the borders of Illyria. And now, weighed down with woes and years, while they were dwelling again on the first disasters of their house and going back over their sufferings in talk, Cadmus said: "That serpent I ran through with my spear — was it a sacred one, back when I set out from Sidon and sowed the viper's teeth across the ground, a strange new seed? If the gods are avenging it with such unerring rage, then I pray that I myself may be stretched out as a serpent into a long belly." He had barely spoken when, as a serpent, he was stretched into a long belly, and felt scales rising over his toughening skin, and his darkening body flecked with spots of blue-green. He fell forward onto his breast; his legs, fused into one, tapered slowly to a smooth, rounded point. His arms were all that was left. He stretched out the arms that were left, and, with the tears still running down his face — a face still human — he said, "Come here, my wife, come here, most wretched of women, and while some part of me is still here, touch me, take my hand while it is still a hand, while the snake has not taken all of me." He wanted to say more, but his tongue split suddenly into two, and the words would not come to him though he willed them, and every time he tried to force out some lament, he hissed. That was the voice Nature left him.

His wife struck her bare breast with her hand and cried out: "Cadmus, stay! Unhappy man, free yourself from this monstrous shape! Cadmus, what is this? Where are your feet, where your shoulders and your hands, your color, your face — where, while I am still speaking, is all the rest of you? Why, gods of heaven, do you not turn me too into the same serpent?" So she cried; and he licked his wife's face, and slid into her dear breast as though he knew her, and gave her the old embraces and reached for the neck he knew so well. Everyone there — for companions were present — was horrified; but she stroked the smooth, crested neck of the dragon, and all at once there were two of them, gliding along in joined coils, until they slipped into the cover of a nearby grove. Even now they neither flee from a man nor strike him with a wound: gentle serpents, they remember what they once were.

Yet, changed as they were, the two of them found great comfort in their grandson — Bacchus, whom a conquered India worshipped as a god, and whom Achaia honored with temples raised in his name. Only Acrisius, son of Abas, sprung from the same line, was left to bar the god from the walls of Argos and take up arms against him and refuse to believe that he was any son of Jupiter. He did not believe Perseus a son of Jupiter either — Perseus, whom Danae had conceived in a shower of gold. But soon (such is the force of truth) Acrisius was sorry both that he had wronged the god and that he had failed to recognize his grandson: for the one was already set in heaven, while the other, carrying the memorable spoils of the viper-haired monster, was slicing through the yielding air on whirring wings. And as the conqueror hung over the Libyan sands, drops of blood fell from the Gorgon's head; the earth caught them and quickened them into snakes of every kind — which is why that land, to this day, swarms and seethes with serpents.

Carried on from there through the vast sky by warring winds, borne now here, now there, like a rain-heavy cloud, he looked down from the heights on the far-off earth below and flew over the whole round world. Three times he saw the cold Bears, three times the claws of the Crab; often he was swept off toward the west, often toward the east. And now, as the day was failing, afraid to trust himself to the night, he came to rest in the western part of the world, in the kingdom of Atlas, and looked for a little rest until the Morning Star should call up the fires of Dawn, and Dawn the chariot of the day.

Here lived Atlas, the son of Iapetus, greater than all other men in the sheer bulk of his body. Under this king lay the world's farthest edge, and the sea that takes in the panting horses of the Sun and receives his weary chariot. A thousand flocks and a thousand herds roamed his pastures, and no neighbor crowded his land. And here leaves of gleaming, radiant gold hid golden branches and golden fruit. "Friend," Perseus said to him, "if the glory of a great line means anything to you, Jupiter is the father of my blood; and if you admire great deeds, you'll admire mine. I ask for shelter and rest." But Atlas remembered an old prophecy — the one Themis of Parnassus had given him: "A time will come, Atlas, when your tree will be stripped of its gold, and a son of Jove will carry off the glory of the prize." Dreading it, Atlas had walled his orchard round with solid stone and given it over to a huge dragon to guard, and kept all strangers out of his land. To Perseus, too, he said, "Get far away from here, or the glory of the deeds you lie about — and Jupiter with it — will be far off when you need them!" And he added force to his threats, and tried to shove him from the door as Perseus held back and mixed brave words with gentle ones. Weaker in strength — for who could match Atlas for strength? — Perseus said, "Well, since my friendship means so little to you, then take this gift!" And, turning his own face away, he thrust out, on his left, the ghastly head of Medusa.

Huge as he was, Atlas became a mountain. His beard and his hair passed into forests; his shoulders and his arms became ridges; what had been his head was now the peak at the mountain's top; his bones turned to stone. Then, swelling out on every side, he grew to an immense height — so you ordained it, gods — and the whole sky, with all its stars, came to rest on him.

The grandson of Hippotas — Aeolus, keeper of the winds — had shut the winds up in their eternal prison, and the Morning Star, that rouses men to their work, had risen bright in the high heavens. Perseus took up his wings again, strapped them to his feet on either side, buckled on his hooked sword, and cut through the clear air on his beating ankle-wings. Leaving countless nations behind him, around and below, he came in sight of the Ethiopians and the fields of Cepheus. There the unjust judgment of Ammon had condemned the innocent Andromeda to pay the penalty for her mother's boasting tongue.

The moment Perseus saw her, her arms bound to the hard rock — but for the light breeze stirring her hair and the warm tears running from her eyes, he would have taken her for a statue of marble — he caught fire without knowing it, and stood amazed; seized by the vision of the beauty before him, he all but forgot to keep beating his wings in the air. When he had landed, he said, "You do not deserve these chains — only the chains that bind eager lovers to each other. Tell me, since I ask it, the name of this land and your own name, and why you are in chains." At first she was silent — a girl, she dared not speak to a man — and she would have hidden her modest face in her hands, if she had not been bound. Her eyes, all she could do, she filled with welling tears. When he pressed her again and again, she told him — so that she would not seem unwilling to confess some fault of her own — the name of her country and her own name, and how great her mother's confidence in her own beauty had been. Not everything was yet told when the water roared, and a monster came looming over the boundless sea, its head raised high, its breast covering the wide expanse of water. The girl shrieked. Her grieving father and mother were there, both of them wretched — though the mother more justly so — and they brought no help with them, only tears fit for the moment and loud lamentation, and they clung to her bound body.

Then the stranger spoke: "There will be a long time left for tears; the hour for bringing help is short. If I were to ask for her hand — I, Perseus, son of Jove and of the woman whom Jove filled, shut in her tower, with his fertile gold; Perseus, who overcame the snake-haired Gorgon, and dared to travel the winds of the air on beating wings — surely I would be preferred above all others as your son-in-law. But I'll try to add merit of my own to gifts like these, if only the gods are with me: I bargain only that she be mine, once she is saved by my courage." Her parents accepted the terms — who could hesitate? — and begged for his help, and promised the kingdom too, as a dowry on top.

Look now: as a ship with its beak fixed at the prow plows the water, driven on by the sweating arms of young rowers, so the monster came, shoving the waves aside with the thrust of its breast. It was as far from the rocks as a Balearic sling can send its whirled lead across the middle of the sky, when suddenly the youth, kicking off from the ground, shot high into the clouds. As the hero's shadow fell on the surface of the sea, the monster hurled all its fury at the shadow it saw. And just as the bird of Jupiter, when it spots in an open field a serpent baring its blue-black back to the sun, takes it from behind, and, to keep it from twisting its savage mouth around, sinks its greedy talons into the scaly neck — so the winged hero, plunging headlong through the empty air in his swift flight, pressed down on the monster's back, and, as it roared, drove his sword up to the curved hook into its right shoulder.

Torn by the deep wound, the beast now reared high into the air, now dove beneath the water, now wheeled about like a wild boar hemmed in and terrified by a baying pack of hounds. Perseus dodged the greedy jaws on his swift wings, and wherever the beast lay open he struck with his curved blade — now its back, crusted with hollow shells, now the ribs of its flanks, now where the tail thinned away and ended in a fish. The beast vomited from its mouth a flood of seawater mixed with red blood, and the spray soaked the hero's wings and weighed them down. Perseus no longer dared trust his dripping ankle-wings; he spotted a rock that stood clear of the water at its highest point when the sea lay calm, though now it was hidden by the heaving waves. Bracing himself on it, gripping the top of the ridge with his left hand, three or four times he drove the iron through the monster's guts. A shout of applause filled the shores and the high halls of the gods; Cassiope and Cepheus the father rejoiced, and hailed him as their son-in-law, and confessed that he was the prop and the savior of their house. Freed from her chains, the girl came walking forward — at once the reward of his labor and its cause.

Perseus washed his victorious hands in water dipped from the sea; and so that the harsh sand would not bruise the snake-bearing head, he softened the ground with leaves, spread over it fronds grown beneath the water, and laid Medusa's face, the daughter of Phorcys, on them. The fresh fronds, still alive with their thirsty pith, drank in the monster's power and hardened at its touch, taking a strange new stiffness in branch and leaf. And the sea-nymphs tried the wondrous thing on more fronds, and, delighted by the same result, scattered the seeds of them across the waves. Even now that same nature stays in the coral: it takes its hardness from the touch of the air, and what was a soft plant under the sea becomes stone above it.

To three gods he built three altars of turf: the left one to Mercury, the right one to you, warlike maiden — Minerva — and Jove's altar in the middle. A cow was sacrificed to Minerva, a calf to the wing-footed god, and a bull to you, greatest of the gods. And straightaway he took Andromeda, the prize of so great a deed, with no dowry asked. Hymen and Cupid waved the wedding torches before them; the fires were heaped high with rich perfumes; garlands hung from the houses; and everywhere lyres and flutes and pipes and singing rang out, the glad tokens of a joyful heart. The doors were thrown open, and the golden halls stood wide and displayed, and the nobles of King Cepheus came in to a banquet laid out in splendor.

When the feast was done and their spirits opened with the gift of generous Bacchus, Perseus asked about the ways and the character of the country and of its people. And when one of them had told him, he said, "Now, bravest Perseus, tell us, I beg you, by what courage and by what arts you carried off that head bristling with serpents." Perseus told them of a place lying beneath cold Atlas, safe behind a bulwark of solid rock; how at its entrance lived two sisters, the daughters of Phorcys, who shared the use of a single eye between them; how, by sly craft, he slipped his hand in the way as the eye was being passed from one to the other, and stole it; and how, through hidden, pathless country, over crags bristling with rough woods, he reached the home of the Gorgons; and how, along the fields and the roads, he saw the shapes of men and beasts turned from their own forms into stone at the sight of Medusa. Yet he himself had looked at Medusa's face only in the bronze of the shield on his left arm, in the reflection thrown back from it; and while a deep sleep held her and her snakes, he had struck the head from her neck — and Pegasus, the swift-winged, and his brother had been born from their mother's blood. He added, too, the dangers of his long journey — no invention, those — what seas and what lands he had seen far below him from on high, and what stars he had brushed with his beating wings.

Yet he fell silent sooner than they expected. Then one of the nobles took it up, asking why she alone of the sisters wore snakes braided in among her hair. And the stranger said: "Since you ask a thing worth the telling, hear the reason. She was once famous for her beauty, the envied hope of many suitors; and in her whole person there was no part more striking than her hair — I've met a man who said he'd seen it. The ruler of the sea, they say, forced himself on her in the temple of Minerva. Jove's daughter turned away and covered her chaste eyes with her shield; and, so that it would not go unpunished, she changed the Gorgon's hair into hideous water-snakes. And now, to strike terror into the enemies she stuns with dread, she still wears on her breast the very snakes that she made."

Book V

And while Perseus — the son of Danaë — was still in the middle of telling these tales to the gathered lords of Cepheus's people, the royal halls filled with the roar of a raging crowd. This was no clamor that sings a wedding; it was the din that cries out war. You could have compared that banquet, turned in an instant to uproar, to a calm sea that the wild fury of the winds churns up, lashing its waters into waves.

At the head of it all came Phineus, the reckless author of this attack, brandishing an ashwood spear tipped with bronze. "Look — here I am," he shouted, "here I stand, come to avenge the bride stolen from me! Now let your wings save you, if they can; let Jupiter save you, turned to his lying shower of gold — neither will tear you out of my hands!" And he drew back to hurl the lance.

But Cepheus cried out, "What are you doing? What madness, brother, drives you to this crime? Is this the thanks you pay for a service so great? Is this the bride-price you give for the life that was saved? It was not Perseus who took her from you — if you want the truth of it — but the terrible power of the Nereids, and horned Ammon, and the sea-beast that came to gorge itself on flesh torn from my own child. She was lost to you at the very hour she was doomed to die — unless, cruel as you are, that is exactly what you want: that she should die, so my grief can be your comfort. Was it not enough that you stood and watched her chained, and lifted not a finger to help — you, her uncle and her betrothed both? Now will you grieve on top of it that another man saved her, and rob him of his reward? If that reward looks so precious to you, you should have gone to fetch it off the rock where she hung bound. Let the man who did go — the man through whom my old age is not left childless — keep what he earned, by his deeds and by our given word. And understand this: he was not chosen over you, but over certain death."

Phineus said nothing back. He only looked from Cepheus to Perseus and back again, unsure which of them to strike first. Then, after a short pause, he threw his spear — hurled it with all the force his rage could lend — and threw it for nothing: it stuck fast in the couch. At that Perseus sprang up from the cushions at last, and in his fury he would have driven the weapon back through his enemy's chest — but Phineus had ducked behind an altar, and so (the shame of it) an altar sheltered a scoundrel. Still, the spear did not fly wasted: it lodged in the forehead of Rhoetus, who dropped, and when the iron was wrenched from the bone he thrashed on the ground and spattered the laid tables with his blood.

Then indeed the mob blazed up into ungovernable rage and flung their weapons. There were some who said that Cepheus deserved to die along with his son-in-law — but Cepheus had already gone out through the doorway, calling on right and good faith and the gods of hospitality to witness that this violence was done against his will. Warlike Minerva was there: she covered her brother Perseus with her aegis and put heart into him.

There was an Indian named Athis, born — so it is believed — beneath the glassy waters of the Ganges, to Limnaee the river's daughter. He was strikingly beautiful, and his rich dress only heightened it. He was in his prime, just sixteen, wrapped in a Tyrian purple cloak edged with a golden hem; a chain of gold set off his throat, and a curved clasp held back his hair, glossy with myrrh. He had been trained to hit a target however far off with a thrown javelin, but he was better still at drawing the bow. Even now, as he was bending the pliant horns of that bow with his hands, Perseus caught up a smoldering brand from the middle of the altar and struck him down, smashing his face into the splinters of his own skull.

When Lycabas the Assyrian — Athis's closest companion, and no man to hide the true love he bore him — saw those features, so often praised, rolling now in blood, he wept for Athis as the youth breathed his life out through that bitter wound. Then he snatched up the bow Athis had been bending. "Now the fight is with me," he said. "You will not gloat long over a boy's death — a thing that wins you more hatred than glory." He had not yet finished speaking when the piercing arrow leapt from the string. Perseus dodged it, and it hung caught in the folds of his robe. He turned on Lycabas the curved falchion that had already proved itself in the killing of Medusa, and drove it into his chest. Even as he died, his eyes swimming in a black night, Lycabas looked round for Athis, sank down against him, and carried to the shades below the comfort of a death shared with the one he loved.

Then Phorbas of Syene, Metion's son, and Amphimedon the Libyan, both hungry to join the fight, went down as they slipped in the blood that lay warm and wide across the floor; as they struggled up, the sword met them — driven into the ribs of the one, into the throat of Phorbas. But against Erytus, Actor's son, whose weapon was a broad double-axe, Perseus did not use his hooked sword. Instead he lifted in both hands a huge mixing-bowl, all deep-carved figures and massive weight, and smashed it down on the man. Erytus vomited red blood, toppled backward, and beat the ground with his dying head. Then Perseus struck down Polydegmon, of Semiramis's line, and Abaris from the Caucasus, and Lycetus the son of Sperchius, and long-haired Helix, and Phlegyas, and Clytus — and trampled the mounded heaps of the dying he had piled up.

Phineus, not daring to close with his enemy hand to hand, hurled a javelin — but a slip sent it into Idas instead, a man who had held back from the war in vain and taken up arms for neither side. Idas glared at cruel Phineus with hard eyes. "Since you drag me into a side after all," he said, "then take the enemy you have made, Phineus — and pay for this wound with a wound." He was just drawing the dart from his body to throw it back when he crumpled, collapsing on limbs emptied of blood.

Here too Hodites — next in rank among Cepheus's men after the king himself — fell beneath the sword of Clymenus. Hypseus cut down Prothoenor, and Perseus cut down Hypseus. Among them was old Emathion as well, a man who loved justice and feared the gods; since his years forbade him the sword, he fought with his voice instead, condemning and cursing those wicked weapons. As he clung to the altars with trembling hands, Chromis struck his head off; it fell straight onto the altar, and there, on the half-living tongue, it still spat out its curses before he breathed his last soul into the flames.

After them the twin brothers Broteas and Ammon — unbeaten with the boxing-glove, if only swords could be beaten by boxing — died by Phineus's hand; and Ampycus too, the priest of Ceres, his temples bound with a white fillet. And you, Lampetides — you were never meant for work like this, but for tuning the lyre to your voice, the labor of peacetime; you had been asked to grace the feast and the festival with your song. As you stood off to one side, holding your harmless plectrum, Pedasus jeered at you: "Go sing the rest to the ghosts down by the Styx!" and fixed his sword-point in your left temple. You fell, and your dying fingers plucked once more at the lyre's strings, so that as you dropped you struck out, by chance, one last mournful song.

Fierce Lycormas would not let him die unavenged: he tore a stout bar from the right-hand doorpost and slammed it against the bones at the center of Pedasus's neck, and Pedasus dropped to the ground like a felled bullock.

Pelates of the Cinyps was trying to wrench the oak bar from the left-hand post — and as he tried, his right hand was pinned to it by the spear of Corythus the Marmarid, nailed fast to the wood. While he hung there caught, Abas ran him through the side; but he did not fall — dying, he hung from the post that still gripped his hand.

Melaneus went down too, a man of Perseus's own side, and Dorylas, the richest lord of Nasamonian land — Dorylas, rich in acres, whom no one matched for the breadth of his fields or the heaps of incense they yielded him. The hurled steel stuck slantwise in his groin, a killing spot. When Halcyoneus of Bactria, who had dealt the wound, saw him sobbing out his soul and rolling his eyes, he said, "Out of all your fields, keep this one patch of earth you lie on," and left the lifeless body.

But Perseus, as his avenger, wrenched the still-warm spear from the wound and flung it back at Halcyoneus; it took him full in the nostrils, drove clean through his neck, and jutted out on either side. And while Fortune was steadying his hand, he laid low Clytius and Clanis, sons of one mother, each by a different wound: through both of Clytius's thighs an ash spear was driven by a strong arm, while Clanis bit down on the javelin in his mouth. Down went Celadon of Mendes; down went Astreus, born of a Palestinian mother and no certain father; and Aethion, once sharp-eyed to read the things to come, now fooled by a false omen; and Thoactes, the king's armor-bearer; and Agyrtes, infamous for the murder of his own father.

Still more work remained than was already done, for the whole mob had set its mind on crushing one man. From every side the sworn ranks pressed the fight, in a cause that made war on merit and good faith alike. On Perseus's side his father-in-law stood loyal — and helpless — with the new bride Andromeda and her mother, who filled the halls with their shrieks of encouragement; but the clash of weapons and the groans of the dying drowned them out. Bellona, the goddess of war, was flooding the household gods, defiled now, with a tide of blood, and stirring the battle to fresh life.

Phineus and the thousand who followed Phineus ringed Perseus in, alone. Their darts flew past him thicker than winter hail — past both his flanks, past his eyes, past his ears. He set his shoulders against the stone of a great pillar, and with his back thus guarded he turned to face the crowd bearing down on him and held them off. On his left pressed Molpeus of Chaonia, on his right Ethemon of Nabataea. Like a tiger that hears, from two different valleys, the lowing of two herds, and, goaded by hunger, cannot choose which to charge though it burns to charge at both — so Perseus, torn between right and left, drove off Molpeus with a wound clean through the leg and was content to let him flee. Ethemon gave him no such time, but raged in, mad to sink a wound deep in Perseus's neck; he swung with reckless force, and his sword shattered against the column's edge; the blade sprang off and lodged in its own owner's throat — though the blow had not force enough to kill him. Perseus stabbed the trembling man, who stretched out his unarmed hands in vain, with the falchion Mercury had given him.

But when Perseus saw his courage giving way at last to sheer numbers, he called out, "Since you force my hand yourselves, I will fetch help from an enemy. Turn your faces away, every friend of mine here!" — and he raised the Gorgon's head. "Find someone else to work your marvels on," said Thescelus — and even as his hand made ready to hurl its deadly javelin, he froze in that very pose, a statue of marble. Next to him, Ampyx thrust his sword at the breast of Perseus, that man of great and daring heart — and in the thrusting his right hand stiffened, unable to move forward or back. But Nileus, who had falsely bragged that the seven-mouthed Nile had fathered him, and had engraved its seven channels on his shield, half in silver, half in gold, cried, "Look, Perseus, at the source of my line! You will carry a great comfort down to the silent shades — that you fell to a man as great as I am." The last of his words was choked off in the middle of the sound; you would think his half-open mouth was still trying to speak, but it gave his words no way through. Eryx rounded on them: "It is your own cowardice that has frozen you, not the Gorgon's locks! Charge in with me and throw down this boy and his sorcerer's weapons!" He was about to charge when the ground caught his feet, and he stood there an immovable stone, an armed statue.

These men earned their punishment. But there was one, Aconteus, a soldier of Perseus's own: fighting for his lord, he caught sight of the Gorgon and hardened, stone rising over him. Astyages, thinking he was still alive, struck him with a long sword — and the sword rang out with a shrill, clear note. While Astyages stood there stunned, he took on the same nature himself, and the look of a man caught in astonishment stayed fixed on his marble face. It would take too long to name the men of lower rank. Two hundred bodies were still left for the fight; two hundred bodies stiffened at the sight of the Gorgon.

Now at last Phineus was sorry for his unjust war. But what could he do? He saw the statues standing in their scattered poses, recognized his own men, and, calling each by name, begged them for help. Not yet believing it, he touched the bodies nearest him: marble. He turned his face away, and — a suppliant now — stretched out his hands and held his arms aside in confession of his guilt. "You win, Perseus!" he said. "Take away your monster, take that stone-making face of your Medusa, whatever she is — take it away, I beg you! It was not hatred, not the lust for a throne, that drove me to war — it was for a wife that I took up arms. Your claim was the better one in merit, mine only in time. I am not ashamed to yield. Grant me nothing, bravest of men, but this one life; let all the rest be yours."

As he said all this, not daring even to look at the man he was pleading with, Perseus answered: "What I can give you, most craven Phineus — and it is a great gift for a coward — that I will give. Put away your fear: no weapon shall touch you. Better still, I will make you a monument to last forever; in my father-in-law's house you will always be on show, so that my wife can console herself with the likeness of the man she was promised to." And with that he swung Medusa, the daughter of Phorcys, round to the side toward which Phineus had turned his frightened face. Then, even as Phineus struggled to twist his eyes away, his neck went rigid, and the very moisture of his eyes hardened into stone. But still the fearful face, the pleading look, the hands hanging down, the guilty crouch — all of it stayed, exactly as it was.

Perseus came home victorious, his wife beside him, and entered the walls of his fathers' city. There, as the champion and avenger of his wronged grandfather Acrisius, he moved against Proetus — for Proetus had driven his brother out by force of arms and seized the citadel of Acrisius. But neither his weapons nor the stronghold he had so wrongly taken could save him against the grim eyes of the snake-haired monster.

And yet you, Polydectes, lord of little Seriphus — the young man's courage, proven through so many trials, had not softened you, nor had his sufferings; you nursed instead a hard and unbending hatred, and there was no end to your unjust spite. You even belittled his fame, and claimed that the killing of Medusa was a lie. "Then we will give you proof of the truth," said Perseus. "Shield your eyes, the rest of you!" — and with Medusa's face he turned the king's own face to bloodless stone.

All this while, Minerva had stayed at the side of her brother — Perseus, the child of the golden shower. Now, wrapped in a hollow cloud, she left Seriphus behind, with Cythnus and Gyarus on her right, and, taking the shortest way across the sea, made for Thebes and Helicon, the mountain of the virgin Muses. Reaching it, she came to rest and spoke to the learned sisters: "The fame of a new spring has come to my ears — one that the hard hoof of the winged horse, sprung from Medusa's blood, struck open. That is what brings me here. I wanted to see the marvel for myself; I saw the horse himself born out of his mother's blood." Urania answered, "Whatever brings you to our home, goddess, you are most welcome to us. The story is true: Pegasus is indeed the maker of this spring" — and she led Minerva to the sacred waters.

Minerva marveled a long while at the pool struck open by a hoof, then looked round at the groves of the ancient woods, the caves, the grass picked out with countless flowers, and called the daughters of Mnemosyne happy alike in their art and in their home. One of the sisters answered her: "O Minerva — you who would have come to be one of our number, had your valor not carried you off to greater deeds — you speak truly, and you are right to praise both our art and our home; ours is a happy lot, if only we can be safe. But nothing is off-limits to wickedness any longer, and so everything frightens our virgin minds. Cruel Pyreneus still turns before our eyes, and I have not yet fully come back to myself. That savage man had seized the lands of Daulis and Phocis with his Thracian troops and ruled them by force. We were on our way to the temple on Parnassus; he saw us going, and, pretending to honor our godhead with a false face — for he knew who we were — he said, 'Daughters of Mnemosyne, stop here. Do not scruple, I beg you, to shelter under my roof from the foul weather and the rain' — for it was raining — 'the gods above have often gone into humbler cottages than mine.' Moved by his words and by the weather, we agreed and stepped into the front rooms of his house. The rain soon stopped; the North Wind mastered the South, and the dark clouds fled from a clearing sky. We meant to leave — but Pyreneus shut up his house and made ready to force us, and we escaped him only by taking wing. He stood high on his rooftop as though to follow: 'Whatever way is open to you,' he said, 'will be open to me as well' — and in his madness he flung himself from the top of the tower, and fell on his face, and shattered the bones of his skull, and dying beat the ground he had stained with his wicked blood."

So the Muse was speaking — when wings whirred through the air, and a voice, as if in greeting, came down from the high branches. Jupiter's daughter looked up, searching for where such clear-speaking tongues could be sounding from, and thought a human being had spoken. But it was birds — nine magpies, perched on the boughs, bewailing their fate, mimicking every sound. As the goddess wondered at them, Urania began: "These too, not long ago, were beaten in a contest and added to the flock of birds. Pierus, rich in the fields of Pella, was their father; their mother was Euippe of Paeonia, who called nine times on Lucina, the goddess of childbirth, and nine times gave birth. Puffed up with their own number, the foolish sisters came marching through all the cities of Thessaly and Achaea, and challenged us to a contest with words like these: 'Stop cheating the ignorant crowd with your empty sweetness. If you have any faith in yourselves, contend with us, you goddesses of Thespiae — we will not be beaten in voice or in skill, and there are just as many of us. Either yield, if you lose, the spring that Medusa's steed opened and the fountain of Aganippe as well, or we will give up the plains of Emathia as far as the snowy Paeonians. Let the nymphs be the judges between us.' It was shameful to compete with them — but to back down seemed more shameful still. The chosen nymphs swore their oath by the rivers and took their seats on thrones of living rock. Then, without even drawing lots, the one who had first proposed the contest began to sing: the wars of the gods, and false glory heaped on the Giants, and the great gods belittled. She sang how Typhoeus, sent up from the deepest realm of the earth, had struck terror into the dwellers in heaven, so that they all turned their backs and fled, until the land of Egypt took them in, exhausted, and the Nile that splits into seven mouths. She told how earth-born Typhoeus came even there, and the gods above hid themselves in borrowed shapes. 'Jupiter,' she said, 'became the leader of a flock — which is why, even now, Libyan Ammon is shown with curling horns. Apollo hid as a crow, Bacchus, Semele's son, as a goat, Diana, Apollo's sister, as a cat, Juno, the daughter of Saturn, as a snow-white cow, Venus as a fish, and Mercury under the wings of an ibis.'

"So far she had raised her singing voice to the lyre. Then it was our turn — we of Aonia were called on to sing. But perhaps you have no leisure for this, no time to spare your ears to our songs?" "Have no doubt of it — tell me your song, in its proper order," said Minerva, and she settled down in the light shade of the grove. So the Muse went on. The whole conduct of the contest, she told Minerva, had been handed to one of their number; and that one rose now, and bound up her loose-flowing hair with ivy.

Calliope brushed her thumb across the plaintive strings to try them, and to the struck chords she wedded this song:

Ceres was the first to break the clod with the curved plough, the first to give the earth its grain and its gentle nourishment, the first to give it laws. Everything we have is the gift of Ceres. She must be my song — if only I could sing verses worthy of the goddess. And she is surely worthy of a song.

The vast three-cornered island of Sicily lies heaped upon the limbs of a giant, and with its enormous weight it pins down Typhoeus, who once dared to hope for a home in heaven. He strains, of course, and struggles again and again to rise — but his right hand is trapped beneath Pelorus, the cape toward Italy, his left beneath you, Pachynus, and his legs are crushed under Lilybaeum, while Etna weighs down his head. Beneath it Typhoeus lies on his back, flinging up sand and vomiting flame from his savage mouth. Often he wrestles to shove off the weight of the land, to roll the towns and the great mountains from his body — and then the earth shudders, and even the king of the silent dead trembles, afraid the ground will split, gape wide in a great chasm, and let the daylight come pouring in to terrify the cowering ghosts.

Fearing that ruin, the lord of the dead had come up out of his shadowy kingdom, and, riding in his chariot behind black horses, he went carefully round the foundations of the Sicilian land. When he had searched enough to be sure that no part of the ground was giving way, he laid his fear aside. But Venus, sitting on her own mountain of Eryx, saw him wandering there, and, drawing her winged son into her arms, she said: "My son — my weapon, my hands, my power — take up those arrows of yours that conquer everyone, Cupid, and drive your swift shafts into the heart of the god who drew the last lot when the three brothers split the world three ways, the one who got the realm of the dead. You master the gods above, and Jupiter himself; you master the beaten powers of the sea and the very one who rules them. Why should the underworld hold out? Why not spread your empire — and your mother's — over it too? A third of the whole world is at stake. And yet even in heaven, such is the patience I've been reduced to, we are treated with contempt, and the power of Love dwindles along with mine. Don't you see how Pallas has slipped away from me, and Diana with her hunting-javelin? And Ceres' daughter, too, will stay a virgin, if we let her — for she is reaching after the very same hopes. So, for the sake of the realm we would share, if that means anything to you, join the goddess to her uncle." So Venus spoke. He opened his quiver and, as his mother wished, set apart out of his thousand arrows a single one — but the sharpest of them all, the surest, the most obedient to the bow. He braced the pliant horn against his knee, bent it, and struck Pluto through the heart with the barbed reed.

Not far from the walls of Henna lies a deep lake named Pergus. Not even the Cayster, gliding along, hears more swan-song on its waters than that lake does. A wood crowns the pool, ringing it on every side, and with its leaves, like an awning, it keeps off the blows of the sun. The branches give coolness, the moist ground gives flowers of Tyrian purple. It is spring there without end. In this grove Proserpine was playing, gathering violets or white lilies, filling her baskets and the fold of her dress with a girl's eager delight and racing her friends to pick the most — when, almost in the same instant, Pluto saw her, loved her, and swept her away. So headlong was his love. Terrified, the goddess cried out in her grief for her mother and her friends — but for her mother most of all. And when she tore her dress from its upper hem, the flowers she had gathered spilled from the loosened folds. So untouched was she in her childish years that even this loss — the fallen flowers — stirred a sorrow in her. Her captor drove his chariot on, urging his horses forward, calling each by its name, shaking along their necks and manes the reins stained a dark iron-rust. He was carried past deep lakes, past the pools of the Palici that reek of sulphur and boil up where the earth has burst open, and past the place where the Bacchiadae — a people sprung from Corinth of the two seas — built their city walls between two harbors of unequal size.

Between Cyane and Pisaean Arethusa there is a stretch of water that draws together, penned in by two narrow horns of land. Here lived Cyane, most famous of the Sicilian nymphs, and the pool took its name from her. From the middle of the water she rose, bare to the waist, and knew the god. "You'll go no further!" she cried. "You cannot be Ceres' son-in-law against her will. The girl should have been asked for, not stolen. And if I may compare small things with great, Anapis loved me too — but he wooed me, and I married him won over, not frightened into it, the way this girl has been." She spoke, and, spreading her arms wide to either side, she blocked his way. The son of Saturn held his rage no longer. Spurring on his terrible horses, he swung his royal sceptre with his strong arm and drove it down into the very bottom of the pool. The struck earth opened a road down to Tartarus and took the plunging chariot into the mouth of the crater.

But Cyane, grieving for the goddess snatched away and for the slighted rights of her own spring, carried an inconsolable wound in her silent heart, and wasted entirely into tears. Into the very waters whose great presiding spirit she had just now been, she melted away. You could see her limbs soften, her bones grow supple and bend, her nails lose their hardness. The slenderest parts of her went first, dissolving — her sea-blue hair, her fingers, her legs and feet, for it is a short passage from such thin limbs into cold water. After these her shoulders, her back, her sides, her breast faded away into thin trickling streams. At the last, in place of living blood, clear water crept into her failing veins, and nothing was left that a hand could hold.

Meanwhile the frightened mother searched for her daughter through every land, over every sea — and searched in vain. Dawn, coming up with dew still in her hair, never found her resting; neither did the evening star. In both hands she kindled pine torches at the fires of Etna and carried them, never resting, through the frost-bound dark. And again, when the kindly day had dimmed the stars, she went on hunting for her daughter from the setting of the sun to its rising. Worn out with her labor, she grew thirsty, and no spring had washed her lips, when by chance she caught sight of a little cottage roofed with thatch, and knocked at its small door. Out came an old woman, who saw the goddess, and when Ceres begged for water gave her a sweet drink she had covered over with toasted barley-meal. While Ceres drank what she was given, a bold, hard-faced boy stood in front of her, laughed, and called her greedy. The goddess took offense, and with the part of the drink not yet swallowed she flung the barley-and-water full in his face as he spoke. His skin drank in the spots. Where a moment before he had arms, now he had legs; a tail was added to his altered body. He shrank into a tiny shape — so there would be no great power in him to do harm — smaller in measure than a little lizard. The old woman stared and wept and reached to touch the strange thing, but it fled from her and made for a hiding place. Its name suits its shame: the star-spotted newt, its body flecked all over with scattered spots.

Through what lands and what waters the goddess wandered would take too long to tell. The whole world ran out before her searching did. She came back to Sicily, and, as she ranged over everything on her way, she reached Cyane too. Had Cyane not been changed, she would have told her everything; but though she longed to speak, she had no mouth and no tongue, nothing left to speak with. Still, she gave a plain sign: on the surface of her sacred pool, where it happened to have fallen, she showed Proserpine's sash, which the mother knew at once. The moment she recognized it — as if only now she had learned her daughter was stolen — the goddess tore at her disheveled hair and struck her breast again and again with her open hands. She still did not know where the girl was, but she cursed every land, called them ungrateful and unworthy of her gift of grain — and Sicily above the rest, where she had found the traces of her loss. So there, with a savage hand, she smashed the ploughs that turn the clods; in the same fury she struck down the farmers and their working oxen and gave them to death; she ordered the fields to betray the seed entrusted to them, and she blighted the seed itself. Sicily's famous fertility, its fame spread across the wide world, now lay exposed as a lie: the crops died in the first green blade; now too much sun scorched them, now too much rain. Stars and winds did their damage; greedy birds picked up the scattered seed; darnel and thistle and stubborn couch-grass wore down the wheat.

Then Arethusa lifted her head from her Elean waters and swept the dripping hair back from her forehead to her ears, and said: "You who have sought your daughter over the whole world, mother of the harvest — put an end to your measureless toil, and don't turn your anger and violence on a land that has been faithful to you. The land has done nothing to deserve it; it opened against its will to the theft. And it isn't for a homeland of my own that I plead — I came here a stranger. Pisa is my homeland; my origin is from Elis. I live in Sicily as a foreigner, yet this land is dearer to me than any soil on earth. This is Arethusa's home now, these my household gods, this my dwelling — spare it, gentlest goddess. Why I was moved from my own place and carried across so great a sea to Ortygia, there will come a fitting hour to tell, when you are eased of your grief and your face is happier. The earth gives me passage: carried down through its deepest caverns, I lift my head here and look on stars I had long done without. And so, while I glide underground through the Stygian stream, there with my own eyes I saw your Proserpine. She was sad, and her face not yet free of fear — but a queen even so, the greatest lady of that shadowed world, the mighty consort of the tyrant of the dead."

The mother stood stunned at these words, as if turned to stone, and for a long while she seemed a woman thunderstruck. When at last the crushing grief gave way to a fury as crushing as itself, she rode out in her chariot into the upper air. There, her whole face clouded, her hair streaming loose, she stood before Jupiter, a figure of grievance, and said: "I have come to you as a suppliant, Jupiter, for my own blood and for yours. If a mother counts for nothing, let the daughter move the father. Don't, I beg you, let your care for her be worth less because I was the one who bore her. Look — my daughter, sought so long, is found at last — if you call it finding to lose her more surely, or if you call it finding merely to learn where she is. That she was carried off, I will bear — so long as he gives her back. A daughter of yours does not deserve a robber for a husband — if she is no longer a daughter of mine."

Jupiter answered: "She is a pledge and a charge we share, this daughter, yours and mine alike. But if we care to give things their true names, this was no wrong that was done — it was love. And that son-in-law will be no shame to us, if only you are willing, goddess. Even if all else were lacking, how much it is to be Jupiter's brother! And in fact nothing else is lacking, and he yields to me in nothing but the draw of the lots. But if your desire for the parting is so strong, Proserpine shall return to heaven — on one fixed condition: that there she has touched no food with her lips. For so it has been settled by the compact of the Fates."

He had spoken; and Ceres was resolved to bring her daughter up. But the Fates would not allow it, for the girl had already broken her fast. While she wandered, all innocence, in the tended gardens, she had picked a red pomegranate from a bending branch, and, taking seven seeds from its pale rind, she had crushed them in her mouth. And of everyone there, one alone had seen it — Ascalaphus, whom the nymph Orphne, no obscure figure among the nymphs of Avernus, is said once to have borne to her own Acheron beneath the black woods. He saw, and by his cruel tattling he robbed her of her return. The queen of Erebus groaned, and turned that unholy witness into a bird: she sprinkled his head with water from Phlegethon and changed it into a beak, into feathers, into great round eyes. Lost to his own shape, he is wrapped in tawny wings; his head swells, his nails curve back into long talons, and he can barely stir the feathers that have sprung along his sluggish arms. He becomes a loathsome bird, the herald of grief to come — the slothful screech-owl, a dire omen to mortals.

He, at least, might be thought to have earned his punishment by his tattling tongue. But you, daughters of Achelous — where did your feathers come from, and your birds' feet, when you still wear the faces of girls? Was it because, learned Sirens, you were among her companions when Proserpine gathered the flowers of spring? After you had searched the whole world for her in vain, you longed — so that the seas too might feel your care for her — to be able to hover above the waves on the oars of wings; and you found the gods willing. And you saw your limbs turn golden with sudden feathers. But so that your singing, born to charm the ear, and so rich a gift of voice, should not lose the use of a tongue, your girls' faces stayed, and your human voice remained.

Then Jupiter, standing between his brother and his grieving sister, split the rolling year into equal halves. Now the goddess, a divinity shared between two kingdoms, spends as many months with her mother as with her husband. At once both her mood and her face are transformed: the brow that only lately could look sorrowful even to Pluto is now bright with gladness. She is like the sun, hidden before behind watery clouds, that now, the clouds beaten back, breaks free of them.

Kindly Ceres, at ease now that her daughter was safely won back, asked Arethusa what had caused her flight — why she was a sacred spring. The waters fell silent, and their goddess raised her head from the deep fountain, dried her green hair with her hand, and told the old story of the river Alpheus and his love.

"I was one of the nymphs of Achaia," she said. "None of them ranged the woodland glades more eagerly than I, none set her hunting-nets more keenly. And though I never sought a name for beauty, though I was all strength and muscle, still I had the name of a beauty. My much-praised looks gave me no pleasure. The gift of body that other girls delight in — I, like some country lass, blushed at it, and thought it a fault to be pleasing.

"I was coming back tired — I remember it — from the Stymphalian wood. The day was hot, and my exertion had doubled the great heat. I came upon a stream running without an eddy, without a murmur, clear all the way to the bottom, so that you could count every pebble deep down, and would hardly believe the water moved at all. Hoary willows and poplars, fed by the water, spread of their own accord a shade along the sloping banks. I went to it, and first dipped the soles of my feet, then went in to the knee. Not content with that, I loosened my belt, laid my soft clothes on a bending willow, and plunged in naked.

"While I struck the water and drew it toward me, gliding in a thousand ways and flinging out my arms, I felt some strange murmur beneath the middle of the stream, and in terror I scrambled onto the edge of the nearer bank. 'Where are you rushing, Arethusa?' Alpheus called from his waters. 'Where are you rushing?' he said to me again, in a hoarse voice. Just as I was, I fled without my clothes — the other bank had them. So much the more he pressed on and burned; and because I was naked, I seemed to him the readier prey. So I ran, and so that wild thing bore down on me — as doves flee the hawk on trembling wings, as the hawk drives the trembling doves. On past Orchomenus and Psophis and Cyllene, past the folds of Maenalus and cold Erymanthus and Elis I held to my running, and he was no faster than I. But, no match for him in strength, I could not keep the pace for long, while he could endure the long chase. Still I ran — over the plains, over the tree-covered mountains, over rocks and crags, and where there was no path at all. The sun was at my back. I saw a long shadow stretch out before my feet — unless it was only my fear that saw it. But surely the sound of his feet terrified me, and the great gusts of his breath were stirring the ribbons in my hair.

"Worn out with the effort of flight, I cried, 'Help me, Diana — I am being caught! Help your armor-bearer, the one you so often let carry your bow and the arrows sealed in your quiver!' The goddess was moved. She took one of the thick clouds and threw it over me. The river searched all round me where I lay hidden in the mist, and, not knowing, hunted about the hollow cloud, and twice, without realizing, circled the very spot where the goddess had hidden me, and twice he called, 'Arethusa! Arethusa!' What were my feelings then, in my misery? What the lamb feels, hearing the wolves growling around the high sheepfold — or the hare that, crouched in a thornbush, sees the enemy jaws of the hounds and dares not stir a muscle. Still he would not leave; for he could find no tracks of my feet leading away. He kept his watch on the cloud and the spot. A cold sweat seized my besieged limbs, and dark-blue drops ran down my whole body. Wherever I moved my foot, a pool welled up; dew dripped from my hair; and faster than I now tell it to you, I was changed into flowing water. But the river knew the waters he loved, and, laying aside the man's shape he had put on, he turned back into his own streams to mingle with me. Diana split the earth, and I, plunging into its blind caverns, was carried to Ortygia — which, dear to me because it bears the surname of my own goddess, was the first to bring me up into the light of day."

So far Arethusa. Then the goddess of the harvest yoked a pair of serpents to her chariot, curbed their mouths with the bridle, and was borne through the air midway between heaven and earth. She guided her light chariot to the city of Athens, to Triptolemus, and gave him seed, bidding him scatter it, part on untilled ground, part on ground brought back to cultivation after long years. By now the young man had been carried high over Europe and the land of Asia, and he turned toward the coasts of Scythia. There Lyncus was king, and Triptolemus entered the king's house. Asked where he came from, and the reason for his journey, and his name and his country, he said: "My homeland is famous Athens; my name is Triptolemus. I came neither by ship over the waves nor on foot over the land: the open sky gave me a road. I bring the gifts of Ceres, which, scattered over the broad fields, will yield you fruitful harvests and gentle nourishment." The barbarian was jealous. So that he himself might be the author of so great a gift, he took his guest in, and when the young man was heavy with sleep he came at him with a sword. As he tried to pierce that breast, Ceres turned him into a lynx, and once more sent the young Athenian on to drive her sacred team through the air.

The greatest of us had by now finished her learned song, and the nymphs with one voice declared that the goddesses who dwell on Helicon had won. Then, when the losers began flinging insults, I said: "Since it is not enough for you to have earned punishment by challenging us, and you add abuse to the offense, and our patience has its limits — we will go on to punishment, and follow wherever our anger calls us." The daughters of Pierus laughed and scorned my threatening words. But as they tried to speak and to raise their insolent hands with a great outcry, they saw feathers sprouting from their nails, saw their arms clothing over with plumage. Each watched the other's face harden into a stiff beak, and new birds join the woods. And when they tried to beat their breasts in grief, the motion of their arms lifted them, and they hung poised in the air — magpies, the scolds of the woodland. Even now, as birds, their old gift for talk remains: a raucous, grating chatter, and a monstrous, unquenchable craving to speak.

Book VI

Minerva had been listening to all of this — the songs of the Muses, the daughters of Aonia — and she approved both their artistry and the justice of their anger. Then she said to herself: "To praise is not enough. Let me be praised as well. Let no one scorn my own divine power and go unpunished." And she turned her mind to the fate of Arachne, a girl of Lydia who, she had heard, refused to grant that anyone outdid her in the craft of working wool.

Arachne owed her fame to no famous birthplace and no famous family — only to her skill. Her father, Idmon of Colophon, dyed thirsty wool in Phocaean purple. Her mother was dead, but she too had been a woman of the common people, no higher than her husband. Even so, in the towns of Lydia the girl had won a name worth remembering for her art, though she came from a humble house and lived in humble Hypaepa. To watch her marvelous work, the nymphs would often leave the vineyards of their own Mount Tmolus, and the nymphs of the river Pactolus would leave their golden streams. And it was not only the finished cloth that gave them pleasure — it was watching her make it, there was such grace in the working. Whether she was winding the raw wool into its first soft balls, or teasing the fleece out again and again between her fingers, drawing it long and fine as mist; whether she was twirling the smooth round spindle with a light thumb, or picking out a picture with her needle — you could tell she had been taught by Minerva herself.

But Arachne denied it. Offended at the very idea of so grand a teacher, she said, "Let her compete with me. There is nothing I would refuse to suffer, if she beats me."

So Minerva took the shape of an old woman. She set false gray hair at her temples, and leaned her weak old limbs on a staff, and began: "Not everything old age brings is to be run from. Long years bring experience. Don't scorn my advice. By all means, seek the greatest fame among mortals for your weaving — but yield to the goddess, you reckless girl, and beg pardon for your words in a humble voice. She will forgive you, if you ask."

Arachne shot her a black look and let the threads drop. She could barely keep her hand still; her fury showed in her face; and she snapped back at the disguised goddess: "You come here out of your wits, worn down by too many years, and living too long is your curse. Save this lecture for a daughter-in-law, if you have one, or a daughter of your own. I know enough for myself, all by myself. Don't imagine your advice has done any good — my mind is unchanged. Why doesn't she come herself? Why does she dodge this contest?"

"She has come," said the goddess — and she threw off the old woman's shape and showed herself as Minerva. The nymphs bowed down, and the Phrygian women bowed; only the girl was not afraid. And yet she blushed. A sudden flush marked her face against her will, then faded again — the way the sky turns crimson when Aurora first stirs at daybreak, and a short while later goes white as the sun climbs. Arachne held to her purpose. Hungry for a foolish victory, she rushed headlong toward her own ruin. And the daughter of Jupiter did not refuse. She gave no more warnings; she put the contest off no longer.

Without delay they took their places, one here, one there, and stretched two webs on the looms with a fine warp. The warp was bound to the beam, a rod kept the threads apart, and the pointed shuttles drove the weft down the middle; the fingers worked it through, and once it was drawn between the warp-threads the notched teeth of the beater struck it home. Both women hurried, their robes hitched up to their breasts, moving their skilled arms, their eagerness making the labor no labor at all. Into the cloth went purple that had drunk the dye of the Tyrian vat, and shade after shade so close you could hardly tell them apart — the way a rainbow, when sunlight strikes the falling rain, arches its long curve across the whole sky: a thousand different colors shine in it, yet the eye that looks cannot catch the moment one becomes the next, so alike is each stripe to the stripe beside it, and only the ends stand far apart. Into the cloth, too, went threads of supple gold, and each loom told an old, old story.

Minerva wove the hill of Mars in Athens, the citadel of Cecrops, and the ancient quarrel over what the land should be named. Twelve gods of heaven sat on high thrones in solemn majesty, with Jupiter in the middle, each rendered so that his own face gave him away: Jupiter's image was every inch a king. She made the lord of the sea stand and strike the rough rock with his long trident, and out of the wound in the stone a rush of seawater leaped — the pledge by which he laid claim to the city. To herself she gave a shield, a spear with a sharp point, a helmet on her head, and the aegis to guard her breast; and she showed the earth, struck by her own spear, bringing forth a pale-green olive shoot heavy with berries, and the gods marveling at it. Victory closed the scene. But so that her rival might learn from examples what reward to expect for such mad daring, she added four contests in the four corners, each bright in its own colors, each picked out in small figures. One corner held Thracian Rhodope and Haemus — cold mountains now, once human beings, who took to themselves the names of the highest gods. Another corner held the pitiful fate of the Pygmy queen: Juno, having beaten her in a contest, ordered her to become a crane and make war on her own people.
