She wove Antigone as well, who once dared to match herself against the wife of mighty Jupiter, and whom royal Juno turned into a bird; neither Troy nor her father Laomedon could save her from sprouting feathers and, as a white stork, applauding herself with a clattering beak. The one corner left held Cinyras, robbed of his children: he lay clasping the temple steps that had once been his daughters' living limbs, and seemed to be weeping on the stone. Minerva bordered the outer edges with peaceful olive, and there she stopped, closing her work with her own tree.

Arachne, the girl of Lydia, drew Europa fooled by the shape of a bull — you would have thought the bull real and the sea real. Europa herself seemed to look back at the land she was leaving, to cry out to her companions, to shrink from the touch of the leaping water and pull up her frightened feet. She drew Asterie gripped by the struggling eagle; she drew Leda lying back beneath the swan's wings. She added how Jupiter, hidden in a satyr's shape, filled beautiful Antiope, the daughter of Nycteus, with twin children; how he was Amphitryon when he took Alcmene, the Tirynthian bride; how he cheated Danaë as a shower of gold, and the daughter of Asopus as fire, and Mnemosyne as a shepherd, and Proserpine, Ceres' daughter, as a speckled serpent. She set down Neptune too, changed into a fierce bull for the sake of the Aeolian girl; how, in the likeness of the river Enipeus, he fathered the giant Aloidae; how as a ram he deceived Theophane, the daughter of Bisaltes; how the golden-haired mother of the grain, Ceres, knew him as a stallion, and the snake-haired mother of the winged horse — Medusa — knew him as a bird, and Melantho knew him as a dolphin. To every one of these she gave its true face and the true look of the place. There was Apollo dressed as a countryman, and how he wore the feathers of a hawk one time and a lion's skin another, and how, as a shepherd, he deceived Isse, the daughter of Macareus. There was Bacchus tricking Erigone with false grapes, and Saturn as a horse fathering the double-natured centaur Chiron. The last strip of the web, framed in a fine border, had flowers woven through with twining ivy.

Neither Minerva nor Envy herself could fault that work. The golden-haired warrior-goddess was cut to the heart by her rival's success, and she tore the woven cloth with its record of the crimes of heaven; then, with the boxwood shuttle still in her hand, three and four times she struck the forehead of Arachne, daughter of Idmon. The wretched girl could not bear it. Full of spirit, she knotted a noose around her throat. As she hung there, Minerva took pity and lifted her up, and said: "Live, then — but hang, you insolent thing. And so that you can never rest easy about the future, let this same sentence fall on your whole line, down to your last descendants." Then, turning to go, she sprinkled her with the juices of an herb of Hecate — and at the touch of that grim drug, Arachne's hair fell away, and with it her nose and her ears. Her head shrank to almost nothing; her whole body turned tiny. Slender fingers clung to her sides in place of legs; all the rest was belly — and from it, even so, she still sends out her thread, and works her old craft, a spider at her web.

All Lydia was in an uproar. The story ran through the towns of Phrygia and filled the wide world with talk. Now, Niobe had known Arachne before her own marriage, back when she was a girl still living in Maeonia, under Mount Sipylus. And yet the punishment of her countrywoman did not teach her to yield to the powers of heaven and use humbler words. She had many things to feed her pride, but neither her husband's artistry, nor the high birth of them both, nor the might of a great kingdom pleased her — much as all of these did please her — as much as her own children. Niobe might have been called the happiest of mothers, if only she had not thought herself so.

For Manto, the daughter of Tiresias, who could read the future, had passed through the streets in the grip of a divine urging, crying her prophecy aloud: "Women of Thebes, go, all of you, and offer Latona and Latona's two children your pious incense and your prayers, and bind your hair with laurel. Latona commands it through my mouth." They obeyed. All the women of Thebes crowned their brows with the leaves the goddess had named, and offered incense and words of prayer to the sacred flames.

And then Niobe came, hemmed in by a crowd of attendants, dazzling in Phrygian robes shot through with gold, and beautiful — as beautiful as anger would let her be. Tossing the hair that fell across both shoulders, holding her lovely head high, she stopped and swept her proud eyes around, and said: "What madness is this, to set gods you have only heard of above gods you can see? Why is Latona honored at altars, while my own godhead still goes without its incense? Tantalus was my father — the only man ever allowed to sit at the tables of the gods. My mother is the sister of the Pleiades. Mighty Atlas, who carries the vault of heaven on his neck, is one of my grandfathers; Jupiter is the other — and I boast of him as my father-in-law too. The peoples of Phrygia fear me. The palace of Cadmus obeys me as its mistress. The walls my husband raised with the music of his lyre, and the people within them, are ruled by me and by him. Whichever way I turn my eyes in this house, I see measureless wealth — and add to it a face fit for a goddess. Add my seven daughters and my seven sons, and soon their husbands and their wives! Now ask yourselves what grounds my pride has. And then dare to prefer to me this Latona, daughter of some nobody Titan named Coeus — Latona, whom the whole broad earth once refused even a scrap of ground to give birth on! Your goddess was taken in by neither sky nor land nor sea; she was an outcast from the world, until Delos took pity on her wandering and said, 'You roam a stranger on land; I am a wanderer on the waves' — and gave her an unsteady resting place. There she became the mother of two. That is one seventh of what I have borne. I am fortunate — who could deny it? — and fortunate I shall stay: who could doubt that? My abundance keeps me safe. I am too great for Fortune to harm; strip away much as she may, far more she must leave me. My blessings have long outgrown my fears. Suppose it were possible for some of this crowd of my children to be taken from me: even robbed like that, I could not be brought down to Latona's two — and with her two, how far is she, I ask you, from being childless? Go — that's enough sacrifice for the occasion — and take the laurel from your hair!" They took it off, they left the rites unfinished, and they did the one thing still allowed them: they worshiped the goddess in silent murmurs.

Latona was outraged. On the highest peak of Mount Cynthus she spoke to her twin children: "Look at me — your mother, so proud to have borne you, ready to yield to no goddess but Juno alone, and now they doubt whether I am a goddess at all. For all the ages to come I am shut out from the altars that were mine, unless you help me, my children. And that is not my only grief. The daughter of Tantalus has piled insults on top of outrage; she has dared to rank her own children above the two of you, and she has called me childless — may that word fall back on her own head! — showing, the wicked woman, a tongue as vile as her father's." Latona was about to add her pleas to the report, but Apollo said, "Stop — your complaint only puts off her punishment." Diana said the same, and in a swift glide down the air the two of them came, wrapped in cloud, to the citadel of Cadmus.

Near the walls there was a level plain, wide open, forever pounded by horses, where the crowd of wheels and hard hooves had softened the clods beneath them. There, some of Amphion's seven sons were up on their spirited horses, pressing the saddlecloths red with Tyrian dye, wielding reins heavy with gold. Ismenus, who had once been the first burden his mother carried, was wheeling his mount's course into a perfect ring, reining in its foaming mouth, when he cried, "Oh, I'm hit!" — an arrow fixed square in the middle of his chest. The reins slid from his dying hand, and little by little he tipped sideways over the horse's right shoulder to the ground. The next in line, Sipylus, heard the whir of the quiver through the empty air and gave his horse its head — the way a helmsman who senses a coming storm flees at the sight of a cloud and lets out every stretch of sail on all sides, so that not the lightest breeze can slip past unused. He gave the horse its head; and even so the unerring shaft ran him down, an arrow quivering where it lodged at the top of his neck, the bare iron jutting out through his throat. Bent forward as he was, he rolled over the loosened neck and the mane, and fouled the ground with his hot blood. Luckless Phaedimus, and Tantalus, heir to his grandfather's name, had finished their usual riding and moved on to the young men's sport in the gleaming wrestling-ground, slick with oil. They had already locked breast to breast in a tight grapple when a single arrow, sped from the taut string, pierced them both just as they were joined together. They groaned in the same instant, in the same instant laid their pain-twisted bodies on the ground, in the same instant rolled their eyes one last time and breathed out their lives. Alphenor saw it and, beating his torn breast, ran to lift their cold limbs in his arms — and fell in the act of that loving duty, for Apollo tore through the depths of his chest with the deadly steel. When the arrow was pulled free, part of a lung came out on the barbs, and his blood, and his life with it, poured into the air. But it was no single wound that struck long-haired Damasicthon: he was hit where the leg begins, where the muscled hollow of the knee makes the joint soft; and while his hand was still trying to draw out the killing weapon, a second arrow drove through his throat up to the feathers. His own blood forced it out — it sprang up on its own and shot high, spurting far through the air. Last of them, Ilioneus lifted his arms in a prayer that would do him no good, and cried, "O all you gods together — spare me!" — not knowing that he need not beg them all. The god who bears the bow was moved, but by now the arrow could not be called back. Still, Ilioneus died of the lightest wound of any: the shaft did not strike deep into his heart.

The rumor of the disaster, the grief of the people, the tears of her own household made the mother certain of a ruin so sudden — that the gods above had dared it, that they held such power, left her stunned and enraged. Their father Amphion had already driven a sword through his chest and, dying, put an end to his grief and to the light together. Oh, how different was this Niobe from the Niobe who not long before had driven the people back from Latona's altars and paraded head-high through the middle of the city, the envy of her own — a Niobe now to be pitied even by an enemy! She flung herself on the cold bodies and scattered her last kisses over all her sons, sparing none. From them she raised her bruised arms to the sky and said: "Feed on my grief, cruel Latona — feed! Gorge your breast on my mourning, glut your savage heart with seven deaths. Through seven funerals I am carried to my grave. Gloat, triumph, my conquering enemy! But why conquering? In all my misery I still have more than you have in all your happiness. After so many deaths, I win." She had barely spoken when the string twanged from the bent bow — a sound that terrified everyone but Niobe alone. Disaster had made her bold.

The sisters were standing in black, hair loose, before their brothers' biers. One of them, drawing out the arrow that clung in her flesh, sank fainting toward death, her face falling across a brother's body. Another, trying to comfort her wretched mother, fell suddenly silent, doubled over by a wound no one saw, and did not close her lips until her breath had gone. Another dropped in a hopeless attempt to flee; another died across her sister; another hid herself; and another you could see shaking. Six had been given to death, wounded each in a different way, and only the last was left. Her mother, covering her with her whole body, her whole spread robe, cried out, "Leave me one — the youngest! Out of so many I beg for the smallest, only the one." And even as she begged, the girl she begged for was struck down.

Childless, Niobe sat among her dead sons and dead daughters and her husband, and hardened in her grief. No breeze stirred a hair of hers. The color in her face was bloodless. Her eyes stood fixed in her mournful cheeks; there was nothing living in the image. Her very tongue froze against the roof of her hard mouth; her veins stopped being able to move; her neck could not bend, her arms could not gesture, her feet could not walk. Inside her too, deep in her body, was stone. And still she wept; and, caught up in a whirlwind of mighty wind, she was carried off to her native land. There, fixed on a mountaintop, she melts away — and even now the marble weeps its tears.

Then indeed every man and woman feared the plainly shown anger of the goddess, and one and all worshiped with fresh zeal the great power of the goddess who bore the twins. And, as tends to happen, one recent event set them telling older ones. One of them said: "Once, in the rich farmland of Lycia, some country people insulted the goddess — and not for nothing. It is little known, because the men were nobodies, but it is a marvel all the same. I have seen the spot myself, the pool and the lake made famous by the miracle. My father, too old and worn for the road by then, had sent me to bring back some choice oxen from that country, and gave me a native guide for the journey. As we crossed the pastures together, look — there in the middle of a lake stood an ancient altar, black with the ash of old sacrifices, ringed round with trembling reeds. My guide halted and whispered, frightened, 'Be good to me,' and in the same whisper I said, 'Be good to me' as well. But I asked him whether the altar belonged to the Naiads, or to Faunus, or to some local god, and the man answered:

"'No mountain spirit owns this altar, young man. She claims it for her own whom royal Juno once banished from the world — she whom drifting Delos, when it was still a light island swimming on the sea, barely took in at her pleading. There, leaning against a palm and against Minerva's tree, the olive, Latona gave birth to twins in spite of their stepmother Juno. From there, too, the story goes, the new mother fled from Juno, carrying her two children, two divine powers, against her breast. And by now the goddess had come to the land of Lycia, the country that bred the Chimaera, worn out with her long journey and parched by the blazing summer sun; her hungry babies had drained her milk dry. By chance she caught sight of a lake of good water down in the bottom of a valley. Country folk were there, gathering bushy willow-shoots and rushes and the sedge that loves the marsh. Latona came up, knelt, and pressed the ground to scoop up the cool water and drink — and the crowd of peasants told her no. So she spoke to them: "Why do you keep me from the water? Water belongs to everyone. Nature made neither the sun nor the air nor the flowing stream the property of any one person. I have come to a gift meant for all — and yet I beg it of you humbly. I was not going to wash my body or my weary limbs here; I only want to ease my thirst. My mouth is dry even as I speak; my throat is parched; my voice can barely find a way through. A drink of water will be nectar to me, and I will admit that with it I have received my life from you: in that water you will be giving me life. And let these move you too, who stretch their little arms toward you from my breast" — and by chance the children were stretching out their arms.

"'Who would not have been softened by the goddess's gentle words? Yet they went on refusing her, and added threats on top of it, and abuse, telling her to clear off — and that was not enough. They churned up the lake itself with their hands and feet, and stirred the soft mud from the very bottom, leaping this way and that out of pure spite. Anger drove out her thirst. The daughter of Coeus would beg such worthless men no longer, could no longer stand to speak words beneath a goddess. She lifted her hands to the stars and said, "Then live forever in that pool of yours!" The goddess got her wish. It delights them to be under the water, now to plunge their whole bodies in the deep pool, now to lift their heads, now to swim along the surface; often to perch on the bank, often to leap back into the cold lake. But even now they keep working their filthy tongues in quarrels, and shameless — though they are under the water — under the water they still try to hurl their insults. Their voices are hoarse now, their bloated necks swell up, and their very cursing stretches their gaping mouths wide. Their backs run into their heads; their necks look cut away; their spines are green, and their bellies, the largest part of them, are white — and as brand-new frogs they go leaping about in the muddy pond.'"

When this man — whoever he was — had told how those people of Lycia were destroyed, another remembered the satyr Marsyas, whom Apollo, the son of Latona, had beaten in a contest on the reed pipe Minerva invented, and then had punished. "Why are you tearing me out of myself?" the satyr had screamed. "Oh, I'm sorry — oh, a flute isn't worth this much!" And as he screamed, the skin was stripped clean off the surface of his body, until he was nothing but one open wound. Blood ran everywhere; his exposed sinews lay bare; his veins throbbed and flickered with no skin to cover them. You could have counted the organs quivering inside him, and the bright fibers of his lungs behind his ribs. The country-dwellers wept for him — the fauns, the spirits of the woods, his brother satyrs, and Olympus, dear to him even then, and the nymphs, and everyone who pastured woolly flocks and horned herds on those mountains. The rich earth was soaked, and soaking, drank down the falling tears into her deepest veins; and when she had turned them to water, she sent them out into the open air. From there, running to the sea in a rush between steep banks, it bears the name of Marsyas — the clearest river in all of Phrygia.

From tales like these the people came back at once to the here and now, and mourned Amphion cut off with all his line. The mother was the object of everyone's hatred. Yet even her, they say, one man — her brother Pelops — did mourn; and when he pulled his robe down from his shoulder toward his chest, he laid bare the ivory on his left shoulder. At his birth this shoulder had matched the right one in color and had been flesh. Long ago, the story goes, his father cut him limb from limb, and afterward the gods pieced him back together; when they had found the other parts, only the piece between the throat and the top of the arm was missing. Ivory was set in to stand for the part that could not be found — and by that means Pelops was made whole again.

The neighboring lords gathered, and the nearby cities begged their kings to go and comfort Pelops in his grief — Argos, and Sparta, and Mycenae, the city of his own line; Calydon, not yet hateful to stern Diana; teeming Orchomenos, and Corinth famous for its bronze; fierce Messene, and Patrae, and lowly Cleonae; Pylos of Neleus, and Troezen, not yet named for Pittheus; and all the other towns shut in by the Isthmus between its two seas, and all those that lie outside it and look back toward that same two-sea Isthmus. Who could believe it? Athens alone — you alone, Athens — sent no one. War stood in the way of the kindness. A barbarian army, ferried in across the water, was hammering at her walls with terror. Tereus of Thrace had broken that army with his allied troops and won a shining name by the victory, and so Pandion, king of Athens, bound the man to his own house — a man rich in wealth and men, and, as it happened, tracing his blood back to mighty Mars himself — by giving him his daughter Procne in marriage.

Juno, who watches over weddings, did not come to that one. Neither did Hymen, god of the marriage-song, nor the Graces. It was the Furies who held the torches that night — torches snatched from a funeral pyre. The Furies made up the marriage-bed. And a filthy owl, that bird of ill omen, settled on the roof and brooded there above the bridal chamber. Under that bird Procne and Tereus were wed; under that bird they became parents. Thrace, of course, congratulated them; they themselves gave thanks to the gods; and they declared two days holy — the day the king had taken Pandion's daughter to wife, and the day Itys, their son, was born. So completely does our real fortune hide itself from us.

By now the Sun had drawn the wheeling seasons through five autumns, when Procne, coaxing her husband, said: "If I have earned any love from you, either send me to see my sister, or let my sister come here. Promise my father she'll be back before long — you'll be giving me a gift worth as much as a god's, just to see her face again."

He ordered the ships hauled down to the water, and with sail and oar he came into the harbor of Athens and stepped onto the shores of Piraeus. The moment he could reach his father-in-law, hand was clasped in hand, and — with a black omen hanging over it — the two of them began to talk. Tereus had just begun to explain why he had come, and to lay out his wife's request, and to promise a quick return for the sister if she were allowed to go — when in walked Philomela, richly dressed in costly finery, and richer still in her own beauty; the kind of girl we imagine when we hear of naiads and dryads moving through the deep woods, if only you dressed and adorned them the same way. The sight of the girl set Tereus alight, exactly as if you touched flame to ripe white wheat, or lit dry leaves and grass stored in a barn. Her beauty was worth catching fire for, true enough — but there was also the lust bred in his bones driving him, for the men of his country run naturally to that vice; he blazed with his people's fault and his own.

His first thought was to buy off her attendants and corrupt the loyalty of her nurse; then to work on the girl herself with lavish gifts, to spend his whole kingdom if he had to — or else to seize her outright and hold what he had seized through savage war. There was nothing this unchained hunger would not dare, and his chest could no longer contain the flames shut inside it. Now delay was unbearable to him. He turned back to Procne's request with hungry eagerness, and used it as cover to plead his own desire. Passion made him eloquent, and every time he pushed further than was decent, he claimed it was Procne who wanted it. He even added tears, as if she had asked him for those too. Great gods above — how much blind night the human heart can hold! By the very effort he put into his crime, Tereus was thought to be devoted, and won praise for his wickedness.

And how does this help? Philomela wants the very same thing. Winding her arms fondly around her father's shoulders, she begs to be allowed to go and see her sister — begs it for her own good, and against her own good. Tereus watches her, and even as he watches he takes hold of her in his mind; and when he sees her kisses, and her arms circling her father's neck, he swallows it all down as goad and torch and fuel for his madness. Every time she embraces her father, he wishes he were that father — though even then his heart would have been no less foul. The old man gives in to the pleading of both of them. Philomela is overjoyed; she thanks her father; and the poor girl counts it a triumph for them both — the very thing that will bring both of them to ruin.

Now only a little of the Sun's day's work was left, his horses striking their hooves down the sloping track of the western sky. A royal feast was laid on the tables, and wine in vessels of gold; then they gave their full bodies over to gentle sleep. But the Thracian king, though he had withdrawn for the night, still burned for her. Turning her face over in his mind, her movements, her hands, he imagined what he had not yet seen exactly as he wished it, and fed his own fire, so that his restlessness drove sleep away.

Day came. Pandion clasped the right hand of his departing son-in-law, and with tears springing up he entrusted the girl to his care. "Dear son-in-law," he said, "since a loving reason has forced me to it, and since both my daughters wished it — and you wished it too, Tereus — I give her into your keeping. And I beg you, as a suppliant, by your honor, by the bond that now joins us, by the gods above: guard her with a father's love, and send her back to me as soon as you can, the sweet comfort of my anxious old age — every day she's gone will be long to me. And you too, Philomela, come home as soon as you can, if you love me at all — it's hard enough that your sister lives so far away." So he charged them, and even as he spoke he kissed his daughter, and soft tears fell between his instructions. He asked for the right hands of them both as a pledge of good faith and joined them together, and asked them to carry his greetings, from his own remembering lips, to his absent daughter and grandson. And with a mouth choked full of sobs he could barely get out the last goodbye — and he shuddered at the warnings of his own heart.

The moment Philomela was set aboard the painted ship, and the sea was churned under the oars, and the land shoved away behind them, Tereus cried out, "I've won! What I longed for is sailing away with me!" The barbarian exulted; he could scarcely hold his joy inside; he never once turned his eyes away from her. It was the look of the eagle, Jupiter's own bird, when its hooked talons have dropped a hare into some high nest: the captive has no escape, and the robber sits and watches his prize.

Now the voyage was over. Now they had stepped out of the weary ships onto his own shore — and the king dragged Pandion's daughter into a high building deep in an ancient wood, hidden among old trees, and there he shut her up. She was pale and shaking, terrified of everything, already weeping, already asking where her sister was. And then he confessed the horror in his mind and took her by force — a virgin, and alone — while she screamed again and again, uselessly, for her father, for her sister, and above all for the great gods. She trembled like a frightened lamb that has been torn, bleeding, from the jaws of a gray wolf and still cannot believe it is safe; like a dove with its feathers soaked in its own blood, still shuddering, still afraid of the greedy talons that gripped it.

Soon her mind came back to her. Tearing her loosened hair, beating her arms like a woman in mourning, she flung out her hands and cried: "You barbarian, you and your monstrous deeds! You cruel thing! Did none of it move you — not my father's charge to you, not his loving tears, not care for my sister, not my virginity, not the laws of marriage? You have thrown everything into ruin. I have been made my sister's rival, and you the husband of us both, and Procne now my enemy, who ought to be punished for what you've done! Why not take this life too, you traitor, so that no crime is left for you to commit? I wish you had killed me before your unspeakable act — then I would have gone down to the shades with a spirit clean of any stain. But if the gods above see these things, if the power of the gods is anything at all, if everything has not died along with me — then one day, whenever it comes, you will pay me for this. I will throw shame aside and tell the world what you have done. If I get the chance, I'll go out among the people; if I'm kept locked in these woods, I'll fill the woods with it and move the very rocks to bear witness. The sky will hear this — and any god there is in it!"

Such words roused the savage tyrant's fury — and his fear was no less than his fury; spurred on by both at once, he pulled from its sheath the sword he wore, seized her by the hair, wrenched her arms behind her back, and forced her into bonds. Philomela, seeing the blade, only bared her throat to it: she had begun to hope for death. But as she cried out in her rage, calling and calling her father's name, struggling to keep speaking, he caught her tongue with a pair of pincers and cut it out with his cruel sword. The last root of it still flickered where it had been torn. The tongue itself fell to the ground, trembling, murmuring against the black earth, and just as the tail of a mangled snake goes on writhing, it twitched — and dying, groped toward its mistress's feet. Even after this — I can hardly bring myself to believe it — they say he came back again and again to satisfy his lust on her ruined body.

After deeds like these he had the nerve to return to Procne. Seeing her husband, she asked at once for her sister; but he groaned false groans and told an invented story of her death, and his tears made it convincing. Procne tore from her shoulders the robes bright with their broad bands of gold, dressed herself in black, built an empty tomb, made offerings to a ghost that did not exist, and mourned the death of a sister who was not, after all, dead to be mourned.

The Sun-god had traveled through all twelve signs; a full year had turned. What could Philomela do? A guard blocked any escape; the walls of her prison stood hard and solid stone; her silenced mouth had no way to name the crime. But grief has a vast ingenuity, and cunning comes to those in misery. She set up a loom in the barbarian fashion and, working the threads with skill, wove crimson signs into the white cloth — the whole crime, spelled out. When it was finished she handed it to a single servant and asked her, with gestures, to carry it to her mistress. The servant carried it to Procne, with no idea what she was carrying. The wife of the savage king unrolled the cloth and read her sister's pitiable fate — and (a wonder she could bear it) said nothing. Grief locked her mouth; her searching tongue could not find words fierce enough; and there was no room even for tears. She rushed straight on, past all thought of right and wrong, wholly given over to the picture of revenge taking shape in her mind.

It was the season when the women of Thrace keep the rites of Bacchus that come round every other year. Night is in on the secret; by night Mount Rhodope rings with the clash of shrill bronze. By night the queen went out from her house, decked in the god's gear, carrying the wild weapons of the festival: her head wound with vine-leaves, deerskins hanging from her left side, a light spear resting on her shoulder. Terrifying, Procne swept through the woods with a crowd of her women around her, driven by the furies of her grief — and pretended, Bacchus, that the frenzy was yours. She came at last to the lonely prison in the wood, shrieked and howled the god's cry, "Euhoe!", broke down the gates, seized her sister, dressed her too in the badges of Bacchus, hid her face under ivy-leaves, and, dragging the bewildered girl along, led her inside her own walls.

When Philomela understood she had reached that accursed house, the poor woman shuddered, and the blood drained from her whole face. Procne found a safe place and stripped off the holy trappings, uncovered the shamed face of her wretched sister, and reached to hold her — but Philomela could not lift her eyes to meet her. She felt herself the woman who had stolen her sister's husband. She kept her face turned down to the ground, and when she wanted to swear, to call the gods to witness that the disgrace had been forced on her, she made her hands speak for the voice she no longer had. Procne caught fire. She could not hold her own rage; she cut short her sister's weeping and said: "This is no time for tears — it's a time for the sword, or for anything, if you have anything, that can go further than the sword. I am ready for any crime, sister. I could set the palace ablaze with torches and throw Tereus into the middle of the flames — the schemer, the artist of all this. I could take the steel to his tongue and his eyes and the parts of him that stole your honor. I could drive his guilty soul out of him through a thousand wounds. Something enormous — that much I've decided; what it will be, I don't yet know."

While Procne was still saying this, in came Itys to his mother. The sight of him showed her what she could do. Looking at him with pitiless eyes, she said, "Ah — how like your father you are." She said no more; grimly she began to plan, seething with a silent rage. And yet, when the boy reached her, and greeted his mother, and pulled her neck down with his small arms, and gave her kisses mixed with a child's coaxing — the mother was moved. Her anger broke and stalled; against her will her eyes filled with forced tears. But the moment she felt her resolve sliding under too much tenderness, she turned away from him, back to her sister's face, and looking from one to the other she said: "Why does the one come with his sweet talk while the other sits silent, her tongue torn out? He calls me mother — why can't she call me sister? Look at the kind of man you're married to, daughter of Pandion! You're betraying your blood. To feel tenderness for the wife of Tereus — that is the crime."

No more delay. She dragged Itys off, the way a tigress on the banks of the Ganges drags a hind's nursing fawn through the shadowed forest. And when they reached a far corner of the high house, as the boy stretched out his hands — for now he saw his fate coming — and cried "Mother! Mother!" and reached for her neck, Procne struck him with the sword where the breast joins the side, and never turned her face away. That one wound was enough to kill him; but Philomela opened his throat with the steel as well. And while the limbs still quivered, still held some scrap of life, the two of them tore the body apart. Part of it leaped in bubbling bronze cauldrons; part hissed on the spits; the inmost rooms ran with gore.

To this feast the wife brought Tereus, who knew nothing, and — pretending it was a sacred rite of her country at which one man alone might be present — she sent away his companions and servants. Tereus himself, sitting high on his ancestral throne, ate, and heaped his own flesh and blood into his own belly. So deep was the darkness over his mind that he said, "Send for Itys — bring him here." Procne could not hide her savage joy. Wanting now to be the one to break the news of the ruin she had made, she said, "The one you're asking for — you have him inside you." He looked around, and asked where the boy was; and while he was still asking, still calling the name, Philomela sprang forward, just as she was, her hair spattered with the frenzied slaughter, and hurled the bloody head of Itys into his father's face. Never had she longed more to have a voice, to speak, to put her joy into the words it deserved.

With a huge cry the Thracian flung the table away from him and summoned the snake-haired sisters, the Furies, up from the valley of the Styx. One moment he wanted, if only he could, to tear open his own chest and empty out the horrible meal, the half-digested flesh; the next he wept and called himself the wretched tomb of his own son; the next he was after Pandion's daughters with his drawn sword. You would have thought the two Athenian women were floating on wings — and they were floating on wings. One made for the woods; the other went up under the eaves of houses. Even now the marks of the murder had not left her breast, and the feathers there were still stained with blood. As for Tereus, made swift by his grief and his craving for revenge, he was turned into a bird with a crest of feathers standing up on its head; a huge beak juts out where the long spear used to be — the bird is called the hoopoe, and its face looks armed for war.

This grief sent Pandion down to the shades of the underworld before his time, before the last stretch of a long old age had run out. Erechtheus took up the scepter of the land and the rule of the state — a man you could not easily say was stronger in his justice or in his strong right arm. He had fathered four sons and as many daughters, and two of the daughters were equal in beauty. One of them, Procris, made Cephalus, the son of Aeolus, a happy husband. But Boreas, the North Wind, was cursed by the memory of Tereus and the Thracians, and for a long time the god went without his beloved Orithyia — because he kept asking, choosing to use prayers rather than force. But when his sweet talk got him nowhere, bristling with the rage that is second nature to him, and only too much at home in that wind, he said: "And I deserve it! Why did I put down my own weapons — my violence, my strength, my anger, my threatening temper — and take up prayers, which don't suit me at all? Force is what fits me. By force I drive off the sullen clouds; by force I shake the seas and throw down the knotted oaks and pack the snow hard and lash the earth with hail. When I meet my brother winds up in the open sky — that's my proper field — I wrestle with such effort that the air between us thunders and fires leap out, struck from the hollow clouds. When I go down into the arched hollows of the earth and set my back fiercely against its lowest caverns, I terrify the ghosts and shake the whole world with earthquakes. That is the power I should have used to win my marriage. Erechtheus should never have been begged to be my father-in-law — he should have been made one."

Having said this, or something no less high and stormy, Boreas shook out his wings, and at the beating of them the whole earth was buffeted and the wide sea shivered from end to end. Dragging his dusty cloak over the mountaintops, he swept the ground, and, wrapped in that darkness, the lover folded his tawny wings around the terrified Orithyia. As he flew his fire burned hotter for the stirring of it, and the ravisher did not rein in his airy course until he reached the people and walls of the Ciconians in Thrace. There the Athenian girl became the wife of the cold king, and a mother too, bearing twin sons who had everything of their mother but their father's wings. Yet they say the wings were not born with their bodies. As long as the red-gold beards were still missing from their cheeks, the boys Calais and Zetes went without feathers. But soon, together, feathers began to fringe both their sides in the way of birds, and together their cheeks began to grow golden with down. And so, once the season of boyhood had passed into young manhood, the two of them sailed off with the Minyae — the Argonauts — in the first ship ever built, over an uncharted sea, chasing the fleece that blazed with its shining wool of gold.

Book VII

And now the Minyans were cutting the sea in their ship built at Pagasae — the Argo. They had visited Phineus, who dragged out a starved old age under an endless night of blindness, and the young sons of the North Wind, Boreas, had chased the maiden-faced birds, the Harpies, away from the poor old man's table. Under their famous captain Jason they had suffered much, and at last they reached the rushing, muddy waters of the river Phasis, in the land of Colchis.

They go before the king, Aeetes, and ask for the fleece that had once been Phryxus's — the Golden Fleece. Aeetes lays down his terms: a dreadful list of mighty labors. And in that same hour the king's daughter, Medea, catches fire. She fought the flame a long time, and when reason could not master the madness, she said: "It's no use, Medea — you fight it and lose. Some god is against me, I don't know which. This is a strange thing — unless what I feel is the thing they call love, or something so like it there's no telling them apart. Why else do my father's terms look too cruel to me? And they are too cruel! Why am I terrified that a man I only just laid eyes on might die? What is the reason for a fear this great? Drive the flames out of your virgin heart, you wretched girl, if you can! If only I could — I'd be saner for it. But a new force drags me against my will. Desire pulls one way, my better judgment another. I see the right course, and I approve of it — and I follow the wrong one.

"Why are you burning for a stranger, you, a princess? Why long for a marriage bed in a world not your own? This land, too, could give you something to love. Whether he lives or dies rests with the gods. Still — let him live! That much I can pray for even without loving him. For what has Jason done wrong? Whose heart, unless it were made of stone, would not be touched by Jason's youth, his birth, his courage? And even if all the rest were missing — whose heart would his face not move? It has certainly moved mine.

"But unless I help him, the bulls will breathe their fire in his face; he'll come up against a crop of enemies sprung from the very ground he sowed; or he'll be flung to the greedy dragon as its living prey. If I let that happen, then I'll admit I was born from a tiger — that I carry iron and rock inside my chest! And why not go further? Why not watch him die, and stain my eyes by looking on? Why not urge the bulls against him, and the savage earth-born men, and the sleepless dragon? The gods forbid it! But these are not things to pray for — they are things to do. So — am I to betray my father's kingdom? To save some stranger by my art, some man I hardly know, so that once I've delivered him he can spread his sails to the wind without me, become another woman's husband, and leave Medea to her punishment? If he could do that — if he could set another woman above me — then let the ingrate die! But no: that is not his face, that is not the nobility in his spirit, not the grace of his body, for me to fear betrayal, fear he'd forget what he owes me. Besides, he'll pledge his faith first, and I'll make the gods stand witness to our bargain. What are you afraid of, when you're this safe? Get ready, then, and put off every delay. Jason will owe you his life forever; he'll join you to himself by the solemn marriage torch, and through all the Greek cities crowds of mothers will hail you as the woman who saved their sons.

"So I'll be carried off by the winds, and leave behind my sister, my brother, my father, my gods, and the soil where I was born? Well — my father is cruel, and my country is barbarous, and my brother is still a baby; my sister's prayers are on my side, and the greatest of the gods lives inside me. I won't be leaving anything great behind. I'll be chasing something great: the glory of having saved the flower of Greece's young men, and knowledge of a better land, and cities whose fame flourishes even here, and the culture and the arts of those places — and the son of Aeson himself, whom I would trade for everything the whole world holds. With him for my husband I'll be called dear to the gods, and I'll touch the stars with the top of my head. Yes — they say there are mountains that crash together in the middle of the sea, and Charybdis, the enemy of ships, who first gulps down the water and then spits it back up, and greedy Scylla, ringed with savage dogs, barking in the Sicilian deep. But so long as I'm holding what I love, cradled against Jason's chest, I'll be carried over the long seas; in his arms I'll dread nothing — or if I fear anything, I'll fear it for my husband alone.

"Marriage, you call it, Medea? You dress your crime in flattering names? No — look hard at how great a wrong you're about to do, and turn away from the guilt while you still can!"

So she spoke, and there before her eyes stood the right, and loyalty, and shame; and Cupid, beaten, was already turning his back to go. She was walking toward the ancient altars of Hecate, the daughter of Perses, hidden away in a shadowy grove deep in the woods. She was strong again now; the fever had been driven off and had ebbed away — when she caught sight of Jason, and the flame she thought was out blazed up again. Her cheeks flushed; her whole face went hot. As a spark, small while it lay buried under the ash heaped over it, feeds on the wind and grows, and, stirred, rises again to its old strength, so her love — sinking now, now so faint you'd have thought it was dying — flared up at the sight of the young man before her. And by chance Jason was more beautiful that day than usual: you could forgive her for loving him. She stares, and holds her gaze fixed on his face as if she were seeing it now for the first time, and in her madness she thinks she is not looking at any mortal face, and she cannot turn away from him. But when the stranger began to speak, and took her right hand, and asked her help in a low voice, and promised her marriage, she said, with the tears spilling over: "I see what I'm doing. It won't be ignorance of the truth that undoes me — it will be love. My gift will save you. Once saved, keep your promise."

He swears by the rites of the triple goddess Hecate, and by whatever divine power lived in that grove, and by the all-seeing Sun, the father of his future father-in-law, and by his own coming ordeals, and by dangers so great. She believed him, and then and there he took from her the enchanted herbs, learned how to use them, and went back to his lodging glad.

The next dawn had chased off the glittering stars. The people gathered on the field sacred to Mars and took their places on the slopes; in the middle of the crowd sat the king himself, in purple, marked out by his ivory scepter. Look — the bronze-footed bulls breathe out Vulcan's fire from their adamant nostrils, and the grass they graze catches and burns. Like furnaces roaring when they're full of flame, or like lime dissolving in an earthen kiln, hissing into fire when sprinkled with running water — so their chests, rolling the shut-in flames around inside them, and their scorched throats, sound and roar. And yet Jason walks straight up to meet them. As he came on, they turned their terrible faces toward him, and their iron-tipped horns, and stamped the dusty ground with cloven hooves, and filled the place with smoking bellows. The Minyans froze with dread. Jason goes in and feels nothing of the breathed-out fire — such is the power of the drugs — and with a bold right hand he strokes their hanging dewlaps, forces them under the yoke, and makes them drag the heavy weight of the plow and cut into a field that had never known iron. The Colchians are amazed; the Minyans swell it with their cheering and put fresh heart in him.

Then he takes the dragon's teeth from a bronze helmet and scatters them over the plowed field. The earth, steeped beforehand in a strong poison, softens the seed, and the sown teeth grow and turn into new bodies. Just as an infant takes on human shape inside its mother's womb, and is formed there part by part, and does not come out into the open air until it's ready, so, when the shape of a man had been finished inside the belly of the pregnant earth, it rose up out of the teeming field — and, stranger still, at the very moment it was born it clashed the weapons that came into being with it. When the Greeks saw these earth-men getting ready to hurl their sharp-pointed spears at the head of the Thessalian youth, their faces fell and their courage sank with fear. Medea herself was frightened too, the one who had made him safe. When she saw the young man, one against so many enemies, she went pale, and all at once, chilled and bloodless, she sat down; and, in case the herbs she'd already given him should prove too weak, she chants a second charm to help him and calls up her secret arts. Jason flings a heavy stone into the middle of his foes, and turns the war away from himself and onto them. The earth-born brothers die of each other's wounds, and fall in a battle among their own. The Greeks cheer, and hold their champion, and cling to him in hungry embraces. You too, barbarian girl, longed to throw your arms around him. Shame stood in the way of that; and yet you would have embraced him — but regard for your good name held you back from it. What you were free to do, you did: in silence you rejoice, in your heart, and you give thanks to your charms, and to the gods who taught them to you.

Now one thing still remained: to lull to sleep with herbs the ever-wakeful dragon, that guardian of the golden tree, marked out by his crest and his three tongues and terrible with his hooked fangs. After Jason had sprinkled him with the juice of Lethe, the herb of forgetting, and had said three times the words that bring calm sleep — words that could still a storm-tossed sea and stop rushing rivers — sleep came at last upon those eyes that had never known it, and the hero, Aeson's son, took the gold. Proud of his prize, and carrying off the woman who had won it for him as a second prize, he reached the harbor of Iolcos in victory, with his wife.

The mothers and gray-haired fathers of Thessaly bring offerings for the safe return of their sons; frankincense heaped on the flames melts away, and the promised victim, its horns gilded, drops dead. But Aeson is not among those giving thanks. He is close to death now, worn out with the years of old age. So Jason says: "My wife — to whom I confess I owe my life — you have given me everything, and the sum of all you've done for me is past believing; and yet, if your spells can do this too — and what can spells not do? — take away some of my years and add the years you take to my father's."

He could not hold back his tears. She was moved by the loyalty of his plea, and the thought of her own father, Aeetes, abandoned, so unlike Jason, rose in her mind — though she confessed nothing of the sort. "What a wicked thing," she said, "has slipped out of your loving mouth! Do I seem to you the kind who could sign over a stretch of your own life to someone else? Hecate would never allow it, and what you ask is not right. But I'll try, Jason, to grant you a gift greater than the one you ask. By my art I'll try to win back my father-in-law's long years — not by spending yours — if only the triple goddess helps me and is here to nod her blessing on so vast a venture."

Three nights were still missing before the horns of the moon would meet and close into a full circle. When the moon shone at her fullest and looked down on the earth with her disk complete, Medea went out of the house, her robe unbelted and flowing, her feet bare, her hair loose over her shoulders, and she took her wandering steps, alone and unescorted, through the still hush of midnight. Deep quiet had loosened the limbs of men and birds and beasts. No sound came from the hedgerows; the leaves hung motionless and silent; the damp air was silent; only the stars glimmered. To them she stretched out her arms, turned herself around three times, three times splashed her hair with water drawn from the stream, and three times loosed a long wailing cry from her mouth. Then, bending her knee to the hard ground, she said:

"Night, most faithful keeper of my mysteries; and you golden Stars, who with the Moon follow the fires of day; and you, three-headed Hecate, who come knowing my purpose and come to help my chants and the arts of magic; and you, Earth, who furnish the sorcerers their potent herbs; you breezes and winds and mountains, you rivers and lakes, and all you gods of the groves and all you gods of the night — be here with me. By your power, when I've willed it, the rivers have run backward to their springs while their banks stared in wonder; with my chant I still the tossing sea and rouse the calm one; I drive the clouds off and I bring them on; I banish the winds and I summon them; I split serpents' throats with my words and my spells; I move living rock, and oaks torn up with their own earth, and whole forests; I order mountains to tremble and the ground to groan and the dead to come out of their tombs. You too, Moon, I draw down, though the Temesan bronze clangs to ease your pangs during the eclipse; my chant even makes pale the chariot of my grandfather, the Sun, and my poisons make Dawn go pale. It was you who blunted the bulls' flames for me, who pressed the yoke on necks that had never borne it; you who set the dragon's brood at furious war among themselves, and lulled to sleep the never-sleeping guardian and, cheating that keeper, sent the gold off to the Greek cities. Now I need juices by which old age, made new again, can come back into bloom and gather up its first young years. And you will give them — for the stars have not flashed for nothing, and not for nothing has the chariot drawn by the necks of winged dragons arrived."

The chariot was there, let down out of the sky. As soon as she had climbed in and stroked the bridled necks of the dragons and shaken the light reins in her hands, she was snatched up on high. She looked down on Thessalian Tempe far below and steered her serpents to particular districts. She scanned the herbs that Ossa bore, and lofty Pelion, and Othrys, and Pindus, and Olympus taller than Pindus, and some she pulled up by the root and some she cut down with the curve of a bronze sickle. Many herbs on the banks of the river Apidanus pleased her too, and many on the Amphrysus; nor did you escape her, Enipeus. The Peneus contributed something, and the Spercheus, and the reedy shores of the lake Boebe. By Euboean Anthedon she plucked a herb of long life, not yet made famous by the transformation of Glaucus, whose body had not yet been changed.

And now the ninth day and the ninth night had watched her sweep over all those fields in her chariot on the wings of her dragons, when she came home. The dragons had been touched by nothing but the smell of those plants — and yet they cast off the skin of their old age, sloughing away the years. When she arrived she stopped short of the threshold and the doors, roofed by the open sky alone, and shrank from any touch of a man. She built two altars out of turf, one on the right for Hecate, one on the left for Youth. When she had wreathed these with vervain and wild boughs, she dug two trenches in the earth nearby, performed the rite, plunged the knife into the throat of a black ram, and drenched the wide trenches with its blood. Then, pouring goblets of clear wine over it, and pouring bronze goblets of warm milk, she spoke her words and called on the gods of the earth and begged the king of the shades and his stolen bride, Proserpine, not to be in any hurry to rob the old man's limbs of their life.

When she had won them over with prayers and long muttered chants, she ordered Aeson's worn-out body carried out to the altars and, lulling it into a deep sleep with her spell, stretched it out on a bed of herbs, looking like a corpse. She tells Jason to go far off, and the attendants to go far off, and warns them to keep their unhallowed eyes away from her mysteries. They scatter at her command. With her hair streaming loose, Medea circles the blazing altars like a worshipper of Bacchus in a frenzy; she dips torches split into many splinters into the trench black with blood, and lights them, so dipped, at the two altars. Three times she cleanses the old man with flame, three times with water, three times with sulfur.

Meanwhile the powerful brew is boiling in the bronze cauldron set over the fire, seething and going white with a swelling froth. In it she cooks roots cut in the valleys of Thessaly, and seeds and flowers and dark bitter juices; she throws in stones fetched from the farthest East, and sands washed by the ebbing tide of Ocean; she adds hoarfrost gathered by moonlight through the night, and the ill-omened wings of a screech-owl, flesh and all, and the guts of a werewolf, that shifting creature that changes its animal shape into a man's; nor was the thin scaly slough of the Cinyphian water-snake missing, nor the liver of a long-lived stag; and on top of these she puts the egg and the head of a crow that had lived through nine generations. When with these and a thousand other nameless things the barbarian woman had made ready her design, greater than any mortal could attempt, she stirred it all up with a long-dry branch of peaceable olive and mixed the bottom with the top. And look — the old branch, turned in the hot bronze, first goes green, then in no long time puts on leaves, and all at once hangs heavy with ripe olives. And wherever the fire flung the froth out of the hollow cauldron, and the hot drops fell to the earth, the ground blooms, and flowers and soft grass spring up.

The moment she saw this, Medea drew her sword and opened the old man's throat; and, letting the old blood run out, she filled the veins with her juices. When Aeson had drunk them in, taking them by the mouth or through the wound, his beard and his hair lost their whiteness and snatched back a black color; his gauntness fled, driven off; his pallor and his squalor were gone; his hollow wrinkles were filled out with new flesh laid under them; his limbs grew full and strong. Aeson marvels at himself and remembers that this was how he had been forty years ago.

From high above, Bacchus had watched the wonders of so great a marvel, and, learning from it that youth could be given back to his own nurses, he asked this gift from the woman of Colchis.

And, so that her arts should never rest, Medea pretends a false quarrel with her husband and flees as a suppliant to the doorstep of Pelias — the man who had wronged Jason's house. Since Pelias himself is weighed down with old age, his daughters take her in; and in a short time the cunning Colchian binds them to herself with the show of a pretended friendship. As she counts, among the greatest of her good deeds, the story that Aeson's decrepitude has been stripped away — dwelling on that part of the tale — the daughters of Pelias are seized by a hope that their own father might be made young again by the same art. This they beg of her, and press her to name her price, whatever it might be. For a little while she says nothing, and seems to hesitate, and keeps their eager minds in suspense with a put-on solemnity.

Then, when she has promised them, she says: "So that you can trust this skill of mine all the more — the ram that leads your flock, the oldest of your sheep, will be turned into a lamb by my brew." At once a fleecy ram, worn out by countless years, is dragged up, his horns curling around his hollow temples. When she had slit his withered throat with the Thessalian knife, barely staining the blade with his thin blood, the sorceress plunged the animal's limbs, and her potent juices with them, into the hollow bronze. The body's limbs shrink; he loses his horns, and with the horns his years; and out of the middle of the kettle a tender bleating is heard. Without any pause, while they are still marveling at the bleating, a lamb leaps out, frisks off in play, and goes looking for a teat of milk.

The daughters of Pelias are stunned. Now that her promises have proved good, they press her all the harder. Three times the Sun had unyoked his horses as they sank into the Spanish sea, and on the fourth night the radiant stars were glittering, when the treacherous daughter of Aeetes set plain water over a leaping fire, and herbs with no power in them at all. And now a sleep like death held the king, his slack body loosened, and held his guards along with their king — a sleep her chants and the power of her enchanting tongue had brought on. At her order the king's daughters had crossed the threshold with the Colchian and gathered around the bed. "Why do you hang back now, useless girls?" she says. "Draw your swords and drain the old blood out, so I can fill his empty veins with young blood! Your father's life and his years are in your hands. If you have any love in you, if the hopes you nurse are not idle, do your duty to your father: drive out old age with your weapons, and thrust in the steel and let the rotten blood out!" At this urging, the more loving each of them is, the more she is the first to be unnatural — and so as not to be wicked, she does a wicked thing. Yet not one of them can bear to watch her own blow; they turn their eyes away, and with their faces turned aside they deal blind wounds with cruel right hands. Streaming with blood, the old man still lifts himself on his elbow and, half-torn-apart, tries to rise from the bed, and there in the middle of all those swords he stretches out his pale arms and says, "What are you doing, my daughters? What arms you against your father's life?" Their spirits failed them, and their hands. He was about to say more when the Colchian took away his throat along with his words, and sank him, so mangled, into the boiling water.

And if she had not risen into the air with her winged dragons, she would not have escaped punishment. She flees aloft, over shadowy Pelion, the home of Chiron, son of Philyra, and over Othrys, and over the places famous for the fate of old Cerambus — who, lifted into the air on wings by the help of the nymphs, when the heavy earth was buried under the flooding sea, escaped un-drowned the waters of Deucalion's flood. On her left she leaves Aeolian Pitane behind, and the stone shape of the long dragon, and the grove of Ida, where Bacchus hid a stolen bullock under the false form of a stag; and the place where the father of Corythus lies buried under a little sand; and the fields that Maera terrified with her strange new barking; and the city of Eurypylus, where the mothers of Cos wore horns at the time Hercules's company was leaving; and Rhodes, dear to Apollo; and the Telchines of Ialysus, whose eyes ruined everything by the very act of looking, until Jupiter, loathing them, thrust them down under his brother Neptune's waves. She passed over the Cartheian walls of ancient Cea, where her father Alcidamas was one day to marvel that a gentle dove could be born from his daughter's body.

Then she looks down on the lakes of Hyrie and Cycnean Tempe, made famous by a swan that came into being there all at once. For there Phyllius, at a boy's command, had handed over birds he had tamed, and a wild lion tamed as well; ordered to master a bull too, he had mastered it; but, angry that his love had been scorned so many times, he refused the bull as the boy's last prize when he begged for it. The other, indignant, said, "You'll wish you'd given it!" and leaped from a high rock. Everyone thought he had fallen — but he had turned into a swan and hung in the air on snow-white wings. His mother, Hyrie, not knowing he was saved, melted away in her weeping and became a pool that bears her own name. Next to these lies Pleuron, where Combe, daughter of Ophis, escaped her sons' wounds on trembling wings. Then she sees the fields of Calaurea, sacred to Latona, which watched their king and his wife turned into birds. On her right is Cyllene, where Menephron was one day to lie with his own mother, in the way of savage beasts. Far off she looks back at the river-god Cephisus, mourning the fate of his grandson, whom Apollo changed into a bloated seal, and at the house of Eumelus, grieving for his son now in the air.

At last, on her serpents' wings, she reached Ephyre by the spring of Pirene — the city we call Corinth. Here the people of the earliest age told how, long ago, mortal bodies sprang from mushrooms grown out of the rain. But after the new bride burned up with the Colchian's poisons, and both seas watched the king's palace go up in flames, Medea's unnatural sword was steeped in the blood of her own sons; and the mother, having taken this monstrous revenge, fled from Jason's reach. Carried off from there by her Titan-born dragons, she came into the city of Pallas, Athens — the city that had seen the just Phene, and old Periphas, flying together as birds, and Polypemon's granddaughter borne up on new-made wings.

Aegeus takes her in — a man to be condemned for this one deed — and, as if giving her shelter were not enough, he even binds her to himself by the bond of marriage. And now Theseus had arrived, his son, unknown to his father, who by his own courage had brought peace to the Isthmus between its two seas. To destroy him, Medea mixes the wolfsbane, the aconite, that she had once brought with her from the shores of Scythia. This, they say, sprang from the teeth of Echidna's dog, Cerberus. There is a dark blind cave with a shadowy mouth, and a path sloping down, along which the hero of Tiryns, Hercules, dragged Cerberus out — the dog fighting him, twisting his eyes sideways from the daylight and the shining rays, straining against the chains of adamant. Roused to a rabid fury, Cerberus filled the air with barking from all three throats at once and flecked the green fields with white foam. This foam, they think, thickened, and, feeding on the rich and fertile soil, took on the power to do harm; and because it grows, full of life, on the hard rock, the country people call it aconite. This poison, by his wife's contriving, Aegeus himself held out to his own son, as if to an enemy. Theseus had taken the offered cup in his unsuspecting right hand when his father caught sight, on the ivory hilt of the sword, of the tokens of his own family — and struck the guilty drink away from the young man's lips. Medea got away from death by raising up a mist with her spells.

But the father, though he rejoices that his son is safe, is stunned all the same that so great a crime could have come within a hair's breadth of being done. He warms the altars with fires and loads the gods with gifts, and the axes strike the muscled necks of oxen with garlands wound around their horns. No more celebrated day, they say, ever dawned on the people of Erechtheus, the Athenians, than that one. The elders keep the feast, and the common folk, and, with wine sharpening their wit, they sing: "You, greatest Theseus — Marathon marveled at you for the blood of the Cretan bull; and that the farmer plows Cromyon safe from the boar is your gift and your work. Through you the land of Epidaurus saw the club-swinging son of Vulcan go down; the banks of the Cephisus saw the pitiless Procrustes fall; Eleusis, sacred to Ceres, saw the death of Cercyon. Sinis fell too, who put his great strength to cruel use — who could bend down whole tree trunks and used to drag pines from on high to the ground, to fling men's bodies scattered far and wide. The road to Alcathoë, the town of the Lelegians, lies open and safe now that Sciron has been laid low; and the land refuses a resting place, the water refuses a resting place, to the robber's scattered bones — which, tossed about a long time, age is said at last to have hardened into rocks. To those rocks the name of Sciron still clings. If we tried to count your titles and your years, your deeds would outnumber your years. For you, bravest of men, we take the public vows; in your honor we drink these draughts of wine." The palace rings with the people's cheers and the prayers of their applause, and there is not one sorrowing place in the whole city.

And yet — so surely is no one's pleasure ever pure, and some worry always breaks in on our joys — Aegeus did not get to enjoy his son's return untroubled. Minos is preparing for war. Though he is strong in soldiers, strong in ships, he is strongest of all in a father's anger, and he means to avenge his son Androgeus's murder with rightful arms. Before the war, though, he gathers friendly forces and ranges over the sea in the swift fleet that makes his strength. To his side he joins Anaphe and the realm of Astypalaea — Anaphe by a treaty, Astypalaea's realm by war; and then lowly Myconos, and the chalky fields of Cimolus, and Syros flowering with thyme, and level Seriphos, and Paros rich in marble, and Siphnos, which the wicked Arne betrayed. She had demanded gold in her greed, and once she took it she was changed into a bird that even now loves gold — the jackdaw, black of foot and cloaked in black feathers.

But Oliaros and Didyme, Tenos and Andros, and Gyaros, and Peparethos rich in its smooth olives — none of these would lend their ships to the king of Knossos. So Minos bore away to the left, to Oenopia, the kingdom of Aeacus. The old name of the island was Oenopia, but Aeacus himself had renamed it Aegina, after his mother. The crowd came pouring out, eager to lay eyes on a man of such fame, and Telamon went to meet him, and Peleus, younger than Telamon, and Phocus, the third of the sons. Aeacus came too, slow with the weight of his years, and asked what had brought the king. Reminded of his own father's grief, the ruler of a hundred cities sighed, and gave his answer:

"I ask you to help the arms I have taken up for my son's sake — to join a war fought out of love. It is comfort for a grave that I am asking."

"You ask in vain," said the grandson of Asopus, "and you ask my city for what it cannot do. No land is bound more closely to Athens than this one. That is the treaty between us."

Minos went away heavy-hearted. "Your treaty will cost you dear," he said — but he thought it wiser to threaten war than to wage it, and to spend his strength here too soon.

The fleet from Knossos could still be seen from the walls of Oenopia when an Athenian ship came on under full sail and glided into the friendly harbor. It carried Cephalus, and with him the request of his homeland. The young sons of Aeacus knew Cephalus at once, though they had not seen him in years, and gave him their hands and led him into their father's house. Still handsome, still keeping some trace of the beauty that had been his, the hero came in holding a branch of his country's olive; being the elder, he had on either side of him two men younger than himself, Clytus and Butes, the sons of Pallas. After the first greetings had passed, Cephalus delivered the plea of the people of Athens: he asked for help, he recalled the old treaties and alliances of their fathers, and he added that Minos was reaching for the mastery of all Greece. When his eloquence had done its work for the cause he carried, Aeacus, his left hand resting on the bright pommel of his scepter, said:

"Do not ask Athens for our help — take it. Count every resource this island holds as your own, and let all the forces of my kingdom march with you. Strength I have to spare — soldiers enough to defend myself and to face the enemy. Thank the gods, this is a fortunate hour, and one that leaves me no excuse to refuse."

"May it stay so," said Cephalus, "and may your city grow with fine citizens. Truly, as I came just now, it gladdened me to see so many young men come to meet me, all handsome and all of an age. And yet I look for many I once saw here, when this city welcomed me long ago."

Aeacus groaned, and answered in a mournful voice.

A happier fortune had followed a grievous beginning — how he wished he could tell them the one without the other. But he would tell it now, plainly, in no set order, so as not to keep them with a long preamble. Those they were asking after, remembering them so well, were bones and ashes now; and how great a part of all he had were they, who had perished!

A dreadful plague had fallen on his people, sent by the anger of unjust Juno, who hated a land named for her rival. As long as it seemed a natural sickness, and the true, poisonous cause of so great a ruin stayed hidden, they fought it with the art of medicine; but the destruction overran all help, and help lay beaten. In the beginning the sky pressed down on the earth with a thick fog and shut a sluggish heat inside its clouds. And while the Moon four times filled her horns to a full circle and four times, thinning, unspun it again, the hot south winds blew with their deadly breath. It is known for a fact that the sickness reached even the springs and the pools; that thousands upon thousands of serpents crawled across the untilled fields and fouled the rivers with their venom. The strength of the sudden disease first showed itself in the dying of dogs and birds and sheep and cattle and the wild beasts. The luckless ploughman stares to see his strong oxen drop at their work and lie down in the middle of the furrow. The wool-bearing flocks bleat weakly; their wool falls off of itself, and their bodies waste away. The horse, once fiery, once famous on the racecourse, forgets his old honors and his victories and stands groaning at the manger, doomed to die a slow, inglorious death. The boar forgets to be angry, the hind no longer trusts her speed, the bears no longer charge the strong herds. A dull weakness holds everything. In the woods, in the fields, along the roads lie foul carcasses, and the air is spoiled with the stench of them.

And here is a strange thing: the dogs would not touch them, nor the greedy birds, nor the gray wolves. The bodies rotted where they fell, and by their reek did their harm, spreading the contagion far and wide.

Then, with heavier ruin, the plague reached the wretched farmers, and took its throne inside the walls of the great city. First the vitals are scorched; a flush of the skin and a labored breath are the sign of the hidden flame. The tongue swells, rough with fever; the parched mouth hangs open, and the sick gulp at the heavy air. They can bear no bed, no covering; they lay their burning chests on the bare ground — and the body is not cooled by the earth, but the earth grows hot from the body. No one can hold the sickness in check. The cruel plague breaks out upon the very healers, and their arts turn against their owners. The nearer a man is, and the more faithfully he tends the sick, the sooner he takes his own share of death. And once the hope of getting well is gone, and they see the only end of the illness in death, they give themselves up to their whims, and there is no care for what might help — for nothing helps. Everywhere, all shame thrown aside, they cling to fountains and rivers and deep wells, and the thirst is not put out until the life is. Many, too heavy to rise, die in the very water — and still another drinks it. So sick are these wretched people of their hated beds that they spring up, or, if their strength will not let them stand, roll their bodies onto the ground; and every man flees his own house, for each one's home seems deadly to him, and since the cause is hidden, the known place gets the blame. You might see them half dead, wandering the roads as long as they could stand; others weeping and sprawled on the earth, rolling their weary eyes in one last movement. They reach their limbs up toward the stars of the overhanging sky, and here and there, wherever death has caught them, breathe out their lives.

What were his feelings then? What should they have been — to hate his own life and long to be gone with his people? Wherever he turned his eyes, there lay the crowd strewn on the ground, like rotten apples falling from a shaken branch, like acorns from a storm-tossed oak. Across from him stood a high temple, raised on its long flight of steps: the temple of Jupiter. Who did not burn incense at those altars, and burn it for nothing? How often, while a husband spoke words of prayer for his wife, or a father for his son, they died at the unmoved altars, the incense still unburned in their hands! How often the bulls, led to the temple while the priest was pronouncing the vows and pouring pure wine between their horns, fell before the stroke could come! When Aeacus himself was sacrificing to Jupiter for himself, his country, and his three sons, the victim let out a dreadful lowing and dropped all at once, with no blow struck, staining the knife thrust into it with its thin blood. The diseased entrails had lost every true mark and every warning of the gods; the sickness struck inward, to the very organs. He had seen the corpses flung down before the sacred doors, before the very altars — as if to make death more hateful to the gods. Some ended their lives with a noose, driving off the fear of death by death, calling on the fate that was already coming. The dead were carried out with no funeral rites, in the old way — for the city gates could not hold the crowd of processions. Either they lay unburied on the ground, or they were laid on the high pyres without the customary honors. By now there was no more reverence: men fought over the pyres and burned their own dead on fires meant for others. There was no one left to weep; unmourned, the souls of sons and fathers, of young men and old, drifted away — and there was no room for the graves, and no trees enough for the fires.

Stunned by so great a whirlwind of misery, he cried out: "O Jupiter! If the stories do not lie, if you truly came into the arms of Aegina, the daughter of Asopus, and if you are not ashamed, great Father, to be my father — then either give me back my people, or lay me too in the tomb." Jupiter gave his sign, with lightning and a favorable roll of thunder. "I take it," he said, "and I pray these are happy tokens of your will. The omen you give me, I hold as a pledge."

By chance, close by, there stood an oak sacred to Jupiter, grown from Dodona's own seed, with wide-spreading but thin-set boughs. There they saw a long column of ants, the harvesters of grain, carrying their heavy loads in their little mouths and keeping to their track along the wrinkled bark. Marveling at their number, he prayed: "Most gracious Father, give me citizens as many as these, and fill my empty walls again." The tall oak trembled and made a sound in its branches, though no wind stirred them. His limbs shuddered with fear, his hair stood on end — yet he kissed the ground and the oak, and would not confess that he hoped; and hope he did, cherishing his wishes in his heart.

Night came on, and sleep took hold of his body, worn out with worry. Before his eyes the same oak seemed to stand, bearing the same boughs and the same creatures on its boughs, quivering with the same motion, and scattering its grain-gathering troop over the fields below; and these, all at once, seemed to grow, to look bigger and bigger, to lift themselves from the earth and stand upright on their own, and to shed their thinness and their many feet and their black color, and to take on human form in their limbs.

Then sleep left him. Awake, he scorned the vision and complained that there was no help from the gods above. But inside the palace there was a great murmur, and he seemed to hear the voices of men — a sound he had grown unused to. While he was supposing that this too was part of his dream, Telamon rushed in, threw open the door, and said: "Father, come out — you will see more than you dared hope or believe!" Out he went, and there, one after another, he saw and knew the very men he had seemed to see in his sleep. They came forward and hailed him as their king. He paid his vows to Jupiter, and shared out the city and the fields left empty of their old tillers among this new-made people; and he called them Myrmidons, and did not rob the name of the mark of its origin. Their bodies Cephalus had seen. The character they had before, they keep still: a thrifty race, patient in toil, holding tight to what they win and laying by what they hold. These, matched to Cephalus in years and courage, would follow him to the war — as soon as the east wind that had carried him so kindly here (for it was the east wind that had brought him) should shift into the south.
