With such talk and much else they filled out the long day. The last of the light was given to the feast, the night to sleep. The golden Sun had raised his beams, but the east wind still blew and held the sails from returning. The sons of Pallas came to Cephalus, who was the elder; and Cephalus, with the sons of Pallas beside him, came to the king — but a deep sleep still held the monarch, and it was Phocus, the son of Aeacus, who received them at the threshold, for Telamon and his brother were levying men for the war. Phocus led the Athenians into an inner room, a handsome chamber, and sat down there with them. As they talked he noticed that Cephalus, grandson of Aeolus, held in his hand a javelin of some unknown wood, its point of gold.

After a few words of idle conversation, he said: "I love the woods and the hunting of wild game; and yet I have been wondering some while now from what tree the shaft of that javelin was cut. If it were ash, it would be tawny; if cornel, it would have knots. Where it comes from I cannot tell — but my eyes have never looked on a lovelier throwing-spear." One of the Athenian brothers answered: "You will admire its use even more than its beauty. Whatever it is aimed at, it strikes; chance does not steer it once it is thrown, and it flies back bloodied, with no one to fetch it." At that the son of Nereus's line asked everything — why it was given, and from where, and who had made him a gift of such worth. What he could tell, Cephalus told; but of the price he had paid for it he was ashamed to speak, and stayed silent. Then, touched with grief for the wife he had lost, he began, with the tears starting from his eyes:

"Son of a goddess, this weapon — who could believe it? — makes me weep, and will do for a long time, if the Fates grant me a long life. It was the ruin of me and my dear wife. Would that I had never had this gift!"

Procris — if by chance the name of Orithyia has more likely reached your ears — was the sister of Orithyia, the girl who was carried off; and if you cared to set the face and the character of the two side by side, Procris was the worthier to be seized. Her father Erechtheus joined her to me; love joined her to me. I was called happy — and happy I was. The gods did not think it should last so; or perhaps, even now, I would be happy still.

The second month was passing since the marriage rites, when the saffron-robed Dawn, driving off the dark, saw me one morning from the top of ever-blooming Hymettus as I was spreading nets for the antlered deer, and carried me off against my will. Let me tell the truth, by the goddess's leave: though she is lovely with her rosy face, though she holds the borders of light and the borders of night, though she is fed on the waters of nectar — still I loved Procris. Procris was in my heart, Procris always on my lips. I kept pleading the sacred bond of marriage, our new-made union, the fresh vows of the bed I had left. The goddess grew angry: "Stop your complaining, you ingrate — keep your Procris. But if my mind sees true, you will wish you never had." And in her anger she sent me back.

On the way home, turning the goddess's words over in my mind, I began to be afraid that my wife had not kept the marriage faith. Her beauty and her youth told me to fear the worst; her character forbade me to believe it. Yet I had been away — and besides, the very one I was coming home from was proof that such a thing could happen — and we who are in love fear everything. So I resolved to search out the very thing that would wound me: to make trial of her chaste faith with gifts. Dawn favored this dread of mine, and altered my shape — so I seemed to feel. Unrecognizable now, I came to Athens, the city of Pallas, and went into my own house. The house itself was blameless: it gave every sign of chastity, and grieved for the master who had been carried off. Only by a thousand tricks did I at last win my way to the daughter of Erechtheus. When I saw her, I was stunned, and nearly gave up the trial of her faith I had planned. It was all I could do not to tell her the truth, all I could do not to give her the kisses I owed her. She was sorrowful — and yet no one could be lovelier than she was in her sorrow — pining with longing for the husband torn from her. Only think, Phocus, how great her beauty must have been, that even grief so became her!

Why should I tell how often her chaste ways threw back my attempts? How often she said, "I am kept for one man only; wherever he is, for him I keep my joys"? For any man in his right mind, what greater proof of faith could there be? I was not content. I fought on to wound myself, promising vast sums for a single night, piling reward on reward until at last I forced her to waver. Then I cried out, in my wretched triumph: "Look — a sham lover, and it is your own husband! I am your witness, you faithless woman: you are caught." She said nothing. Only, overwhelmed with silent shame, she fled that house of treachery and its wicked husband; and in her hatred of me she came to loathe the whole race of men, and wandered the mountains, given over to the pursuits of Diana.

Then, left alone, a fiercer fire reached my bones. I begged forgiveness, confessed my fault, owned that I too might have fallen the same way if gifts so great had been offered me. When I had confessed it — her wounded honor first avenged — she came back to me, and we passed sweet years in harmony. And she gave me besides, as if in giving herself she had given too small a thing, a dog for a present: her own Diana, when she handed it to her, had said, "It will outrun them all." She gave me a javelin too — this one you see in my hand.

You ask what became of the first gift, the dog? Then hear a marvel — the strangeness of it will astonish you. The son of Laius had solved the riddle that no wit before him had understood, and the dark prophetess had flung herself down, forgetting her own riddles. But kindly Themis, of course, does not leave such things unavenged: at once another scourge was loosed on Aonian Thebes, and many country folk fed a savage beast with the ruin of their cattle and of themselves. We of the neighborhood came and ringed the broad fields with our nets. With a light leap the creature cleared them and bounded over the highest of the toils we had set. The dogs were slipped from their leashes, but it fled them as they gave chase and made sport of the pack, no slower than a bird. With one loud voice they all called for my Laelaps — Tempest, that was the name of my wife's gift. He had long been fighting to be free of the leash, straining it against his neck. He was scarcely well let go before we could no longer tell where he was: the warm dust held the prints of his feet, but he himself was snatched from our eyes. No spear flies faster, nor pellets whirled from a twisted sling, nor the light arrow from a Cretan bow. A hilltop rose in the middle, looking down on the plains below. I climbed it and watched that strange chase, in which the beast one moment seemed caught, the next to slip out from under the very bite. Crafty, it would not run straight and clear away, but tricked the mouth that pursued it and wheeled in circles, so its enemy could get no full charge at it. The dog hung close, matched it stride for stride, and — for all the world like a dog with its prey in its teeth — closed on nothing, and snapped again and again at the empty air. I was turning to my javelin for help; while my right hand was balancing it and I was working my fingers into the throwing-loop, I glanced away — and when I looked back to the same spot, there in the middle of the plain, incredibly, I saw two marble shapes: you would swear the one was fleeing, the other barking in pursuit. Some god, surely — if any god was watching them — willed that neither should be beaten in that contest of speed."

He said this much, and fell silent. "But what is the fault in the javelin itself?" said Phocus. And Cephalus told him the javelin's offense:

"Let my joys be the first part of my sorrow, Phocus; those I will tell first. It gladdens me even to remember that blessed time, son of Aeacus, when through the first years of our marriage I was happy in my wife, and she was happy in her husband. A shared devotion, a wedded love, held us both. She would not have set the bed of Jupiter above my love, nor was there any woman who could have taken me — not if Venus herself had come. Equal flames burned in the breasts of us both.

When the Sun was just striking the mountaintops with his early rays, I used to go, with a young man's eagerness, hunting in the woods. I took no servants with me, no horses, no keen-nosed hounds, no knotted nets: I was safe with my javelin. And when my hand had had its fill of the slaughter of wild game, I would seek out the cool and the shade, and the breeze that came breathing from the cold valleys. That gentle breeze I looked for in the heat of the day; for that breeze I waited — it was my rest after my labor. 'Come, breeze,' I would sing, for I remember it well, 'come, most welcome, and enter my breast; be willing, as you always are, to ease the heat that burns me.' Perhaps I added — my fate was drawing me on — still more endearments; perhaps I used to say, 'You are my great delight; you refresh me and cherish me; you make me love the woods and the lonely places, and my mouth is forever reaching for your breath.'

Someone — I do not know who — gave a credulous ear to these double-meaning words, and took the name of the breeze I called on so often to be the name of a nymph: he believed I was in love with some nymph named Aura. And, rash informer of a crime that never was, he went straight to Procris and whispered to her what he had heard. Love is a credulous thing. Struck down by sudden grief, she fainted, so I am told; and recovering after a long while, she called herself wretched, born to a cruel fate, and grieved over my faithfulness. Stirred up by a groundless charge, she dreads a thing that is nothing, fears a name without a body, and in her misery mourns as though over a real rival. Yet often she doubts, and in her wretchedness hopes she is deceived, and refuses to believe the tale, and will not condemn her husband's guilt unless she sees it with her own eyes.

The next morning's light had driven off the night. I went out and made for the woods; and, victorious among the fields, I said, 'Come, breeze, and ease my pain.' And suddenly, in the middle of my words, I thought I heard some sort of groan; but still I said, 'Come, most welcome one.' When the falling leaves made a soft rustle again, I took it for a wild beast and threw my flying weapon. It was Procris. Clutching the wound in the middle of her breast, she cried, 'Ah, wretched me!' When I knew the voice of my faithful wife, headlong and out of my mind I ran toward it. I found her dying, her scattered garments fouled with blood, drawing her own gift — wretched that I am! — out of the wound. In my arms, gentler than my own, I lifted the body dearer to me than my own; I tore the clothing from my breast and bound her cruel wound, and tried to stanch the blood, and begged her not to leave me guilty of her death. She, her strength gone, already dying, forced herself to speak these few words:

'By the bond of our marriage, by the gods above and my own gods, by whatever good I have done you, and by the love that even now, as I die, is the cause of my death — I beg you, do not let Aura take my place in our marriage bed.' She spoke; and only then did I understand — and tell her — the mistake in the name. But what use was the telling? She sinks; her little strength flies out with her blood. And as long as she can look at anything, she looks at me, and breathes out her unhappy life against my face — yet she seems to die at peace, with a more contented look."

In tears, the hero told them this, and they wept to hear it. And now Aeacus came in, with his two sons and his newly levied soldiers; and Cephalus received them, well armed and brave.

Book VIII

Now the Morning Star unveiled the bright day and drove off the hours of night; the east wind dropped, and the moist clouds rose. A gentle south wind gave the sons of Aeacus and Cephalus their passage home, and, carried on it prosperously, they reached the harbor they were bound for sooner than they had hoped.

Meanwhile Minos was laying waste the coast of the Lelegians, trying out the strength of his arms against the city of Alcathoüs, ruled by Nisus — Nisus, among whose honored gray hairs a single lock, bright with purple, grew from the middle of his crown, and on it hung the safety of his great kingdom.

The horns of the rising moon were coming round for the sixth time, and still the fortune of the war hung in the balance, and long did Victory hover between the two on wavering wings. There was a royal tower set against the city's singing walls, on which — so the story goes — the son of Latona once laid his golden lyre; the sound of it sank into the stone. Often, in time of peace, the daughter of Nisus used to climb up there and strike the ringing stones with a little pebble. In wartime, too, she often watched from that tower the struggles of hard warfare; and by now, as the siege dragged on, she knew the names of the chieftains, their weapons and horses, their dress and their Cretan quivers. Above all she had learned the face of the leader, the son of Europa — learned it better than mere knowing needed.

In her eyes, whether Minos had shut his head in a plumed and crested helmet, he was beautiful in the helmet; or whether he had taken up his gold-gleaming shield, taking up the shield became him. When he drew back his arm and hurled the whipping javelin, the girl praised the skill that went with the strength; when he laid an arrow on the wide bow and bent it, she would swear that Apollo stood just so when he took up his arrows. But when he bared his face by lifting off the bronze, and, robed in purple, pressed the back of a white horse gay with embroidered trappings, and reined in its foaming mouth — then the daughter of Nisus was hardly her own mistress, hardly in her right mind. Happy, she called the javelin he touched; happy the reins he pressed with his hand. Her whole impulse was to carry her maiden steps through the enemy ranks, if only she could; her impulse was to fling her body from the top of the towers into the Cretan camp, or to open the bronze-bound gates to the enemy — or anything else that Minos might want. And as she sat gazing at the white tents of the Cretan king, she said:

"I do not know whether to be glad or sorry that this grievous war is fought. I grieve that Minos is the enemy of the woman who loves him; but if there had been no war, he would never have been known to me! And yet — he could lay down the war and take me as a hostage: me for his companion, me as the pledge of peace. If she who bore you, most beautiful of kings, was such as you are yourself, then with reason a god burned for her. Oh, three times happy would I be, if I could rise on wings through the air and light on the camp of the Cretan king, and, confessing myself and my love, ask him at what price he would be bought — only let him not ask for my father's citadel! For let the marriage I long for perish, rather than that I win it by treason! — and yet, the mercy of a gentle conqueror has often made it a blessing to many to be beaten. He wages a just war, surely, for his murdered son, and he is strong both in his cause and in the arms that guard his cause. We shall be beaten, I think; and if that is to be this city's end, why should his own warfare open these walls to him, and not my love? Better for him to win without slaughter and delay and the spending of his own blood. At least I need not fear, Minos, that someone may wound your breast without knowing you — for who is so hard-hearted that he would dare, unless in ignorance, to aim a cruel spear at you? The plan pleases me, and I am resolved: to hand over my country as my dowry and put an end to the war. But to be willing is not enough! A guard watches the gates, and my father keeps the keys. Him alone, in my misery, do I dread; he alone stands in the way of my desire. Would the gods might make me fatherless! But surely everyone is a god to themselves: Fortune has no patience with idle prayers. Another woman, fired by a passion this great, would long since have taken joy in destroying whatever blocked her love. And why should any woman be braver than I am? I would dare to go through fire and swords — and yet here there is no need of fire or swords; I need only my father's lock of hair. That purple lock is more precious to me than gold: it will make me happy, will make me mistress of my own wish."

As she said such things, night came on — the greatest nurse of cares — and with the darkness her boldness grew. The first sleep had come, when sleep takes hold of hearts worn out with the day's worries. Silently she entered her father's chamber and — the horror of it! — the daughter robbed the father of his fatal lock. Having won her unspeakable prize, she carried the plunder out through the very midst of the enemy — so great was her confidence in the service she had done — and came to the king; whom, as he stood aghast, she thus addressed:

"Love urged the crime. I am Scylla, the royal daughter of Nisus, and I hand over to you the fortunes of my country and my own. I ask no reward but you. Take this purple lock as the pledge of my love — and believe that I am handing you not a lock of hair, but my father's life." And in her guilty hand she held out the gift. Minos shrank back from what she offered, and, shaken at the thought of so monstrous a deed, he answered: "May the gods banish you, you disgrace of our age, from their whole world; may earth and sea alike be denied you! I at least will not allow so great a monster to touch Crete, the cradle of Jupiter, which is my realm."

He spoke; and when, most just of lawgivers, he had set his terms on the conquered, he ordered the fleet's cables loosed and the bronze-beaked ships driven on by the oars. When Scylla saw the launched keels cutting the water, and saw that the prince gave her no reward for her wickedness, her pleading spent, she turned to violent rage, and, stretching out her hands, hair streaming, cried in her frenzy:

"Where do you flee, leaving behind the maker of your victory — O you whom I set above my country, above my father? Where do you flee, heartless man, whose triumph is both my crime and my service? Did the gift I gave not move you, nor my love, nor that all my hope was heaped on you alone? For where shall I go, deserted? Home? It lies conquered. But suppose it stood — my treason has closed it to me. To my father's face? The father I handed to you? My people rightly hate me; my neighbors fear my example. I have shut myself out of the whole world, that Crete alone might be open to me — and you forbid me even that, and leave me, ingrate? Then Europa was not your mother, but the pitiless Syrtis, some Armenian tigress, or Charybdis churned by the south wind! Nor are you Jupiter's son, nor was your mother tricked by the shape of a bull. That tale of your birth is a lie: it was a real bull that fathered you, wild, and charmed by love of no heifer. Take your vengeance on me, Nisus my father! Rejoice at my ruin, walls I have just betrayed! For I confess I have deserved it, I am worthy to die. Yet let one of those I so wickedly wronged destroy me. Why should you, who have conquered by my crime, punish that crime? What was treason to my country and my father, let it be a kindness to you! She is a fit wife for you, the one who tricked the savage bull with the wooden cow and carried in her womb that mismatched thing. Do my words reach your ears at all — or do the winds carry off my empty words and your ships together, ungrateful man? Ah, now, now it is no wonder Pasiphaë preferred a bull to you: you were the more savage beast! Wretch that I am — he bids them hurry! The water roars, cleft by the oars, and my own land, and I with it, dwindle from his sight. It is no use — no use your forgetting all I have earned. In spite of you I will follow, and, gripping the curved stern, be dragged across the long seas."

She had scarcely spoken when she leaped into the waves and, with love lending her strength, caught up to the ships and clung, a hateful passenger, to the ship of Knossos. When her father saw her — for now he hung in the air, just made a sea eagle with tawny wings — he swooped to tear her with his hooked beak. In terror she let go the stern; but the light air seemed to hold her as she fell, so she would not touch the sea. It was feathers holding her: changed into a bird, she is called Ciris, the cutter, and she takes that name from the lock she cut away.

Minos, once he stepped from his ships and touched the land of Crete, paid his vow to Jupiter — a hundred bulls' bodies — and his palace was decked with the spoils he hung there. But the reproach of his house had grown, and his wife's shameful adultery was laid bare by the monstrous, two-shaped creature she had borne. Minos resolves to remove the disgrace from his home, to shut it away in a house of many divisions, a dwelling full of blind mazes. Daedalus, most famous of men for his craftsman's skill, laid out the work and confused its marks, leading the eye astray through the tangle of its winding ways. Just as the clear Maeander plays in the fields of Phrygia and flows now forward, now back, in its shifting course, and, meeting itself, looks on its own oncoming waters, and turns now toward its source, now toward the open sea, keeping its uncertain waters at work — so Daedalus filled his countless paths with turnings, and could scarcely find his own way back to the threshold, so great was the trickery of the place.

Here he shut up the double form of the bull and the young man; and when the monster, twice gorged before on Athenian blood, was subdued at the third tribute drawn by lot every ninth year — and when the difficult door, retraced by none who had entered before, was found by the help of the maiden and her gathered-up thread — at once the son of Aegeus, carrying off the daughter of Minos, set sail for Dia, and there on that shore cruelly abandoned his companion. To her, deserted and bitterly weeping, Bacchus brought his embrace and his help; and so that she might shine in a lasting star, he took the crown from her brow and flung it into the sky. Through the yielding air it flew, and as it flew its jewels turned to gleaming fires and settled in their place, the shape of the crown still kept, midway between the Kneeling One and the Serpent-holder.

Meanwhile Daedalus, sick of Crete and of his long exile, and touched by love of his native land, was shut in by the sea. "Though Minos block the land and the water," he said, "still the sky at least is open; we will go that way. Let Minos own all else — he does not own the air." So he spoke, and turned his mind to arts unknown, and made nature new. For he lays feathers in order, beginning with the smallest, a shorter following a longer, so you would think they had grown on a slope — just as a rustic pan-pipe rises little by little on its uneven reeds. Then he binds the middle ones with thread, the lowest with wax, and, so arranged, bends them into a gentle curve, to imitate real birds. His son Icarus stood beside him, and, not knowing that he was handling his own peril, kept catching with a bright face at the feathers the wandering breeze had ruffled, or working the yellow wax soft with his thumb, and by his play hindered his father's marvelous work. When the last touch had been put to the undertaking, the craftsman balanced his own body on the twin wings and hung poised in the beaten air. He fitted his son with wings too, and told him: "Icarus, I warn you, keep to the middle way. If you fly too low, the sea will weight your feathers; too high, and the fire of the sun will scorch them. Fly between the two. And do not watch Boötes, or the Great Bear, or the drawn sword of Orion: take your course with me for guide." At once he gives him the rules of flight and fits the untried wings to his shoulders. As he worked and warned, the old man's cheeks grew wet, and his father's hands trembled. He gave his son kisses never to be given again; and, lifted on his wings, he flew ahead, afraid for his companion, like a bird that has led her tender young out of the high nest into the air. He urges the boy to follow, teaches him the deadly art, moves his own wings, and looks back at his son's.

Some angler, plying his quivering rod, or a shepherd leaning on his crook, or a ploughman on his plow-handle, saw them and stood amazed, and believed that those who could cut the sky must be gods. And now Samos, sacred to Juno, was on the left (Delos and Paros were behind them), and on the right lay Lebynthos and Calymne rich in honey — when the boy began to delight in his bold flight, and left his guide, and, drawn by longing for the sky, drove his course higher. The nearness of the blazing Sun softened the fragrant wax that bound his feathers; the wax melted. He beat his bare arms, and, having lost his oarage, caught no air, and his mouth, crying out his father's name, was taken into the blue water, which drew its name from him.

But the unhappy father — a father no longer — cried, "Icarus, where are you? In what quarter shall I look for you? Icarus!" he cried — and then he saw the feathers on the waves, and cursed his own arts, and buried the body in a tomb; and the land was called by the name of the boy laid there. As he was laying his poor son's body in the ground, a chattering partridge watched him from a leafy holm-oak, and clapped its wings, and sang out its delight. It was then the only bird of its kind, never seen in earlier years, and, newly made a bird, a lasting reproach to you, Daedalus. For his sister, not knowing what fate held, had once handed her son to him to be taught — a boy of twelve, with a mind quick to learn. The boy it was who studied the backbone he noticed in the middle of a fish and, taking it for a model, cut a row of teeth in sharp iron and so invented the saw; he was the first, too, to bind two iron arms at a single joint, so that, held equally apart, one might stand fixed while the other traced a circle. Daedalus envied him, and threw him headlong from the sacred citadel of Minerva, lying that he had slipped. But Pallas, who favors the clever, caught him and made him a bird and clothed him with feathers in mid-air; yet the force of that quick genius passed into his wings and feet, while his name stayed as before, Perdix. This bird does not raise its body high, or build its nest in branches and treetops; it flutters near the ground and lays its eggs in hedgerows, and, remembering that old fall, it dreads the heights.

And now the land of Etna held Daedalus in his weariness, and Cocalus, who took up arms at his plea, was praised for his kindness. By now, too, Athens had ceased to pay its mournful tribute, thanks to the deeds of Theseus. The temples are hung with garlands; they call on warlike Minerva, with Jupiter and the other gods, honoring them with the blood of vowed victims, with gifts laid down, with censers of incense. Wandering Fame had spread the name of Theseus through the Argive cities, and the peoples of rich Achaia begged his help in their great dangers. Calydon too, though it had Meleager, sought him with anxious, suppliant prayer. The cause of the plea was a boar, the servant and avenger of Diana's wrath.

For they say that Oeneus, for a year full of blessings, had offered the first fruits of the corn to Ceres, his wine to Bacchus, and the oil of Pallas to golden-haired Minerva. Begun with the country gods, that coveted honor went round to all the powers above — all but the altars of Latona's daughter, which alone, they say, were passed over and left without incense. Anger touches even the gods. "But I will not take this quietly," she said; "and I who am dishonored will not be called unavenged as well." And, slighted, she sent an avenging boar through the fields of Oeneus, larger than the bulls that grassy Epirus rears, and bigger than the fields of Sicily have. His eyes glare with blood and fire; his rough neck stands stiff; his bristles stand up like spikes, like a palisade of tall stakes. Boiling foam runs down his broad shoulders with a harsh hiss; his tusks match the tusks of India; lightning comes from his mouth, and the leaves burn at his breath. One while he tramples the growing crops in the blade; another he reaps the ripe hopes of the farmer doomed to weep, and cuts down the grain in the ear. In vain the threshing-floor, in vain the barns wait for the promised harvest. Down go the heavy clusters with their long vine-shoots, down the berries with the boughs of the ever-green olive. He rages against the flocks too: neither shepherd nor dog can guard them, nor the fierce bulls defend the herds. The people scatter and think themselves safe only within a city's walls — until Meleager and a chosen band of young men band together, hungry for glory:

the twin sons of Tyndareus, one famous with the boxing-glove, the other on horseback; and Jason, builder of the first ship; and Theseus with Pirithoüs, a happy pairing; and the two sons of Thestius; and the sons of Aphareus, Lynceus and swift Idas; and Caeneus, now no longer a woman; and fierce Leucippus, and Acastus famed for the javelin, and Hippothoüs, and Dryas, and Phoenix the son of Amyntor, and the matched sons of Actor, and Phyleus, sent from Elis. Nor was Telamon missing, nor the father of great Achilles; and with the son of Pheres and the Boeotian Iolaüs came tireless Eurytion and Echion, unbeaten in the race, and Lelex of Naryx, and Panopeus, and Hyleus, and bold Hippasus, and Nestor, still in his early years; and those Hippocoön sent from ancient Amyclae, and the father-in-law of Penelope with Ancaeus of Parrhasia, and the far-seeing son of Ampycus, and the son of Oecleus, still safe from his wife — and Atalanta of Tegea, the glory of the Lycaean woods. A polished clasp fastened the top of her robe; her plain hair was gathered in a single knot; the ivory guard of her arrows rattled, slung from her left shoulder, and her left hand held the bow. Such was her dress; and her face was one you might truly call a girl's in a boy, a boy's in a girl. The Calydonian hero saw her and, at the same moment, longed for her — though the god was unwilling — and drank in the hidden flame, and said, "Happy the man she thinks worthy of her!" But the moment and his sense of shame let him say no more: the greater work of the great contest presses on.

A wood thick with timber, which no age had felled, rose from the level ground and looked out over the fields below. When the men came there, some spread the nets, some slipped the dogs from their leashes, some followed the pressed prints of the tracks, each eager to find his own danger. There was a hollow gully where rainwater used to drain; at the bottom of the pool grew pliant willows, smooth sedge, marsh rushes, osiers, and thin reeds on their long stalks. Roused from here, the boar hurls himself violently into the midst of his enemies, like lightning struck from bursting clouds. The grove is flattened by his charge; the shoved trees give a crash. The young men shout, and hold their weapons out in strong right hands, the broad iron quivering. On he rushes and scatters the dogs, cutting down with sidelong strokes whichever bays at him in his fury. The spear first hurled by Echion's arm did no good, and only nicked a maple trunk. The next, had it not used too much of the thrower's strength, seemed sure to lodge in the back it was aimed at; but it flew too far — Jason of Pagasae had thrown it. "Phoebus," said the son of Ampycus, "if I have worshiped you and worship you still, grant me to hit my mark with an unerring shot!" The god granted his prayer as far as he could: the boar was struck by him, but without a wound — Diana took the iron head from the flying shaft, and the wood came without its point. The beast's fury was roused, and it blazed no gentler than the lightning: light flashed from its eyes, flame breathed from its chest. As a boulder flies, launched by a taut rope, when it is aimed at walls or towers packed with soldiers, so with sure onset the wound-dealing boar is carried among the young men, and lays low Eupalamus and Pelagon, who guarded the right wing; their comrades snatched them up as they fell. But Enaesimus, son of Hippocoön, did not escape the deadly stroke: as he trembled and made to turn his back, the cut sinews of his knee gave way. Perhaps the Pylian Nestor too would have perished before the time of Troy, but, taking a spring from his planted spear, he leaped into the branches of a tree standing close by and, safe in his perch, looked down on the enemy he had fled. The boar, whetting his tusks on an oak trunk, stood fierce and ready for their ruin, and, trusting his newly sharpened weapons, gored the thigh of great Othriades with his hooked tusk. But the twin brothers — not yet stars of the sky — both conspicuous, both riding horses whiter than snow, both shaking the poised points of their lances with a trembling motion, would have dealt wounds, had the bristly beast not gone into the shadowy woods, a place no javelin or horse could reach. Telamon pursues him and, careless in the heat of the chase, falls headlong, tripped by a tree root. While Peleus lifts him, the girl of Tegea set a swift arrow to the string and let it fly from the bent bow: fixed under the beast's ear, it grazed the top of his body and reddened the bristles with a little blood. Yet she was no more glad at the success of her shot than Meleager was. He is thought to have seen the blood first and first to have shown it to his comrades, and to have said, "You will win the honor your courage has earned." The men flushed with rivalry and cheered one another on, raising their spirits with shouts, and threw their weapons without order; the very crowd of them fouled the throws and baffled the strokes they aimed.

Look — the Arcadian, wielding his double axe and rushing against his fate, cried, "Learn, young men, how much a man's weapons beat a woman's, and give way to my work! Though Latona's daughter herself should shield him with her own arms, still, in spite of Diana, my right hand will destroy him." So he boasted with his swelling mouth; and, lifting the double-edged axe in both hands, he stood on tiptoe, poised for the downward blow. The beast forestalled the bold man, and where the way to death is quickest drove both tusks into the top of his groin. Ancaeus fell, and his coiled bowels slid out in a rush of blood, and the earth was soaked with gore. Pirithoüs, the son of Ixion, was advancing straight at the enemy, shaking his hunting-spear in his strong hand, when the son of Aegeus called to him from a distance: "Stop, O dearer to me than myself, better part of my soul! There is no shame in valor at long range: rash courage was the death of Ancaeus." He spoke, and hurled his heavy cornel spear with its bronze point; well aimed, and about to grant his wish, it was stopped by a leafy bough of a beech. The son of Aeson threw his javelin too, which chance turned aside from the boar to the death of an innocent hound: flung through its belly, it pinned the belly to the ground. But the hand of Oeneus's son had different luck. Of two he threw, the first spear stood fixed in the earth, the second in the middle of the boar's back. There is no delay: while the beast rages, wheeling its body round and spewing hissing foam with fresh blood, the giver of the wound comes up, provokes his enemy to fury, and buries his bright hunting-spear deep in the shoulder facing him. His comrades bear witness to their joy with a glad shout, and reach to clasp the victor's right hand in theirs, and gaze in wonder at the huge beast sprawled over so much ground; and they do not yet think it safe to touch him — but each, all the same, stains his own weapon in the blood.

Then Meleager himself, setting his foot on the deadly head, pressed it down and said, "Take, girl of Nonacris, the spoil that is mine by right, and let my glory be shared with you." At once he gives her the hide, rough with its stiff bristles, and the head marked by its great tusks. The gift, and the giver of the gift, delighted her; but the others envied it, and a murmur ran through the whole company. Of these the sons of Thestius, stretching out their arms, cried in a great voice: "Come, put them down, woman, and don't take our honors from us; and don't let confidence in your beauty fool you — your lovesick giver may be far off when you need him." And they took the gift from her, and from him the right to give it. The war-god's son could not bear it: gnashing in swelling rage, he said, "Learn, you robbers of another's honor, how far deeds stand from threats" — and he ran his cruel sword through the breast of Plexippus, who feared no such thing. Toxeus, still in doubt what to do, at once wanting to avenge his brother and afraid to share his brother's fate, Meleager did not let waver long: he warmed the weapon, still hot from the first killing, again in a brother's blood.

Althaea was carrying gifts to the temples of the gods, her son the victor, when she saw her dead brothers being carried home. She beat her breast and filled the city with mournful cries, and changed her gold-embroidered robes for black. But the moment the author of the deaths was named, all her grief fell away, and turned from tears to a lust for punishment.

There was a billet of wood which, when the daughter of Thestius lay in childbed, the three Sisters, the Fates, had laid on the fire, and, spinning the threads of destiny under their pressing thumbs, had said: "To you, newborn child, and to this wood, we give the same span of life." When they had spoken the charm and gone, the mother snatched the burning branch from the fire and doused it with water. Long had it lain hidden in an inmost room, and, kept safe, had kept safe, O youth, your years. Now the mother brings it out and orders torches and kindling laid, and, when they are laid, sets the hostile fire to them. Then, four times trying to put the branch on the flames, four times she stops. Mother and sister fight in her, and the two names pull one heart two ways. Often her face went pale with dread of the coming crime; often the boiling anger in her eyes gave it a flush; and now her look was like someone's who threatens something cruel, now one you would think full of pity. And when the fierce heat of her rage had dried her tears, tears were found even so. As a ship that the wind seizes, and the tide running against the wind, feels the double force and unsteadily obeys them both, so the daughter of Thestius wavers between two warring loves, laying down her anger and rousing it again. Still, the sister begins to win out over the mother; and, to soothe with blood the kindred shades, she is dutiful in an unnatural way. For when the deadly fire had gained strength, she said, "Let that pyre burn my own flesh." And, holding the fatal wood in her ruthless hand, she stood, poor woman, before the sepulchral altars and said: "You three goddesses of punishment, you Furies, turn your faces to these dread rites. I am avenging, and I am doing wrong. Death must be atoned by death, crime piled on crime, funeral on funeral; through heaped-up grief let this unnatural house perish. Shall Oeneus rejoice in his victorious son, and Thestius be left childless? Better that you both should mourn. Only you, ghosts of my brothers, newly made spirits, feel this service of mine, and take the offering bought at so great a cost — the guilty pledge of my own womb. Ah, wretched me! Where am I carried? Forgive me, brothers, the feelings of a mother! My hands fail at what I began. I own he has deserved to die; but that I should be the cause of his death — that I cannot bear. So shall he go unpunished? Alive, victorious, swollen with his very success, shall he hold the throne of Calydon, while you lie a little cold heap of ashes and lifeless shades? No — I will not endure it. Let the guilty man perish, and drag down with him his father's hope, his kingdom, and his country's ruin. But where is a mother's heart? Where the loving bonds of a parent, and the pangs I bore for twice five months? Oh, that you had burned as an infant in that first fire, and that I had let it be! You lived by my gift; now by your own deserts you shall die. Take the reward of your deeds, and give me back the life twice given you — first at your birth, then when I saved the brand — or lay me too in my brothers' tomb. I long to do it, and I cannot. What shall I do? One moment my brothers' wounds are before my eyes, the image of that dreadful slaughter; the next, love and a mother's name break me. Wretch that I am! To my sorrow you will win, brothers — but win; only let me follow the comfort I give you, and you yourselves." She spoke, and, turning away, with trembling right hand threw the funeral brand into the middle of the flames. The wood gave, or seemed to give, a groan, and, caught by the unwilling fire, it burned.

Far off and unaware, Meleager is scorched by that flame, and feels his vitals seared by hidden fires, and by his courage masters the great pain. Yet he grieves that he dies a bloodless, inglorious death, and calls the wounds of Ancaeus a happy lot; and, with a groan, he calls on his aged father, his brothers, his loving sisters, and, with his last words, the companion of his bed, and perhaps his mother too. The fire and the pain swell, then sink again; both are put out together, and little by little his breath slips away into the light air, as little by little the white ash veils the embers.

Lofty Calydon lies low. Young and old mourn; commons and nobles groan; the mothers of Calydon by the Evenus tear their hair and wail. His father sprawls on the ground, fouling his white hair and aged face with dust, and rails at his long life. As for the mother — her own hand, conscious of the dreadful deed, exacted the penalty, the sword driven through her body. Not if a god had given me a hundred mouths ringing with tongues, and a great genius, and the whole of Helicon besides, could I tell out the sorrowful laments of his unhappy sisters. Careless of dignity, they beat their bruised breasts; and while the body lasts, they warm it and warm it again, they kiss it, they kiss the bier where it lies. After he is ashes, they press the gathered ashes to their breasts and lie sprawled about the tomb, and, clasping his name carved in stone, pour their tears upon the name. Them at last the daughter of Latona, sated with the ruin of the house of Parthaon, lifts up on wings springing from their bodies — all but Gorge and the daughter-in-law of noble Alcmena — and stretches long wings along their arms, makes their mouths horny, and sends them, so changed, through the air.

Meanwhile Theseus, his part in the shared labor done, was making his way to the citadel of Erechtheus, the city of Minerva.

But Acheloüs, swollen with rain, blocked his way and held him up as he went. "Come under my roof, famous son of Cecrops," he said, "and do not trust yourself to the racing flood. It is used to carrying off solid beams and rolling down boulders sideways with a great roar. I have seen high sheepfolds close to the bank swept away, flocks and all; and there it did the strong cattle no good to be strong, nor the horses to be swift. Many a young man's body, too, this torrent has drowned in its whirling eddies when the mountain snows melted. Rest is safer, until the river runs in its usual channel, until its own bed holds only the slender stream." The son of Aegeus agreed. "I will use your house and your advice, Acheloüs," he answered — and he used them both.

He entered a hall built of porous pumice and rough tufa; the floor was damp with soft moss, and shells arched the roof in alternating rows of purple. And now, Hyperion having measured out two parts of the daylight, Theseus and his fellow laborers lay down on the couches — on one side the son of Ixion, on the other Lelex, the hero of Troezen, his temples now flecked with a little gray, and others whom the river of the Acarnanians, overjoyed with so great a guest, had graced with the same honor. At once barefoot nymphs set the tables with the feast, and, the dishes cleared, poured wine in jeweled bowls. Then the mighty hero, looking out over the seas below, said, "What is that place?" — and pointed with his finger — "and tell me the name that island bears, though it does not seem to be one alone."

The River answered: "It is not one thing that you see; five lands lie there — the distance blurs the boundaries between them. And so you may wonder less at what slighted Diana can do, these were once Naiads, who, having killed twice five bullocks and called the country gods to a sacrifice, danced their festal dances and forgot me. I swelled — as great as I ever run at my fullest — and, doubled in rage and in flood, I tore woods from woods and fields from fields, and, together with the ground under them, rolled the nymphs into the sea, who then, at last, remembered me. My waves and the sea's split the once-unbroken land and broke it into as many pieces as you see islands there, the Echinades, out in the waves. But look — as you can see for yourself — one island has drawn far off from the rest, an island dear to me: the sailors call it Perimele. From her, my beloved, I took the name of virgin, which her father Hippodamas took so ill that he pushed the body of his daughter, near her time, from a cliff into the sea. I caught her, and, bearing her up as she swam, I prayed: 'O bearer of the Trident, you who won by lot the realm next below heaven, the waters where we sacred rivers end and to which we run — come, Neptune, and hear me kindly. Her whom I bear I have wronged. Grant her a place, I beg you, plunged in the water by her father's cruelty, or let her become a place herself. Even so I will embrace her.' The king of the ocean nodded, and shook all the waters with his assent. The nymph was afraid, but still she swam. As she swam I touched her breast, throbbing with a trembling motion — and while I felt it, I felt her whole body harden and her breast grow covered with spreading earth. Even as I spoke, fresh soil closed round her floating limbs, and a heavy island grew upon her changed body."

At this the River fell silent. The marvel had moved them all. But the son of Ixion laughed at their belief; a scorner of the gods and fierce of spirit, he said, "You tell a fable, Acheloüs, and think the gods more powerful than they are, if they can give and take away the shapes of things." All were shocked and did not approve such words; and before the rest Lelex, ripe in mind and years, spoke thus:

"The power of heaven is measureless and has no end, and whatever the gods above have willed is done. And that you may doubt it the less — on the Phrygian hills there is an oak beside a linden tree, ringed by a low wall. I have seen the spot myself, for Pittheus once sent me into that land of Pelops, ruled long ago by his father. Not far off is a marsh, once habitable ground, now busy with divers and the coots that love a fen. To this place Jupiter came in mortal shape, and with him his son, the grandson of Atlas, Mercury, his wings laid aside and the caduceus put by. To a thousand houses they went, seeking lodging and rest; a thousand houses shut their bolts against them. Yet one took them in — a small one, thatched with straw and marsh reeds. But a devout old woman, Baucis, and Philemon of the same age had been joined in that cottage in their youth, and in it had grown old together; and by confessing their poverty they made it light, and bore it with a mind not out of tune. It is no use asking there for masters or for servants: the whole household is two, the same who obey and command.

"So when the dwellers of heaven reached this little home and, ducking their heads, went in at the humble door, the old man set out a bench and bade them rest their limbs, and busy Baucis threw a rough cloth over it. Then she raked the warm ashes on the hearth and stirred up yesterday's fire, feeding it with leaves and dry bark and coaxing it to flame with her old woman's breath; she brought down split faggots and dry branches from under the roof, broke them small, and set them under a little copper pot. The greens her husband had gathered in the well-watered garden she strips of their leaves. With a two-pronged fork he lifts down a smoky side of bacon hanging from a black beam, and cuts a small piece from the long-kept chine, and softens it, cut, in boiling water. Meanwhile they beguile the passing hours with talk, and do not let the time be felt. There was a beechwood tub hanging by its handle from a peg; they fill it with warm water to bathe and rest the guests' feet. In the middle of the room, on a couch whose feet and frame were of willow, lay a cushion of soft sedge; over this they spread cloths they were used to lay out only on festival days — and even these were cheap and old, no shame to a willow couch.

"The gods took their places. The old woman, apron on, shaking, sets the table before them — but the third leg of the table was too short, and a potsherd slipped beneath made it level; and once the tilt was gone, green mint wiped the leveled top clean. On it are set the two-colored olives of chaste Minerva, and autumn cornel-berries pickled in dregs, and endive, and radishes, and a lump of curdled cheese, and eggs turned gently in the cooling ash — all in earthenware. After this an embossed mixing-bowl of the same clay is set out, and cups of beechwood, coated where they are hollow with yellow wax. A short pause, and the hearth sent up the warm dishes; and wine of no great age is brought again, then, set a little aside, gives way to the second course: here are nuts, here dried figs mixed with wrinkled dates, plums, and fragrant apples in wide baskets, and grapes gathered from the purple vines. In the middle is a white honeycomb — and, over it all, kind faces, and a good will neither idle nor grudging.

"Meanwhile, each time they see the emptied bowl fill itself of its own accord and the wine rise up again by itself, astonished at the strange sight, Baucis and frightened Philemon lift their hands in prayer and beg pardon for the meal and its poor preparation. There was a single goose, the guardian of that tiny farm, which its owners were making ready to kill for their divine guests; but the goose, quick on the wing, wore out the pair, slow with age, and dodged them a long while, and at last seemed to flee to the gods themselves for safety. The gods forbade its killing. 'We are gods,' they said, 'and this godless neighborhood will pay the penalty it has earned; but you shall be spared this ruin. Only leave your house and follow our steps, and come with us up the high mountain.' Both obey, and, leaning on their staffs, struggle to set their feet up the long slope. They were as far from the top as a shot arrow can carry when they turned their eyes and saw all the rest sunk in a marsh, and only their own house left standing. And while they wondered at it, while they wept for the fate of their people, that old cottage, too small even for its two owners, turned into a temple: columns rose where the forked posts had been, the thatch went golden, the ground was covered in marble, the doors seemed carved, and the roof of gold. Then the son of Saturn spoke, with gentle mouth: 'Tell us, good old man, and you, wife worthy of so good a husband, what you desire.' Philemon spoke a few words with Baucis, then told the gods their shared wish: 'We ask to be your priests and to keep your shrine; and, since we have spent our years in harmony, let the same hour take us both, so that I may never see my wife's grave, nor be buried by her.' Their prayer was granted. As long as life was given, they kept the temple; and when, worn out with years and age, they chanced to be standing before the sacred steps, telling the story of the place, Baucis saw Philemon put out leaves, and old Philemon saw Baucis put out leaves. And now, with the treetops growing over their two faces, while still they could they traded words, and together said, 'Farewell, my dear one' — and at the same moment the bark closed over and hid their faces. The people of Tyana still show there the neighboring trunks made from their two bodies. Honest old men, with no reason to deceive me, told me this; and I myself saw garlands hanging on the branches, and, laying fresh ones there, I said, 'Let those the gods love be gods, and let those who worshiped be worshiped.'"

He had finished; and the story, and the teller of it, had moved them all, and Theseus most of all. As he longed to hear more of the wonderful works of the gods, the Calydonian river, leaning on his elbow, spoke to him thus:

"There are some, bravest of men, whose shape has been changed once and stayed so changed. There are others whose privilege it is to pass into many forms — like you, Proteus, dweller in the sea that clasps the earth. For men have seen you now a young man, now a lion; now you were a raging boar, now a serpent they feared to touch; now horns made you a bull; often you might be seen as a stone, often as a tree; sometimes, mimicking the look of running water, you were a river, sometimes fire, water's very opposite.

"And no less than his is the power of the wife of Autolycus, the daughter of Erysichthon. Her father was one who scorned the majesty of the gods and burned no incense on their altars. He is said, too, to have profaned a grove of Ceres with the axe and violated her ancient woods with iron. In them stood an oak with an aged trunk, a whole wood in itself; fillets and votive tablets and garlands ringed its middle, tokens of prayers that had been answered. Often beneath it the wood-nymphs led their festal dances; often, hands linked in a ring, they measured the girth of its trunk, and the oak's circumference filled fifteen ells; and the rest of the wood stood as far below it as the grass stood below the whole wood. Yet not for that did the son of Triopas hold his axe from it, but bade his servants cut the sacred tree; and when he saw them hesitate at the order, the wicked man snatched an axe from one of them and said, 'Were it not only dear to a goddess, but the goddess herself, still it should touch the ground now with its leafy top.' He spoke, and, poising his weapon for a slanting stroke, the oak of Ceres shuddered and gave a groan, and at once its green leaves and its acorns began to pale, and its long branches to take on a pallor. And when his impious hand had made a wound in the trunk, blood ran from the cut bark just as it pours from the severed neck of a great bull-victim falling before the altar. All were struck dumb, and one of them dared to check the outrage and stay the cruel axe. The Thessalian eyed him: 'Take the reward of your piety,' he said, and turned the axe from the tree onto the man, and struck off his head, and hacked at the oak again — when from within the wood came these words: 'I, a nymph most dear to Ceres, am within this wood; and, dying, I foretell to you that the punishment of your deeds is at hand — the comfort of my death.' He pressed on with his crime; and at last, weakened by countless blows and hauled down with ropes, the tree fell, and with its weight flattened a great stretch of the wood.

"Aghast at the loss — the grove's and their own — all the wood-nymphs, her sisters, went in black robes to Ceres, mourning, and begged for Erysichthon's punishment. She granted it, and, most lovely of goddesses, with a nod of her head shook the fields heavy with their harvest, and devised a kind of punishment to be pitied — if his deeds had left him deserving of any pity: to tear him with deadly Famine. And since the goddess herself may not approach Famine (for the Fates do not let Ceres and Famine come together), she called a rustic mountain-nymph, an Oread, and said: 'There is a place at the far icy edge of Scythia, a dreary soil, a land without grain and without trees; there dwell numbing Cold, and Pallor, and Trembling, and starving Famine. Bid her bury herself in the breast of this sacrilegious wretch; let no plenty of food overcome her, and let her beat my powers in the contest. And so the long road may not frighten you, take my chariot, take the dragons you may drive on high with the reins.' And she gave them.

"Carried through the air on the granted chariot, the nymph came to Scythia, and on the peak of a steep mountain — they call it Caucasus — unyoked the dragons' necks, and saw Famine, whom she sought, in a stony field, tearing at the scattered herbs with nails and teeth. Her hair was matted, her eyes hollow, her face pale, her lips white with scurf, her throat rough with rust; her skin hard, and through it the guts could be seen; her dry bones jutted under her hooked loins; instead of a belly there was only the place for a belly. You would think her chest hung loose, held up only by the frame of her spine. Leanness had swollen her joints, her kneecaps bulged, her ankles stood out in great knobs. As soon as the Oread saw her from a distance — for she did not dare go near — she gave the goddess's orders; and, staying but a short while, though she kept her distance and had only just arrived, she seemed even so to feel hunger; and, turning the reins, she drove the dragons back on high to Thessaly.

"Famine did as Ceres bade, though she is forever the goddess's enemy; carried by the winds through the air to the appointed house, at once she entered the bedroom of the sacrilegious man and, sunk as he was in deep sleep — for it was night — folded him in her two wings, and breathed herself into him, blowing on his throat and chest and mouth, and scattering starvation through his empty veins. Her task done, she left the fruitful world and went back to her famished home, her wonted fields. Gentle Sleep still soothed Erysichthon with its calming wings. In his dream he craves food, works his jaws to no purpose, grinds tooth on tooth, tires his throat with imaginary meat, and instead of a feast devours in vain the yielding air. But when sleep was driven off, his hunger to eat rages, and holds sway over his greedy jaws and his insatiable gut. Without delay he calls for whatever the sea, the earth, the air brings forth, and complains of hunger with the tables set before him, and looks for a feast in the midst of a feast; and what would be enough for whole cities, enough for a whole people, is not enough for one man, and the more he sends down into his belly, the more he wants. As the ocean takes in the rivers of the whole earth and is never sated with water and drinks up the streams of far-off lands, and as the greedy fire never refuses its fuel and burns beams beyond number, and, the more it is given, craves the more, and grows hungrier by the very heap — so the jaws of impious Erysichthon take in every dish and ask for more at once. In him all food is the ground of more food, and there is always an empty place left by eating.

"And now, through his appetite and the pit of his belly, he had worn down his father's wealth; but even then his dreadful hunger stayed undiminished, and the flame of his ravenous throat burned on. At last, his whole fortune swallowed into his guts, only his daughter was left — a daughter who deserved a better father. Her too he sells, in his need. Nobly born, she cannot bear a master, and, stretching her hands over the nearby sea, she cried, 'Free me from my master, you who hold the prize of my ravished virginity!' — that prize Neptune had taken. He did not scorn her prayer; and though the moment before her master had seen her in pursuit, the god changed her shape and gave her the look of a man and the dress of one who catches fish. Her master, seeing her, said, 'O handler of the rod, who hide the hanging bronze hook with a little bait — so may the sea be calm for you, so may the fish in the water be ever credulous and feel no hook till caught — tell me where she is who a moment ago was standing on this shore (for I saw her standing there), her hair loose and in humble dress; for her footprints go no farther.' She saw that the god's gift had turned to good, and, glad to be asked after herself by herself, answered his question: 'Whoever you are, forgive me: I have turned my eyes nowhere from this water, but, busy at my work, have kept to my task. And that you may doubt me the less — so may the god of the sea aid this craft of mine — no man has stood on this shore for some time now, myself excepted, nor any woman.' Her master believed her, and, turning on his heel, paced off across the sand, deceived; and her own shape was given back to her.

"But when her father found she had a body that could be transformed, he sold the granddaughter of Triopas again and again to different masters; and she kept escaping — now a mare, now a bird, now a cow, now a stag — and so found a dishonest living for her hungry father. Yet after the violence of his sickness had used up all provision and heaped fresh fuel on his dreadful malady, he himself began to tear his own limbs with biting, and the wretched man fed his body by making it less. But why do I dwell on the cases of others? I too, young men, have the power to change my body — though only so many times. For one moment I appear as what I now am; the next I am coiled into a snake; then, as the leader of a herd, I take strength into my horns — my horns, so long as I could. Now one side of my brow is stripped of its weapon, as you see for yourself." And a sigh followed his words.

Book IX

The Neptunian hero asked the cause of the god's groan and of his broken brow; and the Calydonian river, his unkempt hair bound with reeds, began:

"You ask a mournful thing. For what beaten man wants to recount his own battles? Yet I will tell it in order; it was not so shameful to be beaten as it is glorious to have fought, and so great a conqueror is a great comfort to me.

"If the name of Deianira has at last reached your ears — once she was a most beautiful girl, the envied hope of many suitors. When, with the rest, I entered the house of the man I wished for a father-in-law, I said, 'Take me, son of Parthaon, for your son-in-law.' Hercules said the same; the others gave way to us two. He offered Jupiter for a father-in-law, and the fame of his labors, and the tasks his stepmother had set and he had overcome. Against this I said — for I thought it a disgrace for a god to yield to a mortal, and he was not yet a god — 'You see before you a king of the waters, flowing in winding channels through your realm; I am no stranger sent you as son-in-law from foreign shores, but one of your own people, a part of your state. Only let it not count against me that royal Juno does not hate me, and that no punishment of ordered labors comes near me. For you, son of Alcmena, who boast your birth — either Jupiter is your pretended father, or your real one through a crime. By your mother's adultery you claim a father: choose, then, whether you had rather Jupiter were falsely your father, or that you were born of disgrace.'

"As I said this, for a while he glared at me with a grim eye, and did not very well master his kindled anger, and gave me back just these words: 'My right hand is better than my tongue. Only let me win at fighting, and you may win at talking' — and he came at me fiercely. I was ashamed, after such big words, to give way. I threw off the green robe from my body, put up my arms against his, held my hands on guard, curved before my breast, and set my limbs for the fight. He scooped up dust in his hollow hands and threw it over me, and in his turn went yellow with the tawny sand cast on himself. He grabbed now at my neck, now at my legs as they shifted, or you would think he did, and pressed me from every side. My bulk defended me, and he attacked in vain — no differently than a breakwater the waves batter with a great roar: it stands, safe in its own weight. We drew apart a little, then closed again for battle, and stood our ground, resolved not to yield; foot was locked to foot, and I, leaning in full with my chest, pressed finger on finger and forehead on forehead. Just so I have seen strong bulls charge, when the loveliest cow in all the pasture is the prize of the fight, and the herds look on and tremble, not knowing which the mastery of so great a realm awaits. Three times, without success, Hercules tried to fling my chest off him as it bore hard against him; the fourth time he shook off my hold, loosed my clinched arms, and, striking me a blow — I am resolved to tell the truth — spun me right round and clung, a mighty weight, to my back. If you will believe me — and I seek no glory by a false tale — I seemed to have a mountain laid on me. Barely could I work my arms, streaming with sweat, back in; barely loosen his hard grip from my body. He pressed me, panting, gave me no chance to gather strength, and got hold of my neck. Then at last the earth was pressed by my knee, and I bit the sand.

"Weaker in strength, I turned to my arts, and slipped from the hero, changed into a long snake. When I had wound my body into coils and darted my forked tongue with a dreadful hiss, the Tirynthian laughed, and mocking my arts said, 'It was my cradle's work to master snakes; and though you outdo all other serpents, Acheloüs, how small a part of the Lernaean Hydra will you be, a single snake? She grew by her own wounds, and not one head of her hundred could be cut off without new danger — her neck came back stronger, with two heirs for one. Her, branching with serpents sprung from each cut and growing by her losses, I mastered and laid open. What do you think will become of you, who move borrowed weapons in a false snake's shape, hidden under a form you only borrowed?' He spoke, and clamped his fingers on the top of my neck. I was choked, as if my throat were gripped in pincers, and I fought to tear my jaws free of his thumbs.

"Even so beaten, I still had a third shape left, the fierce bull; changed to a bull, I renewed the fight. He threw his arms over my brawny neck on the left, and, dragging as I ran, followed me; and, seizing my horns, he drove them into the hard ground and flattened me on the deep sand. That was not enough: his savage right hand, gripping the stiff horn, broke it and tore it from my mutilated brow. This the Naiads filled with fruit and fragrant flowers and made holy; and now bounteous Plenty is rich by my horn." He spoke; and a nymph, girt like Diana, one of his attendants, her hair loose on either side, came in, and brought all the wealth of autumn in that overflowing horn, and choice fruit for the second course.

Day comes on; and with the first sun striking the peaks the young men leave, for they will not wait until the streams win back their peace and gentle flow and the whole flood sinks. Acheloüs hid his rustic face and his broken-horned head in the middle of the waves. He grieves at the loss of that one stolen honor; in all else he is whole, and the hurt to his head is hidden by a willow branch, or reeds laid over it.

But love of that same girl had been the ruin of you, fierce Nessus, shot through the back by a swift arrow. For the son of Jupiter, returning to his native city with his new bride, had come to the rushing waters of the Evenus. The river was swollen higher than usual with winter rains, full of whirlpools and impassable. Nessus came up — bold for himself but anxious for the wife — strong of limb and knowing the fords: "By my service she shall be set on that far bank, Hercules; use your strength to swim." So the Aonian hero handed the frightened Calydonian woman, pale with fear and dreading both the river and Nessus himself, to Nessus. Then, just as he was, weighed down by his quiver and the lion's skin — for he had thrown his club and curved bow to the far side — he said, "Since I have begun, the river must be crossed," and did not hesitate, or look for where the stream was gentlest, or stoop to be ferried by the river's favor. And now, having gained the bank and lifting the bow he had thrown across, he heard his wife's voice, and, as Nessus made to steal what had been entrusted to him, he shouted: "Where does vain confidence in your feet carry you, you ravisher? To you I speak, two-shaped Nessus! Listen, and do not seize what is mine. If no respect for me moves you, still your father's turning wheel might have kept you from forbidden embraces. You will not escape, for all your trust in your horse's speed: with a wound, not with my feet, I will overtake you." His last words he made good by the deed, and shot the fleeing back through with an arrow. The barbed steel stood out from the breast; and when it was wrenched free, the blood spurted from both wounds, mixed with the Lernaean poison's venom. Nessus caught it, and said to himself, "I shall not die unavenged" — and gave his robe, dyed in the warm blood, as a gift to the woman he was carrying off, as if a charm for love.

Long was the stretch of time between; and the deeds of great Hercules, and his stepmother's hatred, had filled the earth. Victorious, home from Oechalia, he was preparing a sacrifice vowed to Cenaean Jupiter, when chattering Rumor — who loves to add lies to truth, and swells from a tiny start by her falsehoods — ran ahead to your ears, Deianira: that the son of Amphitryon was gripped by love of Iole. In love, she believes it; terrified by the report of this new passion, at first she gives way to tears, and pours out her grief, poor woman, in weeping. Then she says, "But why do I weep? My rival will delight in these tears. Since she is coming, I must hurry, must try something new while I still can, while another has not yet taken my bed. Shall I complain, or keep silent? Go back to Calydon, or stay? Leave the house — or, if nothing else, block her way? What if, Meleager, remembering I am your sister, I steel myself to some desperate deed, and, by cutting my rival's throat, show what injury and a woman's grief can do?" Her mind runs through many courses. Above them all she chooses to send the robe steeped in Nessus's blood, to give strength back to her fading love. Not knowing herself what she gives, she hands the cause of her own sorrows to the unsuspecting Lichas, and, poor wretch, with coaxing words bids him carry this most deadly gift to her husband. Ignorant, the hero takes it, and puts on his shoulders the venom of the Lernaean serpent.

He was laying incense on the first flames, saying the words of prayer, pouring wine from the bowl on the marble altars, when the power of the poison grew warm and, loosed by the flames, ran melting far and wide through the limbs of Hercules. As long as he could, he held back his groans with his old courage. When his endurance was beaten by the pain, he shoved the altars over and filled woody Oeta with his cries. At once he tries to tear off the deadly robe: where it is pulled, it pulls the skin, and — a thing dreadful to tell — either it clings to the limbs when he tries in vain to strip it, or it lays bare the mangled flesh and the huge bones. The blood itself hisses, as a red-hot blade hisses when dipped in cold water, and boils with the burning venom. There is no limit to it; greedy flames drink up his vitals, a blue-black sweat pours from his whole body, his scorched sinews crackle, and, his marrow melted by the hidden decay, he lifts his hands to the stars and cries: "Daughter of Saturn, feed on my ruin — feed, and look down from on high, cruel one, on this destruction of mine, and glut your savage heart! Or, if I am to be pitied even by an enemy — for an enemy I am to you — take away a life racked and hateful to me, born only for toil. Death will be a gift to me; it fits a stepmother to grant such a gift.

"Was it for this I subdued Busiris, who fouled the gods' temples with strangers' blood? For this I tore from savage Antaeus the strength his mother gave him? Did the Iberian herdsman's triple shape not shake me, nor your triple shape, Cerberus? Did you, my hands, grip the horns of the mighty bull? Elis holds your work, and the waters of Stymphalus, and the Parthenian grove. By your valor was won the belt inlaid with the gold of Thermodon, and the apples guarded in vain by the sleepless dragon. Could the Centaurs stand against me, or the boar that ravaged Arcadia? Did it help the Hydra to grow by its own loss and take back double strength? And when I saw the Thracian horses fat with human blood and their mangers full of torn bodies, did I not throw them down and kill both the master and the horses? By these arms the Nemean lion lies crushed; on this neck I carried the sky. Jupiter's cruel wife grew weary of commanding — but I was tireless in doing. Now a new scourge is on me, one no valor, no weapons, no arms can resist. A devouring fire wanders in the depths of my lungs and feeds on all my limbs. And Eurystheus lives and thrives! And are there still men who can believe the gods exist?"

And then, racked with pain, he ranges over high Oeta, no differently than a bull that carries the hunting-spears fixed in its body while the man who threw them flees. Often you might have seen him groaning, often bellowing, often trying to tear off the whole robe, and leveling trees, and raging at the mountains, or stretching his arms toward his father's sky. Look — he spies Lichas, trembling, hidden in a hollow rock; and as his pain gathered all its fury, he cried, "Was it you, Lichas, who brought this deadly gift? Will you be the cause of my death?" The man trembles, pales in terror, and timidly stammers out excuses. As he speaks, reaching to clasp the hero's knees, Hercules seizes him, and, whirling him three and four times, hurls him into the Euboean waves harder than a siege-engine could. Hanging in the air, he hardened; as they say showers freeze in cold winds and turn to snow, and the whirling snow packs soft and rounds into thick hail, so, thrown through the void by those strong arms, drained of blood by fear and with no moisture left in him, he was turned — the old ages tell — into hard flint. Even now a low rock juts from the deep Euboean sea and keeps the traces of a human shape, which sailors fear to tread, as if it could feel, and they call it Lichas.

But you, famous child of Jupiter, cut down the trees that lofty Oeta bore, built them into a pyre, and bade the son of Poeas take the bow and the roomy quiver and the arrows that would visit the Trojan realm again; by his hand the flame was set beneath. And while the heaped wood is caught by the greedy fires, you spread the Nemean lion's skin over the top of the pile and lay down with your neck on your club, no other look on your face than a banqueter's reclining among full cups, wreathed in garlands.

And now the flames, gathering and spreading on every side, roared, and reached the untroubled limbs of the man who scorned them. The gods feared for the earth's champion; and Jupiter, son of Saturn — for he saw it — addressed them with joyful face: "This fear of yours is my delight, gods of heaven, and with all my heart I gladly congratulate myself that I am called the ruler and father of a grateful people, and that my offspring too is safe in your goodwill. For though this is owed to his own mighty deeds, still I am bound by it as well. But do not let your loyal hearts quail with empty fear: scorn the flames of Oeta! He who has conquered all will conquer the fires you see; he will feel the flame's power only in the part he got from his mother. What he drew from me is eternal, exempt and immune from death, to be tamed by no fire. That part, done with earth, I will receive into the heavenly regions, and I trust this act of mine will please all the gods. Yet if anyone — anyone — should grieve that Hercules is a god and grudge him the honor, still he shall know Hercules deserves it, and, unwilling, shall approve." The gods agreed; and even the royal wife seemed to bear the rest of his words with no hard look — only the last with a hard look, taking it ill that she was so plainly named.

Meanwhile whatever the flame could destroy, Vulcan had consumed; and no recognizable shape of Hercules remained, nothing drawn from his mother's likeness — only the marks of Jupiter were kept. And as a fresh serpent, sloughing old age with its skin, grows lush and gleams in new scales, so, when the Tirynthian shed his mortal limbs, he thrived in his better part, and began to look greater and to become awful in august dignity. Him the all-powerful Father, caught up among the hollow clouds, bore in his four-horse chariot up into the shining stars. Atlas felt the weight.

Even now Eurystheus, son of Sthenelus, had not laid aside his wrath, and, fierce, worked out his father-hatred on the offspring. But the Argive Alcmena, worn with long cares for her son, had Iole to pour out her old woman's laments to, to tell of her son's deeds attested by the whole world, and of her own misfortunes. At Hercules' command Hyllus had taken Iole into his bed and heart, and filled her womb with noble seed. To her Alcmena thus began:

"May the gods at least be kind to you, and cut short the long hours when, your time come, you call on Ilithyia, who watches over women in the fear of labor — the goddess whom Juno's favor made pitiless to me. For when the birth-hour of Hercules, born for so many toils, was near, and the tenth sign of the Zodiac was weighed down with the great star, the burden stretched my womb; and what I carried was so great you could easily call Jupiter the father of the hidden weight. I could bear the pains no longer. Even now, as I speak, a cold shudder takes my limbs, and part of the pain is remembering it. Tortured seven nights and as many days, worn out with agony, arms stretched to heaven, I called with loud cries on Lucina and the two Nixi. She came, indeed — but bought beforehand, ready to give my life to vengeful Juno. When she heard my groans, she sat on that altar before the door, and, her right knee pressed on her left, her fingers laced like a comb, she held back the birth; she said charms too, in a low voice, and the charms held back the birth already begun. I strained, and in my frenzy hurled vain reproaches at ungrateful Jupiter, and longed to die, and complained in words that would have moved hard stones. The women of Thebes were with me, made their vows, and cheered me in my pain.

"There was one of my maids, of the common people, Galanthis, golden-haired, quick to do my bidding, dear for her good service. She sensed that something was being worked by hostile Juno; and, going often in and out the door, she saw the goddess sitting on the altar, arms locked on her knees by the fingers, and she said, 'Whoever you are, wish my mistress joy: the Argive Alcmena is delivered; her prayer is won, the child is born.' The goddess of the womb leaped up, and, startled, loosed her joined hands — and I, on the loosing of those bonds, was delivered. They say Galanthis laughed at fooling the goddess. As she laughed, the cruel goddess dragged her by the very hair, and, when she tried to lift her body from the earth, stopped her, and changed her arms to forefeet. Her old quickness stays, and her back has not lost its color; but her shape is not what it was.
