Because she helped me in labor with a lying mouth, she gives birth from the mouth, and, as before, she haunts my house."

She spoke, and, moved by the memory of her old servant, she sighed. Her daughter-in-law spoke to her as she grieved: "Yet the stolen shape of one no kin to your blood moves you, mother. What if I told you the strange fate of my own sister? — though tears and grief hinder me and forbid me to speak. Dryope, the most beautiful of the Oechalian girls, was her mother's only child (my father had me by another wife). Robbed of her virginity, having suffered force from the god who owns Delphi and Delos, she was married by Andraemon, who was thought lucky in his wife.

"There is a lake whose shelving edge makes the shape of a sloping shore, with myrtle-groves crowning the top. Here Dryope came, knowing nothing of her fate, and — that you may be angrier at her lot — bringing garlands for the nymphs. On her breast she carried her son, not yet a year old, a sweet burden, feeding him with the help of her warm milk. Not far from the lake a water-lotus was blooming, mimicking the Tyrian dye, in hope of berries to come. Dryope had picked some of its flowers to give her child as toys, and I was about to do the same — for I was there — when I saw drops of blood fall from the blossom and the boughs shiver with a trembling dread. For, as the country folk now say too late, the nymph Lotis, fleeing the lust of Priapus, had passed her changed features into this plant, her name still kept.

"My sister did not know it. When, in her alarm, she tried to draw back and leave, having prayed to the nymphs, her feet were held fast by a root. She fights to tear them up, but moves nothing except her upper parts. From below the bark climbs slowly and by degrees covers all her groin. When she saw it, and made to tear her hair, she filled her hand with leaves — leaves were covering all her head. But the boy Amphissos — for his grandfather Eurytus gave him that name — felt his mother's breast go hard, and the milky stream no longer followed his sucking. I watched your cruel fate, sister, and could give you no help; and, as long as I could, I hugged the rising trunk and branches and held them back, and — I confess it — I longed to be hidden under the same bark. Look — her husband Andraemon and her most wretched father come, asking for Dryope; and when they ask for Dryope I show them the lotus. They kiss the wood still warm with life, and, flung down, cling to the roots of their own tree. Now my dear sister had nothing but her face that was not tree. Tears drop on the leaves made from her changed body; and, while she can, while her mouth still gives passage to her voice, she pours out into the air such laments as these:

"'If any faith is owed the wretched, I swear by the gods I did not deserve this cruelty. I am punished without a crime. I have lived in innocence; if I lie, may I dry up and lose the leaves I bear, and be cut down with axes and burned. But take this infant from his mother's branches and give him to a nurse; and often, beneath my tree, let him drink milk, and beneath my tree let him play; and when he can speak, let him greet his mother, and say in sorrow, "My mother is hidden in this trunk." Yet let him fear the ponds, and pick no flowers from the trees, and think all shrubs are the bodies of goddesses. Farewell, dear husband; and you, sister; and you, father — if there is any love in you, guard my branches from the sharp pruning-knife and the bite of the flocks. And since I may not bend down to you, reach your limbs up here and come for my kisses while I can still be touched, and lift up my little son. I can say no more. Already the soft bark creeps up my white neck, and I am buried at the top of my head. Take your hands from my eyes: without your help, let the closing bark cover my dying sight.' At the same moment her mouth ceased to speak and to be; and long after the body was changed, the new-made branches stayed warm."

And while Iole was telling her sister's wretched fate, and while Alcmena was drying the daughter of Eurytus's tears with her fingers — herself weeping too — a new event hushed all their sorrow: for Iolaüs stood at the high threshold, almost a boy again, his cheeks veiled in faint down, his face changed back to the first years of manhood. Hebe, the daughter of Juno, had granted him this favor, won over by her husband's pleas. As she was about to swear she would grant such gifts to no one after him, Themis stopped her: "For now," she said, "Thebes stirs up civil war, and Capaneus cannot be beaten except by Jupiter, and the two brothers will fall equal in their wounds, and the seer Amphiaraüs, the earth opening, will see his own ghosts while still alive; and a son, avenging one parent by killing the other, will be dutiful and wicked in the same act, and, driven mad by his miseries, robbed of reason and of home, will be hounded by the faces of the Furies and his mother's ghost, until his wife demands of him the fatal gold, and the Phegeian sword drains his kinsman's side. Then at last Callirhoë, the daughter of Acheloüs, will beg mighty Jupiter as a suppliant for these years of youth for her infant sons, and not let the avenger's death go unavenged. Jupiter, moved for them, will bestow the gift of his own stepdaughter and daughter-in-law, and make them men in their childhood years."

When Themis, foreknowing the future, had spoken this with prophetic voice, the gods above murmured in mixed talk, and the complaint was, why the same gifts might not be granted by others. Aurora, the daughter of Pallas, complained of her husband's aged years; gentle Ceres complained that Iasion was going gray; Vulcan asked a second life for Erichthonius; concern for the future touched Venus too, and she bargained to renew the years of Anchises.

Every god has some favorite, and the unruly quarrel grew with their partiality, until Jupiter opened his lips and said: "O, if you have any respect for me, where are you rushing? Does any one of you think himself so powerful as to overcome even the Fates? By the Fates has Iolaüs come back to the years he has spent; by the Fates must the sons of Callirhoë grow young — not by ambition or by force of arms. And you too — that you may bear this with a better mind — even me the Fates govern. Could I change them, no late years would bend my Aeacus; Rhadamanthus would keep the everlasting flower of youth, and my Minos with him, who now is despised for the grievous weight of old age and no longer reigns with the dignity he once had."

The words of Jupiter moved the gods; and no one kept complaining when they saw Rhadamanthus and Aeacus and Minos worn with years — Minos, who in his prime had terrified great nations with his very name, but was now feeble, and feared Miletus, the son of Deione, proud in the strength of youth and in Apollo his father. And though Minos believed Miletus was reaching for his throne, still he did not dare drive him from his native home. Of your own accord, Miletus, you fled, and in a swift ship measured the Aegean waters, and in the land of Asia founded a city that bears its founder's name. Here, as she followed the windings of her father's bank, Cyanee, the daughter of Maeander that so often returns to the same place, a woman of surpassing beauty, came to know you, and bore twin children, Byblis and her brother Caunus.

Byblis is a warning that girls should love only what the law allows: Byblis, seized with passion for her brother, the descendant of Apollo, loved him not as a sister loves a brother, and not as she ought. At first she understands nothing of the flame, and thinks it no fault to give him so many kisses, to throw her arms round her brother's neck; and long she is fooled by this false likeness of natural love. Little by little the affection slips awry; she comes dressed up to see her brother, is too anxious to look beautiful, and, if any woman there is lovelier, envies her. But she is not yet clear to herself, and under that fire she forms no wish — yet inwardly she burns. Already she calls him her darling; already she hates the words of kinship; already she would rather he call her Byblis than sister.

Still, awake, she does not dare let shameful hopes into her mind; but loosened in soft sleep she often sees what she loves — she even seems to embrace her brother, and she blushes, though she lies buried in sleep. Sleep goes; long she is silent, calling back the vision of her rest, and speaks with a wavering mind:

"Wretched me! What does this vision of the silent night mean? How I would not have it come true! Why have I dreamed this dream? He is beautiful, even to unfriendly eyes; he pleases me, and, were he not my brother, I could love him, and he would be worthy of me. But it is my ruin that I am his sister. Only let me try, awake, to do no such thing — but let sleep return often with the same shape! There is no witness in sleep, and the pleasure it copies is not withheld. O Venus and winged Cupid with your tender mother, what joys I have known! What plain, palpable delight has touched me! How I lay dissolved to the marrow! How sweet to remember it — though that pleasure was brief, and the night sped on, jealous of what I had begun. Oh, if I could be joined to you under a changed name, how good a daughter-in-law I could be, Caunus, to your father; how good a son-in-law you could be, Caunus, to mine! Would the gods grant that all were shared between us — all but our ancestors! I could wish you were the nobler-born. So then, most beautiful one, you will make some woman I do not know a mother; but to me, who have wretchedly drawn the same parents as you, you will be nothing but a brother. The one tie we have is the one that bars all the rest.

"What then do my visions mean? What weight have dreams — do dreams have any weight? The gods fare better: the gods have married their own sisters. So Saturn wed Ops, his kin by blood; Ocean wed Tethys, and the ruler of Olympus, Juno. The gods above have their own laws! Why do I try to measure the ways of heaven by human rule, when the two are so different? Either this forbidden fire will be driven from my heart — or, if I cannot do that, I pray I may die first, and be laid out dead on my bed, and my brother kiss me as I lie. And yet the thing needs the will of two. Suppose it pleases me: to him it will seem a crime. And yet the sons of Aeolus did not shrink from their sisters' beds — but how do I know of them? Why have I furnished myself with such precedents? Where am I carried? Away, far from here, you unclean flames! Let my brother be loved by me only as a sister may. And yet — if he had first been seized with love of me, perhaps I could yield to his madness. So then, I, who would not have refused him had he asked, shall I ask myself? Can you speak it? Can you confess it? Love will force me — I can. Or if shame holds my lips, a secret letter shall confess the hidden fire."

This thought pleases her; this settles her wavering mind. She raises herself on her side, leaning on her left elbow, and says, "Let him see it — let me confess my mad love! Wretched me, where am I sliding? What flame is my mind kindling?" And, with trembling hand, she sets down her well-weighed words. Her right hand holds the iron pen, the other the blank wax. She begins, and hesitates; she writes and blames what she has written; she marks and deletes, alters, condemns, approves; and by turns she throws the tablet down and takes it up again. She does not know what she wants; whatever she seems about to do displeases her. In her face is boldness mixed with shame. She had written "sister"; then she chose to erase "sister" and cut such words as these into the smoothed wax:

"The health that she herself will never have unless you give it, your lover sends you. I am ashamed — ah, ashamed to speak my name; and if you ask what I desire, I could wish my case might be pleaded without a name, and that I not be known as Byblis until my hopes were sure. You might have had, as a sign of my wounded heart, my pallor, my wasting, my downcast looks, my eyes often wet, sighs with no plain cause, frequent embraces, and kisses that — if you happened to notice — could not be felt to be a sister's. Yet, though I bore a heavy wound in my soul, though a fiery madness was within, I did everything — the gods are my witnesses — to be cured at last; long, in my misery, I fought to escape the cruel weapon of Cupid, and endured more than you would think a girl could bear. Beaten, I am forced to confess my passion, and with timid prayers to beg your help. You alone can save, you alone destroy the one who loves you: choose which you will do. It is no enemy who begs this, but one who, though already most closely bound to you, longs to be bound closer still, tied by a nearer knot. Let old men know the ordinances, and ask what is lawful and wicked and right, and keep the balance of the laws. A love that dares all suits our years. What is allowed, we do not yet know, and we believe all things allowed, and follow the example of the great gods. No stern father, no regard for reputation, no fear will stop us — only let the cause of fear be gone. Under a brother's name we will hide our sweet stolen joys. I have the freedom to talk with you in private, and we give embraces and exchange kisses in the open. How little is it that is lacking! Pity one who confesses her love — who would not confess it, did not utmost passion compel — and do not earn a place as the cause written on my tomb."

The full wax fell short of her hand as it vainly traced such words, and the last line clung to the margin. At once she seals her own crime with the stamp of a signet, which she wets with tears, for the moisture had fled her tongue. In shame she called one of her servants and, coaxing the frightened man in timid tones, said, "Carry these, most faithful one, to my—" and, after a long pause, added, "brother." As she gave them, the tablets slipped from her hands and fell. She was shaken by the omen, but sent them still. The servant, finding a fit moment, went and delivered the hidden words. The young son of Maeander's line, seized with sudden anger, threw down the tablets he had taken when he had read part; and, barely holding his hands from the trembling servant's face, he said, "Flee while you can, you cursed pander to forbidden lust! — who, if your ruin would not drag my honor with it, would have paid me with your death." The man fled in terror and reported to his mistress the fierce words of Caunus.

You turn pale, Byblis, hearing of the refusal, and your body, gripped by an icy chill, is struck with dread; yet when your senses return, so does your frenzy, and your tongue barely gives such words as these to the beaten air: "And I deserve it! For why, rashly, did I make this wound known? Why did I so quickly trust to a hasty letter words that should have been hidden? I should first have felt out the leanings of his mind with ambiguous speech. To be sure the wind would follow as I went, I should have watched, with only part of my sail, what breeze it was, and run over a safe sea; and now I have filled my canvas with untried winds. So I am driven upon the rocks, and, capsized, buried under the whole ocean, and my sails have no way back. And was I not forbidden by sure omens to indulge my love, when the wax fell as I bade him carry it, and made my hopes drop to the ground? Should not the day have been changed, or my whole intent — rather, the day? The god himself was warning me, giving sure signs, had I not been out of my mind. And I should have spoken myself, not trusted to wax, and shown my madness in person. He would have seen my tears, seen the face of the woman who loved him; I could have said more than the letter held. I could have thrown my arms round his unwilling neck; and, if refused, could have seemed at the point of death, and clasped his feet, and, flung down, begged for my life. I would have done all this; and if each thing alone could not bend his hard heart, all together might have. Perhaps, too, there is some fault in the servant I sent: he did not approach him well, or choose, I think, a fit time, or seek an idle hour and a free mind. This has undone me. For he was not born of a tigress, nor does he carry hard flint or solid iron or adamant in his breast, nor did he suck a lioness's milk. He will be won! He must be tried again, and I will take no weariness of what I have begun, so long as this breath stays in me. For the best thing — could I recall what is done — was not to have begun; the next best is to press on with what is begun. Even if I gave up my desire, he cannot ever forget that I dared it; and, because I stopped, I will seem to have wanted it lightly, or even to have been testing him, to have solicited him meaning to betray — or at least be thought conquered not by this god who burns me so, but by mere lust. In any case I can no longer be free of a shameful deed: I have written, I have solicited; my will is defiled. Though I add nothing more, I cannot be called innocent. What remains adds much to my desire, little to my guilt."

So she says; and — so great is the discord of her wavering mind — though she is loath to try him, she longs to try him, and, past all bounds, poor woman, lays herself open to be refused again and again. At last, when there is no end to it, he flees his country and the crime, and founds a new city in a foreign land.

Then indeed, they say, the daughter of Miletus wholly lost her mind in grief; then she tore the robe from her breast and beat her arms in frenzy. Now she raves openly, confessing her hope of an unlawful union, and, leaving her country and her hated home, follows the tracks of her fleeing brother. As the Thracian Bacchants, roused by your thyrsus, son of Semele, keep your festivals each third year, so the women of Bubasus saw Byblis howling across the wide fields. Leaving them, she wandered through the land of the Carians, the warlike Leleges, and Lycia; and now she had left behind Cragos and Limyre and the waters of the Xanthus, and the ridge where the Chimaera had fire in its middle, the breast and face of a lioness, the tail of a serpent. The woods gave out at last, when you, Byblis, worn with the chase, fall down, and, your hair on the hard ground, lie silent and press the fallen leaves with your face. Often the Leleges' nymphs try to lift her in their tender arms; often they urge her to master her passion, and bring comfort to a mind that cannot hear. Silent Byblis lies, tearing the green herbs with her nails and watering the grass with her stream of tears. Beneath these tears, they say, the Naiads set a channel that could never run dry — for what greater gift had they to give? At once, as drops ooze from the cut bark of the pitch-pine, or sticky bitumen wells from the heavy earth, or as water frozen with cold melts in the sun at the coming of the gentle west wind, so Byblis, the descendant of Apollo, consumed by her own tears, turns into a fountain, which even now in those valleys bears its mistress's name, and flows beneath a dark oak.

The fame of this new marvel might have filled the hundred cities of Crete, had Crete not lately borne a nearer wonder of her own, in the change of Iphis.

For once the land of Phaestus, next to the kingdom of Knossos, produced a man of no famous name, Ligdus, of the free-born common folk; his means no greater than his rank, but his life and honor untainted. When his wife's delivery was near, he warned her ears with these words: "Two things I pray for: that you be eased with the least pain, and that you bear a boy. The other lot is a heavier trouble, and fortune has denied us the means. What I abhor — if by chance a girl should be born at your delivery — I command it against my will; forgive me, natural love — let it be put to death." He spoke, and both bathed their faces with streaming tears — he who commanded, and she to whom the command was given. But still Telethusa pressed her husband with fruitless prayers not to shut his hope in so narrow a bound. Ligdus's mind was fixed.

And now she could scarcely carry her womb, heavy with its ripe burden, when in the middle of the night, in a vision of sleep, the daughter of Inachus, attended by her train of holy rites, stood, or seemed to stand, before her bed. On her brow were the horns of the moon, and ears of corn bright with shining gold, and the royal splendor of the diadem; with her were barking Anubis, and holy Bubastis, and the dappled Apis, and the god who hushes his voice and bids silence with a finger; there were the sistrums, and Osiris never sought enough, and the foreign serpent full of sleep-bringing venom. Then, as though Telethusa were roused from sleep and seeing all clearly, the goddess spoke: "O Telethusa, one of my worshipers, lay down your grievous cares, and cheat your husband's command. Do not hesitate, when Lucina has eased you with the birth, to raise whatever it shall be. I am a helping goddess, and when called on I bring aid; you shall not complain that you worshiped an ungrateful power." She counseled her, and withdrew from the chamber. The Cretan woman rose from her bed rejoicing, and, lifting her pure hands to the stars as a suppliant, prayed that her vision might prove true.

When her pains grew and the burden forced itself into the light, and a girl was born to the father who did not know, the mother ordered the child raised, pretending it a boy; and the thing was believed — no one but the nurse knew the truth. The father paid his vows and gave the child its grandfather's name: the grandfather had been Iphis. The mother was glad of the name, for it belonged to both sexes, and by it she would deceive no one. From then the pious lie lay hidden. The child was dressed as a boy; and its face was such that, whether you gave it to a girl or a boy, either would be lovely.

Meanwhile the thirteenth year had come, when your father, Iphis, betrothed to you golden-haired Ianthe, most praised of the girls of Phaestus for her beauty's dower, the daughter of Dictaean Telestes. Their age was equal, their beauty equal; and they had their first lessons, the rudiments of their years, from the same teachers. From this, love touched the untried hearts of both and gave each an equal wound — but how unequal their hopes! Ianthe awaits the wedding and the promised time of the torches, and believes that the one she thinks a man will be her husband. Iphis loves one she despairs of ever enjoying, and this very thing feeds her flame; a maid, she burns for a maid. Scarcely holding back her tears, she says: "What end awaits me, held by a passion no one has known, monstrous and strange? If the gods wished to spare me, they should have spared me; if not, and they wished to destroy me, they should at least have given me a natural affliction, one common to my kind. Cow does not burn for cow, nor mare for mares; the ram burns for the ewe, the doe follows her stag. So too the birds mate; and among all the animals no female is seized with desire for a female. Would that I did not exist! Yet, so that Crete might breed every kind of monster, the daughter of the Sun loved a bull — a female, yes, a male. My love, if I confess the truth, is madder than hers. Still she followed the hope of union; still, by a trick, in the shape of a cow, she gave herself to the bull, and there was a lover to be deceived. Though the world's whole ingenuity gathered here, though Daedalus flew back on his waxen wings, what could he do? Could he, by his clever arts, make me a boy from a girl? Could he change you, Ianthe?

"Why not steady your mind, Iphis, and gather yourself, and shake off this foolish, senseless fire? See what you were born — unless you fool yourself as well — and seek what is allowed, and love what, as a woman, you should. It is hope that makes, hope that feeds love; this the very case takes from you. No guard keeps you from her dear embrace, no watchful husband's care, no father's harshness; she herself does not refuse you when you ask — and yet she cannot be yours; and, though all were done, you could not be happy, though gods and men should labor for you. Even now no part of my prayers is empty; the kind gods have given what they could, and what I wish, my father wishes, and she herself, and my father-in-law to be. But nature, more powerful than all these, wills it not — she alone harms me. Look, the longed-for time comes; the wedding-day is here, and Ianthe will soon be mine — and never come to me. In the midst of water I shall thirst. Why, Juno, guardian of weddings, and why, Hymenaeus, do you come to these rites, where there is no man to marry the bride, where we are two women wed?" With that she closed her lips. And no less the other girl burns, and prays you, Hymenaeus, to come quickly.

Telethusa, dreading the very thing Ianthe desires, now puts off the day, now draws out delay with a feigned illness, often pleads omens and visions for excuse. But now she had used up all her store of fictions; the long-delayed wedding-day was upon them, and only one day remained. Then she takes the fillets from her own head and her daughter's, and, embracing the altar with loosened hair, cries: "O Isis, who dwell in Paraetonium and the Mareotic fields and Pharos, and the Nile parted into its seven horns — bring help, I beg, and heal our fear! You, goddess, you and these your emblems I once saw, and knew them all — your attendants, your torches, the sound of the sistrums — and I marked your commands in a mindful heart. That this girl sees the light, that I am not punished — this is your counsel and your gift. Pity us both, and aid us with your help." Tears followed her words. The goddess seemed to move — and did move — her altar; the temple doors shook; her moon-like horns shone, and the ringing sistrum sounded. Not free of care, but glad of the good omen, the mother left the temple; and Iphis followed her as she went, with a longer stride than before. The fairness does not stay in her face; her strength grows, her features sharpen, her loose hair is shorter, and there is more vigor in her than she had as a girl. For you who were a girl just now are a boy! Bring gifts to the temple, and rejoice with no timid faith! They bring their gifts to the temple, and add an inscription too, a short line that ran:

Iphis, a boy, pays the gifts that as a girl he vowed.

The next dawn had opened the wide world with its rays, when Venus and Juno and Hymenaeus came to the wedding fires, and the boy Iphis won his own Ianthe.

Book X

From there, wrapped in his saffron cloak, Hymenaeus passed through the measureless air and made for the land of the Ciconians, called — in vain — by the voice of Orpheus. He came, indeed, but he brought no solemn words, no glad face, no lucky omen. The very torch he held kept hissing with a tearful smoke, and, for all his shaking, found no flame. The outcome was heavier than the omen: for the new bride, wandering through the grass with her train of Naiads, fell dead, a serpent's fang in her ankle.

When the bard of Rhodope had wept for her enough in the upper air, he dared, so as to try the shades below as well, to go down to the Styx by the gate of Taenarus; and through the weightless peoples and the ghosts that had had their burial he came to Persephone and to the lord of the shades who holds that joyless realm, and, striking his strings in tune with his words, he sang:

"O powers of the world set beneath the earth, to which we all fall back, all of us who are born to die — if I may, and if you let me speak the truth, laying aside the winding tricks of a false tongue: I have not come down here to see dark Tartarus, nor to bind the triple throat of the Medusan monster, shaggy with snakes. My wife is the reason of my journey — a viper, trodden underfoot, poured its poison into her and cut short her growing years. I wished to be able to bear it, and I will not deny that I tried; but Love has won. That god is well known in the world above; whether he is known here too, I doubt — and yet I think that even here he is, and, if the tale of the old rape is not a lie, Love joined you two as well. By these places full of dread, by this vast Chaos, by the silence of these boundless realms, I beg you: unravel the too-swift fate of Eurydice. To you we all belong; we linger a little while above, then, sooner or later, hurry to this one home. This way we all are bound; this is our last house; you hold the longest reign over the human race. She too, when in due time she has lived out her rightful years, will fall under your law. I ask her use as a gift; and if the Fates deny this favor for my wife, I am resolved not to return — take your joy in the death of us both."

As he spoke this, striking the strings to his words, the bloodless spirits wept. Tantalus did not snatch at the fleeing water; Ixion's wheel stood still in wonder; the birds did not tear at the liver of Tityus; the daughters of Belus paused at their urns; and you, Sisyphus, sat down on your stone. Then, for the first time — so the story goes — the cheeks of the Furies, mastered by the song, were wet with tears; and neither the royal wife nor the ruler of the depths could bear to refuse him, and they called Eurydice. She was among the newly come shades, and came forward with a slow step from her wound. The Rhodopeian hero took her, and with her this condition: that he not turn his eyes back until he had left the vales of Avernus — or the gift would be void.

Up the sloping path they climb through the mute silence, steep, dark, thick with black fog. And now they were not far from the edge of the upper earth, when he, in love, afraid she might fail and hungry to see her, turned his eyes — and at once she slipped back. Reaching out her arms, straining to be held and to hold him, the poor woman caught nothing but the yielding air. And now, dying a second time, she made no complaint of her husband — for what could she complain of but that she was loved? She spoke one last "Farewell," which he could barely catch, and was swept back again to the same place.

No otherwise was Orpheus stunned at his wife's double death than the man who, trembling, saw the three necks of the dog, the middle one in chains, and whom fear did not leave before his old nature did, as stone spread over his body; or than Olenus, who took another's guilt upon himself and chose to seem the guilty one, and than you, unhappy Lethaea, so sure of your beauty — hearts once most closely joined, now stones that watery Ida holds. He begged, and longed in vain to cross again, but the ferryman drove him off. Still, for seven days he sat squalid on the bank, without the gifts of Ceres; care and grief of mind and tears were his food. Crying that the gods of Erebus were cruel, he withdrew to high Rhodope and to Haemus, battered by the north winds.

Three times the sun had ended the year bounded by the ocean's Fishes, and Orpheus had shunned all love of women, whether because it had gone so badly for him, or because he had given his word; yet many women burned to join themselves to the bard, and many grieved to be refused. He was even the first to teach the people of Thrace to turn their love to tender boys, and to pluck the brief spring and the first flowers of their youth this side of manhood.

There was a hill, and on the hill a level stretch of open ground, which the blades of grass made green; the place lacked shade. When the god-born bard sat down there and struck his sounding strings, a shade came over the spot. The tree of Chaonia was not missing, nor the grove of the Heliades, nor the tall-branched winter-oak, nor the soft lindens, nor the beech, nor the unwed laurel, nor the brittle hazels, nor the ash good for spears, nor the knotless fir, nor the holm bent under its acorns, nor the friendly plane, nor the maple of many colors, nor the willows that grow by rivers, and the water-lotus, and the evergreen box, and the slender tamarisks, and the two-colored myrtle, and the tine with its dark-blue berries. You too, ivy of the winding foot, came, and with you the tendriled vines and the elms clothed in vines, the mountain-ash and the pitch-pines, the arbutus loaded with its blushing fruit, the pliant palm, the victor's prize, and the pine with its tufted top bristling — dear to the Mother of the Gods, since for her sake Cybele's Attis put off the man and hardened into that trunk.

Among this throng was the cypress, shaped like a cone — now a tree, but once a boy, beloved of the god who fits the lyre and the bow alike with strings. For there was a great stag, sacred to the nymphs who hold the fields of Carthaea, that gave a wide shade to its own head with its far-spreading antlers. His horns shone with gold; a jeweled collar hung from his smooth round neck down to his shoulders; a silver disc, tied with little straps, played on his brow; and matching pendants of bronze gleamed on his two ears about his hollow temples. Free of fear, his natural shyness laid aside, he used to haunt the houses and offer his neck to be stroked by any hands, even strangers'. But above all others he was dear to you, Cyparissus, most beautiful of the people of Cea. You led the stag to fresh pasture and to the water of the clear spring; now you wove flowers of many colors through his horns, now, seated on his back like a rider, you steered his gentle mouth this way and that with a purple bridle.

It was summer, the middle of the day, and the curving arms of the shore-loving Crab glowed with the sun's heat. The stag, weary, had laid his body on the grassy ground and was drinking the cool of a tree's shade. Without meaning to, the boy Cyparissus pierced him with his sharp javelin; and when he saw him dying of the cruel wound, he resolved to die himself. What comfort did Phoebus not offer, urging him to grieve in measure, and no more than the loss deserved! Still the boy groaned, and asked this last gift of the gods: to mourn forever. And now, his blood drained by endless weeping, his limbs began to turn green, and the hair that lately hung over his snow-white brow became a bristling bush, and, stiffening, pointed to the starry sky with a tapering top. The god groaned and, grieving, said: "You will be mourned by me, and you will mourn for others, and stand by those who sorrow."

Such a wood the bard had drawn about him, and he sat in the midst of a gathering of wild beasts and a crowd of birds. When he had tried the strings enough with his thumb and heard that the different tones, though they sounded apart, still chimed together, he lifted his voice in this song:

From Jove, my mother Muse — all things bow to the reign of Jove — set my song moving! Often before I have sung the power of Jove; in a heavier strain I sang the Giants and the victorious bolts scattered over the Phlegraean plains. Now I need a lighter lyre: let me sing of boys the gods have loved, and of girls struck by forbidden fires who earned punishment for their lust.

The king of the gods once burned with love for Phrygian Ganymede, and something was found that Jupiter would rather be than what he was. Yet he stooped to turn himself into no bird but the one that could carry his bolts. Without delay, beating the air on borrowed wings, he snatched away the boy of Ilium — who even now mixes his cups and, though Juno is unwilling, serves Jove his nectar.

You too, descendant of Amyclas, Phoebus would have set in the sky, if the grim Fates had given him time to set you there. Still, so far as may be, you are immortal; and as often as spring drives off winter, and the Ram follows the watery Fish, so often you rise and bloom on the green turf. My father loved you beyond all others, and Delphi at the center of the earth went without its guardian god while he haunted the Eurotas and unwalled Sparta; his lyre and his arrows were held in no honor. Forgetting himself, he did not refuse to carry the nets, to hold the dogs, to go as a companion over the ridges of the harsh mountains; and by long habit he fed his flame.

Now Titan stood almost midway between the coming and the gone night, an equal distance from both, when they stripped their bodies and shone with the juice of the rich olive and began the game of the broad discus. Phoebus first sent it, well poised, into the airy breeze, and split the facing clouds with its weight. After a long while the heavy mass fell to the solid ground, showing skill joined with strength. At once, thoughtless and driven by eagerness for the sport, the boy of Taenarus hurried to lift the disc — but the hard ground bounced it back with a rebounding stroke into your face, Hyacinthus. The god went as pale as the boy, and caught up the sinking limbs, and now warms you, now stanches your sad wound, now checks the fleeing life with herbs pressed to it. His arts are no use; the wound was past healing. As when, in a well-watered garden, someone breaks off violets, or a stiff poppy, or lilies bristling with their yellow tongues, and, withered, they suddenly droop their heavy heads, and cannot hold themselves up, and look at the ground with their tops — so the dying face sinks, and, its strength gone, the neck is a burden to itself and leans on the shoulder.

"You are falling, son of Oebalus, cheated of your first youth," said Phoebus, "and I see your wound, my own crime. You are my grief and my guilt: my right hand must be charged with your death; I am the author of your dying. Yet what is my fault — unless to have played can be called a fault, unless to have loved can be called a fault? And oh, that I might die with you, or give my life for yours! But since we are held by the law of fate, you shall always be with me, and dwell on my remembering lips. My songs, my lyre struck by my hand, shall sound of you; and, a new flower, you shall copy my laments in your markings. The time will come, too, when a most valiant hero shall join his name to this flower, and it shall be read on the same petals."

While such things were told by Apollo's truthful lips, look — the blood that, spilled on the ground, had stained the grass, ceases to be blood, and a flower springs up, brighter than Tyrian purple, taking the shape lilies have, but for the color: theirs is silver, this is purple. This was not enough for Phoebus — for he was the author of the honor: he himself wrote his laments on the petals, and the flower bears the letters AI, AI inscribed, the mournful marks traced there. Nor is Sparta ashamed to have borne Hyacinthus; his honor lasts to this day, and the Hyacinthia comes round each year to be kept, with its solemn procession, in the manner of old.

But if you should ask Amathus, rich in ores, whether she would wish to have borne the Propoetides, she would say no — as she would to those whose brows were once rough with two horns, from which they took their name, the Cerastae. Before their doors stood an altar of Jupiter the Guest — a scene of horror: if some stranger had seen it stained with blood, he would have thought suckling calves had been killed there, and Amathusian sheep. It was guests who were slaughtered! Kindly Venus, offended at the wicked sacrifices, was making ready to abandon her own cities and the Ophiusian fields. "But how have my pleasant places, how have my cities sinned?" she said. "What crime is theirs? Let this impious race pay instead by exile, or death, or something between death and flight — and what can that be, but the punishment of a changed shape?" While she hesitates what to change them into, she turns her eyes to their horns, and is reminded that these may be left them; and she transforms their huge limbs into fierce bulls.

Yet the vile Propoetides dared to deny that Venus was a goddess. For that, through the goddess's anger, they are said to have been the first to sell their bodies and their good name; and when shame was gone and the blood of their faces had hardened, they were turned, by a small change, into flint.

Because Pygmalion had seen these women living out their lives in crime, offended by the many faults nature had given the female mind, he lived unmarried, without a wife, and long had no partner of his bed. Meanwhile, with wonderful skill he carved snow-white ivory and gave it a beauty no woman could be born with, and fell in love with his own work. The face was that of a real girl, one you would think alive, and, if modesty did not forbid, wishing to move: so well did the art hide its own art. Pygmalion marvels, and draws into his breast the fire of that pretended body. Often he lays his hands on the work to test whether it is flesh or ivory, and will not yet admit it is ivory. He gives it kisses, and thinks they are returned; he speaks to it, holds it, and believes his fingers sink into the limbs he touches, and fears a bruise may come on the pressed flesh. Now he uses soft words, now brings it gifts that please girls: shells and smooth pebbles, little birds and thousand-colored flowers, lilies, painted balls, and the amber tears of the Heliades fallen from the trees. He dresses its limbs too, puts gems on its fingers, a long necklace on its neck; light pearls hang from its ears, ribbons on its breast — all become it, and it seems no less lovely bare. He lays it on a couch dyed with Sidonian purple, calls it the companion of his bed, and rests its reclining neck on soft down, as if it could feel.

The festival day of Venus, kept most solemnly through all Cyprus, had come; heifers with snow-white necks, their curved horns tipped with gold, had fallen at the stroke; the incense smoked; when, his offering made, Pygmalion stood at the altar and shyly said, "If you gods can give all things, let my wife be, I pray" — not daring to say "the ivory maid" — "one like my ivory." Golden Venus, present at her own festival, understood what that prayer meant; and as a sign that the god was kind, three times the flame flared and drew a tongue of fire up through the air. When he came home, he sought the image of his girl, and, leaning on the couch, gave it kisses: it seemed warm. He puts his mouth to it again, feels its breast with his hands — the ivory softens at the touch, and, its hardness gone, yields to the fingers, gives way as Hymettian wax grows soft in the sun and, worked by the thumb, is bent into many shapes and made usable by the very handling. While he stands amazed, and doubtfully rejoices, and fears he is deceived, again and again the lover touches with his hand the object of his prayer. It was a body! The veins throb, tested by the thumb. Then indeed the Paphian hero pours out the fullest words to thank Venus, and at last presses his mouth on a mouth no longer false; and the girl felt the kisses given her, and blushed, and, lifting her timid eyes to the light, saw at once her lover and the sky. The goddess was present at the marriage she had made; and now, when the moon's horns had nine times been gathered to a full circle, the girl bore Paphos, from whom the island keeps its name.

Of him was born that Cinyras who, had he been childless, might have been counted among the happy. I will sing of dreadful things. Daughters, be far from here; parents, be far — or, if my songs charm your minds, do not believe me in this part, do not believe it happened; or, if you will believe it, believe as well in the punishment of the deed. If nature does let such a crime be seen to have been done, then I count this land lucky that it lies far from the regions that bred so great an outrage. Let the land of Panchaia be rich in balsam, and bear its cinnamon and its costmary and its incense sweated from the wood and its other flowers — so long as it bears myrrh too: the new tree was not worth so much. Cupid himself denies, Myrrha, that his arrows hurt you, and clears his torches of that charge; one of the three Sisters breathed on you with a Stygian brand and swollen vipers. To hate a parent is a crime; this love is a crime greater than hate.

On every side chosen nobles want you; from all the East the young men come to the contest for your bed. Choose one man out of them all, Myrrha — only let that one, out of them all, not be your father. She indeed feels her guilt and fights against her foul passion, and says to herself: "Where is my mind carrying me? What am I attempting? I beg you, gods, and natural love, and the sacred bonds of parents — forbid this crime, stop my wickedness, if indeed it is wicked. But they say natural affection does not condemn this love: the other animals couple without distinction; it is no shame for a heifer to bear her sire, a horse takes his own daughter to mate, the he-goat goes with the flocks he fathered, and the bird conceives by the one whose seed conceived her. Happy those to whom such things are allowed! Human care has made spiteful laws, and what nature permits, envious ordinances forbid. Yet there are said to be nations where mother is joined to son and daughter to father, and love grows greater by the doubled bond. Wretched me, that it was not my luck to be born there, and that I am wronged by the accident of place! But why do I dwell on this? Away, forbidden hopes! He is worthy to be loved — but as a father. So then, were I not the daughter of great Cinyras, I could lie with Cinyras. Now, because he is already mine, he is not mine, and my very nearness is my ruin: a stranger, I would be stronger. I would gladly go far from here and leave my country's borders, if only I might escape the crime; but a wicked fire holds me back as I go, so that I may look at Cinyras in the flesh, and touch him, and speak to him, and give him kisses, if nothing more is allowed. But can you hope for anything more, unnatural girl? Do you feel how many ties and names you are confusing? Will you be your mother's rival and your father's mistress? Will you be called your son's sister and your brother's mother? Will you not dread the Sisters with black snakes for hair, whom guilty minds see aiming their cruel torches at their eyes and faces? But you — while you have suffered no crime in body — do not conceive it in mind, and do not defile all-powerful nature's law with a forbidden bed. Suppose you wished it: the thing itself forbids you; he is good, and mindful of what is right — and oh, that the same madness were in him!"

She had spoken; but Cinyras, whom a worthy crowd of suitors puts in doubt what to do, asks the girl herself, running through their names, whose wife she would be. At first she is silent, and, fixed on her father's face, she burns and fills her eyes with warm dew. Cinyras, taking this for a virgin's fear, forbids her to weep, dries her cheeks, and gives her kisses. Myrrha is too glad of the kisses; and, asked what kind of husband she would have, she said, "One like you." He praised the answer he did not understand, and said, "Always be so loving." At the word "loving" the girl, conscious of her guilt, cast down her face.

It was midnight, and sleep had loosed the cares and bodies of mortals; but the daughter of Cinyras, wide awake, is devoured by an unquenched fire and turns over her mad longings. Now she despairs, now she wants to try; she is ashamed, and desires, and cannot find what to do. As a great tree, wounded by the axe, when the last stroke is left, is doubtful which way to fall and is feared on every side, so her mind, shaken by wound after wound, wavers this way and that and takes an impulse both ways; and no bound or rest is found for her love but death. Death pleases her. She rises, resolves to fit her neck in a noose; and, tying her girdle to the top of the doorpost, she said, "Farewell, dear Cinyras, and understand the cause of my death" — and set the band to her pale neck.

The murmur of her words, they say, reached the faithful ears of her nurse, keeping the threshold of her charge. The old woman rises, opens the door, and, seeing the tools of the death she had planned, cries out at the same moment, and beats herself, and tears her breast, and, snatching the band from her neck, rips it apart. Only then had she leisure to weep, to embrace her, to ask the cause of the noose. The girl is silent, as if dumb, and, unmoving, stares at the ground, and grieves that her slow attempt at death is caught. The old woman presses her, and, baring her gray hair and her empty breasts, begs her by her cradle and her first nourishment to trust her with whatever grieves her. The girl turns away from the asking and groans. The nurse is set on finding it out, and pledges not only faith: "Tell me," she says, "and let me help you: my old age is not idle. If it is a frenzy, I have one who can heal with charm and herbs; if someone has hurt you by a spell, you shall be cleansed by magic rite; if it is the gods' anger, that anger can be soothed by sacrifice. What else can I think? Surely your fortune and your house are safe and thriving: your mother and father are alive." At the word "father," Myrrha drew a sigh from the depth of her breast. Even now the nurse conceives no crime in her mind, yet senses it is some love; and, holding to her purpose, she begs her to tell, whatever it is, and lifts the weeping girl into her aged lap, and, so clasping her limbs in her weak arms, says, "I have understood — you are in love! And in this my care will serve you; your father shall never know." The girl sprang up in a frenzy from her lap, and, pressing her face into the bed, said, "Go, I beg, and spare my wretched shame!" And when the nurse pressed on, she said, "Go, or stop asking why I grieve. What you are laboring to know is a crime." The old woman shudders, and stretches out her hands, trembling with years and fear, and falls a suppliant before her nursling's feet; and now she coaxes, now, if she is not told, she frightens her, threatening to reveal the noose and the attempted death, and pledges her service if the love is confided. The girl lifted her head and filled the nurse's breast with bursting tears, and, often trying to confess, as often checked her voice, and hid her blushing face in her robe, and said, "O mother, happy in your husband!" — this much, and groaned. A cold shudder pierced the nurse's limbs and bones — for she understood — and the white hoariness stood bristling all over her head; and she added many words to drive off, if she could, so dreadful a love. But the girl knows she is not warned falsely; still she is resolved to die if she cannot have what she loves. "Live, then," the nurse says, "you shall have your—" and, not daring to say "father," fell silent, and confirmed her promise by a god.

The pious matrons were keeping the yearly festival of Ceres, when, their bodies clothed in snow-white robes, they offer garlands of corn-ears as the first fruits of the harvest, and for nine nights count the touch of a husband among forbidden things. Cenchreis, the king's wife, is in that company, at the secret rites. So, while his lawful wife's bed is empty, the nurse, wickedly diligent, finding Cinyras heavy with wine, tells him of a real love under a false name, and praises the girl's beauty. When he asks the maiden's age, she says, "The same as Myrrha's." Bidden to bring her, and back home, she says, "Rejoice, my child: we have won." The unhappy girl does not feel the joy through her whole heart; her foreboding breast is sad — and yet she is glad too: so great is the discord of her mind.

It was the hour when all things are silent, and Boötes had turned his wain with the pole slanting among the stars. She comes to her crime. The golden moon flees the sky; black clouds hide the lurking stars; the night is without its fires. You first, Icarus, cover your rising face, and you, Erigone, hallowed by holy love of your father. Three times she was called back by the omen of a stumbling foot; three times the funeral owl gave a warning with its deadly cry; yet she goes on, and the darkness and the black night lessen her shame. With her left hand she holds her nurse's, with the other she gropes the blind way. Now she touches the chamber's threshold, now opens the door, now is led inside — but her knees shook and gave beneath her, her color and her blood fled, and her courage left her as she went. The nearer she is to her crime, the more she dreads it; she repents her daring, and would wish she could turn back unknown. As she hesitates, the aged woman leads her by the hand and, bringing her to the high bed, delivers her up: "Take her, Cinyras," she said, "she is your own" — and joined the doomed bodies. The father takes his own flesh into the unclean bed, calms her virgin fears, and encourages her as she trembles. Perhaps, too, by reason of her age, he called her "daughter," and she called him "father," so that the fitting names should not be missing from the horror.

Full of her father, she left his chamber, carrying his unholy seed in her womb, bearing her conceived crime. The next night doubles the deed, nor is there an end in that; until at last Cinyras, eager after so many nights to know his lover, brought in a light and saw both the crime and his daughter. His words checked by grief, he tore his gleaming sword from its hanging sheath. Myrrha fled: saved from death by the dark and the gift of blind night, she wandered over the wide fields, left the palm-bearing Arabs and the Panchaean lands, and roamed through nine horns of the returning moon, until at last, weary, she rested in the Sabaean land; and scarcely could she carry the burden of her womb. Then, not knowing what to wish, between the fear of death and weariness of life, she framed this prayer: "O powers, if any of you are open to those who confess — I have deserved, and I do not refuse, a grim punishment; but, so that I may not defile the living by surviving, nor the dead by dying, drive me from both realms, and, changing me, deny me both life and death." Some power is open to those who confess: her last prayers at least found their gods. For the earth came over her legs as she spoke, and a root spread out slantwise through her bursting nails, a firm base for the tall trunk; her bones turn to hard wood, and, the marrow staying in the middle, her blood runs into sap, her arms into great branches, her fingers into small ones, her skin hardens into bark. And now the growing tree had bound tight her heavy womb, had covered her breast, and was making to close over her neck. She could not bear the delay, and, meeting the rising wood, she sank down and sank her face into the bark. Though she lost her old senses with her body, still she weeps, and warm drops run from the tree. There is honor even in her tears, and the myrrh that drips from the bark keeps its mistress's name, and will be unspoken of in no age.

But the child conceived in guilt had grown beneath the wood and was seeking a way to push out, having left his mother; the swollen belly bulges in the middle of the tree. The burden strains the mother, but the pains have no words of their own, and Lucina cannot be called by the voice of the one giving birth. Yet the tree is like a woman in labor, and, bending, gives frequent groans, and is wet with falling tears. Gentle Lucina stood by the aching boughs, laid on her hands, and spoke the words of childbirth: the tree splits into cracks and, through the cloven bark, gives back its living burden, and the boy cries; the Naiads laid him on soft grass and anointed him with his mother's tears. Even Envy would have praised his face; for such as the bodies of naked Cupids are painted in a picture, such was he — but, that their dress may make no difference, either give him, or take from them, the light quivers.

Winged time slips by unseen and deceives us, and nothing is swifter than the years. He, born of his own sister and his grandfather, so lately shut in a tree, so lately born, just now a most beautiful infant, is now a youth, now a man, now more beautiful than himself; now he pleases even Venus, and avenges his mother's fire. For, while the quiver-bearing boy was kissing his mother, he grazed her breast, unknowing, with a jutting arrow; the hurt goddess pushed her son away with her hand — the wound was driven deeper than it seemed, and had at first deceived even her. Taken with the beauty of the youth, she no longer cares for the shores of Cythera, nor seeks again sea-girt Paphos, or fishy Cnidos, or Amathus rich in ores; she stays away even from the sky — Adonis is preferred to the sky. Him she holds, him she keeps company; and she who was always used to indulge herself in the shade, and to improve her beauty by care of it, now wanders over ridges, through woods, over bushy rocks, her dress girt to the knee in Diana's fashion, and cheers on the dogs and hunts harmless quarry — the fleeing hares, or the high-antlered stag, or the does; she keeps away from the strong boars, and avoids the ravening wolves, the claw-armed bears, and the lions gorged with the slaughter of herds. You too she warns to fear these, Adonis, if warning could do any good, and says: "Be brave against those that flee; against the bold, boldness is not safe. Do not be rash at my peril, young man, and do not provoke the beasts nature has armed, lest your glory cost me dear. Neither your age nor your beauty, nor the things that have moved Venus, move lions and bristly boars and the eyes and hearts of wild beasts. Fierce boars carry lightning in their curved tusks; there is fury in the tawny lions and a boundless rage; and the whole race is hateful to me." When he asks the reason, she says, "I will tell you, and you will marvel at the strange result of an old fault. But this unaccustomed toil has tired me now, and look — a poplar invites us with its welcome shade, and the turf gives a couch: I should like to rest here with you" — and she rested — "on the ground," and pressed both the grass and himself, and, her neck reclined on the young man's breast, she spoke thus, mingling kisses among her words:

Perhaps you have heard how a certain girl outran the swiftest men in the contest of speed. That report was no idle tale — she did outrun them; nor could you have said whether she was more distinguished by the merit of her feet or the gift of her beauty. When she asked the oracle about a husband, the god said: "You have no need of a husband, Atalanta; flee the use of a husband. Yet you will not escape it, and, still living, you will lose yourself." Frightened by the god's answer, she lives unwed in the shady woods, and drives off the pressing crowd of suitors with a fierce condition: "I am not to be won unless first beaten in running. Race with me on foot: a wife and a wedding will be given to the swift; death is the price of the slow. Let that be the law of the contest." She was cruel indeed; but — so great is the power of beauty — a rash crowd of suitors came to that condition.

Hippomenes had sat as a spectator of the unfair race, and had said, "Is a wife sought by anyone through such perils?" — and had condemned the young men's excessive love. But when he saw her face, and her body with the clothing laid aside — such as mine is, or such as yours would be, Adonis, if you became a woman — he was stunned, and, raising his hands, said, "Forgive me, you whom I just now blamed! I did not yet know the prize you contended for." In praising her, he catches fire, and hopes that none of the young men may run faster than she, and fears it in his envy. "But why is the chance of this contest left untried by me?" he says. "The god himself helps the daring." While Hippomenes weighs this within himself, the girl flies on winged foot. Though she seemed to the Aonian youth to go no less swiftly than a Scythian arrow, still he marvels the more at her grace, and the very running makes her graceful. The breeze bears back the wings on her swift feet; her hair is tossed over her ivory shoulders, and the ribboned bands below her knees with their bright border; and over her girlish whiteness her body had drawn a blush, just as when a purple awning over a white hall tints it with a matching shade. While the stranger marks this, the last lap is run, and victorious Atalanta is crowned with the festival wreath. The beaten men groan and pay the penalty by the agreement.

Yet, not daunted by the young men's end, Hippomenes stood up in the midst and, fixing his eyes on the girl, said: "Why seek an easy fame by beating the sluggish? Match yourself with me! If fortune makes me the winner, you will not think it shame to be beaten by so great a man; for my father was Megareus of Onchestus, his grandfather Neptune, and I am the great-grandson of the king of the waters — nor is my worth less than my birth. Or if I am beaten, in beating Hippomenes you will have a great and memorable name." As he spoke, the daughter of Schoeneus looked on him with a soft face, and wavered whether she would rather be beaten or win, and said: "What god, an enemy to the beautiful, wants to destroy this youth, and bids him seek this marriage at the risk of his dear life? In my own judgment I am not worth so much. Nor am I moved by his beauty — though I could be moved by that too — but because he is still a boy; it is not himself that moves me, but his age. And is it not that he has courage, and a mind undismayed by death? And that he is counted fourth from the sea's own line? And that he loves me, and thinks marriage with me worth so much that he would die if hard fortune denied me to him? While you may, stranger, go, and leave this bloodstained wedding. A match with me is cruelly dangerous. No woman will be unwilling to marry you, and a wise girl might well desire you. But why do I care for you, when so many have already died? Let him look to it! Let him die, since he was not warned by the slaughter of so many suitors, and is driven to weariness of life. So he will die, then, because he wished to live with me, and suffer an undeserved death, the reward of his love? My victory will not bear the hatred of it. But it is no fault of mine! I wish you would give up — or, since you are mad, I wish you were swifter! But what a girlish look there is in his boyish face! Ah, poor Hippomenes, I wish you had never seen me! You were worthy to live. And if I had been luckier, and the grudging Fates had not denied me marriage, you are the one man I would have shared my bed with." So she said; and, untried and touched by love for the first time, not knowing what she does, she loves, and does not feel that she loves.

And now the people and her father demand the usual race, when Hippomenes, the descendant of Neptune, called on me with anxious voice: "I pray that Cytherea may favor my daring, and aid the love she has given me." A breeze, not unfriendly, carried the tender prayer to me; I was moved, I confess, and there was no long delay in giving aid. There is a field the natives call the Tamasenian, the choicest part of the Cyprian land, which the elders of old made holy to me and ordered added as an endowment to my temples. In the middle of it a tree shines, tawny of leaf, its branches rattling with tawny gold. Coming from there, by chance, I was carrying three golden apples I had picked in my hand; and, seen by none but him, I came to Hippomenes and showed him what use to make of them. The trumpets had given the signal, when each darted forward from the barrier and skimmed the surface of the sand with quick feet: you would think they could graze the sea with dry step, and run over the standing ears of white grain. Shouts and cheers give the youth heart, and the cries of those who call, "Now, now is the time to press on, Hippomenes! Hurry! Now use all your strength! Away with delay — you will win!" It is doubtful whether the hero of Megara or the daughter of Schoeneus was the gladder of these words. O how often, when she could have passed him, she slowed, and unwillingly left behind the face she had long gazed on! A dry, panting breath came from his tired mouth, and the goal was far off; then, at last, the son of Neptune threw one of the three fruits of the tree. The girl was amazed and, in her longing for the shining apple, turned aside from her course and picked up the rolling gold. Hippomenes passes her; the stands ring with applause. She makes up for her delay and the time lost with a swift run, and again leaves the youth behind. Slowed by the throw of a second apple, again she overtakes and passes the young man. The last stretch of the race remained. "Now," he said, "be with me, goddess who gave the gift!" — and, with a young man's throw, he flung the shining gold slantwise to the side of the field, where she would come back the slower. The girl seemed to doubt whether to fetch it; I made her take it up, and, when she had taken it, added weight to the apple, and hindered her both by the burden's heaviness and by the delay of reaching it. And, that my story may not be slower than the race itself — the girl was outrun; the winner led away his prize.

Was I not worthy, Adonis, that he should thank me and bring me the honor of incense? But, ungrateful, he gave me neither thanks nor incense. I was thrown into sudden anger; and, stung at being slighted, and lest I be despised in time to come, I guarded against it by an example, and roused myself against them both. They were passing a temple hidden in shady woods, which famous Echion had once built by vow to the Mother of the Gods; and the long journey urged them to rest there. There an untimely desire to embrace his wife seized Hippomenes, stirred by my power. Near the temple was a dim recess, like a cave, roofed with native pumice, holy by an ancient rite, where the priest had gathered many wooden images of the old gods. This he entered, and defiled the shrine with a forbidden shame. The sacred images turned away their eyes, and the tower-crowned Mother wondered whether to plunge the guilty in the Stygian stream. That seemed too light a penalty; so tawny manes cover their necks, so lately smooth; their fingers curve into claws; their shoulders become forelegs; all their weight passes into their chests; their tails sweep the surface of the sand. Their look has anger in it; instead of words they give growls; instead of chambers they haunt the woods; and, lions dreaded by others, they champ Cybele's bit with tamed jaws. These, my beloved, and with them every kind of beast that turns not its back in flight but its breast to the fight, avoid — lest your courage be the ruin of us both.

She warned him thus, and, yoking her swans, took her way through the air; but his courage stood against her warning. By chance his dogs, following a sure track, roused a boar, and, as it made to leave the wood, the son of Cinyras pierced it with a glancing stroke in the side. At once the fierce boar struck out the blood-stained hunting-spear with its curved snout, and pursued him as he trembled and sought a safe retreat, and buried its whole tusks in his groin, and stretched him dying on the tawny sand. Cytherea, borne in her light chariot through the middle air, had not yet come to Cyprus on her swans' wings; she knew the groan of the dying from afar and turned her white birds that way; and when, from the high sky, she saw him half dead, his body thrashing in its own blood, she leaped down, and tore both her robe and her hair, and beat her breast with unworthy hands, and, reproaching the Fates, said: "Yet all shall not be in your power. The memorial of my grief, Adonis, will last forever; and the yearly re-enactment of your death shall carry out an image of my mourning. But your blood shall be changed into a flower. Was it once allowed you, Persephone, to change a woman's limbs into fragrant mint — and shall the hero, the son of Cinyras, be a grudge against me if he is changed?" So saying, she sprinkled his blood with sweet-smelling nectar; and, touched by it, the blood swelled up, as a clear bubble is wont to rise in tawny mud; and no longer than a full hour's delay had passed when a flower sprang from the blood, its own color, such as the pomegranates bear that hide their seeds beneath a tough rind. Yet the enjoyment of it is short-lived; for the very winds that give it its name beat it down, since it clings poorly and falls by its own lightness.

Book XI

While the Thracian bard was drawing the woods and the hearts of wild beasts and the following rocks with such a song, look — the women of the Ciconians, their raving breasts covered with the skins of beasts, spied Orpheus from a hilltop as he set his voice to the sounded strings. One of them, tossing her hair on the light breeze, cried, "See! see — here is the man who scorns us!" and hurled her spear at the singing mouth of Apollo's bard; wreathed with leaves at the tip, it made a mark without a wound. Another's weapon was a stone, which, thrown, was overcome in the very air by the harmony of his voice and lyre, and lay before his feet like a suppliant for so mad a daring. But the reckless warfare swells, all measure is gone, and frenzied Fury reigns. All their weapons would have been softened by his song — but the huge clamor, the Berecynthian pipe with its blaring horns, the tambourines, the clapping and the Bacchic howling drowned the sound of the lyre; and then at last the stones grew red with the bard's blood, for now he could not be heard.

First the Maenads seized the countless birds still spellbound by his singing voice, and the snakes, and the throng of beasts — the glory of Orpheus's audience; then with bloodied hands they turned on Orpheus, and flocked together as birds do when they see the bird of night wandering by day; and, as when a stag doomed to die in the morning arena is a prey to the dogs in the built-up amphitheater, they set upon the bard and hurled their thyrsi, green with leaves, never made for such use. Some throw clods, some branches torn from trees, some flung flints; and, so their fury should not lack weapons, by chance some oxen were turning the earth with the driven ploughshare, and not far off some brawny farmers were digging the hard fields, winning their harvest with much sweat. Seeing the frantic troop, they flee and leave the tools of their work: hoes and heavy rakes and long mattocks lie scattered through the empty fields. Snatching these, and tearing apart the oxen with their threatening horns, the women rush back to the bard's undoing; and, as he stretched out his hands and for the first time spoke words that did nothing, moving them not at all with his voice, the sacrilegious women killed him — and through that mouth, O Jupiter, heard by rocks and understood by the senses of wild beasts, his breath went out and passed away on the winds.

The mournful birds wept for you, Orpheus, the throng of beasts, the hard flints, the woods that had so often followed your song; the tree, shedding its leaves, mourned you, shorn of its hair. They say the rivers too swelled with their own tears, and the Naiads and Dryads wore dark mourning and loosened hair. His limbs lie in scattered places. His head and lyre you receive, Hebrus; and — a marvel — as they float down the middle of the stream, the lyre laments some sad thing, the lifeless tongue murmurs something sad, and the banks answer sadly. And now, borne out to sea, they leave their native river and reach the shore of Methymnaean Lesbos. Here a fierce serpent went for the head cast up on the foreign sand and the hair dripping with dew; but at last Phoebus came, and drove it off as it made to sting, and froze the serpent's open jaws to stone, hardening the gaping mouth just as it was.

His ghost sinks under the earth, and knows again all the places he had seen before; and, seeking Eurydice through the fields of the blessed, he found her and folded her in his eager arms. Here now they walk side by side; now he follows as she goes before, now goes ahead and leads — and now, in safety, Orpheus looks back at his Eurydice.

Yet Bacchus did not let this wickedness go unpunished. Grieving for the lost bard of his rites, he at once bound down in the woods, by a twisting root, all the Edonian women who had seen the crime. He drew out the toes of each one's feet, just as she had pursued him, and drove their points into the solid earth; and, as a bird whose leg has caught in a snare the cunning fowler hid, when it feels itself held, beats its wings and, fluttering, tightens the noose by its struggles, so each of them, as she stuck fast, fixed in the ground, tried in vain to flee in her terror; but the tough root held her and checked her as she strained. And while she looks for where her toes are, where her feet, where her nails, she sees wood climbing up her shapely legs; and, trying to strike her thigh with a grieving hand, she struck oak — her breast, too, becomes oak, her shoulders are oak; and you would think her outstretched arms real branches, and thinking it you would not be wrong.

And this is not enough for Bacchus. He resolves to leave the country itself, and with a better troupe seeks the vineyards of his own Tmolus, and Pactolus — though it was not golden then, nor to be envied for its precious sand. His usual band, the Satyrs and Bacchants, throng about him, but Silenus is missing: the Phrygian country folk had taken him, staggering with age and wine, and, bound with garlands, led him to their king Midas, to whom, with the Athenian Eumolpus, Thracian Orpheus had once handed the mysteries of Bacchus. When he knew this partner and companion of the rites, Midas kept a glad festival for the coming of his guest, twice five days and as many nights joined in a row. And now the eleventh Morning Star had driven off the lofty host of the stars, when the king came rejoicing into the Lydian fields and gave Silenus back to his young foster-son. The god, glad at the return of his foster-father, gave Midas the choice of a wish — pleasing, but useless, as it turned out. Midas, doomed to make a foolish use of the gift, said, "Grant that whatever I touch with my body turn to yellow gold." Liber granted his wish and gave the harmful gift, and was sorry he had not asked for something better.

The Berecynthian hero goes off glad, rejoicing in his own ruin, and tests the truth of the promise by touching things one by one. Scarcely believing himself, he pulled a green twig from a low branch of a holm-oak: the twig turned to gold. He lifts a stone from the ground: the stone too paled with gold. He touched a clod: at the mighty touch the clod became a mass of gold. He plucked dry ears of corn: the harvest was golden. He holds an apple picked from a tree: you would think the Hesperides had given it. If he laid his fingers on the tall doorposts, the posts seemed to shine; even when he washed his hands in the clear water, the water flowing from his hands could have fooled Danaë. He can scarcely hold his own hopes in his mind, imagining all things gold. As he rejoices, his servants set before him a table heaped with food, and not lacking roasted grain; but then, whether he touched the gifts of Ceres with his hand, the gifts of Ceres went stiff, or whether he made to tear the food with hungry teeth, a yellow plate pressed the food as his teeth came down; and he mixed the giver of the gift, Bacchus's wine, with pure water — you could see molten gold flowing through his jaws.

Stunned by the strangeness of his misfortune, rich and wretched at once, he longs to flee his wealth, and hates what he lately prayed for. No plenty relieves his hunger; dry thirst burns his throat; and, rightly, he is tortured by the hated gold. Lifting his hands and shining arms to heaven, he cried, "Forgive me, father Lenaeus! I have sinned; but pity me, I pray, and snatch me from this fair-seeming ruin!" The gods' power is gentle. Bacchus, when Midas confessed his fault, restored him, and undid the gift given by their bargain: "So that you may not stay coated with the gold you so unluckily wished, go," he said, "to the river that neighbors great Sardis, and, climbing the ridge, make your way against the falling waters until you come to the stream's source; and there, where it bubbles up most fully, plunge your head under the foaming spring, and your body with it, and wash away your crime at once." The king went under the water as bidden: the golden power tinged the river and passed from the human body into the stream; and even now the fields, taking the seed of that old vein, are hard, pale with gold from their soaked clods.

Hating riches, Midas dwelt in the woods and fields, following Pan, who always lives in the mountain caves; but his dull wit stayed, and, as before, the folly of his stupid mind was fated to hurt its owner again. For lofty Tmolus, looking out wide over the sea, rises steep in its high ascent, and, stretching down on either side, is bounded on one hand by Sardis, on the other by little Hypaepae. While Pan was there boasting of his tunes to the tender nymphs, and warbling a slight song on his waxed reeds, daring to scorn Apollo's playing beside his own, he came to an unequal contest under the judgment of Tmolus. The old judge sat on his own mountain and freed his ears of the crowding trees; only oak wreathes his dark-blue hair, and acorns hang about his hollow temples. Looking at the god of the flocks, he said, "There is no delay in your judge." Pan sounds his rustic reeds and charms Midas with his rough song — for Midas happened to be there. After him, the sacred Tmolus turned his face to the face of Phoebus; his own woods followed his face. Apollo, his golden head wreathed with Parnassian laurel, sweeps the ground with a robe soaked in Tyrian purple, holds up in his left hand his lyre inlaid with gems and Indian ivory, and in the other holds the plectrum; his very posture was an artist's. Then he plucks the strings with a skilled thumb, and, caught by their sweetness, Tmolus bids Pan lower his reeds before the lyre.

The judgment and verdict of the sacred mountain pleased them all — yet it is faulted, and called unjust, by the voice of Midas alone. The Delian god does not let those stupid ears keep their human shape, but draws them out to length, fills them with gray hairs, makes them unsteady at the base, and gives them the power to move. The rest is a man's; in one part alone is he punished, and he wears the ears of a slow-moving ass. He tries indeed to hide them, and to veil his temples, loaded with the ugly shame, in a purple turban. But a servant who used to cut his long hair with the shears saw it; and though he dared not tell the disgrace he had seen, longing to bring it into the open yet unable to keep it in, he went off, dug up the ground, and, in a low voice, whispered into the dug earth what kind of ears he had seen on his master; then he buried this witness of his voice under the earth thrown back, and, the trench covered, stole away in silence. There a thick grove of quivering reeds began to rise; and, as soon as it ripened at the full year, it betrayed the planter: stirred by the gentle south wind, it repeated the buried words and gave away its master's ears.

Avenged, the son of Latona left Tmolus and, borne through the clear air, came to rest on the plains of Laomedon, this side of the narrow sea of Helle. On the right of Sigeum, on the left of deep Rhoeteum, there is an old altar sacred to the Thunderer who hears all voices. From there Apollo sees Laomedon just then building the walls of rising Troy, and that the great work is growing with hard toil and demanding no small resources; and so, with the trident-bearing father of the swelling deep, he took mortal shape, and for the Phrygian king they built the walls, a sum of gold agreed on for the defenses. The work stood done; the king denies the reward, and, to crown his treachery, adds perjury with false words.
