"You will not do this unpunished," said the king of the sea, and drove all his waters against the shores of greedy Troy, and turned the land into the shape of a sea, and carried off the farmers' wealth, and buried the fields in waves. Nor is this penalty enough: the king's daughter too is demanded for a sea-monster. Chained to the hard rocks, she is freed by Hercules, who demands the promised reward, the horses agreed on; and, the wage of so great a work denied, he takes the twice-perjured walls of conquered Troy. Nor did Telamon, a sharer in the war, go off without honor; he won Hesione, given to him.

For Peleus was made famous by a goddess for his wife, no prouder of his grandfather's name than of his father-in-law's — since it fell to more than one man to be Jove's grandson, but to one man alone to have a goddess for a wife. For old Proteus had said to Thetis: "Goddess of the wave, conceive: you will be the mother of a youth who, in his strong years, will surpass his father's deeds and be called greater than he." So, lest the world hold anything greater than Jove, though he had felt no gentle fire in his breast, Jupiter fled the marriage of sea-born Thetis, and bade his grandson, the son of Aeacus, take his place in the wish, and go to the embrace of the sea-maiden.

There is a bay of Thessaly curved into a bending arch; its arms run out; there, were the water deeper, would be a harbor, but the sea just covers the surface of the sand; the shore is firm, keeping no footprint, delaying no step, hidden by no weed; a myrtle grove is near, set with two-colored berries. In the middle is a cave, whether made by nature or art is doubtful — more by art; to which you, Thetis, used often to come, riding naked on your bridled dolphin. There Peleus seized you as you lay bound in sleep; and since, tried by prayers, you resisted, he made ready force, clasping your neck with both arms. And had you not fled, as often, to your familiar arts of changing shape, he would have won his daring attempt: but now you were a bird — yet a bird he held; now a heavy tree — Peleus clung to the tree; your third shape was a spotted tigress, and, frightened by that, the son of Aeacus loosed his arms from your body. Then, pouring wine on the waters, he worshiped the sea-gods with the entrails of sheep and the smoke of incense, until the Carpathian seer said from the middle of the flood: "Son of Aeacus, you will win the marriage you seek. Only, when she rests asleep in the cool cave, bind her unawares with cords and holding bonds. And let her not deceive you by faking a hundred shapes, but hold her, whatever she is, until she takes back the form she had before." Proteus said this, and hid his face in the sea, and let his own waves in over his last words.

Titan was sinking, and, with his chariot-pole tilted down, was taking the western sea, when the beautiful Nereid, leaving the deep, entered her wonted resting-place. Scarcely had Peleus well seized the maiden's limbs when she began her shapes anew, until she felt her limbs held and her arms stretched apart. Then at last she sighed and said, "You do not conquer without a god's help" — and showed herself as Thetis. The hero embraced her thus revealed, won his wish, and filled her with great Achilles.

Happy Peleus in his son, happy in his wife, and one to whom all blessings had fallen, if you take away the guilt of murdered Phocus. The land of Trachis received him, guilty of his brother's blood and driven from his native home. Here Ceyx, born of Lucifer and bearing his father's brightness in his face, held sway without force or bloodshed — Ceyx, who at that time, sad and unlike himself, mourned a brother taken from him. When the son of Aeacus, worn with trouble and the long road, came there and entered the city with a few companions, leaving the flocks and herds he brought not far from the walls in a shady valley, and when he first had the chance to approach the king, he held out the tokens of peace in a suppliant hand, and told who he was and from whom sprung — hiding only his crime, and lying about the cause of his flight; and he begs help, in the city or the country. The Trachinian answered him with gentle lips: "Our bounties are open even to the middle sort, Peleus, and I hold no unwelcoming realm. To this you add strong weights — a famous name, and Jove for a grandfather. Do not waste your time in asking! All you ask you shall have; and call these things, whatever you see, in part your own — I wish you saw them in better case!" And he wept. Peleus and his companions ask what cause moves such grief; and he tells them:

Perhaps you think this bird, which lives by prey and terrifies all birds, always had wings. He was a man — and so unshaken was his spirit that even then he was fierce, savage in war and ready for violence — Daedalion by name. We were born of the same father, the one who calls forth Aurora and leaves the sky last of all. Peace was my care, the keeping of peace and my marriage; my brother loved cruel wars. His valor subdued kings and nations — that valor which now, changed, chases the doves of Thisbe. He had a daughter, Chione, who, most richly dowered with beauty, had a thousand suitors when she came of age at twice seven years. By chance Phoebus and the son of Maia, returning, the one from his Delphi, the other from the height of Cyllene, saw her at the same moment and at the same moment caught fire. Apollo puts off his hope of enjoyment to the hours of night; the other brooks no delay, and touches the girl's face with his sleep-bringing wand: at the mighty touch she lies still and suffers the god's force. Night had strewn the sky with stars; Phoebus, taking an old woman's shape, took the joys already snatched. When her ripe womb had filled its time, from the stock of the wing-footed god was born a crafty child, Autolycus, clever at every theft, who used to make white of black and black of white, no degenerate of his father's art; and from Phoebus (for she bore twins) was born Philammon, famous for tuneful song and the lyre.

But what did it profit her to have borne two children, and pleased two gods, and sprung from a brave father with the Thunderer for ancestor? Is glory a harm to many, too? To her, at least, it was: she dared to set herself above Diana and faulted the goddess's beauty. Fierce anger was roused in the goddess: "Then we will please you by deeds," she said; and without delay she bent her bow, sent an arrow from the string, and pierced with the reed the tongue that deserved it. The tongue fell silent; no voice, no attempted words followed; and, as she tried to speak, her life left her with her blood. Wretched, I held her then, and bore my brother's grief in my heart, and spoke comfort to my loving brother — which the father took no otherwise than rocks take the murmurs of the sea, and bitterly bewailed his daughter torn away. When he saw her burning, four times he made to rush into the midst of the pyre; four times driven back, he gave his swift limbs to flight and, like an ox carrying the stings of hornets on its galled neck, rushed where there was no path. Already he seemed to me to run more than a man, and you would think his feet had taken wings. So he outran all, and, made swift by the longing for death, gained the heights of Parnassus. Apollo, pitying him, when Daedalion had thrown himself from the high rock, made him a bird and held him hovering on sudden wings, and gave him a hooked beak, curved claws on his talons, his old courage, and strength greater than his body; and now, a hawk, kind enough to none, he rages against all birds, and, grieving himself, becomes a cause of grief to others.

While the son of Lucifer was telling these wonders of his brother, panting with haste, Onetor the Phocian, keeper of his herds, ran up. "Peleus, Peleus! I come to you with news of a great disaster," he said. Peleus bids him tell whatever it is; and the Trachinian too hangs in fear at the trembling mouth. The man tells it: "I had driven the weary bullocks to the winding shore, when the Sun at his highest in mid-course could look back on as much of it as he saw still to come; and part of the oxen had bent their knees on the yellow sand and, lying, watched the plains of wide water, part wandered slowly here and there, and others swam, standing above the waves with lofty necks. There is a temple near the sea, bright with neither marble nor gold, but made of thick beams and shaded by an ancient grove: the Nereids and Nereus hold it — a sailor drying his nets on the shore told us these were its gods. Next to it is a marsh, ringed with thick willows, which the water of the standing sea has made a swamp; from there, roaring with a heavy crash, a huge beast — a wolf — terrifies the neighboring places, and comes out of the marsh thicket, its lightning jaws smeared with foam and clotted blood, its eyes suffused with red flame. Though it rages with both fury and hunger, it is fiercer with fury; for it does not care to end its fast and its dread hunger with the slaughter of oxen, but wounds the whole herd and lays them all low like an enemy. Some of us too, defending them, were killed by the deadly bite; the shore and the near waves are red with blood, and the fens are filled with lowing. But delay is ruinous, and the case does not allow us to waver: while something is still left, let us all band together and take up arms — arms, I say — and bear our weapons in one body!"

So spoke the countryman. But the loss did not move Peleus; remembering his crime, he judges that the bereaved Nereid had sent these ills as an offering to dead Phocus. The king of Oeta bids his men put on their armor and take up stout weapons; and he was making ready to go with them, when Alcyone his wife, roused by the uproar, rushed out, her hair not yet fully done, and threw even the done part into disorder, and, flung on her husband's neck, begged him with words and tears to send help without himself, and so save two lives in one. The son of Aeacus said to her: "Lay aside your lovely, loyal fears, queen! Your kindness is thanks enough. It does not please me to move arms against this new monster; the god of the sea must be worshiped." There was a high tower, a beacon on its top, a welcome sight to weary ships. They climb up there and see with groans the bulls strewn on the shore and the cruel ravager with bloody mouth, its long hair fouled with gore. Then Peleus, stretching his hands toward the open sea, prays the sea-blue Psamathe to end her wrath and bring aid; but she is not bent by the words of the pleading son of Aeacus. Thetis, interceding for her husband, won that favor. Still the wolf holds on, not called back from the fierce slaughter, keen with the sweetness of blood, until she changed it to marble as it clung to the neck of a mangled heifer. The body kept everything but its color; the stone's color shows it is no longer a wolf, and need no longer be feared. Yet the Fates do not let the exiled Peleus settle in that land; the wandering outcast goes to the Magnetes, and there receives from Thessalian Acastus his cleansing of the murder.

Meanwhile Ceyx, his heart troubled by his brother's fate and by the wonders that had followed it, prepared to go to the god of Claros, to consult the sacred oracle, that comfort of mortals — for the profane Phorbas and his Phlegyans had made the oracle at Delphi impossible to reach. But first, most faithful Alcyone, he told you his plan; and at once a chill went to the marrow of your bones, a pallor much like boxwood came over your face, and your cheeks were wet with gushing tears. Three times she tried to speak, three times she drenched her face with tears; and, sobs breaking her loving complaints, she said:

"What fault of mine, dearest, has changed your mind? Where is the care for me that used to come first? Can you now be away without worry, your Alcyone left behind? Now a long journey pleases you? Now am I dearer to you at a distance? But I suppose your road is by land, and I shall only grieve, not fear as well, and my worry will be free of dread. It is the sea that frightens me, the grim face of the ocean. Lately I saw broken planks on the shore, and often I have read names on tombs with no body buried there. And do not let a false confidence touch your mind because the grandson of Hippotas is your father-in-law, who shuts the strong winds in prison and calms the seas when he wishes. Once the winds are let loose and have taken the deep, nothing is forbidden them; every land and every sea is disregarded; they harass even the clouds of heaven and strike out red fires by their wild collisions. The more I know them — and I do know them, for as a child I often saw them in my father's house — the more I think they must be feared. But if your resolve can be bent by no prayers of mine, dear husband, and you are all too set on going, then take me too! Surely we will be tossed together, and I shall fear nothing but what I suffer; together we will bear whatever comes, together be carried over the wide seas."

The starry husband is moved by such words and tears of the daughter of Aeolus — for the fire of love is no less in him. But he will neither give up the voyage he has proposed nor let Alcyone share the danger, and he answers many things to soothe her fearful heart. Yet she does not for that approve his reasons. He added this one comfort, which alone bent his loving wife: "Every delay will be long to me; but I swear to you by my father's fire, if only the Fates let me back, that I will return before the moon fills her circle twice." When by these promises a hope of his return had been raised, at once he ordered a ship drawn from the docks, launched, and fitted with its gear. Seeing it, Alcyone shuddered again, as if foreseeing the future, and shed flowing tears, and gave him embraces, and at last, in her misery, said "Farewell" with a sad voice, and sank down with her whole body.

But the young men, while Ceyx still sought excuses for delay, draw the oars in double rows to their hardy chests and cleave the sea with even strokes. She lifted her wet eyes and saw her husband standing on the curved stern, waving his hand to give her the first signals, and she returned them. When the land had drawn far off, and her eyes could no longer make out his face, still, while she could, she followed the fleeing ship with her gaze; and when that too, borne away, could not be seen for distance, still she watched the sails floating at the masthead; and when she saw the sails no more, she sought her empty bed and laid herself on the couch. The bed and the place renew Alcyone's tears, and remind her what part of herself is gone.

They had left the harbor, and the breeze stirred the ropes; the sailor turns the hanging oars to the sides, sets the yards at the masthead, and lets down the whole canvas from the mast, catching the coming winds. Less than half, or at most half, the sea had been cut by the ship, and each land was far off, when, toward night, the sea began to whiten with swelling waves and the headlong east wind to blow more strongly. "Lower the high yards at once," the master cries, "and reef the whole sail to the spars." He orders, but the head-on storm blocks the orders, and the roar of the sea lets no voice be heard. Yet of their own accord some hurry to draw in the oars, some to brace the sides, some to deny the sails to the winds; this one bails the flood and pours the sea back into the sea, that one hauls the yards down. While these things are done without order, the harsh storm grows, and on every side the fierce winds wage war and churn the raging waters. The master himself is afraid, and himself confesses he does not know how they stand, nor what to order or forbid: so great is the mass of the disaster, so much stronger than any skill. The men roar with shouts, the ropes with creaking, wave crashes on heavy wave, the sky with thunder. The sea rises in billows and seems to reach the heavens and to sprinkle the low-hung clouds with spray; now, when it stirs the yellow sand from the bottom, it is the color of it; now it is blacker than the Stygian wave; sometimes it lies flat, white with hissing foam. The Trachinian ship, too, is driven by these turns: now aloft as if from a mountaintop it seems to look down into the valleys and the depths of Acheron; now, when the curving sea has hemmed it low, it seems to look up at the sky above from an infernal gulf. Struck on its side by the wave, it often gives a huge crash, and sounds no lighter beaten than an iron ram or a catapult shaking a battered tower. And as fierce lions, gathering strength for the charge, rush chest-on against the weapons and the leveled spears, so the water, when it had risen with the risen winds, came on against the ship's rigging and towered far above it. And now the joints give way; stripped of their wax coating, the seams gape and offer a way to the deadly waters.

Look — heavy showers fall from the loosened clouds; you would think the whole sky was coming down into the sea, and the swollen sea climbing up into the tracts of heaven. The sails are soaked with rain, and the sea's waters mix with the sky's; the firmament is without its fires, and blind night is crushed by its own darkness and the storm's. Yet the lightnings scatter this and give a flashing light: the rains catch fire with the flames of the thunderbolt. Now the flood leaps inside the hollow frame of the ship; and as a soldier, above all the rest of the number, when he has often sprung at the walls of a defended city, at last wins his hope and, fired with love of glory, though one among a thousand, mounts the wall alone — so, when the waves had struck the high sides nine times, the onset of a tenth wave, rising vaster, rushes down and does not leave off battering the weary ship until it drops, as it were, within the walls of a captured vessel. So part of the sea was still trying to break into the ship, part was inside it; and all tremble no less than a city trembles when some are undermining the wall outside and others hold it within. Skill fails, courage sinks, and as many deaths seem to rush and break in as there are waves that come. One cannot hold his tears; one is stunned; one calls those happy whom burial awaits; one worships the gods with prayers, and, lifting his hands in vain to the sky he cannot see, begs for help. To one his brother and parent come to mind; to another his home and his children and whatever he has left behind.

The thought of Alcyone moves Ceyx; on Ceyx's lips is nothing but Alcyone, and, though he longs for her alone, he is glad she is away. Gladly, too, he would look back to his native shore and turn his last gaze toward his home — but he does not know where it is: the sea boils in so great a whirl, and, under the shadow of the pitch-black clouds, all the sky is hidden, and the face of night is doubled. The mast is broken by the storm's assault; the helm too is broken; and the wave, proud over its spoils and standing like a conqueror, looks down on the waves below. Then, no less heavily than if a god should hurl Athos or Pindus, torn from their roots, into the open sea, it plunges down, and with weight and blow together sinks the ship to the bottom. With her a great part of the crew, pressed down in the heavy gulf, never rising again to the air, met their fate; others cling to fragments and broken pieces of the ship. Ceyx himself grips a plank of the wreck with the hand that used to hold the scepter, and in vain, alas, calls on his father and his father-in-law. But most on the swimmer's lips is his wife Alcyone: her he remembers and names; he prays the waves to carry his body before her eyes, that dead he may be buried by the hands of friends. As he swims, whenever the billows let him open his mouth, he names the absent Alcyone and murmurs it into the very waves. And now a black arch of water broke over the middle of the waves and buried his sinking head under the bursting wave. The Morning Star was dark that night, past knowing; and, since he could not leave the sky, he veiled his face in thick clouds.

Meanwhile the daughter of Aeolus, knowing nothing of such great ills, counts the nights, and now hurries to ready the clothes he will put on, now those she herself will wear when he comes, and promises herself a return that will never be. She was piously offering incense to all the gods, but before all she tended the temple of Juno and came to the altars for a husband who no longer was, praying that he be safe and return and set no woman above her — and of all her prayers, this one alone could be granted her. But the goddess could no longer bear to be entreated for a dead man; and, to keep the death-polluted hands from her altars, she said: "Iris, most faithful messenger of my voice, go quickly to the sleep-bringing hall of Sleep, and bid him send Alcyone a dream in the shape of dead Ceyx, telling her true disaster." She spoke. Iris puts on her thousand-colored robe and, marking the sky with her arched curve, seeks the house of the king hidden beneath its cloud.

Near the Cimmerians there is a cave in a long recess, a hollow mountain, the home and inmost chamber of lazy Sleep, where the sun can never come, rising or in mid-course or setting; fogs mixed with darkness breathe up from the ground, and a twilight of doubtful light. No watchful cock there calls forth the dawn with the crowing of his crested head; no anxious dogs break the silence, nor geese keener than dogs; no wild beast, no cattle, no boughs stirred by a breeze, no outbursts of the human tongue make a sound. Mute Quiet dwells there; yet from the bottom of the rock runs a stream, the water of Lethe, whose wave, gliding with a murmur over the rattling pebbles, invites sleep. Before the doors of the cave poppies bloom in plenty, and countless herbs, from whose milk dewy Night gathers sleep and scatters it over the darkened earth. There is no door in the whole house, to grind on a turning hinge; no porter at the threshold. But in the middle is a couch, raised high on black ebony, feathered, dark-colored, hidden by a dark coverlet, where the god himself lies, his limbs loosed in languor. Around him, everywhere, lie empty Dreams mimicking many shapes, as many as the harvest bears ears of grain, the wood leaves, the shore cast-up sands.

As soon as the maiden had entered there and pushed aside with her hands the dreams that blocked her way, the sacred house shone with the splendor of her robe; and the god, heavy with slow drowsiness, barely lifting his eyes and again and again sinking back, his nodding chin striking the top of his breast, at last shook himself free of himself, and, raised on his elbow, asked why she had come — for he knew her. And she said: "Sleep, rest of all things, gentlest of the gods, peace of the mind, from whom care flees, who soothe the bodies worn with hard service and refit them for toil — bid a Dream that matches real shapes in its likeness go to Alcyone in Herculean Trachis, in the form of the king, and take on the look of a shipwrecked man. Juno commands it." Her task done, Iris left; she could no longer bear the force of the drowsiness, and, feeling sleep creep into her limbs, fled and went back along the arch by which she had just come.

But the father, out of the crowd of his thousand sons, wakes Morpheus, a craftsman and mimic of shapes: no one more cleverly renders the walk, the face, and the sound of speech; he adds the dress too, and the words each most often uses. But he imitates men only; another becomes a wild beast, a bird, a long-bodied serpent — him the gods call Icelos, the crowd of mortals Phobetor. There is a third of a different art, Phantasos: he changes himself, deceiving, into earth and stone and water and a tree and all things that lack breath. These show their faces by night to kings and generals; others roam among the peoples and the commons. These the old god passes by, and chooses from all his brothers Morpheus alone to carry out the commands of the daughter of Thaumas; and again, loosed in soft drowsiness, he dropped his head and sank back into the high couch.

Morpheus flies through the dark on noiseless wings and, in a short space of time, reaches the Thessalian city; and, laying the wings from his body, he takes the shape of Ceyx, and in that shape, wan, like a bloodless man, without any clothing, he stood before the bed of the wretched wife. The hero's beard seems wet, and heavy water to run from his dripping hair. Then, leaning on the bed, tears pouring down his face, he said: "Do you know your Ceyx, most wretched wife — or is my face so changed by death? Look: you will know me, and instead of your husband you will find your husband's ghost. Your prayers, Alcyone, brought me no help; I am dead. Do not, deceived, promise yourself my return. The cloudy south wind caught my ship in the Aegean sea and, tossing it with its mighty blast, broke it apart; and the waves filled my mouth as it cried your name in vain. It is no doubtful messenger who tells you this, no vague rumor you hear it by: I myself, shipwrecked, in person, tell you my fate. Rise, come, weep, put on mourning, and do not send me unlamented down to empty Tartarus." To these words Morpheus added a voice she would believe her husband's — he even seemed to shed real tears — and his hands had the gestures of Ceyx.

Alcyone groaned, weeping, and moved her arms in her sleep, and, reaching for his body, grasped the air, and cried out, "Stay! Where do you rush? We will go together." Shaken by her own voice and the sight of her husband, she threw off sleep, and first looked around to see if he who had just been seen was there; for the servants, roused by her voice, had brought in a light. When she found him nowhere, she struck her face with her hands, tore the clothes from her breast and beat the breast itself, and did not care to loose her hair — she tore it; and to her nurse, asking the cause of her grief, she said: "There is no Alcyone, none! She is dead, with her Ceyx. Away with words of comfort! He has died by shipwreck. I saw him, I knew him, and, as he left, I stretched out my hands, longing to hold him back. It was a ghost — but even so a clear ghost, the true ghost of my husband. He did not, if you ask, have his wonted look, nor shine with the face he had before; pale and naked and with dripping hair I saw him, poor man. Here, on this very spot, the wretched one stood" — and she looks for any footprints left. "This, this is what I feared in my foreboding mind, and I begged you not to flee me and follow the winds. But surely I could wish, since you went to die, that you had taken me too: it would have been much better for me to go with you; for then I would have spent no part of my life apart from you, nor would death have divided us. Now, absent, I have died; absent, I too am tossed on the waves, and the sea holds me without me. My heart would be crueler than the sea itself if I strove to draw out my life longer, and fought to outlive so great a grief. But I will not fight, nor leave you, poor one; at least I will come now as your companion; and if not an urn, then an inscription in the tomb shall join us; if I do not touch your bones with my bones, still I will touch your name with my name." Grief forbids more, and wailing breaks in on every word, and groans are dragged from her stricken heart.

It was morning: she goes out from the house to the shore and, in her grief, seeks the place from which she had watched him go, and says, "Here he loosed the cables; on this shore he kissed me as he left" — and while she recalls the acts marked by places, and looks out on the sea, she sees, far off on the flowing wave, something like a body; and at first it was doubtful what it was. When the water had brought it a little nearer, and, though still far, it was plainly a corpse, not knowing who it might be — because it was shipwrecked, she was moved by the omen, and, as if giving a tear to a stranger, said, "Alas, poor man, whoever you are, and if you have a wife!" Driven by the waves, the body comes nearer; and the more she looks at it, the less and less she is mistress of her mind; and now, brought close to the land, now that she can make it out, she sees: it is her husband. "It is he!" she cries, and at once tears her face, her hair, her robe, and, stretching her trembling hands to Ceyx, says, "Is it so, dearest husband, is it so, poor man, that you come back to me?"

There is a breakwater, made by human hands, next to the waves, which breaks the sea's first fury and blunts the onset of the waters. Onto this she leaped — and it was a marvel she could: she was flying, and, beating the light air with newly-formed wings, a wretched bird, she skimmed the surface of the water; and, as she flew, her mouth, chattering with its slender bill, gave a sound like one in sorrow, full of complaint. But when she touched the mute, bloodless body, she folded the beloved limbs in her new wings and gave him cold kisses in vain with her hard bill. Whether Ceyx felt this, or seemed to lift his face by the motion of the wave, the people were in doubt; but he had felt it. And at last, by the pity of the gods, both are changed into birds; subject to the same fate, their love stayed even then, and the marriage bond was not dissolved in the birds: they mate and become parents, and for seven calm days in the winter Alcyone broods on her nest floating on the sea. Then the passage of the deep is safe; Aeolus keeps the winds in and holds them from going out, and grants a smooth sea to his grandchildren.

Some old man watched them flying together over the wide sea and praised their love kept to the end of their lives. One near him — or the same, if chance had it so — said, "This one too, which you see cutting the sea with its legs drawn up," pointing at a diver-bird with its broad throat, "was of royal birth. And if you want to come down to him in an unbroken line, his ancestors are Ilus and Assaracus and Ganymede snatched by Jupiter, and old Laomedon, and Priam, whose lot was Troy's last days; this one was Hector's brother, and, had he not met a strange fate in his early youth, perhaps he would have a name no less than Hector's — though the daughter of Dymas bore that one, while Aesacus, they say, was born in secret to Alexirhoë, the daughter of two-horned Granicus, under shady Ida. He hated cities, and, far from the shining court, kept to the lonely mountains and the humble fields, and went but rarely among the crowds of Ilium. Yet his heart was not rustic, nor proof against love: often chased through all the woods, he sees Hesperia, the daughter of Cebren, on her father's bank, drying her hair, tossed over her shoulders, in the sun. The nymph, seen, takes flight, like a frightened hind from a tawny wolf, or a river-duck caught far from her wonted pool by a hawk. The Trojan hero pursues her, and, swift with love, presses the swift girl swift with fear. Look — a snake, hidden in the grass, grazed her fleeing foot with its barbed tooth and left its venom in her body; with her flight her life was cut short. Frantic, he embraces her lifeless, and cries, 'I grieve, I grieve that I ever chased you! But I did not fear this, nor was winning worth so much. We two have destroyed you, poor girl: the wound came from the snake, but the cause from me. I would be more guilty than it, unless I send you, by my death, a comfort for your death.' He spoke, and from a rock the hoarse waves had eaten away he flung himself into the sea. Tethys, pitying him as he fell, caught him softly, and covered the swimmer with feathers, and the power to gain the death he sought was not given. The lover is angry, forced to live against his will, and thwarted when his spirit longed to leave its wretched home; and, as he had taken new wings on his shoulders, he flies up and again throws his body over the sea: the feathers break the fall. Aesacus rages, and headlong plunges into the deep, and endlessly tries the way to death. Love made him lean: the spaces between the joints of his legs are long, his neck stays long, his head is far from his body. He loves the sea, and keeps his name because he plunges into it."

Book XII

His father Priam mourned Aesacus, not knowing that, having taken wings, he still lived; and Hector, with his brothers, made empty offerings at a tomb that bore his name. The presence of Paris was missing from that mournful rite — Paris, who soon after brought a long war home to his country along with a stolen wife; and a thousand sworn ships followed him, and with them the whole Greek nation. Nor would vengeance have been delayed, had not the raging winds made the seas impassable, and the Boeotian land held the ships, ready to sail, in fishy Aulis. Here, when they had prepared a sacrifice to Jupiter in their fathers' way, as the old altar glowed with the kindled fire, the Greeks saw a blue serpent creep up a plane tree that stood near the sacrifice they had begun. On the top of the tree was a nest of twice-four birds; the serpent seized them all, and the mother as she fluttered around her loss, and buried them in its greedy maw. All stood amazed. But Calchas, the son of Thestor, a seer foreseeing the truth, said, "Rejoice, Greeks — we will conquer! Troy will fall, but the length of our toil will be long"; and he assigned the nine birds to the years of the war. The serpent, just as it was, coiled around the green branches, becomes stone, and keeps the stone in the shape of a serpent.

Nereus stayed rough in the Ionian waves and did not drive the sails on; and there are some who think Neptune spared Troy, because he had made the city's walls. But not so the son of Thestor: for he neither failed to know, nor kept silent, that the wrath of the virgin goddess must be soothed by a virgin's blood. When the public cause had beaten natural love, and the king the father, and Iphigenia, ready to give her chaste blood, stood before the altar while the priests wept, the goddess was appeased, cast a mist before their eyes, and — so it is said — amid the rite and the crowd and the voices of the praying, changed the Mycenaean girl for a substituted hind. So, when Diana had been soothed by a death more fitting, and at the same moment the wrath of Phoebe and of the sea passed, the thousand ships took the winds astern and, after much hardship, reached the Phrygian shore.

There is a place in the middle of the world, between land and sea and the tracts of heaven, the meeting-point of the threefold universe; from it is seen whatever is anywhere, however far off, and every voice pierces the hollow ears. Fame holds it, and chose herself a house on the top of a tower, adding countless entrances and a thousand openings, and closing the doorways with no gates. Night and day it stands open; it is all of sounding bronze, all humming, echoing the voice and repeating what it hears. Within there is no rest, no silence anywhere; yet there is no shout, but the murmur of a low voice, such as comes from the sea's waves if you listen far off, or the sound the end of the thunder makes when Jupiter has clashed the black clouds. A crowd fills the hall; the fickle rabble come and go, and a thousand rumors, false mixed with true, wander up and down and roll about their muddled words. Some of these fill idle ears with talk, some carry off elsewhere what they were told; the measure of the fiction grows, and each new teller adds something to what he heard. There is Credulity, there rash Error, and empty Joy, and startled Fears, and sudden Sedition, and Whispers of doubtful source. Fame herself sees whatever happens in heaven and sea and earth, and searches the whole world.

She had made it known that Greek ships were coming with valiant troops; nor does the enemy appear in arms unlooked-for. The Trojans block the landing and guard the shore; and you, Protesilaus, are the first, by fate's decree, to fall by Hector's spear; and the battles now joined, and the brave spirits of the Trojans, and Hector, known then for the first time, cost the Greeks dear. Nor did the Phrygians learn at small cost of blood what the Greek right hand could do; and now the shores of Sigeum were red, now Cycnus, the son of Neptune, had given a thousand men to death, now Achilles pressed on in his chariot, leveling whole ranks with the stroke of his Pelian spear; and, seeking through the lines either Cycnus or Hector, he meets Cycnus — Hector was put off to the tenth year. Then, urging on his horses, their white necks pressed under the yoke, he steered his chariot at the enemy and, shaking his quivering spear in his arm, said, "Whoever you are, young man, take this comfort in your death: that you were killed by Haemonian Achilles." So far the grandson of Aeacus; his heavy spear followed his words. But though there was no missing in the sure spear, still the sharp point, so hurled, did nothing, and only bruised the breast with a blunt blow. "Son of a goddess — for by report we knew you beforehand," said Cycnus, "why do you wonder that wounds are kept from me?" — for Achilles was wondering. "This helmet you see, tawny with a horse's mane, and the hollow shield, the burden of my left arm, are no help to me; grace alone is sought from them — Mars too takes up arms for that. All the service of this covering shall be removed, and still I will go off unscratched. It is something to be born not of a Nereid, but of one who rules Nereus and his daughters and the whole sea."

He spoke, and hurled at the descendant of Aeacus a spear meant to lodge in the shield's rim; it broke through the bronze and the next nine folds of bull's hide, but stopped in the tenth circle. The hero wrenched it out and again hurled the quivering weapon with a strong hand; again the body was without a wound, unharmed; nor could a third point so much as graze Cycnus, open and offering himself. Achilles blazed no otherwise than a bull in the open arena, when with its dreadful horns it charges the purple cloths that provoke it and feels its wounds evaded. Still he checks whether the point has fallen from the spear: it was clinging to the shaft. "Is my hand weak, then," he says, "and has it poured out on one man all the strength it had before? Surely it was strong enough when I first threw down the walls of Lyrnessus, or when I filled Tenedos and Eëtion's Thebes with their own blood, or when the Caÿcus flowed purple with the slaughter of its people, and Telephus twice felt the work of my spear. Here too, with so many slain, whose heaps along this shore I have both made and see, my right hand has proved mighty, and is mighty." He spoke, and, as if he distrusted his earlier deeds, hurled his spear at Menoetes, one of the Lycian crowd, standing opposite, and tore through both his mail and the breast beneath. As Menoetes beat the solid earth with his dying head, Achilles drew the same weapon from the reeking wound and said, "This is the hand, this the spear, with which I have just conquered; I will use them on him — and I pray the end may be the same." So saying, he aimed again at Cycnus, and the ash spear did not miss; it rang, unavoided, on his left shoulder, then was thrown back as if by a wall or a solid rock. Yet, where he had struck, Achilles saw Cycnus marked with blood, and rejoiced in vain: there was no wound — the blood was Menoetes'.

Then indeed, roaring, he leaped headlong from his high chariot, and, striking at his fearless foe hand to hand with his bright sword, he sees that the shield and the helmet are pierced by his blade, but that his weapon too is blunted on the hard body. He bore it no longer; drawing back his shield, three and four times he struck the hero's face and his hollow temples with the sword-hilt, and, pressing on as the other gave ground, confounded him, drove him back, and gave him no rest in his daze. Terror takes Cycnus, darkness swims before his eyes; and, as he carried his backward steps in retreat, a stone in the middle of the field stood in his way. Over it Achilles, with great force, threw Cycnus on his back and dashed him to the earth; then, pressing his chest with shield and hard knees, he drew tight the straps of the helmet, which, fastened under the pressed chin, crushed the throat and took away the breath and the passage of life. He was making ready to strip the beaten man — and saw only the armor left behind; the god of the sea had turned the body into a white bird, whose name he had so lately borne.

This labor, this battle, brought a rest of many days, and both sides paused and laid down their arms. And while a watchful guard kept the Phrygian walls, and a watchful guard kept the Greek trenches, a festival day had come, on which Achilles, the conqueror of Cycnus, was appeasing Pallas with the blood of a fillet-decked heifer. When he had laid its cut portions on the glowing altars, and the smell, welcome to the gods, rose into the sky, the sacred rite had its share, and the rest was served at the table. The chiefs reclined on couches and filled themselves with roasted meat, and eased their cares and thirst with wine. No harps, no songs, no long boxwood pipe of many holes delights them; but they draw out the night in talk, and valor is the matter of their words. They tell of the battles of the enemy and their own, and often gladly recount, in turn, the dangers they have met and come through. For of what else should Achilles speak, or what should be spoken before great Achilles? But most of all the recent victory over Cycnus was the theme: it seemed a marvel to all that the youth's body was pierced by no weapon, took no wound, and blunted the steel itself. Both the grandson of Aeacus and the Greeks were wondering at it, when Nestor said:

"Cycnus was the only despiser of weapons in your time, and pierced by no blow. But I myself once saw Caeneus of Perrhaebia bear a thousand strokes with his body unhurt — Caeneus of Perrhaebia, who, famous for his deeds, lived on Othrys; and, that this might be the more wondrous in him, he had been born a woman." Whoever is there is moved by the strange marvel, and they beg him to tell it; and Achilles among them: "Tell it, come — for we all have the same wish to hear, eloquent old man, wisdom of our age: who was this Caeneus, and why changed to the opposite sex? In what war, in what contest of battle did you know him? By whom was he conquered, if by anyone?"

Then the old man said: Though slow age is against me, and many things I saw in my early years escape me now, still I remember most; and among so many deeds of war and peace, nothing clings more firmly to my mind than that. And if a long life could make anyone a witness of many deeds, I have lived two hundred years; now my third age is being spent.

Caenis, the daughter of Elatus, was famous for her beauty — the loveliest maiden of Thessaly, longed for in vain by the prayers of many suitors through the neighboring cities and through your cities, Achilles, for she was your countrywoman. Perhaps Peleus too would have tried that marriage; but by then either your mother's marriage had fallen to him, or been promised. Caenis married no one; and, walking a lonely shore, she suffered force from the god of the sea — so report told. And when Neptune had taken the joys of this new love, he said, "Let your wishes be safe from all refusal: choose what you desire." (The same report told this too.) Caenis said, "This wrong makes my wish a great one — that I may be able to suffer no such thing again. Grant that I be no longer a woman: you will have granted me everything." She spoke the last words in a deeper tone, and the voice could seem a man's, as it was; for already the god of the deep sea had granted her wish, and had given besides that he could be wounded by no blows and fall to no steel. Glad of the gift, the son of Atrax went off, and spent his life in a man's pursuits, roaming the Peneian fields.

Pirithous, born of bold Ixion, had married Hippodame, and had bidden the cloud-born monsters take their places at the tables set in a tree-shaded cave. The Thessalian nobles were there; I too was there; and the festive palace rang with the mingled crowd. Look — they sing the wedding song, the halls smoke with fires, and the bride comes in, ringed by a company of matrons and young wives, outstanding in beauty. We called Pirithous lucky in his wife — an omen we almost proved false. For your breast, Eurytus, most savage of the savage Centaurs, is fired as much by the wine as by the sight of the girl, and drunkenness, doubled by lust, reigns. At once the overturned tables throw the feast into uproar, and the new bride is dragged off by force, seized by the hair. Eurytus snatches Hippodame; the others each seize the one they fancied or could; and it was the very look of a captured city. The house rings with women's cries. Quickly we all rise, and Theseus first cries, "What madness drives you, Eurytus, that while I live you provoke Pirithous, and, blind, wrong two men in one?" And, that the great-hearted hero might not say it in vain, he pushes off the pressing crowd and takes the seized girl from them in their fury.

Eurytus says nothing in reply — for he cannot defend such deeds with words — but attacks her champion's face with insolent hands and strikes his noble breast. By chance there was near an ancient mixing-bowl, rough with jutting figures, which — huge as it was — the still huger son of Aegeus lifted and hurled full in Eurytus's face; and he, vomiting from wound and mouth together clots of blood and brains and wine, kicked on his back on the soaking sand. His twin-formed brothers blaze at the killing, and all, vying, with one voice cry "Arms! arms!" The wine gave them spirit, and in the first fighting flung cups fly, and shattered jars and curved cauldrons — things once for feasting, now fit for war and slaughter.

First Amycus, son of Ophion, did not fear to strip the inner house of its gifts, and first tore from the shrine a chandelier thick with blazing lamps, and, lifting it high like one who makes to break the white neck of a bull with the sacrificial axe, dashed it against the forehead of Celadon the Lapith and left the bones a shapeless mash in his face. The eyes leaped out, and, the face-bones scattered, the nose was driven back and fixed in the middle of the palate. Pelates of Pella, tearing off the foot of a maple table, laid him low on the ground, his chin sunk on his chest, and, as he spat out teeth mixed with black blood, sent him by a second wound to the shades of Tartarus.

Gryneus, standing next and glaring at the smoking altar, said, "Why not use this?" and lifted the huge altar, fire and all, and threw it into the midst of the Lapiths, and crushed two, Broteas and Orios — Orios, whose mother was Mycale, known to have often drawn down the horns of the struggling moon by her singing. "You will not do it unpunished, if only I get a weapon!" cried Exadius, and, for a weapon, seizes the antlers of a votive stag that hung on a tall pine. With the double branches Gryneus is pierced through the eyes and has them scooped out — part clinging to the antlers, part running down his beard and hanging clotted with gore.

Look — Rhoetus snatches a blazing brand of plum-wood from the middle of the altar and, on the right side, breaks through the temples of Charaxus, covered with yellow hair. His locks, caught by the swift flame, burned like dry corn, and the blood seared in the wound gave a dreadful hissing sound, as red-hot iron often does when the smith draws it out with the curved tongs and plunges it in the trough: it hisses and sinks bubbling in the water. Wounded, Charaxus shakes the greedy fire from his shaggy hair, and lifts to his shoulders a threshold torn from the ground, a wagon's load — whose very weight keeps him from throwing it at the enemy; the stone mass crushed his own comrade Cometes, standing too near. Rhoetus cannot hold his joy: "So, I pray, may the rest of your camp's crowd be brave!" he cries, and renews the wound with the half-burnt stake, and three and four times breaks the sutures of the skull with heavy blows, and the bones sank into the running brain.

Victorious, he passes to Evagrus, Corythus, and Dryas. Of these, when Corythus, his cheeks covered with their first down, had fallen, Evagrus said, "What glory have you won by killing a boy?" Rhoetus lets him say no more, and fiercely buried the red flames in the open mouth of the man as he spoke, and through the mouth into the breast. You too, cruel Dryas, he pursues, whirling fire around his head — but the same end did not wait for you: as he exulted in his run of unbroken slaughter, you pierced him with a fire-hardened stake where the neck joins the shoulder. Rhoetus groaned and barely wrenched the stake from the hard bone, and, drenched in his own blood, fled. Orneus fled too, and Lycabas, and Medon, wounded in the right shoulder, and Thaumas with Pisenor, and Mermeros, who had lately beaten all in the footrace but now went slower for his wound, and Pholus and Melaneus and Abas the boar-hunter, and Astylos the augur, who had vainly warned his own side against this war — he even said to Nessus, dreading his wounds, "Do not flee! You are saved for the bow of Hercules." But Eurynomus and Lycidas and Areos and Imbreus did not escape death; the right hand of Dryas struck them all as they faced him. You too, Crenaeus, took your wound facing him, though you had turned your back in flight: looking back, you took the heavy steel between the two eyes, where the nose joins the brow.

Amid all that din, Aphidas lay dead asleep from the wine he had drunk without stop, unwaking, holding the mixed cup in his slack hand, sprawled on the shaggy skin of an Ossaean bear. When Phorbas saw him from afar, wielding no weapon, he fitted his fingers into the strap and said, "You will drink your wine mixed with the Styx," and, without more delay, hurled the javelin at the youth; and the steel-tipped ash was driven into his neck as he lay by chance on his back. Death came without his feeling it, and the black blood flowed from his full throat onto the couch and into the very cup.

I saw Petraeus trying to tear an acorn-bearing oak from the earth; and while he grips it in his arms and shakes it this way and that and works the loosened trunk, the lance of Pirithous, thrown at Petraeus's ribs, pinned his straining breast to the tough oak. By the valor of Pirithous, they said, Lycus fell, and by the valor of Pirithous Chromis — but each gave less glory to the victor than Dictys and Helops. Helops was run through by a javelin that pierced his temples and, thrown from the right, reached his left ear. Dictys, slipping from the sheer point of a rock as he fled the pursuing son of Ixion in terror, fell headlong and, by the weight of his body, broke a great mountain-ash and impaled his own guts on it. Aphareus comes as avenger, and tries to hurl a rock torn from the mountain; but, as he tries, the son of Aegeus forestalls him with an oaken club and breaks the huge bones of his arm; and, with no leisure or care to give the useless body to death, he leaps onto the back of tall Bianor, not used to carry anyone but himself, and, planting his knee in his ribs and gripping the hair in his left hand, shatters the man's face and threatening features and hard temples with the knotty oak. With that oak he lays low Nedymnus, and Lycopes the javelin-thrower, and Hippasus, his breast covered by his flowing beard, and Ripheus, who towered above the treetops, and Thereus, who used to carry home alive the raging bears he caught in the Thessalian mountains.

Demoleon could bear no longer to see Theseus enjoying this success in the fight, and with vast effort tried to tear an aged pine from the thick wood; and, since he could not, he hurled it, broken short, at his enemy. But Theseus drew far back from the coming missile, warned by Pallas — so at least he wished it thought. Yet the tree did not fall for nothing: it sheared from the throat of tall Crantor both his breast and his left shoulder. He had been your father's armor-bearer, Achilles — one whom Amyntor, king of the Dolopians, beaten in war, had given the son of Aeacus as a pledge of peace. When Peleus saw him from afar, mangled with the foul wound, he cried, "Take this offering, Crantor, dearest of young men," and with a strong arm and all his force hurled his ash spear at Demoleon, which broke through the cage of his ribs and quivered, sticking in the bones. He drew out the shaft without the point by hand — that too barely followed; the point stayed in his lung. The very pain gave his spirit strength: sick, he reared against the enemy and trampled the man with his horse's feet. Peleus takes the ringing blows on helmet and shield, guards his shoulders, holds his arms out before him, and through the shoulders pierces two breasts at one stroke. But first, from afar, he had killed Phlegraeus and Hyles, and, in close combat, Iphinoüs and Clanis; to these is added Dorylas, who wore a wolf's skin on his temples, and, for a cruel weapon, the crooked horns of oxen reddened with much blood.

To him I said — for courage gave me strength — "See how far your horns fall short of my steel," and threw my javelin. When he could not dodge it, he held up his right hand before the brow that would take the wound; the hand was pinned to the brow. A shout went up; and Peleus — for he stood nearer — struck him as he stuck fast, overcome by the cruel wound, with his sword beneath the middle of the belly. He leaped forward, fiercely dragged his own bowels on the ground, trod on them as he dragged, burst them as he trod, tangled his legs in them too, and fell with an emptied belly.

Nor did your beauty save you in battle, Cyllarus — if we grant beauty to that monstrous nature. His beard was starting, the color of his beard gold, and golden hair hung from his shoulders to the middle of his flanks. There was a pleasing spirit in his face; his neck and shoulders and hands and breast were near the praised statues of the artists, in every part where he was a man; nor was the horse-shape beneath faulty or worse than the man's. Give him a horse's neck and head, and he would be worthy of Castor: so fit for a rider was his back, so raised with muscle his chest. He was all blacker than black pitch, but his tail was white, and the color of his legs was white too. Many of his own kind sought him, but Hylonome alone won him, than whom no lovelier female lived among the half-beasts in the high woods. She alone holds Cyllarus by coaxing and by loving and by confessing her love, and by her grooming too, as far as those limbs allow: so that her hair is smoothed with a comb, that she twines herself now with rosemary, now with violet or rose, sometimes wears white lilies, and twice a day washes her face in the springs that fall from the height of the Pagasaean wood, and twice dips her body in the stream, and throws over her shoulder or her left side no skins but such as suit her, of chosen beasts. Their love was equal: they wandered the mountains together, went into the caves together; and now they had entered the Lapith house together, together were waging the fierce war. The thrower is unknown, but a javelin came from the left and pierced you, Cyllarus, just below where the breast joins the neck. The heart, hurt by that small wound, grew cold with the whole body once the weapon was drawn out. At once Hylonome takes his dying limbs, warms the wound with her hand laid on it, sets her mouth to his, and strives to stop the fleeing life; and when she sees him dead, having said something the din kept from my ears, she fell on the weapon that had pierced him, and, dying, embraced her husband.

He stands before my eyes too — Phaeocomes, who had bound six lions' skins together with knots, and covered both man and horse: he, hurling the trunk of a tree two yoke of oxen could hardly move, broke Tectaphon, son of Olenus, on the top of his head. The very broad round of his skull was crushed, and through his mouth and hollow nostrils and eyes and ears his soft brains poured, as curdled milk drips through oaken wickerwork, or as liquid oozes under a well-pierced sieve and is squeezed out thick through the many holes. But I, while he was making to strip the fallen man of his arms — your father knows this — plunged my sword into the base of his belly as he plundered. Chthonius too, and Teleboas, lie slain by my sword; the first had borne a two-forked bough for a weapon, the other a javelin, and with his javelin he wounded me — you see the marks; the old scar still shows. Then I should have been sent to the taking of Troy; then I could have stayed, if not overcome, the arms of great Hector with mine! But at that time there was no Hector, or only a boy — and now my age fails me. Why tell you of Periphas, conqueror of two-formed Pyraethus, or of Ampyx, who fixed his pointless cornel-spear full in the face of four-footed Echeclus? Macareus laid low the Pelethronian Erigdupus by driving a crowbar into his breast; and I remember a hunting-spear thrown by Nessus's hand buried in the groin of Cymelus. And do not think Mopsus, son of Ampycus, only foretold the future: at Mopsus's throw a two-formed monster fell, and Hodites tried in vain to speak, his tongue nailed to his chin and his chin to his throat.

Caeneus had put five to death — Styphelus and Bromus and Antimachus and Elymus and axe-bearing Pyracmon: their wounds I do not remember, but I marked their number and names. Latreus, huge in limbs and body, sallied out, armed with the spoils of Emathian Halesus, whom he had slain. His age was between youth and old man; his force was a youth's; gray flecked his temples. Conspicuous with shield and helmet and Macedonian pike, turning his face to both ranks, he shook his arms, rode in one same circle, and poured out these many words boldly into the empty air: "And shall I put up with you too, Caenis? For to me you will always be a woman, to me always Caenis. Does your birth-origin not shame your spirit? Does it not come to mind by what deed you got your reward, and at what price the false shape of a man? See what you were born, or what you submitted to; go, take up a distaff with your work-baskets and twist the thread with your thumb — leave war to men." As he boasted such things, Caeneus, with a hurled lance, tore open his stretched-out side as he ran, where man was joined to horse. He raves with pain and strikes at the bare face of the Phyllean youth with his pike: it bounces back no otherwise than hail from a housetop, or as if one struck a hollow drum with a little pebble. He comes to close quarters and struggles to bury his sword in the tough side — but the parts are impervious to the sword. "Yet you will not escape me! You will be killed by the middle of the blade, since the point is blunt," he says, and slants the sword against his side, and clasps his belly with his long right arm. The blow gives a groan, as a body of marble struck; and the shattered blade flew apart on striking the neck. When Caeneus had shown his unhurt limbs enough to his amazement, "Come now," he said, "let us try your body with my steel!" and up to the hilt he drove his deadly sword into the shoulder, and worked his hand, unseen, into the entrails, and made a wound within the wound.

Look — the double-limbed monsters, enraged, rush in with a vast roar, and all hurl and thrust their weapons at him alone. The weapons fall blunted; unstabbed and bloodless the Elatean Caeneus stays from every blow. The strange thing left them stunned. "O huge disgrace!" cries Monychus. "A whole people, we are beaten by one, and scarcely a man — though he is a man, and we, by our craven deeds, are what he once was. What use are our huge limbs? Our double strength? That our twofold nature has joined the strongest creatures in us? I do not believe we were born of a goddess, nor of Ixion, who was so great he could hope for lofty Juno. We are beaten by a half-male foe! Heap on him stones and beams and whole mountains, and crush out his stubborn breath with cast-down woods! Let a whole wood choke his jaws, and weight will stand for wounds." He spoke, and, chancing on a tree thrown down by the mad force of the south wind, hurled it at the strong foe; and he was an example to the rest, and in a short time Othrys was bare of trees and Pelion had no shade. Buried under the huge heap, Caeneus struggles beneath the weight of the wood and bears the piled oaks on his hard shoulders; but when the load grew over his face and head and his breath had no air to draw, at times he faints, at times he tries in vain to lift himself into the open and to roll off the cast woods — and sometimes he moves it, as, look, if lofty Ida were shaken by earthquakes. The end is doubtful: some said his body was thrust down to roomy Tartarus by the mass of wood; the son of Ampycus denied it, and saw, from the middle of the heap, a bird with tawny wings go out into the clear air — which then for the first time, and then for the last, was seen by me. When Mopsus saw it surveying his camp in gentle flight and clanging around it with a great cry, following it with eyes and heart, he said, "Hail, glory of the Lapith race, once greatest of men, but now the only bird of your kind, Caeneus!" The thing was believed on his word. Grief added anger, and we bore it hard that one had been overwhelmed by so many foes; nor did we stop working our weapons in blood before part were killed and flight and night took the rest away.

As the Pylian told this fight between the Lapiths and the half-human Centaurs, Tlepolemus could not bear his grief that Hercules had been passed over in silence, and said, "It is strange, old man, that you should forget the deeds of Hercules; my father himself often used to tell me that these cloud-born monsters were tamed by him." The Pylian, sad at this, said, "Why do you force me to remember my sorrows, to tear open grief hidden by the years, and to confess my hatred and offense against your father? He did indeed do things beyond belief, and filled the world with his services — which I could rather wish could be denied; but we praise neither Deiphobus nor Polydamas nor Hector himself: for who would praise an enemy? That father of yours once leveled the walls of Messene, and destroyed guiltless cities, Elis and Pylos, and drove sword and fire into my home. And, to say nothing of the others he killed, we were twice six sons of Neleus, a fine band; twice six fell to the might of Hercules, all but me alone. That the others could be beaten, one might bear; but the death of Periclymenus is a marvel, to whom Neptune, founder of the Neleian line, had given the power to take whatever shapes he chose and, once taken, to lay them aside. When he had in vain turned into every other shape, he turned into the bird that carries the lightnings in its curved talons, dearest to the king of the gods; using the bird's strength — its wings, its hooked beak, its clawed talons — he tore the hero's face. The Tirynthian aims at him his too-unerring bow and hits him as he carries his limbs aloft among the clouds and hangs in the air, where the wing joins the side. The wound was not great, but the sinews, cut by it, fail him and deny him motion and the strength to fly. He fell to the earth, his weakened wings not catching the air; and where the light arrow had stuck in the wing, it was pressed farther by the weight of the falling body, and driven up through the top of the side into the left of the neck. Do I seem, then, to owe praises to the deeds of your Hercules, most handsome leader of the Rhodian fleet? Yet I will avenge my brothers no further than by keeping silent on his brave deeds; my friendship with you is firm."

When the son of Neleus had said this with his sweet tongue, and the gift of Bacchus was taken up again after the old man's tale, they rose from the couches; the rest of the night was given to sleep.

But the god who rules the sea's waters with his trident grieves, with a father's love, for the body of his son turned into the bird named for Sthenelus's son, and, hating cruel Achilles, presses his remembering wrath more than in ordinary measure. And now, the war dragged out nearly twice five years, he speaks thus to the unshorn Smintheus: "O dearest by far to me of my brother's sons, who built with me the walls of Troy in vain — do you not groan when you see these towers so soon to fall? Do you not grieve that so many thousands are slain defending these walls? Does not — to name no others — the ghost of Hector, dragged around his own Pergamum, come to you? And still fierce Achilles, bloodier than war itself, lives on, the ravager of our work. Let him but give himself to me, and I will make him feel what I can do with my triple spear; but since we may not meet the enemy hand to hand, destroy him unawares with a hidden arrow." Apollo nodded, and, indulging both his uncle's wrath and his own, veiled in a cloud, came to the Trojan army, and, in the midst of the slaughter, saw Paris scattering his darts, at intervals, among the common Greeks; and, revealing himself as a god, said, "Why waste your arrows on the blood of the rabble? If you have any care for your own, turn on the grandson of Aeacus, and avenge your slaughtered brothers." He spoke, and, pointing at the son of Peleus mowing down Trojan bodies with the sword, turned the bow at him and guided the sure arrow with a deadly hand. This was the one thing that old Priam could rejoice at after Hector. So you, Achilles, conqueror of such great men, are conquered by the cowardly stealer of a Greek wife! But if you had to fall by a woman's war, you would rather have fallen by the Amazon's axe.

Now that terror of the Phrygians, the glory and shield of the Greek name, the grandson of Aeacus, a head unconquerable in war, had burned: the same god had armed him and had burned him. Now he is ash, and of Achilles, so great, there remains some little thing that would not well fill an urn — but his glory lives, to fill the whole world. This measure suits that man, and in this the son of Peleus is equal to himself, and knows no empty Tartarus. Even his shield stirs a war, so you may know whose it was; and arms are wielded for arms. The son of Tydeus does not dare claim them, nor Ajax son of Oïleus, nor the younger son of Atreus, nor the elder, greater in war and years, nor any others: only the sons of Telamon and of Laertes had confidence for so great a prize. To spare himself the burden and the odium of a decision, the descendant of Tantalus bade the Greek leaders sit in the midst of the camp, and passed the judgment of the dispute to them all.

Book XIII

The chiefs sat down, and, with a ring of the common people standing around, Ajax, lord of the sevenfold shield, rose before them; and, impatient in his anger, he looked back with a grim face on the Sigean shores and the fleet on the beach, and, stretching out his hands, said:

"We plead our cause before the ships, O Jupiter, and Ulysses vies with me! But he did not hesitate to give way before Hector's fires, which I withstood, which I drove from this fleet. It is safer, then, to fight with invented words than with the hand. But neither is speaking easy for me, nor acting for him; and as much as I am worth in fierce war and in the line, so much is he worth in talking. Nor do I think, Greeks, that my deeds need be told to you — for you have seen them. Let Ulysses tell his, which he does without a witness, of which night alone is aware. I own the prize sought is great; but a rival cheapens its honor: it is no proud thing for Ajax to hold whatever Ulysses has hoped for, great though it be; he has already gained the reward of this contest — that, when he is beaten, he will be said to have contended with me.

"And I, if my valor were in doubt, would be strong in noble birth, the son of Telamon, who took the walls of Troy under brave Hercules and entered the Colchian shore in the ship of Pagasae. His father was Aeacus, who gives law to the silent dead there, where the heavy stone weighs down Sisyphus, son of Aeolus. Highest Jupiter owns Aeacus and confesses him his offspring: so Ajax is the third from Jove. And yet let this line not help my cause, Greeks, if it is not shared with great Achilles: he was my cousin — I ask what belonged to my cousin! Why do you, sprung from the blood of Sisyphus and most like him in thefts and fraud, thrust a stranger family's name in among the sons of Aeacus? Are the arms to be denied me because I took up arms first, with no informer, while he seems preferable who took them last and shirked service by feigned madness, until the son of Nauplius, cleverer than he but unluckier for himself, unmasked his coward's ruse and dragged him to the arms he had dodged? Shall he now take the best, because he was willing to take none — and shall I go dishonored, robbed of my cousin's gifts, because I offered myself first to danger?

"And would that his madness had been real, or believed — that this counselor of crimes had never come with us to the towers of Phrygia! Then, son of Poeas, Lemnos would not hold you cast away, by our fault; you who now, they say, hidden in the woodland caves, move the very rocks with your groans and pray for the son of Laertes what he deserves — which, if there are gods, you do not pray in vain. And now he who was sworn on the same campaign with us, one of our leaders, alas — the man whose bow, as heir, uses the arrows of Hercules — broken by disease and hunger, is clothed and fed by birds, and, hunting fowl, spends on them the shafts owed to the fate of Troy. Yet he lives, because he did not go with Ulysses. Unhappy Palamedes too would rather have been left — then he would be alive, or at least have had a death without a charge; whom Ulysses, remembering too well the exposing of his madness, framed as betraying the Greek cause, and proved his false charge, and showed the gold he had buried before. So by exile or by death he has robbed the Greeks of their best strength: that is how Ulysses fights, that is why he is to be feared.

"Though he outdo faithful Nestor himself in speech, he will never make me think Nestor's abandonment no crime — Nestor, who, when he begged Ulysses' help, slowed by his horse's wound and worn with age, was deserted by his companion. That I do not invent these charges, the son of Tydeus knows well, who, calling him by name again and again, rebuked him and reproached his trembling friend with his flight. The gods above watch the deeds of men with just eyes: see, he who gave no help now needs it, and, as he left another, so was he to be left — the law he made for himself! He shouted to his comrades: I came, and saw him trembling, pale with fear, quaking at the death to come. I set the mass of my shield before him, covered him as he lay, and saved — this is the least of my glory — his cowardly life. If you insist on the contest, let us go back to that place: give back the enemy, and your wound, and your old fear, hide behind my shield, and under it contend with me! But after I had rescued him, he whom his wounds had left no strength to stand fled, slowed by no wound at all.

"Hector comes, and brings the gods with him to battle; and where he charges, not only you, Ulysses, are afraid, but the brave too — so much terror he brings. Him, as he exulted in his bloody success, I laid flat from afar with a huge stone; him, demanding someone to face, I alone withstood; and you, Greeks, prayed the lot might fall to me — and your prayers prevailed. If you ask the issue of that fight, I was not beaten by him. Look — the Trojans bring fire and sword and Jove himself against the Greek fleet: where now is eloquent Ulysses? I, with my breast, protected a thousand ships, the hope of your return: grant me the arms for so many ships. And, if I may speak the truth, a greater honor is sought for them than for me: our glory is joined, and it is Ajax that is sought for the arms, not the arms by Ajax. Let the Ithacan set beside these deeds Rhesus, and unwarlike Dolon, and Helenus the son of Priam taken with the stolen Pallas: nothing done by day, nothing without Diomedes! If you give these arms for services so cheap, divide them, and let Diomedes' share be the greater.

"But why these for the Ithacan, who works by stealth, always unarmed, and cheats the unwary enemy by tricks? The very shine of the helmet, gleaming with bright gold, will betray his plots and show him where he hides. Nor will the Dulichian head bear so great a weight under Achilles' helm, nor the Pelian spear be anything but heavy and burdensome for unwarlike arms; nor will the shield engraved with the image of the vast world suit a timid left hand made for theft. Why ask, wretch, for a gift that will only weaken you? If the Greek people's mistake gave it you, it would be a reason to be stripped, not feared; and your flight — the one thing in which, most cowardly of men, you beat all others — will be slowed as you drag so great a load. Add that your shield, so rarely in the fight, is unhurt; mine, gaping with a thousand blows from bearing weapons, needs a new successor. In short — what need of words? Let us be tried in action! Let the arms of the brave man be thrown among the enemy: order them fetched from there, and deck out with them the one who brings them back."

The son of Telamon had ended, and a murmur of the crowd followed his closing words, until the Laertian hero stood, and, holding his eyes a little on the ground, raised them to the chiefs and loosed the voice they awaited; nor was grace missing from his eloquent words:

"If my prayers had prevailed with yours, Greeks, the heir of so great a contest would not be in doubt: you would have your arms, Achilles, and we would have you. But since unfair Fates have denied him to me and to you" — and here he wiped his eyes with his hand as if weeping — "who should better succeed great Achilles than the man through whom great Achilles joined the Greeks? Only let it not help him that he seems as dull as he is; and let my talents, which have ever served you, be no harm to me; and let this eloquence of mine, if there is any, which now pleads for its owner and has often pleaded for you, be free of envy — and let no man disown his own gifts. For as to descent, and ancestors, and the things we did not make ourselves, I scarcely call these our own; but since Ajax has boasted himself Jove's great-grandson, Jupiter is the founder of my line too, and I am just as many steps from him: for Laertes is my father, his was Arcesius, his Jupiter — and none of these was condemned or exiled. Through my mother, too, Cyllenian Mercury adds another nobility: on both parents' side there is a god. But not because I am nobler-born on my mother's side, nor because my father is innocent of a brother's blood, do I claim these arms. Weigh the case by merit — only let it be no merit in Ajax that Telamon and Peleus were brothers, and let not blood-order but the honor of valor be sought in these spoils. Or if nearness and the next heir is wanted, Peleus is Achilles' father and Pyrrhus his son: what place is there for Ajax? Let the arms go to Phthia or Scyros! And Teucer is no less Achilles' cousin than he — yet does he claim, does he expect to carry them off?

"Since, then, the contest is simply of deeds — I have done more than I can easily put in words, yet I will go in order. His mother, the Nereid, foreseeing his death, hid her son in a disguise, and the trick of the assumed dress deceived all, Ajax among them. I mixed arms, to stir a manly mind, among a woman's wares; and the hero had not yet thrown off the maiden's dress when, as he gripped a shield and spear, I said, 'Son of a goddess, Troy keeps itself to fall by you: why delay to overthrow mighty Troy?' And I laid my hand on him and sent the brave man to brave deeds. So his deeds are mine: I subdued Telephus fighting with his spear, and healed him when he begged, beaten. That Thebes fell is my doing; believe that I took Lesbos, took Tenedos, Chryse and Cilla, cities of Apollo, and Scyros; consider that the walls of Lyrnessus fell, shaken by my right hand; and, to name no others, I gave you the one who could kill fierce Hector — through me the famous Hector lies dead! By the arms through which Achilles was found, I claim these arms: to the living I gave them; after his death I ask them back.

"When the grief of one had reached all the Greeks, and a thousand ships filled Euboean Aulis, the winds long awaited were either lacking or against the fleet, and the harsh oracles bade Agamemnon slay his innocent daughter for cruel Diana. The father refuses, and rages at the gods themselves — a king, yet still a father. By my words I turned the parent's gentle nature to the public good. This cause — I confess it, and let Atrides forgive the confession — I upheld before a biased judge, a hard case; yet the good of the people, and his brother, and the height of the granted scepter, move him to balance praise against blood. I was sent, too, to the mother, who was not to be persuaded but tricked by craft; had the son of Telamon gone, our sails would even now be without wind. I was sent, a bold envoy, to the towers of Troy; the senate-house of lofty Troy was seen and entered by me, still full of its heroes; undaunted, I pleaded the common cause that all Greece had entrusted me, and accused Paris, and demanded back the plunder and Helen, and moved Priam and Antenor at Priam's side; but Paris and his brothers and the ravishers under him barely held their wicked hands — you know this, Menelaus — and that first day of danger was mine, with you.

"It would be long to tell what I did well, by counsel and by hand, in the length of that war. After the first battles the enemy kept long within the city walls, and there was no chance of open war; only in the tenth year did we fight. What were you doing meanwhile, you who know nothing but battles? What use were you? But if you ask my deeds: I lay ambushes for the enemy, ring the defenses with a trench, cheer the allies to bear the tedium of a long war with a calm mind, teach how we are to be fed and armed, and am sent where need requires. Look — by Jove's warning, the king, deceived by a shape in his sleep, orders the war's care dismissed; he can defend his word by its author. Ajax should not have allowed it, but demanded Troy be razed, and fought — the one thing he can do. Why does he not stop those about to leave? Why not take up arms and give the wavering crowd a lead? That was not too much for one who never says anything but grand. And did you yourself flee? I was witness, and ashamed to see, when you turned your back and readied the sails of disgrace. Without delay I cried, 'What are you doing? What madness stirs you, friends, to let Troy go, all but taken, and to carry home, in the tenth year, nothing but disgrace?' With such words, and others grief itself made me eloquent, I led the turned-away back from the fleeing fleet. The son of Atreus calls the allies, struck with terror; and still the son of Telamon dares not open his mouth — yet Thersites dared to attack the kings with insolent words, and not unpunished by me! I rise, and rouse the frightened citizens against the foe, and by my voice restore their lost courage. From that time, whatever this man may seem to have done bravely is mine, who drew him back as he turned his back.

"In short, who of the Greeks praises you, or seeks you out? But the son of Tydeus shares his deeds with me, approves me, and always trusts in Ulysses his comrade. It is something, out of so many thousands of Greeks, to be singled out by Diomedes!
