Nor did the lot order me to go; yet, scorning the peril of night and enemy, I killed Dolon of the Phrygian race, who dared the same as we — but not before I made him tell everything, and learned what treacherous Troy was planning. I had found out all, with nothing more to spy, and could have returned with praise going before me; not content with that, I sought the tent of Rhesus, and in his own camp killed him and his men, and so, a victor with my wish won, I rode home in the captured chariot like a joyful triumph. The horses whose owner the enemy had demanded as the price of one night's work — deny me the arms, and let Ajax be your greater benefactor! Why speak of the ranks of Lycian Sarpedon laid waste by my sword? With much bloodshed I felled Coeranus son of Iphitus, and Alastor, and Chromius, and Alcander, and Halius, and Noemon, and Prytanis, and put to death Thoön with Chersidamas, and Charops, and Ennomus driven by his cruel fate, and others less famous who fell by my hand beneath the walls. I too have wounds, citizens, honorable in their place; do not trust his crafty words — look!" And with his hand he pulled his garment open: "This is the breast," he said, "that has ever been busy in your service! But the son of Telamon has spent no blood on his friends in all these years, and has a body without a wound.

"Yet what of that, if he says he bore arms for the Greek fleet against the Trojans and Jove himself? I confess he did — nor is it mine to belittle others' good deeds with malice — but let him not alone seize what is common to all, and let him give you too some of the honor. The descendant of Actor, safe in Achilles' likeness, drove the Trojans and their champion from the ships about to burn. He fancies, too, that he alone dared meet Hector in combat, forgetting the king and the chiefs and me — ninth in that duty, and preferred by the favor of the lot. But what was the issue of your combat, bravest of men? Hector went off, hurt by no wound. Wretched me — with how much grief I must recall the time when Achilles, the wall of the Greeks, fell! No tears, no grief, no fear held me from lifting his body from the ground: on these shoulders — these shoulders, I say — I bore the body of Achilles, and his arms with it, which now too I am striving to bear. I have strength enough for such a weight, and surely a soul that will feel your honors. Was his sea-blue mother, then, so anxious for her son, that a rough soldier, one without a mind, should put on the heavenly gifts, the work of so great an art? For he will not understand the shield's engravings — the Ocean, the lands, the stars with the lofty sky, the Pleiades and Hyades and the Bear that shuns the sea, and the different cities, and the bright sword of Orion: he demands to receive arms he does not understand!

"And that he charges me with shirking the hard duties of war and coming late to the toil begun — does he not see that in this he slanders great Achilles? If you call feigning a crime, we both feigned; if delay is a fault, I was earlier than he. A loving wife held me back, a loving mother Achilles; the first of our time was given them, the rest to you. I do not fear a charge shared with so great a man, even if I could not defend it — yet he was found out by Ulysses' cleverness, but not Ulysses by Ajax's. And let us not wonder that he pours out the abuse of his stupid tongue on me, when he casts at you too things worthy of shame. Is it base for me to have charged Palamedes with a false crime, and honorable for you to have condemned him? But the son of Nauplius could not defend a deed so great and so plain, and you did not merely hear the charges against him — you saw them, and the bribe itself made the charge plain. Nor did I deserve blame because Vulcan's Lemnos still holds the son of Poeas — defend your own act, for you agreed to it! — nor will I deny I advised him to withdraw from the toil of war and voyage, and to try to ease his cruel pains by rest. He obeyed — and lives! The advice was not only loyal but lucky, though loyal was enough. And since the seers demand him to destroy Troy, do not send me! The son of Telamon will go better, and by his eloquence soften the man maddened by disease and rage, or cleverly bring him back by some art! Sooner will Simois flow backward and Ida stand without leaves, and Achaia promise aid to Troy, than the skill of stupid Ajax help the Greeks while my breast is idle in your cause. Though you rage at friends and king and me, hard Philoctetes, though you curse and devote my head without end, and long to have me thrown in your way and to drink my blood — still I will approach you, and strive to bring you back with me, and win your arrows as surely (may Fortune favor) as I won the Dardanian seer I took, as I laid open the answers of the gods and the fates of Troy, as I stole the inmost image of Phrygian Minerva from the midst of the enemy.

"And does Ajax match himself with me? The Fates forbade Troy to be taken without that statue: where is the brave Ajax? Where are the great man's huge words? Why do you fear here? Why does Ulysses dare to go through the watches and trust himself to the night and, through cruel swords, enter not only the walls of Troy but its highest towers, and tear the goddess from her shrine and carry her, so torn, through the enemy? Had I not done it, the son of Telamon would have borne the seven bull-hides on his left arm in vain. On that night the victory over Troy was won by me: then I conquered Pergamum, when I made it able to be conquered. Stop showing me the son of Tydeus with your looks and muttering: a share of the glory in these things is his. Nor were you alone when you held your shield for the fleet: a crowd was with you; to me fell one. Who, did he not know that a fighter is less than a wise man, and that the prize is not owed to an untamed hand, would himself be claiming these arms — the more discreet Ajax would claim them, and fierce Eurypylus, and the son of famous Andraemon, and no less Idomeneus and Meriones of the same land, and the brother of the greater son of Atreus. But these, brave in action and no second to you in war, have all yielded to my wisdom. Your right hand is useful in war; your temper needs my guidance. You have strength without a mind; mine is the care for the future. You can fight; the son of Atreus, with me, picks the time for fighting. You serve only with your body; we with the mind; and as much as the one who steers the ship surpasses the oarsman's work, as much as the general is greater than the soldier, so much do I surpass you. In our body, too, the heart is worth more than the hand: all vigor lies there.

"But you, chiefs, give the reward to your watchman; and for the care of so many years I have spent in anxiety, return this honor to weigh against my services. The toil is now at its end: I have removed the opposing Fates, and by making it possible to take lofty Pergamum, I have taken it. By our shared hopes, by the walls of Troy doomed to fall, by the gods I lately took from the enemy, by anything that remains to be done by wisdom, by anything still to be dared from a desperate place — remember me! Or, if you do not give me the arms, give them to this," and he showed the fatal image of Minerva.

The body of chiefs was moved; and then it appeared what eloquence can do, and the fluent man carried off the arms of the brave one. He who alone had so often withstood Hector, and the sword, and fire, and Jove himself, could not withstand one thing — his own grief; and grief conquered the unconquered man. He seized his sword and said, "This at least is my own — or will Ulysses claim this too for himself? This must I use against myself; and the blade often wet with Phrygian blood will now be wet with its owner's death, that no one may conquer Ajax but Ajax." He spoke, and drove the deadly sword into his breast — then for the first time taking a wound — where it lay open to the steel. His hands could not draw the fixed weapon out; the blood itself forced it out; and the earth, reddened with blood, bore from the green turf a purple flower, the same once born from the wound of Hyacinthus. Letters shared by the boy and the hero are written in the middle of the petals: these for the name, those for the lament.

The victor, Ulysses, set sail for the country of Hypsipyle and famous Thoas, the land infamous for the old slaughter of husbands, to bring back the arrows, the weapons of Hercules. When he had carried them back to the Greeks, their owner along with them, the last hand was at length put to the long war. Troy and Priam fell together; Priam's wife, poor woman, after losing everything else, lost her human shape and frightened a foreign air with new barkings, where the long Hellespont narrows.

Ilium was burning, and the fire had not yet died, and the altar of Jove had drunk the scanty blood of old Priam; the priestess of Apollo, dragged by the hair, stretched to heaven her hands that would not avail; the Dardanian mothers, embracing while they could the images of their country's gods and clinging to the burning temples, the victorious Greeks drag off, an envied spoil. Astyanax is hurled from the towers from which, shown by his mother, he used often to watch his father fighting for himself and defending his ancestors' kingdom. And now Boreas urges them to go, and the sails, stirred by a fair breeze, resound; the sailor bids them use the winds. "Troy, farewell! We are torn away," the Trojan women cry, and kiss the soil, and leave the smoking roofs of their land. Last to board the fleet — a pitiful sight — is Hecuba, found among the graves of her children; clutching the tombs and kissing the bones, Dulichian hands dragged her off — yet she took up the ashes of one, and carried Hector's ashes with her in her breast; on Hector's tomb she left the gray hair of her head, a poor offering, her hair and her tears.

There is, opposite Phrygia where Troy stood, a land inhabited by the men of Bistonia. There stood the rich palace of Polymestor, to whom your father, Polydorus, secretly entrusted you to be raised, and removed you far from the Phrygian war — a wise plan, had he not added great riches as well, the reward of crime, the spur of a greedy heart. When the fortunes of the Phrygians fell, the wicked king of Thrace took a sword and drove it into the throat of his foster-child, and, as if the crime could be removed with the body, threw him lifeless from a cliff into the waters below.

On the Thracian shore the son of Atreus had moored his fleet, until the sea should be calm and the wind kinder. Here, suddenly, Achilles, as great as he used to be alive, rose from the earth that split wide, and, like one threatening, wore the face of that hour when he fiercely attacked lawless Agamemnon with the sword, and said: "Do you leave, forgetful of me, Greeks? Is all thanks for my valor buried with me? Do not! And, so that my tomb may not be without honor, let Polyxena be slain to appease the ghost of Achilles." He spoke; and his companions obeying the pitiless shade, the girl — brave and unhappy, and more than a woman — was torn from her mother's bosom, which she now cherished almost alone, and led to the tomb, and made a victim at the cruel pyre.

Mindful of herself, when she had been brought to the cruel altar and knew the savage rite was prepared for her, and saw Neoptolemus standing with sword in hand, fixing his eyes on her face, she said: "Use my noble blood at once; there is no holding back. Bury your weapon in my throat or my breast" — and bared throat and breast together. "Surely Polyxena would not want to be any man's slave; nor by such a rite will you appease any god. Only I could wish my death might be hidden from my mother. My mother is my one grief, and lessens the joy of my death — though it is not my death but her own life that should be mourned. Only, that I may not go unfree to the shades below, stand off, if what I ask is just, and keep men's hands from a maiden's touch: my blood will be the more welcome, whoever he is you make ready to appease by my killing, if it is free. Yet if the last words of my mouth move any of you — it is King Priam's daughter, not a captive, who asks — give my body back to my mother unransomed, and let her buy the sad right of a grave not with gold but with tears. When she could, she used to buy it with gold too."

She spoke; and the people did not hold back the tears she held. Even the priest, weeping and unwilling, split the offered breast with the driven steel. She, sinking to the ground on her failing knees, kept an unafraid face to her last moment; and even then it was her care, as she fell, to veil the parts that should be hidden, and to keep the honor of chaste modesty.

The Trojan women take her up and count over the mourned children of Priam, and how much blood one house has spent, and mourn you, girl, and you, so lately called a royal wife and royal mother, image of flourishing Asia, now even the wretched lot of the plunder — whom the victor Ulysses would not have wished his, but that you had borne Hector: Hector barely found his mother a master! She, embracing the body void of that brave soul, gave to it too the tears she had so often given her country, her sons, her husband; she pours her tears into the wounds, covers them with kisses from her mouth, beats the breast she is now so used to beating, and, tearing her gray hair clotted with blood, said much, and this too, tearing at her breast:

"Daughter, last grief of your mother — for what is left? — daughter, you lie dead, and I see your wound as my own. See: lest I lose any of my children without bloodshed, you too have a wound. Because you were a woman, I thought you safe from the sword — and yet, a woman, you have fallen by the sword; and the same Achilles who destroyed so many of your brothers has destroyed you, the ruin of Troy, the robber of me! But after he fell to the arrows of Paris and Phoebus, 'Now at least,' I said, 'Achilles is not to be feared' — and even now he was to be feared by me: the very ashes of the buried man rage against this house, and even in the tomb we have found him an enemy. For the son of Aeacus I was fruitful! Great Ilium lies fallen, and the public disaster is ended in a heavy end — but ended; only for me does Pergamum remain, and my grief still runs its course. So lately the greatest of all, strong in so many sons and daughters and in-laws and my husband, now I am dragged off an exile, destitute, torn from the tombs of my kin, a gift for Penelope — who, showing me to the Ithacan matrons as I spin my allotted task, will say, 'This is that famous mother of Hector, this is the wife of Priam.' And now you, who alone eased your mother's grief after so many losses, have made atonement at the enemy's tomb! An offering for the enemy I bore! Why do I hold out, hard as iron? Why do I linger? For what do you keep me, aged old age? For what, cruel gods, unless to see fresh deaths, do you reprieve an old woman so long-lived? Who would have thought Priam could be called happy after Pergamum fell? Happy in his death — he did not see you cut down, and lost his life and kingdom together. But you, I suppose, a royal maiden, will be honored with funeral rites, and your body laid in your ancestors' tombs! This is not the fortune of your house: your mother's gifts will be your lot — tears, and a handful of foreign sand. We have lost all; only one, dearest to his mother, remains, a reason to bear life a little longer — Polydorus, once the youngest of my sons, given to the Ismarian king on these coasts. Why do I delay, meanwhile, to wash her cruel wounds and her face smeared with dreadful blood?"

She spoke, and went to the shore with an old woman's step, tearing her white hair. "Give me an urn, Trojan women," the unhappy mother said, to draw the flowing water — and saw the body of Polydorus cast up on the shore, and the great wounds made by Thracian weapons. The Trojan women cried out; she was struck dumb with grief, and grief itself swallowed both her voice and the tears that rose within, and, most like a hard rock, she stiffened, now fixing her eyes on the ground before her, now lifting her grim face to the sky, now looking at her son's face, now at his wounds — the wounds above all — and she arms and readies herself with rage. As soon as it flared, she resolved to be avenged, as if she were still a queen, and was wholly in the image of punishment. And as a lioness rages, robbed of her suckling cub, and, finding the prints of his feet, follows the enemy she does not see, so Hecuba, having mixed rage with mourning, not forgetting her spirit but forgetting her years, went to Polymestor, the contriver of the dreadful murder, and asked to speak with him — for she wished, she said, to show him a hidden hoard of gold left for her son. The Thracian believed her, and, used to the love of gain, came to a secret place. Then, with coaxing lips, he cunningly said, "Away with delay, Hecuba, give the gift to your son; all you give, and all you gave before, will be his, I swear by the gods above." She eyes him grimly as he speaks and swears falsely, and boils with heaving rage, and, seizing him, calls up the throng of captive mothers and drives her fingers into his treacherous eyes and gouges them from his cheeks — rage makes her strong — and plunges her hands in, and, fouled with his guilty blood, tears out, not the eyes, for none are left, but the sockets of the eyes.

Provoked by their king's ruin, the Thracian people begin to attack the Trojan woman with thrown darts and stones; but she pursues the hurled stone with a hoarse growl and, her mouth set for words, barks as she tries to speak. The place still stands and takes its name from the event; and long remembering her old sorrows, even then she howled mournfully across the Sithonian plains. Her fate moved her own Trojans and her Greek foes, and all the gods too — so much that even the wife and sister of Jove denied that Hecuba had deserved that end.

Though she had favored those same arms, Aurora has no leisure to be moved by the disasters and fall of Troy and Hecuba: a nearer care, a grief at home, torments the goddess — her lost Memnon, whom his saffron mother saw perish by Achilles' spear on the Phrygian plains. She saw it, and that color with which the morning hours grow red went pale, and the sky hid in clouds. But the mother could not bear to watch his limbs laid on the last fire; with loose hair, just as she was, she did not scorn to fall at great Jove's knees and add these words to her tears: "Lowest of all the goddesses the golden sky holds — for my temples are the fewest in all the world — yet a goddess, I come; not that you grant me shrines and days of sacrifice and altars warm with fire, but, if you consider how much I, a woman, do for you when at each new dawn I keep the borders of night, you would think some reward due. But that is not my care, nor is Aurora in a state now to demand deserved honors: bereft of my Memnon I come, who bore brave arms in vain for his uncle, and in his early years — so you willed it — was killed by brave Achilles. Grant him, I pray, highest ruler of the gods, some honor as a comfort for his death, and ease a mother's wounds."

Jove nodded; and suddenly Memnon's high pyre sank with its towering fires, and rolls of black smoke stained the day, as when a river-nymph breathes up fogs and the sun is not let below. The black ash flies, gathers into one body, thickens, takes a shape, draws heat and life from the fire — its own lightness gave it wings — and, first like a bird, then a true bird, it whirred with its wings; and at once countless sisters whirred with it, born of the same origin. Three times they circle the pyre, and three times a joined wailing rises into the air; at the fourth flight they part their company; then two fierce tribes from opposite sides wage war, and with beaks and hooked claws work out their rage and weary their wings and clashing breasts, and fall, kindred bodies, an offering to the buried ash, remembering they were born of a brave man. Their maker gives these sudden birds a name: after him they are called the Memnonides; when the sun has run the twelve signs, they fight again, doomed to die in their parent's honor. So, while to others it seemed sad that the daughter of Dymas now barked, Aurora is intent on her own sorrow, and even now sheds loving tears and drips them in dew over all the world.

Yet the Fates do not let the hope of Troy be destroyed with its walls: the Cytherean hero bears on his shoulders the sacred relics and, another sacred relic, his father, a venerable burden. Out of such great wealth, in his devotion he chooses that prize and his own Ascanius, and with his fleeing fleet is carried over the seas from Antandros, and leaves the accursed thresholds of the Thracians and the earth streaming with Polydorus's blood, and, with helpful winds and favoring tide, enters the city of Apollo with his companions. Here Anius, by whom, as king, men were tended, and, as priest, Phoebus was duly served, received him into temple and house, and showed him the city, the famous shrines, and the two trees Latona once clutched in her labor. Incense given to the flames, wine poured over the incense, the entrails of slain oxen duly burned, they seek the palace, and, reclining on high couches, take the gifts of Ceres with flowing wine. Then devout Anchises said, "O chosen priest of Phoebus, am I wrong, or, when I first saw these walls, did you not have a son and, as I recall, twice-two daughters?" Anius answered, shaking his temples wreathed in snow-white fillets, and said sadly: "You are not wrong, greatest hero; you saw me the father of five children, whom now — such change of fortune turns among men — you see almost bereft of all. For what help is my absent son, whom Andros, a land named for him, holds, keeping that place and kingdom for his father? The Delian god gave him augury; to my daughters Liber gave gifts beyond wish and belief: for at the touch of my daughters all things turned to grain, and the stream of wine, and the olive of Minerva, and rich was the use of them. When the son of Atreus, the destroyer of Troy, learned this — that you may not think we too felt nothing of your storm — he used the force of arms and dragged them unwilling from their father's bosom, and ordered them to feed the Greek fleet with their heavenly gift. Each fled where she could; Euboea was sought by two, and by as many, Andros, their brother's island. The soldiers came and threatened war if they were not surrendered; loyalty, beaten by fear, gave up the kindred bodies to punishment — and you might forgive a timid brother: there was no Aeneas, no Hector to defend Andros, through whom you Trojans held out to the tenth year. And now chains were being readied for their captive arms; lifting to heaven their still-free arms, they cried, 'Father Bacchus, help!' and the giver of their gift did help — if to destroy them in a strange way be called helping. By what means they lost their shape I could not learn, and cannot now tell; the sum of the loss is known: they took wings and became birds of your consort, snow-white doves."

With such talk and other they filled the feast, and, the table removed, sought sleep, and rose with the day, and went to the oracle of Phoebus, who bade them seek their ancient mother and kindred shores. The king sees them off and gives gifts to the departing: a scepter to Anchises, a scarf and a quiver to his grandson, a mixing-bowl to Aeneas, which once Therses of Thebes, his guest, had sent him from the Aonian shore — Therses had sent it, but Alcon of Hyle had made it and carved it with a long design. There was a city, and you might point out its seven gates: these stood for a name and showed which city it was. Before the city, funerals and tombs and fires and pyres, and mothers with loosened hair and bared breasts, showed grief; the nymphs too seem to weep and mourn their dried-up springs; a leafless tree stands bare and stiff, and goats gnaw the parched stones. Look — in the middle of Thebes he shows the daughters of Orion, one giving her bared throat a more-than-womanly wound, the other driving a weapon through her brave breast, dying for her people, and borne through the city in honored funerals and burned in a public place; then from the virgin ashes, lest the race fail, twin youths rising, whom fame calls the Coronae, leading the procession for their mother's ashes. So far for the figures shining on the ancient bronze; the rim of the bowl was rough with gilded acanthus.

Nor do the Trojans send back gifts of less worth: they give the priest an incense-box for the frankincense, a bowl, and a crown bright with gold and gems. Then, recalling that the Teucrians drew their origin from Teucer's blood, they made for Crete, but could not long bear its air; and, leaving its hundred cities, they long to reach the Ausonian harbors. A storm rages and tosses them, and winged Aëllo frightens them, taken into the unsafe harbors of the Strophades. And now, carried on, they had passed the Dulichian harbors, and Ithaca and Same and the Neritian homes, the kingdom of the lying Ulysses; and they see Ambracia, contested in a dispute of the gods, now famous for the Actian Apollo, and the stone that was once the transformed judge, and the land of Dodona, vocal with its oaks, and the Chaonian bays, where the sons of the Molossian king escaped the wicked fires on borrowed wings. They make for the neighboring land of the Phaeacians, planted with lovely fruit; then Epirus, and Buthrotos ruled by the Phrygian seer, and a copied Troy; and, sure of the future — all that Helenus, son of Priam, had faithfully foretold — they enter Sicily. With three points it runs out into the sea: Pachynos turned to the rainy south, Lilybaeum facing the soft west winds, and Peloros looking to the north, away from the open sea. Here the Trojans come, and with oars and favoring tide, at nightfall the fleet gains the sands of Zancle.

Scylla infests the right side, restless Charybdis the left: one swallows and vomits up again the ships it takes; the other, with a maiden's face, has her dark belly girt with fierce dogs — and, if the poets have not left it all a fiction, she too was once a maiden. Many suitors sought her; refusing them, she used to go — most dear to the sea-nymphs — to the nymphs of the sea, and tell of the youths' baffled loves. As Galatea gave her hair to be combed, she addressed her thus, drawing sighs: "Yet you, girl, no ungentle race of men courts, and you can refuse them, as you do, unharmed; but I, whose father is Nereus, whom sea-blue Doris bore, guarded too by a crowd of sisters, could not escape the Cyclops' love except through sorrow" — and tears choked her voice. When the girl had wiped them with her marble thumb and comforted the goddess, she said, "Tell me, dearest, and do not hide from me — I am true to you — the cause of your grief." The Nereid answered the daughter of Crataeis:

Acis was the son of Faunus and the nymph Symaethis, a great delight to his father and mother, but a greater to me; for he had joined himself to me alone. Beautiful, and having passed twice eight birthdays, he had marked his soft cheeks with faint down. Him I pursued; me the Cyclops pursued without end. Nor, should you ask, could I say whether the hatred of the Cyclops or the love of Acis was stronger in me: they were equal. O kindly Venus, how great is the power of your reign! For that savage, dreaded even by the very woods, seen by no stranger and unharmed, the scorner of great Olympus and the gods themselves, now feels what love is, and, caught by a strong desire, burns, forgetting his flocks and his caves. And now you care for your looks, now for pleasing, Polyphemus; now you comb your stiff hair with rakes, now it pleases you to cut your shaggy beard with a sickle, and to look at your fierce face in the water and compose it. Your love of slaughter, your fierceness, your endless thirst for blood cease, and the ships come and go in safety.

Meanwhile Telemus, come to Sicilian Etna — Telemus, son of Eurymus, whom no omen had ever deceived — approached the dreadful Polyphemus and said, "The single eye you carry in the middle of your brow, Ulysses will take from you." He laughed and said, "O most foolish of seers, you are wrong: another has already taken it." So he scorns the one who warns him truly, in vain, and either burdens the shore, striding with huge steps, or, tired, goes back to his shady cave. A hill, wedge-shaped, runs out in a long point into the sea, the ocean's wave flowing round both sides. Here the fierce Cyclops climbed and sat in the middle; his woolly flocks followed with no one leading. When the pine that served him for a staff — fit for a mast — was laid at his feet, and he had taken up his pipe of a hundred reeds, all the mountains felt the shepherd's piping, and the waves felt it. Hidden in a rock and resting in my Acis's lap, I caught from afar with my ears such words as these, and marked them in my mind:

"O Galatea, whiter than the leaf of the snow-white privet, more flowery than the meadows, taller than the tall alder, brighter than glass, friskier than a tender kid, smoother than shells worn by the ceaseless sea, more welcome than the winter sun or the summer shade, more graceful than the tall plane, clearer than ice, sweeter than the ripe grape, softer than swan's down and than curdled milk — and, if you would not flee, lovelier than a watered garden. Yet you, the same Galatea, are wilder than untamed bullocks, harder than aged oak, more treacherous than the waters, tougher than willow twigs and white vines, more immovable than these rocks, more violent than a torrent, prouder than the praised peacock, fiercer than fire, rougher than thistles, more savage than a she-bear with young, deafer than the sea, crueler than a trodden snake — and, what most I would take from you, fleeter not only than the deer driven by loud barkings, but even than the winds and the fleeting air! But if you knew me well, you would repent your flight, and blame your own delay, and strive to keep me. I have, part of the mountain, caves hung with living rock, where neither the sun is felt at noon nor the winter; there are apples that weigh down the boughs, gold-like grapes on the long vines, and purple ones too: both these and those I keep for you. With your own hands you will gather the soft strawberries born under the woodland shade, the autumn cornels, and plums, not only darkened with black juice but of the choicest kind, like new wax. With me for husband you will lack no chestnuts, no arbute fruit: every tree will serve you.

"All this flock is mine; many too wander in the valleys, many the wood hides, many are stalled in my caves; nor, should you ask, could I tell you how many — it is a poor man who counts his cattle. For their praises trust me not at all; you can see for yourself how they can barely carry their swollen udders on their legs. There are lambs, the younger breed, in the warm folds, and kids of equal age in other folds. Snow-white milk I always have: part is kept for drinking, part the curdled rennet hardens. Nor will only common delights and ordinary gifts be yours — does and hares and a she-goat, a pair of doves, or a nest taken from a treetop: I have found on the mountaintop twin cubs of a shaggy she-bear that can play with you, so alike you could scarcely tell them apart; I found them and said, 'These I will keep for my mistress.' Now raise your bright head from the blue sea, now, Galatea, come, and do not scorn my gifts! Surely I know myself, for I lately saw myself in the reflection of clear water, and my form pleased me as I looked. See how huge I am: not Jove in heaven is bigger than this body — for you are wont to tell of some Jupiter reigning; my thick hair hangs over my grim face and shades my shoulders like a wood. And do not think it ugly that my body bristles with stiff hair: a tree is ugly without leaves, a horse ugly unless a mane veils its tawny neck; feathers cover birds, their wool becomes the sheep, a beard and rough hair suit men. I have one eye in the middle of my brow, but it is like a great shield. What of it? Does not the great Sun see all this from heaven — and the Sun has but one eye? Besides, my father reigns in your sea: him I give you for a father-in-law; only pity me and hear a suppliant's prayer! For to you alone I yield, I who scorn Jove and heaven and the piercing bolt; you, daughter of Nereus, I dread — your anger is fiercer to me than the lightning. And I could bear these slights more patiently if you fled all men; but why, rejecting the Cyclops, do you love Acis, and prefer his embrace to mine? Let him please himself and please you too — though I would not have it, Galatea — only let me get the chance: he will feel that my strength matches my great body! I will drag out his living guts and scatter his torn limbs through the fields and through your waves — so let him mingle with you! For I burn, and, slighted, the fire rages fiercer, and I seem to carry Etna, moved with all its flames, in my breast — and you, Galatea, are unmoved."

Having in vain made such complaints — for I saw it all — he rises, and, like a bull raging when the cow is taken from him, he cannot stand still, but wanders the wood and the known glades; when the savage saw me and Acis, unsuspecting and fearing nothing, he cried, "I see you — and I will make that the last union of your love!" And that voice was as loud as an angry Cyclops should have: Etna shuddered at the cry. I, terror-struck, plunged into the near sea; the hero, son of Symaethis, turned and fled, crying, "Help me, Galatea, I beg! Help me, her parents, and take me, about to die, into your realms!" The Cyclops pursued, and threw a piece torn from the mountain; and though only the farthest edge of the rock reached him, it buried all of Acis. But I did the one thing the Fates allowed: I made Acis take on his grandfather's power. Red blood ran from beneath the mass, and in a little the redness began to fade, and became the color of a stream muddied by rain, and cleared with time; then the thrown mass gaped, and through the cracks a living, tall reed sprang up, and the hollow mouth of the rock rang with leaping waters; and — a marvel — suddenly, up to the waist, a youth stood forth, his new horns wreathed with bending reeds, who, but that he was larger and all blue in the face, was Acis. But even so it was Acis, changed into a river; and the stream kept its old name.

Galatea ceased speaking, and, the company breaking up, the Nereids swim off in the calm waves. Scylla returns — for she does not dare trust herself to the open sea — and either wanders without clothes on the thirsty sand, or, when tired, finding a hidden nook of the water, cools her limbs in the enclosed wave. When, look — cutting the deep, a new dweller of the high sea, his limbs lately changed at Anthedon near Euboea, Glaucus comes, and, in his longing for the maiden he has seen, lingers and says whatever he thinks may hold her as she flees. Yet she flees, and, swift with fear, reaches the top of a mountain near the shore. In front of the sea a huge ridge rises to a single peak, curving far over the waves, treeless. Here she stops, and, safe in the place, not knowing whether he is a monster or a god, admires his color and his flowing hair that covers his shoulders and back, and how a coiling fish closes the end of his groin. He sees it, and, leaning on a nearby rock, says: "I am no monster, no wild beast, maiden, but a god of the water; nor have Proteus and Triton and Palaemon, son of Athamas, a greater right over the sea. Yet once I was mortal — but, owed even then to the deep, I was busy in it: for at one time I would haul the nets that drew the fish, at another, seated on a rock, I would work the line with the rod. There is a shore next to a green meadow, one part ringed by water, the other by grass, which no horned heifers had ever cropped, nor you, gentle sheep, nor shaggy goats; no busy bee took gathered blossoms from it, no festive garlands were made from it, no mower's hand had ever cut it. I was the first to sit on that turf, drying my dripping nets; and, to count my captive fish in order, I laid out on it those that chance had driven to my nets or their own credulity to my hooks. It is like a fiction — but what use to me to invent? — at the touch of the grass my prey began to stir, to shift its sides, and to move on land as in the sea; and while I paused and wondered, the whole crowd fled to the waves and left their new master and the shore. I stood amazed, and long in doubt sought the cause: whether some god had done it, or the juice of some herb. 'And what herb,' I said, 'has this power?' and with my hand I plucked the grass and chewed it. Scarcely had my throat swallowed the strange juices when suddenly I felt my heart throb within, and my breast seized with love of another nature; and I could not stay there, and cried, 'Land never to be revisited, farewell!' and sank my body beneath the sea. The sea-gods thought me worthy of a share in their honor, and asked Oceanus and Tethys to take from me whatever was mortal; and I was cleansed by them, and, a purging charm said over me nine times, was bidden to put my breast beneath a hundred streams. No delay: rivers from different springs, and whole seas, poured over my head. So far I can tell you what happened worth telling, so far I remember; the rest my mind did not feel. When it came back, I found myself, in all my body, another than I had been, and not the same in mind: then for the first time I saw this beard, green with its deep hue, and my flowing hair which I sweep along the wide seas, and my huge shoulders and blue arms, and my legs ending, curved, in a finny fish. But what use is this shape, what use to have pleased the sea-gods, what use to be a god, if you are not moved by these things?" As he spoke such things, and would have said more, Scylla left the god; and he, raging, stung by the refusal, made for the marvelous halls of Circe, daughter of the Sun.

Book XIV

And now Glaucus, the Euboean plougher of the swelling waves, had left behind Etna, laid on the Giant's jaws, and the fields of the Cyclops, that knew no harrow, no plow, that owed nothing to the yoked oxen; had left Zancle too, and the facing walls of Rhegium, and the ship-wrecking strait which, pressed between the two shores, holds the border of the Ausonian and Sicilian lands. Thence, swimming with his huge hands through the Etruscan seas, Glaucus came to the grassy hills and the halls of Circe, daughter of the Sun, full of many wild beasts. When he saw her, greetings given and received, he said: "Have pity, goddess, on a god, I pray! For you alone — if only I seem worthy — can ease this love. How great the power of herbs is, daughter of Titan, none knows better than I, who was changed by them. And that the cause of my madness may not be unknown to you: on the Italian shore, opposite the walls of Messina, I saw Scylla. I am ashamed to tell my promises, prayers, caresses, and scorned words; but you, if there is any power in incantation, move the charm with your holy lips, or, if some herb is stronger, use the proven force of a potent herb. I do not ask you to cure me and heal these wounds — there is no need of an end: let her share in the flame." But Circe — for no one has a nature more open to such fires, whether the cause is in herself or Venus, offended by her father's disclosure, makes it so — answered thus: "You would do better to court one who is willing, who wants the same, caught by an equal desire. You are worthy — surely you could be — to be courted of your own accord; and if you give hope, believe me, you will be courted. That you may not doubt it, and that confidence in your beauty not fail you — see, I, though a goddess, and daughter of the shining Sun, though I can do so much with charms and so much with herbs, vow to be yours. Scorn her who scorns you, requite her who follows you, and by one act take vengeance on two." To her attempt Glaucus said: "Sooner will leaves grow in the sea and seaweed spring on the mountaintops than my love change, while Scylla lives." The goddess was outraged; and, since she could not hurt him — nor, loving him, would — she turned her anger on the one preferred to herself; and, stung by the rebuff of her love, at once she grinds herbs infamous for their horrid juices, and mixes with the grinding the charms of Hecate, and puts on sea-blue robes, and comes out through the throng of fawning beasts from the middle of her hall; and, making for Rhegium, opposite the rocks of Zancle, she enters the waves boiling with the tides, and sets her steps on them as on solid ground, and skims dry-footed over the surface of the sea.

There was a little bay, curved into a bent bow, a favorite retreat of Scylla, where she used to withdraw from the heat of sea and sky, when the sun was highest and made the shortest shadows. This the goddess taints beforehand and fouls with monster-breeding drugs; on it she sprinkles the juices pressed from a noxious root, and thrice nine times, with her magic lips, she mutters over the dark charm of strange words. Scylla comes, and had gone in up to the waist, when she sees her loins made hideous with barking monsters; and, at first not believing they are parts of her own body, she flees and drives them off and dreads the rude mouths of the dogs — but those she flees she draws along with her; and, feeling for her thighs and legs and feet, she finds Cerberus-jaws in place of those parts. She stands on the rage of the dogs, and holds the backs of savage beasts beneath her cut-off loins and her jutting belly.

Glaucus, still in love, wept for her, and fled a marriage with Circe, who had used the power of herbs too cruelly. Scylla stayed in that place; and, when the chance was given, in her hatred of Circe she robbed Ulysses of his companions. Soon she would have sunk the Trojan ships too, had she not first been turned into a rock, which even now stands out with its crags — a rock the sailor avoids.

When the Trojan ships had passed her and greedy Charybdis with their oars, and were near the Ausonian shore, they were carried back by the winds to the Libyan coast. There the Sidonian Dido, doomed not to bear easily the loss of her Phrygian husband, received Aeneas into her home and her heart; and on a pyre built under the pretense of sacred rites she fell on the sword, and, herself deceived, deceived all. Fleeing again the new-built walls of the sandy land, and carried back to the seat of Eryx and faithful Acestes, he sacrifices and honors his father's tomb. He looses the ships that Juno's Iris had nearly burned, and passes the realm of the son of Hippotas, the lands smoking with hot sulphur, and leaves behind the rocks of the Sirens, daughters of Acheloüs; and, its pilot lost, the ship coasts Inarime and Prochyte and Pithecusae, set on a barren hill, named for its inhabitants. For the father of the gods, once loathing the frauds and perjuries of the Cercopes and the crimes of that cheating race, changed the men into ugly animals, so that the same beings could look both unlike and like men: he shrank their limbs, flattened and bent back their noses from the brow, furrowed their faces with an old man's wrinkles, and, their whole bodies clothed in yellow hair, sent them to these homes — and first he took away the use of words, and of the tongue made for dreadful perjury, and left them only the power to complain with a harsh screech.

When he had passed these and left the walls of Parthenope on the right, on the left he comes to the tomb of the tuneful son of Aeolus, and enters the shores of Cumae, a region of marsh-sedge, and the cave of the long-lived Sibyl, and begs that through Avernus he may visit his father's shade. She raised her face, long held on the ground, and at last, frenzied with the god within her, said: "You ask a great thing, hero greatest in deeds, whose hand was proved by the sword, whose devotion by the flames. Yet lay aside fear, Trojan: you will win your wish, and with me for guide you will know the halls of Elysium and the last realm of the world, and the dear image of your parent. To valor no way is closed." She spoke, and showed a branch gleaming with gold in the wood of Avernus's Juno, and bade him tear it from its stem. Aeneas obeyed, and saw the powers of dread Orcus, and his own ancestors, and the aged ghost of great-hearted Anchises; he learned, too, the laws of those regions, and what dangers he must face in new wars. Retracing his weary steps by the backward path, he eased his labor in talk with his Cumaean guide. And while he made the fearful journey through the dim twilight, he said: "Whether you are a goddess in person, or dearest to the gods, you will always be to me like a god, and I will confess I live by your gift, who willed that I visit the places of death and escape the death I saw. For these kindnesses, brought up to the airy world, I will build you a temple and give you the honors of incense." The seer looked at him and, drawing sighs, said: "I am no goddess, nor honor a human head with holy incense; and, that you may not err in ignorance — eternal light, and without end, was offered me, had my virginity yielded to Phoebus in love. While he hoped for it, while he wished to bribe me first with gifts, he said, 'Maiden of Cumae, choose what you wish: you will win your wish.' I, pointing to a heap of gathered dust, foolishly asked that as many birthdays might be mine as the dust held grains; it slipped me to ask as well for years still young. Yet he offered me these, and eternal youth, if I would yield to his love: scorning Phoebus's gift, I stay unwed. But now my happier years have turned their back, and sick old age comes on trembling feet, to be long endured. For now I have lived seven ages; still, to equal the number of the dust, three hundred harvests and three hundred vintages remain to be seen. The time will come when length of days will make me tiny from so large a frame, and my limbs, wasted by age, shrink to the least weight; I will seem never to have been loved or pleased a god; and Phoebus himself, perhaps, will not know me, or deny he loved me. So changed I will be, seen by none — yet known by my voice; the Fates will leave me my voice."

While the Sibyl told such things on the upward road, the Trojan Aeneas emerged from the Stygian realms into the Euboean city, and, the sacrifice duly made, came to the shore that did not yet bear his nurse's name. Here too, after long weariness of toil, Macareus of Neritos, a companion of much-tried Ulysses, had rested; and he recognized Achaemenides, once left among the crags of Etna, and, amazed to find him alive so unexpectedly, said, "What chance or god saves you, Achaemenides? Why does a foreign ship carry a Greek? What land does your bark seek?" To his questions Achaemenides — no longer shaggy in his dress, now his own man, his covering pinned with no thorns — replied: "May I see Polyphemus again, and those jaws dripping with human blood, if home and Ithaca are dearer to me than this ship, if I honor Aeneas less than my own father; and, though I did everything, I could never be grateful enough. That I speak and breathe and see the sky and the sun's light — can I be ungrateful and forget it? Through him this life of mine did not go into the Cyclops's mouth; and though even now I left the light of life, I would be buried in a tomb, or at least not in that belly. What were my feelings then — unless terror took all sense — when, left behind, I saw you making for the deep? I longed to shout, but feared to betray myself to the enemy: even Ulysses' shout nearly ruined your ship. I saw him, when he tore a huge rock from the mountain and hurled it into the middle of the waves; I saw him again throwing vast stones with his giant arm, as if driven by a siege-engine, and I dreaded, forgetting I was no longer aboard, that the wave or the wind might sink the ship. And when your flight had saved you from a sure death, he paced all Etna, groaning, groped the woods with his hands, and, robbed of his eye, stumbled on the rocks, and, stretching his gore-stained arms to the sea, cursed the Greek race, and said, 'Oh, if some chance would bring me back Ulysses, or one of his men, on whom my rage might feed — whose guts I might eat, whose living limbs I might tear with my hand, whose blood might flood my throat, whose crushed limbs might quiver under my teeth: how slight the loss of my stolen sight would be!' This, and more, in his fury. A ghastly horror seized me as I watched his face still wet with slaughter, his cruel hands, the empty ring where the eye had been, his limbs, his beard clotted with human blood. Death was before my eyes — yet that was the least of my woes; and I thought he would seize me now, now sink my guts in his; and there stuck in my mind the picture of that time when I saw two bodies of my companions dashed three and four times against the ground, while he, lying over them like a shaggy lion, stowed their guts and flesh and white-marrowed bones and half-living limbs in his greedy belly. A trembling took me; I stood pale and bloodless, watching him chew and belch his bloody feast and vomit lumps mixed with wine; and I fancied such a fate readied for wretched me. Hiding many days, trembling at every sound, both fearing death and longing to die, staving off hunger with acorns and grass mixed with leaves, alone, helpless, hopeless, given over to death, after a long time I saw this ship far off, and by signs I begged for rescue, and ran to the shore, and moved them: a Trojan ship took in a Greek! Now do you tell your adventures, dearest of comrades, and your leader's, and the crew's that trusted the sea with you."

Macareus tells how Aeolus, grandson of Hippotas, rules the Etruscan deep and keeps the winds in prison; how the Ithacan chief received them shut in an ox-hide, a wondrous gift, and for nine days sailed with a fair breeze and saw the land he sought; how, when the dawn after the ninth arose, his companions, beaten by envy and desire for booty, thinking it gold, cut the fastenings of the winds; how with these the ship went back through the very waves it had come, and returned to the harbor of the Aeolian king. "From there," he said, "we came to the ancient city of Lamus the Laestrygonian; Antiphates reigned in that land. I was sent to him, two others with me, and safety was barely won by flight for me and one companion; the third of us stained the Laestrygonian's wicked mouth with his own blood. Antiphates pressed us as we fled and roused his band; they gathered and hurled stones and beams, and sank men and sank ships; only one, that carried us and Ulysses himself, escaped. Grieving for the lost part of our companions, and lamenting much, we came to the land you see far off — far off, believe me, be that island I saw! And you, most righteous of the Trojans, son of a goddess — for, the war ended, you are not to be called an enemy, Aeneas — I warn you: flee the shores of Circe!

"We too, our ship moored on Circe's shore, remembering Antiphates and the savage Cyclops, refused to go and enter her unknown house; but we were chosen by lot to go: the lot sent me and faithful Polites, and Eurylochus, and Elpenor, too fond of wine, and twice nine companions to Circe's walls. As soon as we reached them and stood at the threshold, a thousand wolves, and bears and lionesses mixed with wolves, made us fear at the meeting — but none was to be feared, none would wound our bodies; rather, they wagged coaxing tails in the air and, fawning, followed our steps, until the maidservants received us and led us through marble halls to their mistress. She sits in a lovely alcove on her wonted throne, clad in a shining robe, and over it wrapped in a garment of gold. The Nereids and nymphs, who card no fleece with moving fingers nor draw out threads, arrange herbs and sort the scattered flowers into baskets, and the plants of varied hue; she herself sets the tasks they do, herself knows the use of each leaf, and what harmony there is in the mixture, and, attentive, weighs the herbs she has portioned. When she saw us, greetings given and received, she brightened her face and answered our wishes with good omens; and without delay she orders roasted barley grains mixed, and honey, and the strength of wine, and curds with pressed milk, and secretly adds drugs to lurk beneath the sweetness. We took the cups given by her sacred hand; and as soon as, thirsty, we drained them with parched mouths, and the cruel goddess touched the tops of our hair with her wand, I began — I am ashamed, but I will tell it — to bristle with hair, and could no longer speak, but gave a harsh growl for words, and fell face-down to the ground, and felt my mouth harden into a curved snout, my neck swell with muscle, and, with the part that had just now taken the cup, I made my footprints. With the rest who had suffered the same — so powerful are the drugs — I was shut in a sty; and we saw that Eurylochus alone kept his shape: he alone fled the offered cup. Had he not dodged it, I would be one of the bristle-clad beasts even now, nor would Ulysses, told of the disaster by him, have come as our avenger to Circe. The peace-bringing Cyllenian had given him a white flower — the gods call it moly, held by a black root; safe with it and with heaven's counsel, he entered Circe's house, and, called to the treacherous cups, he pushed back her hand as she tried to stroke his hair with the wand, and, drawing his sword, frightened her off. Then faith was pledged and hands given, and, received into her bed, he asked his companions' bodies as the marriage-gift. We are sprinkled with the kinder juice of a strange herb, struck on the head with the reversed wand, and words are said, the reverse of the words before; the more she chants, the more we rise up from the ground, the bristles fall, the cleft leaves our split feet, our shoulders return, our arms are set below the upper arms; weeping, we embrace our weeping chief, and cling to his neck, and speak no words before those that prove us grateful.

"A year's stay held us there, and in so long a time I saw much and heard much — this too among many, which one of the four maids appointed for such rites privately told me. For while Circe lingered apart with my chief, she showed me a statue of snow-white marble, bearing a woodpecker on its head, set in a sacred shrine and marked with many wreaths. When I asked and wished to know who he was, and why he was honored in the holy shrine, and why he bore that bird, she said: 'Hear, Macareus, and learn from this too what my mistress's power is; give your mind to my words.

"'Picus, the son of Saturn, was a king in Ausonia, keen on horses good for war. His form was such as you see; you may see his beauty here, and judge the true from the copied image. His spirit matched his beauty; and he had not yet, in his years, seen the Greek games at Elis held four times. He had turned to himself the Dryads born on the Latian hills; the fountain-powers wooed him, the Naiads that Albula and Numicus, that Anio's waters and the too-short Almo, and headlong Nar and shady Farfar bore, and those that haunt the woodland pool of Scythian Diana and the neighboring lakes; yet, slighting all, he loved one nymph, whom on the Palatine hill Venilia is said to have borne to two-faced Janus. When she first ripened to marriageable years, she was given to Laurentine Picus, preferred to all; rare indeed in beauty, but rarer in the art of singing, whence she was called Canens. She used to move woods and rocks with her voice, to tame wild beasts, to stay the long rivers, to hold the wandering birds.

"'While she sang her songs in a woman's voice, Picus had gone from his house into the Laurentine fields to spear the native boars, pressing the back of a spirited horse, carrying two javelins in his left hand, in a purple cloak clasped with yellow gold. The daughter of the Sun had come into the same wood; and, to pluck fresh herbs on the fruitful hills, she had left the Circaean fields named for her. As soon as, hidden in the thickets, she saw the youth, she was stunned; the herbs she had gathered fell from her hand, and a flame seemed to wander through all her marrow. When she gathered her mind from that strong heat, she was ready to confess her wish; but the speed of his horse and the ringing guards kept her from approaching. 'You will not escape me,' she said, 'though you be caught up by the wind, if I know myself, if all power in herbs has not vanished, and my charms do not fail me.' She spoke, and made a phantom of a false boar, with no body, and bade it run past the king's eyes and seem to go into a dense-timbered wood, where the forest is thickest and no horse can enter. Without delay Picus, unknowing, follows the shadow of the prey, and, quick, leaves the steaming back of his steed and, chasing a vain hope, wanders on foot in the deep wood. She frames prayers and says magic words, and worships strange gods with a strange charm, by which she is wont to blot the face of the snow-white Moon and draw thirsty clouds over her father's head. Then too, at the chanting, the sky thickens and the ground breathes fog, and his companions wander on the blind paths, and the king's guard is gone. Finding place and time, she said, 'By your eyes, which have caught mine, and by this beautiful form, most handsome one, which makes me, a goddess, your suppliant, favor my love, and take the all-seeing Sun for your father-in-law, and do not, in your cruelty, scorn Circe, daughter of Titan.' She spoke; he fiercely rejects her and her prayers, and says, 'Whoever you are, I am not yours; another holds me captive, and long, I pray, may she hold me, nor will I harm the marriage bond with a stranger's love, while the Fates keep Canens, daughter of Janus, for me.' The daughter of Titan, having tried her prayers in vain, said, 'You will not do it unpunished, nor go back to Canens; and by the event you will learn what one slighted, what one in love, what a woman can do — and Circe is in love, and slighted, and a woman!' Then twice she turned to the west, twice to the east, three times touched the youth with her wand, spoke three charms. He fled — and, marveling that he ran faster than usual, saw wings on his body; and, angry that he was added, a new bird, to the Latian woods, he strikes the hard oaks with his hard beak and, enraged, wounds the long branches; his wings took the purple of his cloak; the gold that had clasped his garment became feathers, and his neck was ringed with tawny gold, and nothing of the old Picus remained but the name.

"'Meanwhile his companions, having called on Picus in vain through the fields and found him nowhere, meet Circe — for now she had thinned the air and let the clouds be scattered by wind and sun — and charge her with true crimes, and demand back their king, and threaten force, and make to attack her with cruel weapons. She scatters harmful venom and poisonous juices, and summons Night and the gods of Night from Erebus and Chaos, and calls on Hecate with long howls. Wondrous to tell, the woods leaped from their place, the ground groaned, the nearby tree paled, the grass grew wet with sprinkled drops of blood, the stones seemed to give hoarse lowings, the dogs to bark, the earth to crawl with black snakes, and the thin ghosts of the silent dead to flit. The crowd, stunned by the marvels, is afraid; she touched their frightened, wondering faces with her poisoned wand, and at its touch the monstrous shapes of various beasts came upon the young men: none kept his own form.

"'The setting sun had now come down on the Tartessian shores, and in vain, with eyes and heart, Canens awaited her husband; her servants and people run through all the woods and carry lights to meet him. Nor is it enough for the nymph to weep and tear her hair and beat her breast — though she does all this — she rushes out and, wild, wanders the Latian fields. Six nights, and as many returning lights of the sun, saw her, without sleep or food, going over ridges and valleys wherever chance led. Last of all Tiber saw her, worn out with grief and wandering, laying her body on his long bank; there, weeping, she poured out words tuned to her very grief in a thin voice, as the dying swan sings its funeral song. At last, melting to her thin marrow with grief, she pined away and by degrees vanished into thin air; yet fame marked the spot, which the ancient Muses rightly called Canens, after the nymph's name.'

"Many such things were told me and seen by me in that long year. Idle and slow from disuse, we were bidden again to take to the sea, again to set sail; the daughter of Titan had said that doubtful ways, and a vast voyage, and the perils of the cruel sea awaited us. I was afraid, I confess, and, reaching this shore, here I stayed."

Macareus had finished; and the nurse of Aeneas, buried in a marble urn, had this short verse on her tomb:

Here Caieta was burned by her foster-child of proven love, snatched from the Argive fire, with the fire that was her due.

The moored cable is loosed from the grassy bank, and they leave far behind the wiles and the halls of the ill-famed goddess, and seek the groves where the Tiber, dark with the shade of trees, breaks into the sea with its yellow sands. Aeneas gains the house and the daughter of Latinus, son of Faunus — but not without war. War is waged with a fierce nation, and Turnus rages for his betrothed wife. All Etruria meets Latium in battle, and long is victory sought with anxious arms. Each side swells its strength with foreign force, and many defend the Rutulians, many the Trojan camp; nor had Aeneas come in vain to the walls of Evander, but Venulus came in vain to the city of the exiled Diomedes. He had founded a great city under Iapygian Daunus, and held the lands given in dower; but when Venulus had done Turnus's bidding and asked for aid, the Aetolian hero pleaded his want of resources: he would not commit his father-in-law's people to war, nor had he men of his own nation to arm. "And, that you may not think this a pretext — though my grief be renewed at the bitter memory — I will bear to tell it. After lofty Ilium burned, and Pergamum had fed the Greek flames, and the Narycian hero, for a maiden ravished, brought on all the vengeance he alone deserved for the maiden, we were scattered and driven by winds over hostile seas; we Greeks endured lightning, darkness, rain, and the wrath of sky and sea, and Caphereus, the crown of our disaster. Not to hold you with these sad events in order — Greece then might have seemed worthy even of Priam's tear. Yet the care of armed Minerva saved me, snatched from the waves; but again I was driven from my fathers' fields, and kindly Venus exacted, in revenge, the penalty for her old wound, and I bore such toils on the deep sea, such amid arms on land, that often I called those happy whom the common storm and grim Caphereus swallowed in the threatening waves — and I wished I had been one of them. My companions, having endured the utmost in war and on the sea, lost heart and asked an end of their wandering; but Acmon, hot of temper and now made bitter by disaster, said, 'What is left, men, that your patience should refuse to bear? What has Cytherea, suppose she wished, that she can do beyond this? For while worse is feared, there is room for prayers; but where one's lot is the worst, fear is trodden underfoot, and the height of misfortune is free of care. Let her hear it herself, if she likes; let her hate, as she does, all the men under Diomedes; still we all scorn her hatred — our great power is bought at no great price.' With such words the Pleuronian Acmon provokes Venus against her will and revives her old anger. His words please few; the greater part of us, his friends, rebuke Acmon; and as he made to answer, his voice and its passage grew thin, his hair turned to feathers, his new-formed neck and breast and back were covered with down, his arms took longer plumes, his elbows curved into light wings; a great part of his foot took toes, his mouth stiffened and hardened with horn and ended in a point. Lycus and Idas, and Nycteus with Rhexenor, and Abas marveled at him; and while they marveled, they took the same shape, and the greater part of the company flew up and wheeled around the oars with beating wings. If you ask the form of these sudden birds — not swans', but nearest to white swans'. With difficulty I, son-in-law of Iapygian Daunus, hold these homes and the parched fields, with a very small remnant of my men."

So far the grandson of Oeneus. Venulus leaves the Calydonian realm, and the Peucetian bays, and the Messapian fields, in which he sees a cave that, shaded by a dense grove and trickling with a smooth stream, half-goat Pan now holds, but once the nymphs held. An Apulian shepherd scared them from that spot and first moved them with a sudden fright; then, when their minds returned and they scorned him as he followed, they danced in time, moving their feet; the shepherd mocked them, and, aping them with rustic leaps, added coarse country abuse in filthy words, nor was his mouth silent until a tree closed his throat — for he is a tree, and by its sap you may know his manners: the wild olive, by its bitter berries, marks the vileness of his tongue; the roughness of his words passed into them.

When the envoy had come back with word that the Aetolian arms were refused them, the Rutulians waged the prepared war without those forces, and much blood was spilled on both sides. Look — Turnus bears the greedy torches against the pine-built ships, and those the waves had spared now dread the fire. And now the flame was burning the pitch and wax and the rest of the fuel, and climbing the tall mast to the sails, and the benches of the curved ships smoked, when the holy Mother of the Gods, remembering these pines were cut on the heights of Ida, filled the air with the clash of struck cymbals and the sound of the blown boxwood pipe, and, borne through the yielding air by her tamed lions, said: "In vain, Turnus, you hurl the flames with sacrilegious hand: I will save them, and the devouring fire shall not, with my leave, burn parts and limbs of my own groves." As the goddess spoke, it thundered, and after the thunder heavy showers fell with bounding hail; the sons of Astraeus stir the air and the swelling sea with sudden clashes and rush to battle. The kindly Mother, using the force of one of them, snapped the hempen cables of the Phrygian fleet, and carries the ships headlong, and sinks them beneath the sea. Their wood softened and changed to flesh, the curved sterns become the features of a head, the oars taper into fingers and swimming feet, the side stays what it was before, and the keel laid beneath the middle of the ship changes to serve as a spine; the cordage becomes soft hair, the yards become arms; their color is blue, as before; and, as sea-Naiads, with their maiden sports they stir the very waves they once dreaded, and, born on the harsh mountains, they haunt the flowing sea, untouched by their origin. Yet, not forgetting how many dangers they often bore on the deep, they often put a hand under tossed ships — unless one carried Greeks. Still keeping in mind the Phrygian disaster, they hated the Greeks; and with glad faces they looked on the wreck of the ship of Neritos, and with pleasure saw the ship of Alcinous harden on the breakers and stone grow over the wood.

There was a hope that, the fleet made living as sea-nymphs, the Rutulian might give up the war in fear at the marvel; but he holds on, and each side has its own gods, and — what is like the gods — they have courage; and now they seek not a dowry-kingdom, nor a father-in-law's scepter, nor you, maiden Lavinia, but only to win, and wage war for shame of stopping. At last Venus sees her son's arms victorious, and Turnus falls; Ardea falls, called mighty while Turnus lived. After the barbarous fire took it and the roofs sank in the warm ash, a bird, then known for the first time, flew up from the middle of the heap and beat the ashes with its wings. Its cry, its leanness, its pallor, all that befits a captured city — even the city's name — stayed in the bird; and Ardea itself is mourned by the beating of its wings.

And now the valor of Aeneas had forced all the gods, and Juno herself, to end their old wrath; when, the power of rising Iulus well founded, the hero, son of Cytherea, was ripe for heaven. Venus had appealed to the gods above, and, hanging round her father's neck, said: "Father, never unkind to me at any time, be gentlest now, I pray, and grant my Aeneas, who from my blood made you a grandfather, a godhead — even the lowest kind — so long as you grant some! It is enough to have seen the joyless realm once, once to have crossed the Stygian streams." The gods agreed; nor did the royal wife keep her face unmoved, but nodded assent with a calmed look. Then the father said, "You are worthy of the gift of heaven, both you who ask and he for whom you ask: take, daughter, what you wish." He spoke; she rejoices and thanks her father, and, borne by her yoked doves through the light air, comes to the Laurentine shore, where Numicius, hidden in reeds, winds to the neighboring sea with his river waters. Him she bids to wash from Aeneas whatever is subject to death, and carry it beneath the sea in his silent course. The horned river does Venus's bidding, and with his waters washes away from Aeneas whatever was mortal, and sprinkled him; his best part remained. His mother anointed the cleansed body with divine perfume, touched his face with ambrosia mixed with sweet nectar, and made him a god, whom the people of Quirinus call Indiges, and received with temple and altars.

From then Alba and the Latin state were under Ascanius of the two names; Silvius followed him; sprung of him, Latinus took back the old name with the scepter; famous Alba came after Latinus; from him was Epytus; after him Capetus and Capys — but Capys was first; Tiberinus took the kingdom after them, and, drowned in the Etruscan river's waters, gave his name to the water; of him were born Remulus and fierce Acrota. Remulus, the elder, an imitator of the lightning, died by a lightning-stroke. Acrota, more moderate than his brother, handed the scepter to brave Aventinus, who lies buried on the same hill he ruled, and gave the hill his name; and now Proca held the height of the Palatine race.

Under this king lived Pomona, than whom no Hamadryad of Latium tended her gardens more skillfully, nor was any keener on the fruit of the trees; from that she takes her name. She cares not for woods or streams, but loves the country and the boughs that bear thriving fruit; her hand is not weighed with a javelin, but with a curved pruning-knife, with which now she checks the too-lush growth and reins in the branches straying every way, now slits the bark and sets in a graft and gives sap to a stranger nursling; nor does she let them feel thirst, but waters the curling fibers of the drinking root with running streams. This is her love, this her pursuit; and no desire has she for love. Yet, fearing the force of the country folk, she shuts her orchard within, and forbids and flees the approach of men. What did the Satyrs, a young crew apt at the dance, not do — and the Pans with pine-wreathed horns, and Silvanus ever younger than his years, and the god who scares thieves with pruning-hook or groin — to win her? But Vertumnus surpassed even these in love, and was no luckier than they. O how often, in the guise of a hardy reaper, he carried ears of corn in a basket, and was the very picture of a reaper! Often, his temples bound with fresh hay, he could seem to have turned the mown grass; often he carried a goad in his sturdy hand, so you would swear he had just unyoked the weary oxen; given a pruning-knife, he was a woodman and a vine-pruner; he put on a ladder — you would think he meant to pick fruit; he was a soldier with a sword, a fisher with a rod taken up; in short, by many shapes he often found his way in, to enjoy the sight of her beauty.

He too, his temples wreathed with a colored kerchief, leaning on a staff, white hair set about his brow, took on the shape of an old woman, entered the well-kept gardens, admired the fruit, and said, "So much the better off are you!" and gave her, thus praised, a few kisses, such as no real old woman ever gave; and, stooping, sat on a clod, looking up at the boughs bent under the weight of autumn. There was an elm opposite, splendid with shining grapes; when he had praised it, with the vine joined to it, he said: "But if that trunk stood single, without the vine, it would have nothing to seek it for but its leaves; and this vine, which finds rest joined to the elm, if it were not wedded to it, would lie flat on the ground. Yet you are not moved by the example of this tree; you flee marriage and do not care to be joined. Would that you wished it! Helen would not be courted by more suitors, nor she who stirred the Lapiths' battles, nor the wife of over-slow Ulysses. Even now, when you flee and turn from those who court you, a thousand men desire you, and demigods and gods, and whatever powers hold the Alban hills. But if you are wise, if you would make a good match and hear an old woman — who loves you more than all of them, more than you believe — reject a common match, and choose Vertumnus for your bed! And take me as his surety: he is not better known to himself than to me; he does not roam wandering over the whole world, he haunts only these places; nor, like most of your suitors, does he love the one he has just seen: you will be his first and last flame, and to you alone he devotes his years. Add that he is young, has the natural gift of grace, will shape himself neatly into any form, and will become whatever he is bidden, though you bid him be everything. And what of this — you love the same things: he is the first to have the fruit that is your delight, and holds your gifts in his glad hand! But now he longs neither for fruit plucked from the tree, nor the herbs the garden grows with their sweet juices, nor anything but you. Pity his passion, and believe that he who woos you is here in person, pleading with my lips. And fear the avenging gods, and the Idalian goddess who hates hard hearts, and the vengeful anger of the goddess of Rhamnus! And, that you may fear them the more — for old age has let me know many things — I will tell you a story famous through all Cyprus, by which you may easily be bent and softened.

Iphis, born of a humble family, had seen the noble Anaxarete, sprung from the line of ancient Teucer — had seen her, and felt the fire in all his bones; and, struggling long, when he could not conquer his madness by reason, he came a suppliant to her doors. Now he confessed his wretched love to her nurse and begged her, by the hopes she placed in her charge, not to be hard to him; now, flattering each of her many servants, he sought their favor with an anxious voice. Often he gave his words to be carried on coaxing tablets; sometimes he hung garlands wet with the dew of his tears on the doorposts, and laid his soft side on the hard threshold, and reproached the cruel bolt. She, deafer than the sea rising when the Kids are setting, harder than the iron the Norican fire refines, and than the rock still held by its living root, scorns and laughs at him, and to her cruel deeds she adds proud words, and robs the lover even of hope. Iphis, unable to bear the torments of long grief, endured them no longer, and before her doors spoke these last words: "You win, Anaxarete, and you will have no more annoyance from me: prepare your glad triumph, call on Paean, and crown yourself with shining laurel! For you win, and I die willingly; rejoice, woman of iron! At least you will be forced to praise something in me, some point in which I please you, and you will confess my merit. Yet remember that my love for you ended no sooner than my life, and that at one moment I lose a twofold light. Nor will report come to you as the messenger of my death: I myself will come, doubt not, and be seen in person, that you may feed your cruel eyes on my lifeless body. But if you gods above see the deeds of mortals, remember me — my tongue can pray no more — and make me spoken of in the long ages, and give to fame the time you have taken from my life." He spoke, and, lifting his swimming eyes and pale arms to the doorposts he had so often decked with wreaths, when he had tied a noose's cord to the top of the doors, he said, "Do these garlands please you, cruel, unfeeling woman?" and put his head in — but even then turned toward her — and hung, a hapless burden, by his strangled throat. The door, struck by the motion of his quivering feet, seemed to give a sound as of one groaning much, and, flying open, betrayed the deed; the servants cried out, and, having lifted him in vain — for his father was dead — carried him to his mother's house. She took him into her lap, and, embracing her son's cold limbs, when she had said the words natural to wretched mothers and done the deeds of wretched mothers, led the tearful funeral through the middle of the city and carried the ghastly limbs on the bier to be burned. By chance her house was near the road where the mournful procession passed, and the sound of wailing came to the ears of hard Anaxarete, whom now an avenging god pursued. Moved, though, she said, "Let us watch this pitiful funeral," and went up to a high room with wide windows; and scarcely had she well seen Iphis laid on the bier when her eyes stiffened, and, a pallor coming on, the warm blood fled her body; and trying to step back she stuck fast, trying to turn her face away she could not, and little by little the stone, long since in her hard breast, took over her limbs. And, that you not think this a fiction, Salamis still keeps the statue in the maiden's shape, and has a temple too under the name of Venus the Watcher. Remembering this, O nymph of mine, lay aside your long disdain and join yourself to one who loves you: so may no spring cold nip your budding fruit, nor rough winds strike off the blossoms!"

When the god, fit for every shape, had said this in vain, he returned to his young form and took off the old woman's garb, and appeared to her such as when the brightest image of the sun overcomes the facing clouds and shines out with nothing to block it; and he made ready force — but force was not needed, and the nymph was caught by the god's beauty and felt an answering wound.

Next the soldiery of wicked Amulius held sway over Ausonia; and by his grandsons' aid aged Numitor won back the kingdom he had lost; and on the festival of Pales the walls of the City were founded. Tatius and the Sabine fathers wage war, and, the way to the citadel laid open, Tarpeia by just retribution lost her life, the arms heaped on her. Then the men of Cures, like silent wolves, hushed their voices with their lips and fell on the bodies overcome by sleep, and made for the gates that the son of Ilia had shut with a strong bolt. But Juno, daughter of Saturn, opened one herself, and made no sound at the turning hinge; Venus alone felt that the bars of the gate had fallen, and would have shut it, but that a god may never undo another god's act.
