The Ausonian Naiads held a spot near the temple of Janus, dripping with a cold spring; of these she begs help. Nor did the nymphs resist the goddess's just request, and gave vent to the springs and streams of their fountain; yet the way to the open temple of Janus was not yet blocked, nor had the water closed the road. They set pale sulphur beneath the plenteous spring and kindled the hollow channels with smoking pitch. By these and other means the heat pierced to the depths of the spring; and you, waters, that so lately dared to rival the cold of the Alps, now yield not to the flames themselves! The two posts smoked with the flaming spray, and the gate, promised in vain to the fierce Sabines, was blocked by the new-made fountain, until the warlike soldiers could arm; and when Romulus had led them out, and the Roman ground was covered with Sabine bodies and with its own, and the wicked sword had mixed the son-in-law's blood with the father-in-law's gore, they agreed to end the war in peace, not to fight it out with the sword, and that Tatius should share the throne.

Tatius was dead, and you, Romulus, were giving laws in common to both peoples, when Mars, his helmet laid aside, addressed the father of gods and men: "The time has come, father — since the Roman state stands on a great foundation and no longer hangs on one guardian — to pay the reward promised to me and to your deserving grandson, and, taking him from earth, to set him in heaven. You once said to me, with the council of the gods present — for I remember, and marked the loving words in a mindful heart — 'There will be one whom you will raise to the blue of heaven.' Let the sum of your words be made good!" The all-powerful god nodded, hid the air in thick clouds, and terrified the City with thunder and lightning. Mars knew this was the signal given him for the promised removal; and, leaning on his lance, he fearlessly mounted behind his steeds, laden on the blood-stained pole, cracked the lash, and, gliding down the steep air, stood on the top of the wooded Palatine, and took away the son of Ilia as he was giving royal laws to his own Quirites: his mortal body dissolved through the thin air, as a leaden bullet shot from a wide sling melts in mid-sky; a beautiful form came on, worthier of the high couches of heaven — the shape of Quirinus in his royal robe.

His wife mourned him as lost, when royal Juno bade Iris go down to Hersilia along her curving path and give the bereft woman her commands: "O matron, chief glory of the Latin and the Sabine race, most worthy to have been the wife of so great a man, and now of Quirinus, stop your weeping; and, if you wish to see your husband, follow me to the grove that greens on the hill of Quirinus and shades the temple of the Roman king." Iris obeyed, and, gliding to earth along her painted bow, spoke to Hersilia the bidden words; she, scarcely lifting her eyes with a modest face, said, "O goddess — for I cannot say who you are, yet it is clear you are a goddess — lead me, oh lead me, and show me my husband's face! If the Fates grant me but once to see it, I will confess I have looked on heaven." Without delay she climbed the hill of Romulus with the daughter of Thaumas; there a star fell from the sky to the earth, and, kindled by its light, Hersilia's hair went up with the star into the air. Her the founder of the Roman city caught in his well-known hands, and, with her body, changed her old name, and called her Hora — the goddess who now is joined with Quirinus.

Book XV

Meanwhile one is sought who can bear so great a weight, and succeed so great a king; and Fame, the herald of truth, marks out famous Numa for the throne. Not content to know the rites of the Sabine nation, in his large mind he conceives greater things, and inquires into the nature of the world. Love of this pursuit led him to leave his country and Cures and travel to the city of the stranger Hercules; and when he asked what founder had set Greek walls on the Italian shore, one of the elder natives, not ignorant of the old days, told him:

The son of Jove, enriched with the oxen of Iberia, is said to have reached the Lacinian shore from the ocean, after a lucky voyage; and, while his herd strayed over the soft pastures, to have entered the home of great Croton, no unwelcoming house, and rested from his long labor; and, at parting, to have said, "In the time of your grandsons this will be the site of a city" — and the promise came true. For there was a certain Myscelus, son of Alemon, an Argive, most dear to the gods of that age. Leaning over him as he lay heavy with sleep, the club-bearer said, "Come, leave your native home; go, seek the pebbly waters of the far-off Aesar" — and threatened many fearful things if he did not obey; then sleep and the god departed together. The son of Alemon rose and turned the fresh vision over in his silent mind, and long his purpose fought within him: the god bids him go, the laws forbid his leaving, and death is set as the penalty for one who would change his country. The bright Sun had hidden his shining head in the ocean, and thickest Night had raised her starry face, when the same god seemed to be there and give the same commands, and threaten more and heavier things if he did not obey. He was afraid, and at once made ready to move his home to a new land; a murmur rose in the city, and he was charged with despising the laws. When the case had been made, and the crime was plain, proved without a witness, the accused, in squalid garb, lifting his face and hands to the gods above, cried, "O you for whom the twice-six labors won the right of heaven, help me, I pray — for you were the cause of my crime!" It was the old custom to condemn the accused with black pebbles, to acquit them with white; and so now the grim sentence was passed, and every black pebble was dropped into the pitiless urn. But when it was turned over to pour out the pebbles for counting, the color of them all had changed from black to white; and the verdict, made favorable by the power of Hercules, freed the son of Alemon. He thanked his father, the son of Amphitryon, and with favoring winds sailed the Ionian sea, passed Spartan Tarentum and Sybaris and Salentine Neretum and the bay of Thurium and the fields of Iapyx, and, scarcely having coasted the lands that face those seas, found the fated mouth of the river Aesar, and, not far off, a mound under which the earth covered the sacred bones of Croton; and there, on the bidden ground, he founded his walls, and drew the name of the buried man onto the city. Such, by sure tradition, was the origin of that place and of the city set on the Italian borders.

There was a man here, Samian by birth, who had fled both Samos and its masters and, in hatred of tyranny, was a willing exile. In his mind he approached the gods, far off in the region of heaven, and what nature denied to human sight he drank in with the eyes of his heart; and when he had searched out all things with mind and watchful care, he gave them to the public to learn, and taught the crowds who sat in silence and marveled — the origins of the great universe, the causes of things, what nature is, what god, whence the snow, what the source of lightning, whether Jupiter or the winds thunder when the cloud is split, what shakes the earth, by what law the stars move, and whatever else lies hidden. He was the first to forbid animals to be served at table, and the first to open his lips — learned indeed, but not believed — in words like these:

Spare yourselves, mortals, from defiling your bodies with wicked food! There is corn; there are apples that bow the boughs with their weight, and swelling grapes on the vines; there are sweet herbs, and some that can be softened and made mild by fire; and the milky stream is not denied you, nor honey fragrant with the thyme-flower. The lavish earth heaps up her riches and her gentle food, and offers feasts without slaughter and blood. Beasts satisfy their hunger with flesh — and not even all of them, for the horse and the sheep and the herds live on grass; but those whose nature is wild and savage — Armenian tigers and raging lions, and bears with wolves — delight in a bloody meal. Alas, what a crime it is for flesh to be buried in flesh, for one greedy body to grow fat on another crammed into it, for one living creature to live by the death of another! In such abundance, which the earth, best of mothers, brings forth, does nothing please you but to gnaw the sad wounds with cruel teeth and bring back the ways of the Cyclops? Can you not calm the hunger of a greedy, ill-ruled belly unless you first destroy another?

But that old age we have named the Golden was blessed in the fruit of the trees and the herbs the ground rears, and did not stain the mouth with blood. Then the birds moved their wings safely through the air, the hare wandered fearless in the open fields, and no credulity had hung the fish on the hook; all was without ambush, fearing no trickery, and full of peace. But when someone — whoever he was, no good adviser — envied this simple diet and buried a meal of flesh in his greedy belly, he made a road for wickedness; and the steel, first stained with blood, may well have grown warm from the slaughter of wild beasts — and that would have been enough. We admit that creatures which seek our destruction may be killed without breach of piety; but, though they might be killed, still they were not to be eaten. From there the wrong went further: the pig is thought to have first earned death, because it grubbed up the seeds with its snout and cut off the year's hope; the goat, for gnawing the vine, is led to the altars of avenging Bacchus — their own faults ruined the two. But what have you deserved, sheep, a gentle flock, born to tend to men, who carry nectar in your full udders, who give your wool for our soft clothing, and help us more by your life than your death? What have the oxen deserved, a beast without fraud or guile, harmless, simple, born to bear labor? Ungrateful indeed, and unworthy of the harvest's gift, is the man who could slaughter his own field-tiller the moment he had lifted off the weight of the curved plow, who could strike with the axe that neck worn bare by work, through which he had so often renewed the hard field and won so many harvests. And it is not enough to commit such a crime: they have charged the gods themselves with it, and believe a power in heaven can rejoice in the killing of the toiling ox. A victim without blemish, and finest in form — for being fair brings ruin — decked with fillets and gold, is set before the altars and, unknowing, hears the prayer, and sees the grain it helped to grow laid between its horns, and, struck, stains with its blood the knives it perhaps saw before in the clear water. At once they examine the entrails torn from the living breast and search the gods' minds in them; and then — so great is man's hunger for forbidden food — you dare to eat! Do not, I beg, and turn your minds to my warning: when you give the limbs of slaughtered oxen to your palate, know and feel that you are chewing your own farmhands.

And since a god moves my lips, I will duly follow the god who moves them, and open my own Delphi and the very sky, and unlock the oracles of a lofty mind. I will sing great things, never traced by the wits of old, things long hidden. It delights me to go among the high stars, to leave the earth and this dull place and ride on the clouds and stand on the shoulders of mighty Atlas, and look down from afar on men wandering aimlessly, lacking reason, and, trembling and fearing death, to hearten them thus and unroll the order of fate.

O race stunned by the dread of icy death, why fear the Styx, the shadows, the empty names, the poets' stuff, the bogeys of a false world? Bodies, whether the pyre takes them with flame or age with decay, cannot suffer any harm. Souls have no death, and, leaving their former seat, they live in new homes and dwell there, received. I myself — for I remember — in the time of the Trojan war was Euphorbus, son of Panthous, in whose breast once stuck the heavy spear of the younger son of Atreus; lately I recognized the shield, the burden of my left arm, in the temple of Juno at Argos! All things change, nothing perishes: the spirit wanders and comes from there to here, from here to there, and takes possession of any limbs it will, and passes from beasts into human bodies and ours into beasts, and never in any time does it die; and as pliant wax is stamped with new figures and does not stay as it was or keep the same shape, but is still the same wax, so I teach that the soul is always the same, but migrates into different forms. Therefore, lest piety be beaten by the belly's craving, cease, I warn you, to drive out kindred souls by wicked slaughter, and let not blood be fed with blood.

And since I am carried over a great sea and have given full sails to the winds: there is nothing in all the world that stays fixed. All things flow, and every image is formed in passing; time itself glides on in ceaseless motion, no differently than a river — for neither the river nor the fleeting hour can stop, but, as wave is driven by wave, and the one before is pressed by the one that comes and presses the one before, so the moments flee alike and follow alike, and are always new: for what was before is left behind, and what was not comes to be, and every instant is renewed.

You see the spent nights press on toward the light, and this bright radiance follow the black night; nor is the color the same in the sky when all things lie weary in mid-rest, and when Lucifer comes out bright on his white horse, and again when the daughter of Pallas, herald of the light, tints the world she will hand to Phoebus. The god's own disk, when it rises from the earth's edge, is red in the morning, and red when it hides at the earth's edge; at its height it is bright white, because there the nature of the aether is purer and flees far the earth's infection. Nor can the shape of nocturnal Diana ever be equal or the same, but always today's is less than tomorrow's if she is waxing, greater if she draws in her circle.

And do you not see the year pass through four seasons, giving a likeness of our life? For in early spring it is tender and milky and most like the age of a child: then the fresh blade, without strength, swells and is soft and delights the farmer with hope; all is in flower, and the kindly field plays with the colors of blossoms, and there is not yet any strength in the leaves. After spring the year passes, sturdier, into summer, and becomes a strong young man: no age is more robust, more fruitful, or burns with more warmth. Then comes autumn, the ardor of youth laid by, ripe and mild, midway between young man and old, temperate, its temples flecked with a little gray. Then comes aged winter, bristling with trembling step, its hair either stripped or white.

Our own bodies too are always changing, without rest, and what we were or are, we shall not be tomorrow. There was a day when, only as seeds and the first hope of men, we lay hidden in our mother's womb; nature laid on her skillful hands and would not have our bodies cramped, shut in the stretched mother's belly, and sent us out from that home into the empty air. Brought to the light, the infant lay strengthless; soon, on all fours, it moved its limbs like the beasts, and little by little, trembling, on knees not yet firm, it stood, its sinews helped by some support. Then it was strong and swift, and passed the span of youth; and, the years of middle life served out, it slides down the sloping path of failing age. This undermines and pulls down the strength of former years: old Milo weeps when he sees the arms that once, in the mass of their solid muscle, were like Hercules', hang loose and weak; the daughter of Tyndarus weeps too when she sees the old woman's wrinkles in her mirror, and asks herself why she was twice carried off. Time, devourer of things, and you, envious Age, destroy all, and, gnawed by the teeth of the years, you consume all things little by little in a slow death.

Nor do these things we call the elements stay fixed; give me your minds, and I will teach the changes they go through. The everlasting universe holds four generative bodies; of them two are heavy and are carried downward by their weight — earth and water — and as many lack weight and, with nothing pressing them, seek the heights — air, and fire purer than air. Though they stand apart in space, still all things are made from them and fall back into them: the loosened earth thins into flowing water, the water, made fine, passes off into breeze and air, and the air, its weight taken away, shoots up, finest of all, into the fires of the aether; then they come back again, and the same order is unwoven — for fire, thickened, passes into dense air, this into water, and earth is packed from the water made dense. Nor does its own shape stay with each; and nature, the renewer, makes new shapes from old. In all this vast world, believe me, nothing perishes, but it varies and renews its face; and to be born is to begin to be something other than before, and to die is to cease to be the same — though perhaps these things are moved here, those there, yet in the whole all things endure.

I could not believe anything lasts long under one form. So, ages, you came down from gold to iron; so the fortune of places has so often turned. I have seen what was once most solid earth be sea, and lands made from the sea; far from the ocean sea-shells lay, and an old anchor was found on a mountaintop; a rush of water made a valley of what was a plain, and a flood carried a mountain down into the sea; ground that was swampy is parched with dry sand, and places that had borne drought are wet with standing pools. Here nature has opened new springs, there shut them; and rivers, roused by old earthquakes, have burst forth, or, drying, sunk down. So, when Lycus is swallowed by a chasm in the earth, it rises again far off with another mouth; so the great Erasinus is now drunk up, now, gliding with hidden stream, is given back on the Argive plains; and they say the Mysian Caicus, weary of its source and its former banks, now goes another way; and Amenanus, rolling the Sicilian sands, now flows, now is dry, its springs stopped up. Once the Anigrus was drunk; now it pours waters you would not touch, since — unless all faith is to be taken from the poets — the Centaurs there washed the wounds made by the bow of club-bearing Hercules. And does not the Hypanis too, sprung from the Scythian mountains, once sweet, grow foul with bitter salt? Antissa and Pharos and Phoenician Tyre were once ringed by waves; none of them is now an island. The old settlers had Leucas joined to the mainland; now the sea surrounds it. Zancle too, they say, was joined to Italy, until the sea took away the border and pushed back the land with a wave between. If you seek Helice and Buris, cities of Achaia, you will find them under the water, and sailors still point out the sunken towns with their drowned walls. Near Troezen of Pittheus there is a hill without any trees, once the flattest stretch of a plain, now a hill; for — dreadful to tell — the wild force of the winds, shut in dark caverns, longing to breathe out somewhere and struggling in vain for freer air, since there was no crack in all their prison and no way out for their blasts, swelled the stretched ground, as breath from the mouth inflates a bladder or the hide stripped from a two-horned goat; that swelling stayed on the spot, took the look of a high hill, and hardened in the long years.

Though many instances come to mind, heard and known, I will add a few more. Does not water too give and take new forms? At noon your water is icy, horned Ammon, and at the rising and setting of the sun it is warm; the Athamanians, they say, kindle wood by pouring on their water when the moon has shrunk to her smallest circle. The Cicones have a river that turns the drinker's inwards to stone and lays marble on things it touches; the Crathis, and the Sybaris near it, in our own country, make the hair like amber and gold. And, what is more wondrous, there are waters that can change not only bodies but minds too. Who has not heard of shameful Salmacis and the Ethiopian lake — whose water, if a man drinks it, drives him mad or sinks him in a strangely heavy sleep? Whoever eases his thirst at the Clitorian spring flees wine and, sober, delights in pure water — whether there is a power in the water opposed to heating wine, or, as the natives tell, after the son of Amythaon, by charm and herb, had freed the maddened daughters of Proetus from their frenzy, he threw the mind's cleansings into that stream, and a hatred of wine stayed in the waters. The Lyncestian river runs opposite in effect: whoever draws of it with unruly throat reels as if he had drunk unmixed wine. There is a place in Arcadia the ancients called Pheneos, suspect for its double-natured water: fear it at night — drunk at night it harms, by day it is drunk without hurt. So lakes and rivers have, some one power, some another. There was a time when Ortygia floated on the waves; now it sits still; the Argo feared the Clashing Rocks scattered by the surge of the dashing waters, which now stand unmoved and resist the winds. Nor will Etna, that burns with sulphurous furnaces, always be fiery — nor was it always fiery. For if the earth is a living thing and breathes and has vents in many places that exhale flame, it can change the ways of its breathing and, whenever it stirs, close these caverns and open those; or if light winds are shut in its lowest caves and hurl rock against rock and matter holding the seeds of flame, and it catches fire at the blows, the caves will grow cold when the winds are calmed; or if bituminous forces catch fire, or yellow sulphur burns with a faint smoke — then, when the earth no longer gives food and rich fuel to the flame, its strength spent in the long ages, and nature's nourishment fails its greedy fire, it will not bear the hunger, and, abandoned, will abandon its flames.

There are men, they say, in northern Pallene, who are wont to veil their bodies in light feathers when they have gone nine times under the Tritonian lake; I do not believe it — though the Scythian women, their limbs sprinkled with drugs, are said to practice the same arts. But if any faith is to be given to things proved by experience — do you not see that whatever bodies waste away by time or dissolving heat turn into small living things? Bury slain oxen in a pit — the thing is known by use — and from the rotting flesh, everywhere, flower-gathering bees are born, that, like their parents, work the fields and toil in hope. The warhorse buried in the ground is the source of the hornet; take the curved claws from a shore-crab and put the rest under the earth, and from the buried part a scorpion will come out and threaten with its hooked tail; and the caterpillars that weave gray threads over the leaves — a thing farmers have watched — change their form to the deadly moth. Mud holds the seeds that breed green frogs, and breeds them without feet; soon it gives them legs fit for swimming; and, that the same may be fit for long leaps, the back pair outdo the front. Nor is the cub the bear bears at birth anything but a barely-living lump of flesh: by licking, the mother shapes it into limbs and brings it to a form like her own. Do you not see that the young of the honey-bees, hidden in the six-sided wax, are born without limbs, and only later take on feet and wings? Who would think — did he not know it happens — that the bird of Juno, which carries stars on its tail, and the eagle, the armor-bearer of Jove, and the doves of Venus, and all the race of birds come to be from the middle of an egg? Some believe that human marrow, when the spine has rotted in the closed tomb, changes into a snake.

Yet these draw their origin from other things; there is one bird that renews and reseeds itself: the Assyrians call it the phoenix. It lives not on grain or grass, but on the tears of frankincense and the juice of balsam. When it has filled the five ages of its life, it builds itself, with talons and clean beak, a nest in the branches of an oak or on the top of a swaying palm; and, when it has strewn there cassia and spikenard and bruised cinnamon with tawny myrrh, it lays itself on it and ends its life amid the fragrance. From there, they say, from the parent's body, a small phoenix is reborn, to live as many years; and when age has given it strength to bear the weight, it lifts the nest's burden from the boughs of the tall tree and, dutiful, carries its own cradle and its father's tomb, and, reaching the city of Hyperion through the light air, lays it before the sacred doors in Hyperion's temple.

And if there is anything of strange novelty in these, we may marvel more that the hyena changes its sex, and that the one which just now, a female, took the male, is now itself a male; and that the creature fed on wind and air at once takes on whatever colors it touches. Conquered India gave lynxes to grape-crowned Bacchus, and, they say, whatever their bladder gives off turns to stone and hardens at the touch of the air. So coral too, at the first moment it touches the air, grows hard: it was a soft plant beneath the waves.

The day will fail me, and Phoebus will dip his panting steeds in the deep sea, before I can take in with my words all that is changed into new forms. So we see the ages turn, and these nations gain strength while those fall: so Troy was great in wealth and men and could give so much blood for ten years, but now, laid low, shows only her old ruins and, for riches, the tombs of her ancestors. Sparta was famous, great Mycenae flourished, and the citadels of Cecrops and of Amphion; now Sparta is a worthless spot, lofty Mycenae has fallen; what is Thebes of Oedipus but a name, what remains of Athens of Pandion but a name? Now, too, there is report that Dardanian Rome is rising, which, by the waters of the Apennine-born Tiber, lays the foundations of her greatness under a vast structure: she then, by growing, changes her form, and one day will be the head of the boundless world. So the seers say, and the fate-telling oracles; and, as I recall, Helenus, son of Priam, said to weeping Aeneas, doubtful of his safety, when the Trojan state was failing: "Son of a goddess, if you well grasp the presage of my mind, Troy will not wholly fall while you live! Flame and sword will give you a way: you will go, and carry off ruined Pergamum with you, until a foreign soil, kinder than your native land, falls to Troy and to you; and I see even now that Phrygian descendants are destined to build a city so great as none is, or will be, or was seen in earlier years. Other chiefs will make her mighty through long ages, but one born of the blood of Iulus will make her mistress of the world; and when earth has had the use of him, the heavenly halls will hold him, and heaven will be his end." This, I remember, Helenus prophesied to Aeneas, bearer of the household gods from Troy; and I rejoice that my kindred walls are rising, and that to such good end the Greeks conquered the Phrygians.

But, that we not stray too far with steeds forgetful of the goal — the heavens, and all beneath them, change their forms, and the earth and all in it. We too, a part of the world, since we are not only bodies but fleeting souls as well, and can go into the homes of beasts and be hidden in the breasts of cattle — let us leave safe and unharmed the bodies that may hold the souls of our parents, or brothers, or those joined to us by some tie, or at least of men, and not heap our tables as at the feast of Thyestes! How ill he schools himself, how he readies himself for human blood, the impious man who cuts the calf's throat with the knife and lends a deaf ear to its lowing, or who can kill the kid that cries like a child, or feed on the bird he himself has fed! How little is lacking there for utter crime? What a short step onward is prepared! Let the ox plow, or owe its death to old age; let the sheep give us defense against shivering Boreas; let the well-fed she-goats offer their udders to be pressed by the hand. Away with nets and snares and nooses and cunning tricks! Do not deceive the bird with the limed twig, nor pen the deer with dreaded feathers, nor hide the barbed hook in treacherous bait.

If anything is harmful, destroy it — but even so, only destroy it: keep your mouths free of blood, and take the gentler food!

His heart schooled with such teachings and others, Numa, they say, returned to his country and, invited of their own will, took up the reins of the Roman people. Blessed with a nymph for his wife and the Muses for his guides, he taught the rites of sacrifice, and led a nation used to fierce war over to the arts of peace. When, full of years, he had finished his reign and his life, the Latian matrons and the people and the fathers wept for Numa dead; but his wife left the city and lay hidden in the thick groves of the vale of Aricia, and by her groaning and lament disturbed the rites of Diana, brought there by Orestes. Ah, how often the nymphs of the grove and the lake begged her not to, and spoke words of comfort! Ah, how often the hero, the son of Theseus, said to her as she wept, "Set a limit to it; your lot is not the only one to be mourned. Look at the like disasters of others: you will bear yours the more mildly — and would that examples not my own could lighten your grief! But even mine can.

"If, in talk, it has ever reached your ears that a certain Hippolytus died through his father's credulity, by the trickery of a wicked stepmother, you will be amazed, and I shall hardly prove it — but I am he. Once the daughter of Pasiphaë, having tried in vain to tempt me to defile my father's bed, pretended I had wished what she herself wished, and, turning the charge about — whether more from fear of exposure or from spite at the rebuff — condemned me; and my father, though I was guiltless, drove me from the city and, as I went, cursed my head with cruel prayers. In my flying chariot I was making for Troezen, the city of Pittheus, and now was skirting the shores of the Corinthian gulf, when the sea rose, and a huge heap of water seemed to bend into the shape of a mountain and to grow and to give a bellowing and to split at its very peak; and out of the burst waves a horned bull was thrown, and, reared breast-high into the light air, spewed a mass of sea-water from his nostrils and open mouth. My companions' hearts quailed; my mind stayed unafraid, intent only on my exile — when the fierce horses turned their necks to the sea and shied with pricked-up ears, and, terrified of the monster, dashed the chariot over the high rocks. I struggled, with useless hand, to hold the reins white with foam, and, thrown back, pulled at the slack traces. Yet my strength would not have beaten the madness of the horses, had not the wheel, running against a stump, been broken and torn from the long axle. I was hurled from the chariot, and, the reins tangling my limbs, you might have seen my living guts dragged, my sinews caught on the stump, my limbs partly torn away and partly held back, my broken bones give a heavy sound, and my spent breath breathe out — and no part of my body you could recognize: the whole of me was one wound. Can you, or dare you, nymph, set your misfortune beside mine? I have seen, too, the lightless realm, and bathed my mangled body in the wave of Phlegethon; nor would life have been given back to me but for the powerful medicine of Apollo's son. When, by strong herbs and the healing art, I had it back, against Pluto's will, then Cynthia, that I might not by my presence deepen his anger at the gift, threw thick clouds around me; and, that I might be safe and be seen unharmed, she added years to my look and left me no face that could be known, and long she wavered whether to give me Crete or Delos to hold; and, leaving Crete and Delos, she set me here, and bade me lay aside the name that might remind me of my horses. 'You who were Hippolytus,' she said, 'be now Virbius.' Since then I haunt this grove and, one of the lesser gods, lie hidden under my mistress's power, and am numbered among her own."

Yet the misfortunes of others could not ease Egeria's grief; and, lying at the mountain's foot, she melted into tears, until the sister of Phoebus, moved by the love of her sorrow, made a cool fountain from her body and thinned her limbs into ever-flowing waters.

This new thing amazed the nymphs, and the son of the Amazon was astonished no otherwise than when the Etruscan ploughman saw the fateful clod in the middle of the field move first of itself, no one touching it, then take on a human shape and lose the earth's, and open its new-made mouth with the destinies to come — the natives called him Tages, the first to teach the Etruscan nation to unfold the future; or as when Romulus once saw his spear, fixed on the Palatine hill, suddenly put out leaves, standing now on a new-made root, not on the driven iron, and, now no dart but a tree of pliant twigs, give the wondering onlookers an unlooked-for shade; or as when Cipus saw his own horns in the stream's water — for he did see them — and, thinking the reflection false, kept bringing his fingers back to his brow and touched what he saw; and, no longer blaming his eyes, he stopped where he was, coming home victorious over the beaten enemy, and, raising his eyes and his hands to heaven, cried, "Whatever this marvel foretells, you gods above — if it is happy, let it be happy for my country and the people of Quirinus; if it threatens, let it threaten me." And he soothes the grassy altars, built of green turf, with fragrant fires, and pours wine from bowls, and consults the quivering entrails of slain sheep for their meaning. When the Etruscan seer looked at them, he saw great designs of things in them, but not clear; but when he lifted his keen eye from the sheep's flesh to the horns of Cipus, he said, "Hail, O king! For you, Cipus, you and your horns this place and the Latian towers will obey. Only break off delay, and hurry to enter the open gates! So the fates command: for, once received in the city, you will be king, and safely hold a lasting scepter." He stepped back, and, turning his stern face from the walls of the city, said, "Far, far may the gods drive all such omens! Far more rightly will I pass my life in exile than see the Capitol make me king." He spoke, and at once called together the people and the grave senate; but first he veiled his horns with the laurel of peace, and stood on a mound raised by his brave soldiers, and, praying to the old gods in the ancient way, said, "There is one here who will be king unless you drive him from the city. Who he is I will tell you by a sign, not a name: he wears horns on his brow! The augur warns you that, if he enters Rome, he will make you slaves by his laws. He could have burst through the open gates, but I opposed him — though no one is more closely bound to him than I. Keep the man from the city, Romans; or, if he deserves it, bind him with heavy chains, or end your fear by the death of the destined tyrant." Such a murmur as rises in the tall pines when the fierce east wind whistles through them, or as the sea-waves make if one hears them far off, so the people sounded; but through their confused, roaring words one cry stood out: "Which is he?" And they scan the foreheads and look for the foretold horns. Cipus spoke to them again: "The one you seek, you have," and, tearing the wreath from his head though the people forbade it, showed his temples marked with a twin horn. All cast down their eyes and groaned, and — who could believe it? — unwilling, looked on that head famous for its merits; and, no longer letting it lack its honor, they set the festive wreath on it. And the nobles, since you were forbidden to enter the walls, gave you, Cipus, as a mark of honor, as much land as you could enclose with the plow, oxen yoked, from the sun's rising to its setting; and they carved the horns, keeping their wondrous shape, on the bronze-plated doorposts, to last through the long ages.

Now tell, Muses, the present powers of poets — for you know, and the wide reach of time does not deceive you — how the island washed by the deep Tiber's stream added the son of Coronis to the sacred rites of Romulus's city. A dreadful plague had once fouled the Latian air, and pale bodies were wasted by a bloodless sickness. When, weary with funerals, they saw that mortal efforts availed nothing, and the arts of healers nothing, they sought help from heaven, and, going to Delphi that holds the middle place of the world, the oracle of Phoebus, they begged that he would come to their misery with a health-bringing answer and end so great a city's woes. The place, and the laurels, and the very quivers he holds trembled together, and the tripod gave this voice from the depths of the shrine and shook their frightened breasts: "What you seek here, Roman, you should have sought in a nearer place; and seek it now in a nearer place: you need not Apollo to lessen your grief, but Apollo's son. Go with good omens, and summon my offspring."

When the prudent senate had received the god's command, they searched out which city the young son of Phoebus dwelt in, and sent men to reach the shores of Epidaurus with the winds. As soon as those sent had touched there in the curved ship, they came to the council and the Greek elders and begged them to grant the god who, present, might end the deaths of the Ausonian nation — for so the sure oracle had said. Opinion was split and wavering; some thought aid should not be refused, many urged them to keep their own protector and not send away their guardian god. While they hesitated, the twilight had driven off the fading day, and the earth's shadow had drawn darkness over the world, when in your sleep, Roman, the saving god seemed to stand before your couch, just as he is wont to be in his temple, holding a rustic staff in his left hand and stroking the long hair of his beard with his right, and to speak such words from his gentle breast: "Lay aside your fears! I will come, and leave these statues of mine. Only look at this serpent that winds its coils about my staff, and mark it well with your eyes, so you can know it: into this I will change, but I shall be greater, and seem as great as heavenly bodies ought to be when they are transformed." At once, with these words, the god departed, and with his words and the god sleep departed, and kindly light followed the flight of sleep.

The next dawn had scattered the starry fires; unsure what to do, the nobles met in the god's rich temple and begged him to show, by heavenly signs, in what place he would abide. Scarcely had they ceased when the golden god, in the serpent's form, with high crest, sent forth a hiss to announce his coming, and at his approach shook his statue and the altars and doors and marble floor and gilded roof, and, reared breast-high in the middle of the temple, rolled around his fire-flashing eyes. The frightened crowd trembled; the priest, his chaste hair bound with a white fillet, knew the god and cried, "The god — behold the god! Be of good omen in mind and tongue, whoever is here! May you be seen to our good, most beautiful one, and aid the peoples that keep your rites!" All present worship the god as bidden, and all repeat the priest's words again, and the sons of Aeneas give holy favor in heart and voice. The god nods to this, and, shaking his crest, gives sure pledges with his darting tongue thrice repeated; then he glides down the polished steps and turns his face back, and, about to leave, looks back at his old altars and salutes his wonted home and the temple he has so long lived in. Then, through the ground strewn and covered with scattered flowers, he glides, huge, coiling his folds, and through the middle of the city makes for the harbor guarded by its curving quay. Here he stopped, and, seeming with a calm face to dismiss his train and the dutiful crowd that followed, laid his body on the Ausonian ship; it felt the god's weight, and the keel was pressed low by the god's heaviness. The sons of Aeneas rejoice, and, a bull first slain on the shore, loose the twisted cables of the garlanded ship. A light breeze drove the ship on; the god stands out on high, pressing the curved stern with his neck laid on it, and looks down on the blue water; and with gentle west winds along the Ionian sea, at the sixth rising of the dawn, he reached Italy, and was borne past the Lacinian shore, famed for the goddess Juno's temple, and the Scylacean coast; he leaves Iapygia, and flees the Amphrisian rocks with oars to the left, the steep Cocinthian on the right, and coasts Romethium and Caulon and Narycia, and crosses the strait and the narrows of Sicilian Pelorus, and the home of King Aeolus, grandson of Hippotas, and the mines of Temesa, and makes for Leucosia and the rose-beds of warm Paestum. Then he coasts Capreae, and the headland of Minerva, and the hills noble with the Surrentine vine, and the city of Hercules, and Stabiae, and Parthenope made for ease, and, past it, the temple of the Cumaean Sibyl. Then the warm springs are passed, and mastic-bearing Liternum, and Volturnus dragging much sand under its stream, and Sinuessa busy with white doves, and heavy Minturnae, and the town her foster-child gave a tomb, and the house of Antiphates, and Trachas ringed by marsh, and the land of Circe, and Antium with its rocky shore. When the sailors turned the sail-bearing ship here — for now the sea was rough — the god unfolds his coils and, gliding in many a fold and great winding, enters the temple of his father that skirts the yellow shore. The sea calmed, the god of Epidaurus leaves his father's altars, and, having enjoyed the welcome of the god akin to him, furrows the shore sand with the drag of his rattling scales, and, leaning on the helm, laid his head on the high stern, until he came to Castrum and the sacred seats of Lavinium and the mouths of the Tiber. Here all the people, a crowd of matrons and men alike, rush to meet him — and they too who keep your fires, Vesta — and hail the god with glad shouts. And where the swift ship is steered up the stream, incense crackles on the altars set in rows along both banks, and perfumes the air with smoke, and the felled victim warms the knives thrust in it. And now he had entered Rome, the head of the world: the serpent rises erect, lifts his neck that rested on the masthead, and looks around for a home fit for himself. There is a spot where the flowing river splits in two — it is called the Island — and stretches out from either side equal arms, the dry land in the middle. Here the serpent, son of Phoebus, came from the Latian ship, and, taking back his heavenly form, put an end to the mourning, and came, the bringer of health, to the City.

Yet he came a stranger to our shrines: Caesar is a god in his own city, whom, chief in war and in peace, not his wars ended in triumphs, nor his deeds at home, nor his swift-won glory turned into a new star, a comet with streaming hair, so much as his own offspring. For of all the deeds of Caesar there is none greater than that he was the father of this our Caesar. Was it more, then, to have subdued the sea-girt Britons, and steered victorious ships up the seven-mouthed streams of the papyrus-bearing Nile, and added to the people of Quirinus the rebel Numidians and Cinyphian Juba and Pontus swollen with the fame of Mithridates, and earned many triumphs and held some — than to have fathered so great a man, under whose rule over the world you gods have shown abundant care for the human race? That this man, then, might not be born of mortal seed, Julius had to be made a god. When the golden mother of Aeneas saw this, and saw too that a sad death was being prepared for the high priest, and the conspirators' arms were being moved, she went pale, and said to each of the gods as she met them: "See on how vast a scale treason is plotted against me, and with what great guile that life is sought which alone is left me from Dardanian Iulus! Shall I alone be forever harried by just cares — I, whom now the Calydonian spear of the son of Tydeus wounds, now the walls of Troy, defended in vain, grieve, who have seen my son driven through long wanderings, tossed on the sea, entering the homes of the silent, and waging war with Turnus, or, if we tell the truth, with Juno rather? Why do I now recall the old wrongs of my line? This fear does not let me remember the past. Look, you see the wicked swords whetted — keep them off, I beg, and thwart the crime, and do not, by the priest's murder, put out the fires of Vesta!"

Such anxious words Venus threw in vain through all heaven, and moved the gods above; who, though they could not break the iron decrees of the ancient sisters, still gave no doubtful signs of the coming grief. They say that arms clashing among the black clouds, and dreadful trumpets and horns heard in the sky, warned of the crime; the sad face of the sun, too, gave a lurid light to the anxious earth; often torches seemed to burn among the stars, often drops of blood fell in the rain; the Morning Star was dark, his face sprinkled with black rust, the chariot of the moon sprinkled with blood; in a thousand places the Stygian owl gave sad omens, in a thousand places the ivory statues wept, and dirges, they say, were heard, and threatening words, in the sacred groves. No victim gave a favorable omen; the entrails warned that great tumults were near, and a cut-off head was found among them; and in the Forum, and around the houses and the temples of the gods, dogs howled by night, they say, and the ghosts of the silent walked, and the City was shaken by tremors. Yet the gods' warnings could not overcome treachery and the fate to come, and drawn swords were carried into the temple: for no other place in the whole city pleased them for the crime and the dreadful murder than the senate-house. Then indeed Venus beat her breast with both hands, and made to hide the son of Aeneas in the cloud in which Paris was once snatched from the hostile son of Atreus, and Aeneas escaped the sword of Diomedes. Her father spoke to her thus: "Do you, daughter, alone try to move the unconquerable fate? You may enter the house of the three sisters yourself: there you will see, in a vast structure of bronze and solid iron, the records of things, which fear neither the crash of the sky nor the wrath of the lightning nor any ruin, safe and eternal; there you will find the fates of your line engraved in everlasting adamant. I myself have read them and marked them in my mind, and I will tell them, that you may not still be ignorant of the future. He for whom you labor, Cytherea, has filled his time, the years ended that he owed to earth. That he may enter heaven as a god and be worshiped in temples, you will bring it about, with his son, who, heir to his name, will bear alone the burden laid on him, and, our bravest avenger of his murdered father, will have us to aid him in his wars. Under his command the besieged walls of Mutina, beaten, will sue for peace; Pharsalia will feel him, and Philippi again be soaked with Emathian blood, and the great name will be beaten in the Sicilian waves; and the Egyptian wife of the Roman general, trusting ill in that match, will fall, and in vain will she have threatened that our Capitol should serve her Canopus. Why count you the barbarian lands and the nations by either ocean? Whatever the habitable earth bears will be his — the sea too will serve him! Peace given to the lands, he will turn his mind to civil law, and, most just of lawgivers, bring in laws, and by his own example rule men's ways; and, looking to the age of the time to come and his coming descendants, he will bid the child born of his holy wife bear both his name and his cares; nor, until as an old man he has matched his years to his deeds, will he reach the heavenly seats and his kindred stars. Meanwhile, this soul, snatched from the murdered body, make into a star, so that the deified Julius may always look down from his high place on our Capitol and Forum."

He had barely said this when kindly Venus, seen by none, stood in the middle of the senate-house and snatched the soul, just freed, from the limbs of her Caesar, and, not letting it dissolve into air, bore it up among the stars of heaven; and, as she bore it, she felt it take light and catch fire, and let it go from her breast: higher than the moon it flies, and, drawing a flaming trail through a wide track, gleams as a star; and, seeing his son's good deeds, it owns them greater than its own, and rejoices to be surpassed by him. Though the son forbids his own deeds to be set above his father's, still Fame, free and bound by no commands, prefers him against his will, and in this one thing disobeys him. So great Atreus yields to the glory of Agamemnon, so Theseus outdoes Aegeus, so Achilles Peleus; and, to use examples that match their own case, so too Saturn is less than Jove. Jupiter rules the heavenly halls and the realms of the threefold world; the earth is under Augustus; each is a father and a ruler. Gods, companions of Aeneas, before whom sword and fire gave way; and you, native gods; and Quirinus, father of the City; and Mars, father of unconquered Quirinus; and Vesta, held sacred among the household gods of Caesar; and with Caesar's Vesta, you, Phoebus of the home; and Jupiter, who hold on high the Tarpeian heights; and whatever other gods a poet may rightly call on — late, I pray, be that day, and beyond my own time, on which Augustus, leaving the world he rules, shall reach heaven, and, gone from us, listen favorably to those who pray.

And now I have finished a work that neither the anger of Jove, nor fire, nor sword, nor devouring time can destroy. Let that day, which has power over nothing but this body of mine, end when it will the span of my uncertain life; yet in my better part I shall be borne immortal above the high stars, and my name shall never be blotted out; and wherever Roman power reaches over the conquered lands, I shall be read on the lips of the people, and — if the prophecies of poets have any truth — through all the ages, in fame, I shall live.
